my project

Case Study 1


17 May 1999		 THE GREAT DIVIDE
MEERA SETH
Elrax had acquired two Indian companies -- Bristol and Gapro -- as part of its entry strategy for India. But the merged entity was not a unified one
  DHANRAJ Nayar studied the audience in front of him. Twenty-two men sat there, waiting for the session to begin. He greeted them with a warm smile and asked: "Are the people from the components business and systems and services well mingled?"
   There was an embarrassed silence after which someone said: "That side is Gapro, this side is us." Nayar winced. `Them' and `us'. Who was `us', he wondered, annoyed.
  Michael Wood, the human resources (HR) director for Asia, who sat a foot away from where Nayar stood, looked up, his eyebrows arching up. "That's what we will discuss soon," whispered Nayar, as he presented Wood to the audience.
  Nayar was the HR head of Elrax India and what he had just heard augured well for the theme of his discussions with Wood, later in the day. It had been 15 months since Elrax commenced operations in India and Wood's visit was specifically to review how the Elrax ideology had spread in the Indian team.
  In December 1997, Elrax International, as part of its India entry strategy, took over two companies in Baroda -- Bristol Components, which made components for infrastructure projects, and Gapro Consultants, which implemented projects. Other takeovers were on the cards, but with Bristol and Gapro in the bag, Elrax commenced operations. It was, in a sense, a new business for Elrax, for in no other market did it have components and project installation as joint businesses.
  Now, Bristol and Gapro shared an interesting relationship. For most of its projects, Gapro sourced its component requirements from Bristol. Bristol, in turn, also nosed around for customer jobs, partnered Gapro and monitored project installation and quality control. Of course, these were few, for Bristol was mostly a component manufacturer and had a good reputation in the market, thanks mainly to its English parentage. By the mid-nineties, Bristol, UK, began to lose interest in India. It was exiting the components business across the world, and so the Indian subsidiary was offered to Elrax. So the sale was understood by the Indian subsidiary as being purely for business and strategic reasons. On the other hand, the east European parent of Gapro found its Indian arm unprofitable and tedious to administer, and thus sold the company. Not surprisingly, the Gapro employees felt that they were not good enough.
  Nayar could see that they felt that Elrax had bailed them out. That was combined with an awe of Elrax, the big, smart US company. And this feeling came to the fore all the time. For instance, at the `Welcome to the Elrax Family' evening, Ashwini Singh, senior manager at Gapro, had said: "We are grateful to be part of Elrax. It's an honour to be part of such a great company. We do hope we will meet your expectations and match your standards of excellence." And Pradeep Kumar, speaking for Bristol, said: "This combination can only be good for all of us. Elrax will add to our strength and we will add to theirs."
  Elrax saw the erstwhile customer-supplier relationship between Bristol and Gapro as an advantage. But the difference between the two was reflected in the places where they used to work. Gapro had its offices in Madia House, which was not a very well maintained building. The lifts barely worked and as you climbed the stairs to the fourth floor office, you couldn't help noticing the chipped walls, broken banisters and the huge blotches of paan on the walls.
  Bristol, on the other hand, was located in Jay Plaza, and although it had seen better days, was better maintained. The first two floors had marble steps; luckily for Bristol, its office was on the second floor, and the marble lent it an air of opulence.
  Thus although it was Gapro which executed and implemented the jobs, it was Bristol which bustled with activity, because all meetings were held at Jay Plaza. As Kumar said: "Madia House is a mess. Even a dog won't wander into it by mistake. How then do we take our clients there?"

Bristol and Gapro had a supplier-customer relationship before the merger. However, Bristol's managers considered themselves superior to those at Gapro, treating the latter with disdain.

  So over time Gapro had come to be known as the Madia House office and with it also acquired Bristol's displeasure that was once reserved only for the building. The arrangement continued after the takeover, since Elrax was yet to find office space. And the two divisions continued to be referred to as Madia House and Jay Plaza.
  For Nayar this was undesirable and every time he got a chance he told Hutton, the country manager, that an integration plan had to be put in place. But Hutton had a different viewpoint. "We hardly know these people, even if we know their companies," said Hutton. "Don't rush, observe them for six months and then draw up your plan." But Nayar worried. "Let us at least restructure the business operations and form more cohesive and mixed teams," he suggested. "It is important that they drop their old customer-supplier relationship."

  For Nayar this was undesirable and every time he got a chance he told Hutton, the country manager, that an integration plan had to be put in place. But Hutton had a different viewpoint. "We hardly know these people, even if we know their companies," said Hutton. "Don't rush, observe them for six months and then draw up your plan." But Nayar worried. "Let us at least restructure the business operations and form more cohesive and mixed teams," he suggested. "It is important that they drop their old customer-supplier relationship."
  "That relationship is but natural," said Hutton. "After all, they were supplier and customer once. Even now, don't forget, Gapro will be an internal customer of Bristol. But these differences will sort themselves out over time. We will work on the Elrax ethos and everything else will fall in place."
  And Hutton worked on the Elrax ethos. He brought in Elrax's performance management system, quality management and audit systems. While the feeling at Bristol was that 'all this is just a small tinkering with our systems', Gapro was stunned by the sophistication and detail. For Gapro hardly had any system in its old days. Just a set of forms to be filled and despatched to a faceless name far away. But now, in the face of Elrax's systems, Ashwini depended on Kumar for every little move he had to make, thereby allowing Bristol to dictate even Gapro's operations. Now since Elrax was combining components and service for the first time, and there were no laid-down systems for operations, Bristol slowly elbowed out every Gapro system and the Gapro team cowered in submission. Increasingly, it became apparent to Nayar that the integration of the two companies was not going to be smooth. "This is not one of those regular integrations," he warned his director. "As far as buying into the Elrax ethos is concerned, there is no conflict, since both are delighted with the takeover. But because one was superior to the other, there will be an undercurrent of domination by Bristol if we do not address that first."
  "Just wait for a year," said Hutton. "Let my successor move in. He can take the decision on what is best for the business."
  The person who was chosen to succeed Hutton was the general manager (business operations) of Bristol, Atul Jain. Nayar did not think that was a good idea. "It would be better if a dispassionate third party is brought in as the managing director," he suggested.
  But the parent company did not agree. "Where are we going to find a person who understands the details and intricacies of this business as well as these guys?" asked Hutton. "This is a technically driven business, so let us not have too many new variables. We need continuity to get a headstart in the market. Components manufacturing is critical to the end quality of the service business. It is wise that we appoint a Bristol man as managing director, and later we will see what to do."
  Jain was not partial or biased, but Nayar felt he was still full of Bristol and its past. And Jain was overwhelmed by the enormity of his new position and by Elrax. He felt his new position was also a function of the greatness of Bristol. So every once in a while, Jain would remark: "I see a lot of similarities between Bristol and Elrax. How similar our approach is."
  Like Jain, Gapro, too, looked for similarities, and not finding it, believed that Bristol was in fact the better of the two. To make matters worse, a new inference was drawn that `whatever Elrax is in India today is really Bristol', and the Bristol people started wallowing in that superiority.
  Three months after Jain had taken over, Nayar met him in his new office and appealed to Jain. "We need to shed all these old identities we are carrying. Why don't we start a new Elrax cultural integration, where we shuffle the entire team and create a new team of components and services and a coordination team comprising both? There will be terrific opportunities to upgrade people and skills, find new opportunities for growth for employees and even learn new skills."
  But Jain's response surprised Nayar. "These Gapro people are rather slow on the uptake. It would be a big risk to try what you are suggesting. We need to refresh the people there, decide who we want to keep and who we want retired from that side. We must give it some time."
  What business, wondered Nayar. Will there be any business at all if this rift and difference stays? Why, even the country manager feels they are them and we are us. Bristol is going to decide who it is going to retain from Gapro, he thought.
  It was a unique situation. Elrax India now encountered two companies with entirely different pulls and pushes, different stages of development, people with different skill sets. While both of them had their own set of products and customers, the point was that neither was performing very brilliantly. To add to that, the takeover by Elrax also meant an entirely new vision of the market, the business and, of course, targets that were sacred.
  Thus, while the individual revenues of the two companies were in the vicinity of Rs 15-18 crore, Elrax had set a target of Rs 100 crore for the next five months. If Gapro was taken aback, Bristol was less alarmed. As Kumar of Bristol told Ashwini: "It's not difficult, we (read Bristol) can manage. We have had tough taskmasters before and we have always been profitable. But you need to smarten up and keep all systems in place."
  Ashwini was enthused. Speaking to Kumar always comforted him. "How are you going to do it?" he asked him.
  "It's not difficult," replied Kumar with ease. "We could have done it long ago. After all, isn't the market the same? The market hasn't grown because Elrax has come in; it was always there. It just requires the right combination of intellect and money power to rake in the business. If our parent company had not lost interest in the last four-five years, we would be competing with Elrax!" he said with a grin.
  Nayar could see the growing confidence at Bristol, but it was Gapro's timidity that worried him. Would it be able to handle such a vast services function, he wondered. While he was trying to get them together as one company, the huge cultural abyss caused him anxiety.
  The Bristol people were quicker, street-smart and savvy. In contrast, the Gapro people were diffident, quiet and withdrawn. Nayar couldn't help wondering how these men would survive in Elrax. In fact, he wondered how they had managed to stay afloat even in Gapro. After all they had foreign parents and demands must have been placed on them in terms of conversation and performance.
  But some clues lay in what Ashwini said: "The parents came down thrice a year. Their visit was very businesslike.They stayed at the Taj, but no meeting was held there. We met in the office and they brought along their bottles of mineral water."
  In both companies, the senior positions were all held by 50-plus men. Senior positions had perforce been accorded to the older managers, because they believed business maturity came with age. In the restructuring, therefore, Nayar had to consider the cultural issues of having younger divisional heads or even elevating the younger managers at these companies. The senior managers at Bristol were not perturbed. They still felt they were in the reckoning, that they were still with it, still smart.... But the seniors at Gapro were worried. It was apparent that Elrax worldwide had a much younger set of employees and that was going to be the trend in India. Did the Elrax people think they were smart enough to take part in the growth?
  Whatever the alarm they had felt initially over the targets, the teams were also achieving it, thanks to their own resilience -- and an anxiety to keep their jobs. Most senior managers, unused to the tremendous pressure of managing such a large portfolio, worked harder than they ever had. But it took its toll -- there were a confusion of systems, old versus new, Madia House versus Jay Plaza. The last mentioned summed up for Nayar the crux of the problem. What it said was: "Yes, we are all Elrax, but you are Madia House and I am Jay Plaza, which means I am better than you."

Nayar was worried that the Bistol people were getting the upper hand in the merged entity, relegating Gapro's managers to the sidelines. He felt the two groups had to be integrated.

  Then, there also was the business of aligning themselves to the Elrax ideology. Every meeting, however insignificant, was prefixed with an Elrax value statement, the Elrax way of life, not to mention that much of the action was now happening at the Globe Trade Centre office of Elrax which was opulent, slick and knitted together by a set of systems and a degree of automation that neither team could grasp.
  On the other hand, there was the problem of actually understanding what all the new values meant. For instance, Elrax prescribed a reduction in the bureaucracy. But Ashwini continued to be personally involved with reports and statements. He sent daily reports on costs, project status and revenues to Kumar at Jay Plaza, deferring to Bristol's superiority and because Jain insisted on this. Simultaneously, Ashwini was also sending a different set of reports on the new Elrax formats to his new bosses in the US on different parameters, and, in the process getting snowed under by paperwork.
  All in all, their lives had turned topsy turvy. Not only were they now trying to align with Elrax, but also with each other. So it was actually three personalities coming together, with Bristol subconsciously calling the shots, Gapro struggling to gain Bristol's acceptance and constantly reminding itself that it was now an Elrax company. And Elrax was an enigma -- world class, the world's best, which was waltzing into the country and buying one company after another.
  Then there was Bristol, a 100-year-old company worldwide, a name that had bewildered Gapro. Highly systems-driven, very British, very meticulous, a good lineage of managers, aggressive and savvy. There was Gapro worldwide, which was a product of many mergers and acquisitions since the world war, whose management ethos was inspired and drawn from military practices. Most of its managers had never met and many did not even know who their boss was. The parent company had never invested much in Gapro, making its employees feel that they weren't anything more than a set of jobbers in boiler suits to implement the numerous projects. Bristol was only one of its suppliers of parts and components, and some projects came Gapro's way through its recommendations. That had led to some sort of subservience at Gapro, which was grateful for the business. Gapro did not grow as savvily as Bristol did, but Bristol wasn't so salient either. Today, faced with the streamlined Elrax, whose focus was excellence, growth, profits, both suffered varying levels of trepidation.
  Nayar could see the 22 faces before him listening in rapt attention to Wood extolling the Elrax heritage. "So, that is Elrax," Wood was saying, "a company that has drawn the spirit of resilience and perseverance from a young man who gave birth to a germ of an idea after the war had destroyed the spirit of every individual in his land. From there he went about slowly and steadily building a conglomeration of businesses and by the twenties was a leading name in a number of branded consumer and industrial goods. That man is our chairman, Howard Davies, whose spirit we have transmitted to every generation. That is what makes us world class. Today we are a Fortune 500 company, highly regarded by the discerning American public, most respected by the European Community. You should be proud to be part of such a great heritage. Your lives are going to change, people in the marketplace are going to look at you differently. That you are from Elrax will change the way they do business with you, talk to you, even greet you," he said amid laughter.
  It did not make sense to Nayar and he said so to Wood: "As long as they think of themselves as Gapro and Bristol, or superior and inferior, the Bristol ethos will be all-pervading." "It's time to stop referring to them as such," said Wood. "Gapro and Bristol are now only names in the purchase contract and that is already two years old. They are Elrax members," he reminded Nayar. "The differences will persist as long as you separate them in your mind."
  "But first there must be an attempt to physically integrate the two teams," said Nayar. "Only when they sit together, work together and ride the same lift and suffer the same tea boy will there be a real coming together."
  Nothing had really changed, felt Nayar. The takeover had had little impact. The two companies continued to work as before. Post-takeover, Elrax passed on all new business to the components division which in turn was getting it implemented by Gapro. Unwittingly, Elrax had reinforced Bristol's superiority, he felt.
  "What have we achieved?" asked Nayar. "All we have done is bought two companies. What have we done to bridge the differences between the two?"
  "We brought in every Elrax system!" said Wood. "We took them through the Elrax ideology.... You need to have an audit of why that has not affected them."
  "Can we change the way they feel about each other by merely bringing in the Elrax system? I don't think this is a simple integration issue, Mr Wood. There's much more to it," said Nayar. "Yes, we superimposed the performance management process, the quality management system, but we have not integrated them in the real sense. Thus we are not ready to say `You are no longer Gapro or Bristol, but we are Elrax'. Yet superficially we are saying it is a wonderful thing to come together as one. In our minds we are not allowing it to happen."

ALLAY THE FEARS

Aquil Busrai is the executive director of human resources (India & Saarc countries and global software division, Asia-Pacific) at Motorola.
   IT IS time to consider replacing Dhanraj Nayyar. Fifteen months into the merger and he is still groping for direction, mesmerised by the events around him. So much so that he has become a mere bystander, witnessing the drama unfolding before him and unable to influence its direction.Transfixed, he has done very little to influence the change except perhaps for some wishful thinking about the way the management team in Elrax should conduct itself.
   Nayyar has abandoned the charter of human resources (HR) being a catalyst for organisation change. He has looked for neat, traditional solutions in the merged Elrax, looked for well-defined round pegs in round holes and as a result missed the opportunity for some proactive and creative HR interventions which would have brought about a much smoother transition.
  A corporate merger is stressful for everyone involved. People worry about losing their jobs. They worry about who their new bosses will be. They worry about being relocated to some other place. They worry about the future and fret about the present. The first task in managing a merger is to allay those fears by providing stability, structure and honest answers to hard questions. Only after people feel secure can one get down to the difficult business of combining the companies that have now merged. This is one area in which Nayyar and his HR team could have taken a lead.
  The aftermath of the merger requires several different kinds of strategy. One kind is an operating strategy, which, in the long run, will determine whether the merger is a success or a failure. But almost as important -- and in the short run perhaps even more important -- is a communication strategy: a clear, consistent plan for getting out to critical constituencies the key message about the merger. The first step towards managing the people part of the merger is psychological -- to restore a sense of order and confidence that there is productive work to be done in building the future of the unified organisations.
  Ultimately, success in human resource issues will hinge on making good on the economic promise of unification. Nayyar and his HR team could, and should, have championed this cause.
  Both the Bristol and Gapro managements have not identified themselves fully with Elrax's ethos. As a result, the prevailing sub-cultures have been further reinforced. 
  The Bristol management's confidence in its own ability, and Gapro's lack of it has further alienated both of them from the common Elrax culture. But then, simply grafting the corporate culture of the acquiring company onto the acquired one does not usually work -- or at best, takes a very long time.
  The sheer dynamism and confidence levels of the erstwhile Bristol managers have put them in the driver's seat in the amalgamated Elrax. But the same management has not capitalised on the strength of the parent company's systems and processes. Instead, it has taken upon itself the task of 'reforming' the management practices of the erstwhile Gapro.
   All these are wasteful practices and are in no way contributing to making a stronger Elrax in India. 
  Mergers offer business leaders a chance to pursue innovative and creative solutions to tough problems. In this context, the Elrax management has made its processes and systems available to the merged entity more as a matter of routine. No serious efforts have been made to integrate the parent company's processes with the business needs of the newly acquired companies. Perhaps because the merger has not adversely impacted the business deliveries, giving an overall impression that everything is running smoothly after the merger. Why fix something that is not broken?
  Most corporate change programmes mistake means for ends, processes for outcomes. The solution, however, lies in focusing on results, not activities.
  Consider a corporate setting: an acquiring company looks at its partners' processes and systems, deems it inferior, and then sets about installing systems and processes identical to its own. But the right position for the acquirer to take is: "My system may be better, but is it the best?"
  A merger is a chance to establish new and higher standards for the whole organisation, not an excuse to maintain the status quo -- for either side. The Elrax management has not invested the time to examine this aspect.
  The new leader, Atul Jain, has relied heavily on his Bristol experience, going to the extent of even cloning some of Bristol's management practices in the new Elrax. He means well. He wants improvement initiatives implemented. But his vision is limited to his Bristol background. He has yet to grasp the power of the parent company, Elrax.
  Besides, what he considers improvements are often modifications of Gapro's system to make them similar to that of Bristol's. All these have very little impact on the overall business results of Elrax.  
No serious efforts have been made by the elrax management to integrate its processes with the business needs of the newly acquired companies
 Often the performance improvement efforts of many companies have as much impact on operational and financial results as a ceremonial rain dance has on the weather. This "rain dance" is the ardent pursuit of activities that sound good, look good, and allow managers to feel good -- but in fact contribute little or nothing to bottomline performance.
  These programmes or processes become pet subjects in many organisations, a hallmark by which they are identified. These often include total quality, management empowerment, employee involvement and project processes.
  Companies introduce these programmes under the false assumption that if they carry out enough of the "right" improvement activities, actual performance improvement will inevitably materialise. At the heart of these programmes, which are essentially "activities centered", is a fundamentally flawed logic that confuses ends with means, processes with outcomes. What is needed is an infusion of "results-centered" programmes which begin by identifying the most urgently needed performance improvement plan and an action plan to implement them effectively.
  There is no reason for the Elrax management to acquiesce when their managers plead that they are already accomplishing just about all that can be done or that some factors beyond their control are blocking accelerated performance improvement. Such self-limiting ideas are exactly that: self-limiting. Instead, the Elrax management needs to recognise that there is an abundance of both underexploited capability and dissipated resources in the organisation and both of these need to be harnessed properly if the unified Elrax is to make a mark in the marketplace.
  Mergers involve high risks and high stress levels. The challenge can be overwhelming for a company. But mergers are embarked on by companies because they hold the promise of greater prosperity and growth. The first step towards realising this promise is to make the process of integration manageable, effective and timely.

DUE DILIGENCE FIRST

  Debashish Mitra is the chief operating officer of The Global Green Co Ltd, a specialty foods company, part of the Thapar Group's thrust into the foods and agri-business.
  THE story of Elrax, Gapro and Bristol brings to the forefront a set of core issues that given the success of mergers and acquisitions. These issues are extremely relevant today, with the M&A mania gaining momentum in the West and will, no doubt, happen in India too as the government pushes ahead with the next phase of reforms.
  Data collected during the last 10 years in Europe and the US indicate that a significant number of these mergers/acquisitions failed to achieve their objectives. 
 This was not because they were incorrect or inappropriate from the business strategy or financial point of view, but mainly because the time and effort that were spent on the cultural and people issues were not adequate.
  At the end of the day, organisations are fundamentally about people -- to paraphrase Abe Lincoln, 'of the people, by the people, for the people'. Not 'of money, by money, for money' or 'of systems, by systems, for systems'. The merger of Bristol and Gapro wasn't successful because it failed to pay attention to these aspects.  
The problem began with the direction set by Elrax country manager Hutton. His was a classical top-down approach: he papered over the internal differences, leaving his successor to sort out the problems. In any event, his thinking was that "it soon won't be my problem so why tackle it", which was the first step to the failure of the merger.
  The problem was compounded by the selection of Atul Jain as Hutton's successor; Jain came from Bristol, the more 'successful' part of the merged entity. Given no core objectives on the culture and people front, Jain reinforced by his behaviour the superior-inferior division between Bristol and Gapro.
 Nayar, like most HR leaders, stayed within the boundaries of trying to 'influence' and 'facilitate' (rather than leading the process) and implementing diligently whatever the new owner, Elrax, wanted. He had all the right ideas -- breaking up the current identities by creating mixed teams, common objectives, common structures and so on.
  These were fundamental to the success of the merger, much more than having a common office. But, in his desire to conform, he didn't push hard enough, and, interestingly, ended up making his own life more difficult. At the end, after all the time and money spent on the acquisition, and with top management involvement, if business results are not achieved because of people issues, guess who takes the rap? 
The elrax merger failed because it ignored people issues. Making the problem worse was the appointment of a bristol man to the top job 
 To avoid such problems, it is critical to go though the process of due diligence before going ahead with an acquisition and/or a merger. Due diligence must extend beyond financial and technical issues to HR and systems. This helps in identifying and understanding potential problem areas, individuals and processes and provides enough time to develop a gameplan, whose execution can begin on day one of the acquisition. Had the differences between Bristol and Gapro been understood before the acquisition, Elrax could have avoided the current situation.
  Successful mergers happen because of the effort made in getting the people processes right. Leadership, teams, team membership along with processes that encourage dialogue, building a common future and burying the disparate pasts, are fundamental building blocks in creating synergy through mergers and acquisitions.
  When this happens successfully, the merged 'whole' will always be greater than the sum of its parts.

Case Study 2


31 May 1999		 CHASING THE EXPAT'S SHADOW
MEERA SETH
Was an expatriate head the answer to Lexicon India's problems in making an impact on the premium chocolates market?
  SUKUMAR Sharma was agitated. His finance director was on the line from London, with, as he was saying, a `solution' to his problems. But `Suku' was far from pleased. "Suku, the latest verdict on India is 18 months. You understand?" asked Leon Carey. 
  "HQ has found this bright start-up artiste who has been slaving over our Nevada operations for India. He is pricey, but he is hot. He will either turn the whole thing around or pull the shutters down if that is his verdict. My bet is he will turn it around," said Carey.
  "What is he going to bring to my business which we can't do ourselves?" asked Suku. "You think we haven't tried every trick? The solution is very clear Carey: just set up the plant or allow us to outsource manufacturing. And importantly, allow us to enter the mid-priced segment. Why are you imposing this additional cost of Rs 1 crore per annum which I can ill afford? We could use the money to hire a sensible sales and distribution team and achieve better market reach and volumes in the other segments. Or even acquire a depreciated plant and start production. We can't afford these high-priced expats!"
  Suku was the head of operations at Lexicon India, a chocolate and confectionery company. For four years he had been battling memories of a failed joint venture (JV), dealing with the home market and the parent company and trying to bridge the ideological gap with his country manager. He was also handling two jobs and 14 managers and praying each day for a miracle. Carey was adding a fresh chapter to his nightmare, he felt.
  Five years ago when it set up operations in India, Lexicon went through a nightmarish JV and a business that wouldn't take off. It had been through just about every complication that a new entrant could possibly encounter. Lexicon entered India in 1994 with Nimbus Foods, a medium-sized confectionery company located in Gujarat, as its partner. 
   Nimbus made biscuits, chewing gum, boiled sweets and toffees -- a mix that suited Lexicon. For Lexicon was a successful chocolate company and the products it had planned for India in the first phase were premium chocolates and nutty bars.
   The understanding was that Nimbus would be the marketing arm for Lexicon and the latter would not compete in product lines in which Nimbus was present. Additionally, Lexicon could use Nimbus' plant to manufacture its high-end products.
  The joint venture arrangement was the best Lexicon could ask for. Manufacturing facilities combined with a readymade distribution network, which would take its premium products into the B-class towns, too, which research declared had a very high level of aspiration matched by a healthy disposable income. For Lexicon had concluded through an extensive survey that the Indian middle class was growing rapidly, taking the market for premium chocolates along with it. For the initial stages of the market entry, Nimbus' plant would suffice, but at a later stage, it was agreed that the joint venture would set up a state-of-the-art plant to manufacture the premium chocolates.
  But Lexicon's sales didn't pick up and stocks piled up at the Vadodara plant. Nimbus attributed the low offtake to teething trouble and assured Lexicon of growing retail interest. By mid-1995, however, report after report questioning the size of the middle class appeared in the media, sending the Lexicon team into an abyss of anxiety. Nimbus then began to complain that Lexicon's market estimates were too ambitious. The Lexicon team, on the other hand, concluded that Nimbus did not have the skills to market the premium products.
  When, at the end of the year, Nimbus' efforts did not lead to sales or orders, Lexicon, UK, began to examine the JV contract more keenly to press for performance. That was the time when Lexicon's plan to set up its own plant in Ahmedabad came up for discussion. Nimbus dithered and its managing director Suyash Tripathi threw up his hands. "Where do I bring the money from?" he asked. "What do I tell my shareholders when we are nowhere near the target sales for year one?"
  Tripathi had begun to lose faith in the partnership or in Lexicon's ability to make a dent on the market. The market, he saw, was not as responsive to Lexicon's chocolates as the plans had envisaged. Besides, biggies like Nestle and Cadbury had already turned aggressive and proactive and begun to launch their better-known brands. Lexicon's chocolates may be good, but the pricing was all wrong, the retailers claimed. 
  Naresh Sinha, Lexicon's country manager, decided he had enough. To the parent company he said: "This is absurd! The JV partner is going back on his word. Are we going to put our plans on hold just because Nimbus does not contribute to the plant?" In short, Sinha was recommending that Lexicon buy out Nimbus' share and go it alone in India. He had no doubts that Lexicon's bars would succeed.

When Lexicon India's Joint Venture Partner Nimbus - which manufactured and distributed the products - failed to deliver, Lexicon decided to do it alone. But that was not as easy as it sounded.

  It was London's turn to balk. "But do you have the wherewithal and distribution strengths," it asked. There was no doubt that the relationship with Nimbus was now very fragile, but a divorce would queer the pitch further for Lexicon which at least had the use of Nimbus' Vadodara plant and its distribution network. "Retiring Nimbus is a matter of detail," explained the UK office, "but how are you going to generate the volumes?" Sinha was confident he could. According to him, volumes could be built if they entered the medium-end range of confectioneries and chewing gum. But as it stood, as long as the JV continued, Lexicon could not encroach on Nimbus' market.
  Nimbus and Lexicon parted company in early 1996 with an assurance from the parent company that Lexicon India would get an approval for a plant within six months. In the interim, Sinha would have to utilise the piled-up stocks and maybe import the products. Sinha was told in no uncertain terms that it was now up to him to deliver the viable volumes.
  It was not a happy situation for Sinha. He had to set up a sales team from scratch and create a distribution network in a market that was very competitive. Worse, he now had to worry about how he would source the goods for sales. Luckily for him, the only person he managed to hold back after the divorce was Sukumar Sharma, the operations manager who had worked closely with him. Fired by his new role, Suku helped draw up fresh business plans and sales strategies and drove his team towards his goal. Suku also managed to tie up with biscuit manufacturer Alpine Foods to distribute Lexicon's chocolates in India. Together they worked out an agreement with Alpine's distributors in the north and in western India for sales to these markets and the south.
  But one serious obstacle daunted him: London's stance that a plant could be set up only after a viable volume was achieved. Sinha had three options: one, manufacture on their own -- but the parent company refused to invest in a plant till the agreed volumes were met. Two, manufacture through a third party. Or three, import products.
  The most cost-effective route was to outsource manufacturing, but the parent company wouldn't hear of it. "In the candy and chocolate business, everything needs to be kept under wraps," said the UK head of operations. "Recipes and manufacturing processes are closely guarded secrets. We cannot share them with some third-party confectioner."
  Sinha argued there could be an arrangement like the one with Nimbus, where the manufacturing was done under closely guarded supervision. But the parent company would not hear of it. "After what we have faced with Nimbus, we are not going that route again. We have seen what the market is capable of; but you have to convince me with volumes for a plant to take shape," he said.
  The natural choice was to import. But that also needed investments in a semi-cold chain to carry the chocolates and transport them. Importing also meant a host of other skills and costs: an entire logistics function, a warehouse and the people. In short, Sinha did not feel Lexicon India was equipped to handle imports. Also, with the focus on sales, he would rather channel his funds and energies into building a market.
  There was a locational problem, too. Lexicon was headquartered in Delhi. So if it had to import, it would have to set up an office in Mumbai, Chennai or Calcutta, if not at all three cities so as to be closer to the ports. Suku thought about it. "That makes sense, but how can we afford it?" he asked. Instead he suggested that they speak to Alpine and convince its distributors in the three metros to take on the import function. "Logistically, too, that makes sense. They will be in control of the supply and distribution of the goods. We should work on that."
  Fortunately for Lexicon, Alpine was enthusiastic and so were its distributors. "We can do it, if all costs are to your account," they said. Lexicon India agreed. Its sales team worked the markets, canvassed for business, booked orders and passed it on to Lexicon UK which shipped the goods to Alpine's three distributors under their import order. The distributors, in turn, paid the sales proceeds to the parent company. Lexicon received a small commission for the sale it put through, which was not even enough to cover costs. But as Sinha said: "The idea is to generate demand and develop the market. Soon the plant will be in place and life will be wonderful."

 But life wasn't a bowl of cherries for Lexicon. Soon Alpine was complaining. The original agreement was only about distribution, but now it had the added bother of coping with arduous import procedures. Lexicon did not have a regional sales network, which made it difficult to ensure that the regional distributors processed their imports enthusiastically. Then again, if it took four weeks for a consignment to arrive from London, it took as long as three weeks to clear the material from the docks. All of this affected product quality and reduced shelf life.
  Sinha grew weary of all this. "How do we grow the market without consistent supply? Does the parent company realise what all this costs us in terms of time and effort? Why can't we simply set up a plant?" he asked.   
  Already weighed down by the customs duty and administrative costs of managing the imports, Lexicon now could not afford to incur any more costs. 
  The business was now losing money and employee morale was at an all-time low.
  Worse, with all the time spent on walking a tightrope, managing and monitoring the imports, there was precious little left to woo the retail trade. Suku didn't even have the budget to hire regional sales staff.
  Yet Sinha wasn't about to give up. "We have come a long way," he tried telling the sales team. "We have to look at all these costs, even the dropping morale, as set-up costs. We are going to keep our chin up and get to the goal together!" For Sinha was determined. He had seen the business plans. Once the initial problems were overcome, Lexicon was going to unleash a host of exciting brands on hungry Indian consumers.
  Whether they believed him or not, at least they were willing to strategise their every step together. Arguments and debates were par for the course at Lexicon. "Look, my job is to manage the customer's orders, not his temper," said the sales head. "The administrative burden of importing is becoming an issue. I am building customers for ever, not for a single transaction. We have to do something about the import issue!"
  Sinha then made a momentous decision. "Yes, this has gone too far. Soon we will be manufacturing. We need to learn to handle logistics, so this is a good time to start trading activity as a prelude to manufacturing. So let us start importing directly in our name and set up a full-scale logistics function," he said.
  It was now Suku's turn to debate. "We are making too many short-term decisions based on a dream that we will soon have our own plant. The fact is, we are far away from our target. Can we afford these costs?" he asked. "We have a good product, good brands and probably a good market. What we need to do quickly is set up the plant or urge Lexicon, UK, to permit us to manufacture through third parties," he said to Sinha.
  The fact was that although sales had begun to grow, there was still no sign of the magic volume figure. Lexicon, UK's, insistence that India should sell only the eight varieties of premium chocolates was proving to be a stumbling block. But the low-value, high-volume ranges could be profitable only if Lexicon manufactured the products. Importing them would not be cost effective, it was felt. It was for this very reason that Sinha had not pushed UK for permission to trade in the mid-priced segments. "We must wait," he conceded. "Once we have the clearance to set up the plant, we can start all these product segments, set up more elaborate infrastructure."
  So on and so forth went the tussle between the parent's demand for volumes and the Indian subsidiary's desire to enter all product categories and the unresolved debate over manufacturing on its own versus outsourcing. There was no respite. It was even difficult to forecast the demand with any level of confidence, given the erratic supply and availability of the brands in the retail outlets. Suku was deeply annoyed by the apparent indifference of the parent company to the Indian subsidiary's plight. "The initial estimates and strategy were based on a middle class size of 350 million and its ability and inclination to pay for premium products," he tried explaining to Sinha.

Sinha, the Indian Country head, wanted Lexicon to enter all segments of the Confectionery Market instead of sticking only to premium products, but the parent company wouldn't hear of it.

  "Now where's the sense in sticking to the old strategy of `premium range only' when it is very clear that such volumes cannot be had but at a loss and at least not in the next five years?" But there was opportunity in that error, Suku felt. Lexicon, which had planned to bring in only its premium products, could now relook at the dynamics and bring in all those low-value, high-volume products which would be enormously profitable if they were manufactured in India. To Sinha he said: "Don't push for trading Naresh. The arithmetic is clear -- volumes and profits lie in the middle and lower end ranges. You must convince UK on two counts: one, to either set up a plant or allow us to outsource. Two, enter the mid-priced and low-priced confectionery segments with our international brands." But Lexicon, UK, was so caught up with its image and fears that it refused to look at a market where only its lower-end products would find profitable markets.
  When Carey visited India in early 1998, he sat through a three-hour presentation on `Need for a changed vision'. At the end of it, he asked: "Tell me Suku, how are we going to justify that it is worthwhile remaining in India?"
   "How do large multinationals take decisions on whether they should stay in a market or not?" asked Suku. "Based on profits, on volumes, on presence and long-term growth? You tell me, what is your vision? Never mind what your vision was four years ago. Let's say you have just completed a review of the Indian market and you have found that the middle class is 80 million. Would you enter India? Why do you want to enter India? For immediate profits? To grow a viable market here for the long term? To take part in the foods business in the belief that it is a viable and growing industry? Don't forget that none of the new entrants made profits in the first five or even seven years. Growing a market requires a heart of steel, a stomach of leather and a mind sharp as a razor. Why blame it on a wrong estimate? Your answer lies in your long-term vision," said Suku.
  "All that talk is academic, Suku," interjected Sinha. "The point is, we have the demand, we even have a workable distribution system through Alpine. I know that if the products are available, consumers will come by the hordes. Today the distributors are not adventurous with their offtake because I have no plant. No credibility. They are saying: `Set up a plant, manufacture, make your product available on demand.' I am not being allowed to do that," he said, addressing Carey.
  Carey went back, promising to `work on it'. Then the unusual happened. Believing that the poor showing was because the business was not being managed optimally, Lexicon, UK, decided to sack Sinha and send an expat manager from its home office. A belligerent Suku now decided to fight for survival. "It's clear to me that the parent company is pursuing a narrow vision by insisting on the premium range," he told Sinha. "If they will not invest in a plant, I am going to push for outsourcing and trading in the middle and lower ranges."
  Six months passed and no one was found to head the India business. Meanwhile, Lexicon India's managers ran the business to the best of their ability, happy at having saved the cost of a country head. For Suku, every rupee saved meant more money to hire more sales staff so that they could explore every new market, sell the volumes and attain the targets that would justify setting up their own plant. For the longer it delayed setting up the plant, the more the revenue loss. And that was one reason why Suku was rejecting Carey's offer of an expat country manager.
  "It will cost me my plant," said Suku confidently.
  "Don't exaggerate, Suku," Carey said.
  "It takes a lot of money to run an operation in India. Given our situation, we can't fritter it away. Now, don't send me a white elephant. I can run the business this way myself. Nothing strategic has been achieved in the last three years, despite having a country manager," said Suku. "Granted, we went wrong in our estimates, but so did so many others. But they all survived! The better companies have gone back to the drawing boards and altered their plans; they haven't quit. You compounded the problem by sticking to the premium mantra. Imports cannot be a permanent solution. Sooner or later we have to either set up our own plant or find a viable third party to outsource from. The size of the potential market is tremendous. So by setting up a plant, we won't be erring. But we'll be making a mistake if we stick to the current volume targets and the categories we are in. And that, Mr Carey, is why we are losing money. So let's take this decision now. We don't need to wait for an expat to fly here to decide!" he ended.

AN EXPAT MAY BE USEFUL
N.S. VIJAYA KUMAR is chief coordinator at the Nagarjuna Group chairman's office. He was chief executive officer of Nagarjuna Palma -- a joint venture with an Israeli company -- for five years before becoming a member of its board.

Dear Mr Sharma,
  I can understand how you feel about an expatriate coming to help the Indian operations. Having been so closely involved in your company's operations, you perhaps feel that everything that could have been done has been attempted.   In fact, I can even sense your frustration over your overseas principal's decision to get an expatriate country manager, when, deep in your heart, you feel that you would have made a far better country manager!

  Before we get on to the issue of addressing your feelings on this move, it may be worthwhile to study the issues dispassionately and explore what options are open to all the parties involved.
  Firstly, and this statement is not meant to console you, there are quite a few instances where the overseas organisation's visions for developing countries like ours are a little ahead of time. Theprojections of volumes are based and derived from aggregated data and therefore are not really rooted in the realities of the marketplace.     Secondly, most multinationals want to make a guarded entry into a country like ours and progress rapidly only after they get the feeling that they have sufficiently found their bearings in the Indian market.
  Thirdly, it is quite common for multinationals to advise their affiliates to look at specific product segments, preferably in the premium range, rather than spread the product portfolio wide to cover the entire range. This is because a start-up organisation has limited resources and therefore it makes sense to put these resources into a focused area in a creative manner rather than spreading them.
  This also helps the organisation to continuously innovate and change the rules of the game rather than frittering away its energies and resources in me-too segments. Of course, these decisions also have to be taken after a thorough assessment of the local markets.
  Fourthly, there's a price to be paid in the short run for the `selective' strategy; this takes the form of losses. In this phase, it is wise not to make any investments in creating fixed assets (such as manufacturing facilities) and instead invest in market development and brand-building.
  Going by these, Lexicon's arrangement with Nimbus was perhaps the ideal choice. But every move after that caused the situation to worsen. When Nimbus `failed' on the volumes front, and assuming that Sinha's conclusion that the company did not have skills to market premium wares is correct, I wonder why Sinha did not recommend to the parent company that the joint venture with Nimbus be amended to include only manufacturing and that Lexicon's Indian operations could do the marketing on its own.
  After all, Nimbus erred only in marketing, not in manufacturing. It appears that Sinha had made up his mind about getting into the low-end segments as well and since this was not tenable in the existing Nimbus joint venture, he decided to call off the venture. This, according to me, was the first serious error in decision-making for which both Sinha and Lexicon, UK, are responsible. After that move, a catch-22 situation was sure to follow.    It occurs to me that in the light of the Indian experience, Lexicon, UK, is still not sure of the size of the Indian market and therefore wants to utilise the services of an experienced expatriate to address this crucial issue. The parent company has also realised that Sinha's termination of the Nimbus joint venture without really looking at alternative arrangements (in terms of modification of the joint venture arrangement) was not a wise move and therefore felt the need for another person to take a fresh look at this issue.

Sinha's termination of the Nimbus joint venture without putting alternative arrangements in place was not a wise move.

  The new expat country manager, based on his experience, can reassess the premium market and if the size of the market looks interesting in the foreseeable future, can suggest creative solutions. He can, for example, revive the Nimbus joint venture, changing the agreement so that Nimbus does only the manufacturing.
  Or if putting up a manufacturing base is the only alternative, he could convince the UK office of the need to use the Indian base for exports to other countries, so as to facilitate better capacity utilisation in the initial stages. The more we apply our minds, the greater is the list of possible alternatives.
  Do not worry about the Rs 1 crore cost to the Indian operations that the expat head will entail; Lexicon, UK, is likely to view this as a small investment compared to the cost of the outcome of a wrong strategic decision.
  I would suggest that you accept the expat country manager with an open mind and communicate to him effectively the value of the experience in building the brand and furthering the marketing operations. If things work out favourably, then sooner or later you will replace the expat manager, leaving him free to take on similar assignments for the company. If things don't work out well, you will certainly be richer from the experience that you gained.
  If you have made a good enough impression you could -- if you wanted -- get drafted into Lexicon's overseas operations! Either way, it is a win-win move for you. Wishing you all the best.

BUILD CREDIBILITY
  G. BALAJI is professor of operations management at the Management Development Institute, Gurgaon, Haryana. A mechanical engineer from Bombay University with a doctorate from the Indian Institute of Science, Bangalore, Balaji spent a term at the J.L. Kellogg School of Management, Northwestern University, US, as a visiting scholar. His areas of teaching and research are operations research, manufacturing strategy and management of technology.

SUKUMAR Sharma is indeed in an unenviable situation. On the one hand he faces an apparently recalcitrant market that refuses to be stirred by the known mantras of management; on the other, he has to tackle a conservative hard-nosed board that is looking for clear and unambiguous signals before it commits further resources to its Indian operations.

 Compounding the problem is the fact that the board is not Indian and the products are at the premium end of the consumer goods segment that has stumped many a recent entrant to the Indian market.
  Sweets are an inseparable part of the Indian palate. But non-traditional products have a difficult task endearing themselves to the Indian sweet tooth. Product improvisations -- changes in product characteristics or usage patterns -- which demand a large resource commitment will most probably be required. Merely introducing an existing international product may not be the best way of persuading Indian customers in B-class towns to adopt a new product.
  A firm that wants to make an entry into this business will have to make investments in setting up a complete supply chain that will probably equal those made in manufacturing. Investments in maintaining and servicing a distribution network will have to be complemented by investments in brand building. In this context, it is therefore quite logical for Lexicon to have entered the Indian market through a joint venture with an existing business in this area. This strategy reduces investments in both -- the manufacturing as well as creating a distribution channel. This strategy would have enabled Lexicon India to devote its resources to brand-building and promotion.

Entering the low-end market will lead to a price war in this segment. It won't be sustainable unless the premium products are established.

  The entry into the Indian market was prompted by the apparent size of the Indian middle class. The choice of a JV to enter India indicates a cautious, arm's-length commitment. There seems to be no evidence that the setting up of the Indian operation has been, in any way, integrated with the global operations of Lexicon. The Indian operation appears to have been set up only with an eye on the Indian market. Therefore, when Carey talks of exiting the Indian market, it is not a hollow threat but a logical follow-through of the parent company's investment/divestment strategies. The decision to continue operations in India does not appear to be irreversible.

  I do not expect Sukumar's argument that more products from the Lexicon stable should be introduced in India to cut much ice. Premium confectioneries command high margins primarily owing to their market image, assiduously built up over years of consistent advertising. Lexicon, highly conscious of its image, will be reluctant to take on the Cadburys and Nestles in the low-end markets. Doing so would probably lead to a price war in the low-end segments which will not be sustainable unless the premium products are securely established. Even if it is successful in securing a satisfactory marketshare, Lexicon's brand image in India vis-a-vis its brand image at home will take a beating. This can harm Lexicon's premium products in India as well as other newer markets that it may wish to enter later. Exploiting economies of scale by entering the low-end markets after its premium products are established is one thing and entering the low-end segments to survive is yet another. 
Given its position internationally, I suspect that Lexicon's viability significantly depends on the sales revenues from its premium products.  
  Five years ago Lexicon had two options: enter India on its own or through a JV. Not perceiving any strategic advantage in Indian operations, this arm's-length involvement in the Indian market seemed to be the best option. Today, after the failure of the JV, Lexicon faces two options: consolidate its presence in the Indian market by building a plant or exit. The choice will essentially depend on its estimate of hitting a good market. The delay in setting up a plant and the imminent change in the top management are clear indications that the company is not ready with adequate credible information to make this decision. The "magic volume number" given by the parent company is its threshold level required to commit itself to consolidating the Indian operation. Having waited for a couple of years to see this figure emerge, they now seek a "second opinion" from their soon-to-be-appointed expat CEO.
  Whether they are at fault or not, Sukumar and his team today lack adequate credibility with the top management. If one takes the view that Lexicon is waiting for more adequate and reliable data to commit itself, it would be in the best interest of Lexicon (India) to enable the parent company to get this information. Resisting the appointment of an expat CEO on the ground that it would be too expensive will not help matters.
  Lexicon has been maintaining its operations in India despite losses for the past few years. Spending a couple of crores of rupees more will probably hasten its data acquisition process. Sukumar must use this as an Opportunity, of having a clear line of communication with the top management, to convince Lexicon about the veracity of his arguments.
  Lexicon cannot be accused of not having a vision; at best it may be faulted for being painfully cautious. But seeing the scenario in southeast Asia, this cautiousness may be justified.
Designed by: Epsilon Systems - SQ-80, Hauz Khas Appts, New Delhi-16. Phone: 685-3301, 685-0878, 98100-06431

Case Study 3


14-20 June 1999		 A BENCHMARK FOR DISHONESTY
MEERA SETH
Mini joined the ailing agncy benchmark full Of enthusiasm. But she was shattered when she discovered that her boss was a dishonest man
RUKMINI Sharma sat numb and speechless at her father's bedside in Pune's Blue Meadow Hospital. Surprisingly, he was conscious and despite the tubes and contraptions to which he was hooked, he spoke. "Is everything okay now?" he asked. Mini knew what he meant. 
  Benchmark Advertising Ltd, a growing agency where she was a senior manager, had been in the news ever since its premises were raided by the income tax authorities. For Mini it was a nightmare. Eight months ago, she had offered Benchmark the use of her spare flat in Bandra to accommodate the Mumbai regional manager, Ankush Mallar, whose house was under repair. It was a friendly arrangement and the request itself had come from Debu Guha, the managing director.
  The IT raid was conducted simultaneously at Chemur Industries, a client of Benchmark's, at Benchmark's offices and at the Bandra flat where Mallar lived. Cash and documents worth Rs 16 lakh were found in the flat, which Mallar explained as having been paid by Chemur for a major event. Chemur's troubles with the tax officials dragged Benchmark to the front pages. The Benchmark management had no idea about the cash transaction, it said. But Mini was named in the investigations as a senior officer in whose flat the money was found.
  "It's all okay now," Mini said to her ailing father. It was six months since that episode took place, and she had spoken to her father almost every week to reassure him that she was fine. Three days later Mini's father passed away holding his daughter's hand.
  Mini returned to Mumbai with a feeling of guilt. Had her problems at Benchmark triggered off his stroke?
   For weeks after that she kept a low profile, trying hard to sort out the feelings in her mind.
  Mini had joined Benchmark only eight years ago. Her tryst with advertising began four years before that when she met the 78-year-old Seshashayee Ratnam at a Christmas carnival in Goa. Ratnam ran a small but flourishing ad agency in Hyderabad. Mini had been recently married and without a job. "Take up a job," Ratnam advised. "This country needs good people, good hands," Ratnam had said.
  Mini returned to Hyderabad and joined Ratnam's agency. A year later her husband was transferred to Bangalore. Ratnam spoke to Rajan Kamat, who ran Caption in Bangalore. "She is a gem, you will soon find out," he said. Kamat hired Mini and watched over her career. Within two years Mini's husband was transferred to Mumbai, and she had to move there. Vilas Moitra, a senior group head at Caption, had left to join Benchmark's Mumbai office as senior vice-president. "Come along Mini," he said. "Let's teach these guys some advertising."
  Mini couldn't believe her luck. But Mumbai came as a shock for her. The regional head of Mumbai, Ravin Rai, began a cold war against Moitra, who had been brought in as his second in command. Reason: Rai believed that Moitra was a plant to spy on his activities. Naturally, Mini was caught in the crossfire as she was seen as being in Moitra's camp.
  Rai handed charge of collections to Mini -- a position she felt did not do justice to her skills. But Moitra reassured her. "You are new; they will take time to know you. Meanwhile, work hard. We are here to turn this place into another Caption, remember?" And that was the brief Moitra himself had received from Guha. Benchmark's business was a mess after four clients had pulled out, leaving behind debts of more than Rs 1 crore.    Mini decided to take her role seriously, but the daily wrangles between the Rai and Moitra camps devastated her. She decided to call Ratnam. "Why did you introduce me to advertising?" she asked. "It's a hellhole."
  "Because it should not be so," said Ratnam. "That is why we need good people." That put back the spring in her feet. Mini worked harder than ever before, but the hard work unearthed some murky facts. Benchmark under Rai had been double-billing clients for jobs for which they had already paid!
  Armed with these facts, Mini met Moitra. Moitra was delighted. Within days an audit was instituted and Rai sacked for negligence. Guha then brought in Ankush Mallar from Bangalore to steer the Mumbai branch. Benchmark gained two new accounts under Mallar, but recovery was slow. Steering a cash-strapped agency was tough on Mallar, and the loss of four accounts had already taken its toll on the branch.
  It was in his third year that the raid occurred. Mini was devastated. An act in good faith had got her into a meaningless mess. Her husband was away on a posting to Daman and Mini deemed it fit not to tell him anything. Moitra and Guha stood by her during the trials, reassuring her and reinforcing the cause of Benchmark. Eventually, Mallar was asked to resign and Moitra took over. Mini was elevated to the post of senior manager. She now worked directly under Moitra.
  Mini's resilience during the raid and after won her the respect of the management. Moitra himself trusted her immensely and soon she was part of an informal strategy committee to turn around the fortunes of the Mumbai office. After the Mallar debacle, the branch lost two more accounts, and morale plunged. Fearing that this would impact the overall image of the agency, Guha gave Moitra a long leash. "Do all that you think is necessary to turn this place around," he said.
  Moitra now informally appointed Mini as the `caregiver' at Benchmark. "HR has its limitations," he said. "The agency business is more about people, more about the softer aspects of emotions management, for which I am not sure HR has the skills. What I would like you to do is stay in touch with people at all levels." Moitra's primary objective was to ensure that there was no further erosion of morale -- something that had threatened the survival of the Mumbai office. 
  And he had noticed that Mini had an ability to resolve people-related crises with ease.As for Mini, she looked up to Moitra as a guide and philosopher. She had watched him during the transition period when Rai was the regional head and later during the Mallar trial and seen the composure and resilience with which he had tided over what appeared to be an explosive situation. And if Moitra's mission was to save Benchmark, then it was Mini's mission too. She had seen the destruction caused by Rai and Mallar and its impact on the staff.
  She had also tried to cope with the fundamental difference in attitudes between the management staff and the non-management staff to these episodes. The senior managers had shrugged their shoulders after a brief state of surprise. "These things happen. Business is about sudden shocks and surprises. Nothing is forever." Then it was back to business as usual.
  But down the line there was pandemonium. For the first to be hit by the loss in business were the non-management staff. Surplus staff were retired, increments suspended for two years and no promotions were given. This led to a lot of resentment.
  Mini was determined to change this feeling. "As long as the non-graded staff believe that the managers are a privileged lot, there isn't going to be any unity in our efforts," she said to Aarti Nayyar, another account executive, who had been working closely with Mini on her clients. Aarti had expressed deep interest in Mini's efforts and offered to help with training in copywriting. "Why don't we offer a skills development workshop where staffers can opt to learn either copywriting or print management? Over time, you can even upgrade those who show promise," suggested Aarti.
  Mini then started a weekend skills enhancement course in accounting, computers, copywriting and photography, using in-house managers. The idea worked brilliantly and a number of staffers enrolled. Mini was delighted with the experiment, but one person stood in her way -- the head office accountant Ramakant Naik. "All these madcap experiments are a waste of money and I am not allowing even a paisa for them," he said.      
  Moitra pooh-poohed Naik and told Mini to carry on regardless. When at the end of three months Mini produced an expense statement for Rs 1,200, towards photocopying, stationery and tea, Moitra suggested the unexpected. "Charge it to one of our clients by raising an overheads voucher," he said.
  Mini crumbled. She had not expected this from Moitra. Unable to bear the dishonesty, however small, Mini destroyed the bills and bore the cost herself. More followed. A few months later, while waiting in Moitra's ante-room, she heard his secretary instruct the branch production accountant: "This bill from Mello is to be charged as photographic props, these repair bills for his farm house to be debited to 'office repairs'...."
  Mini could take it no more. Moitra, her hero, had feet of clay. She busied herself with her next client meeting. But she was bothered. Benchmark was slowly turning around, three new accounts had been signed in the last six months... was Moitra going to destroy all that, she wondered.
  The same month Mini discovered she had diabetes, and at a fairly advanced stage. "I am surprised you did not feel the symptoms sooner," said the doctor. But her ailment was far from her mind. She tried to visualise Moitra who even during his Caption days stood for integrity and hard work. What had happened to this simple man from a middle class family with ordinary preoccupations like a family and children? Was he chasing a glitzy life like all of them?

After the murky goings-on at benchmark claimed the heads of two successive bosses, Moitra appointed Mini as the "Caregiver" to ensure that there was no further erosion of morale.

  Mini called Ratnam, just to hear his voice. If she had the courage, she would mention her anxieties, she decided. But Ratnam's opening sentence put paid to her plans. "I am glad you are working for none less than Moitra, such a stellar chap... I am delighted Benchmark is seeing better days under him."
  That week Mini got an offer from Amity, another agency, asking her to join it as group head. It was a good offer and Mini briefly considered taking it up. But Ratnam's words came back to her: "This industry deserves to have good people; that's the only way it will grow respectfully."
  Mini declined the offer. Benchmark needed her, she realised. Aarti, who had been made the same offer, accepted it and resigned from Benchmark. Mini felt lonelier than ever. It was that week that she got a call from Guha in Bangalore. "I am glad you did not accept Amity's offer," he said, much to Mini's surprise. "Benchmark cannot afford to lose people like you. Mini, Benchmark is a great agency even if it is going through bad times. There are few people I can trust here, Moitra and you are two of those. Together we will build Benchmark into the best agency in India."
  Mini had mixed feelings -- elation at what Guha, the MD, had said about his faith in her, pain at what he still believed about Moitra, and helplessness at her own inability to change Moitra's growing dishonesty.
  Around that time, Benchmark won a new food account -- Golden Gate Foods. The account enabled Benchmark to climb the chart by a few respectable rungs and the Mumbai office was finally out of the red. The account was to be handled by Surya Thomas, a group head. Thomas was highly regarded within Benchmark and had a number of successes to his credit. His group was full of beans and brimming with ideas. For long Mini had wanted to work with Thomas but Moitra had admonished her, saying: "We are already low on numbers, if I release you to Thomas who is going to handle your accounts?"
  But this time Mini spoke to Guha to pre-empt a refusal by Moitra. "This is one account I really want to work on," she explained to Guha. "Obviously with Moitra and Thomas being at war with each other, I can see the problem," said Guha. "Mr Guha, can't you help resolve their differences?" asked Mini.
  "Competition is always productive and destructive. Eventually the business benefits," said Guha with a laugh. Moitra agreed to Mini's request readily. But he said: "Thomas is not such a great genius, I must warn you. There is little he can teach and what he knows is not worth knowing."
  Over the next year the horror stories unfolded. Thomas was an old Rai camper and there was a slow but steady plan by Moitra to oust him. Moitra had delayed his promotion and written unsavoury things in his appraisal report.

The tug-of-war began. Despite having allowed her transfer to Thomas' group, Moitra disrupted her time by making irrational demands at crucial moments. That was when Mini confronted Moitra. "Why are you doing this?" she asked. "I have handed over my earlier accounts capably, now I have to devote myself to Golden Gate. I find your interruptions devious," she finally blurted out.
  What followed was explosive. Moitra blasted her for insubordination, irresponsibility and many more things. "I will not have you tell me how to run my agency. If there are urgent tasks to be attended to, I as regional head have the right to expect those done without your advice or counsel."
  The following day Thomas asked Mini to hand over the account to another manager. "Maybe at a later date when you have sorted out the problems on your old accounts, you can return to Golden Gate," he advised.
  Mini was already exhausted by the effects of the drugs she was taking for her diabetes. Thomas' decision made her feel worse and she took the rest of the week off to be by herself with an old aunt. It was while she was browsing at a bookstore that she happened to glance at a blurb on a magazine cover. `Different strokes' announced the blurb, `is a story by Aarti Nayyar which brings out the murky goings on in agency corridors, demystifying the glamour and gloss of the agency business...".
  Three hours later, in her aunt's verandah, Mini passed out. Aarti's story was all about her experiences with ad agencies and a number of anecdotes in her story were all about Moitra and his escapades, although the entire story was fictionalised.
  Mini woke up in Dr Sharma's nursing home in distant Alibag. Even though the doctor warned her that her blood sugar had dropped significantly and advised her a week's rest, Mini convinced her aunt and returned to Mumbai and work.
  But it was an angry Moitra she met. Handing a copy of the magazine to her, he said: "Would you like to read this? After all, you have cut short your leave, you might like to relax with a magazine at work." The sarcasm was not lost on Mini. "That was the reason I returned so soon," said Mini pointing to the magazine. "It was unbearable, especially since she worked so closely with us for over three years."
  "At least it is clear who fed her with the information," said Moitra. Mini wasn't sure she understood. "Who?" she asked. "Don't pretend innocence, Mini," said Moitra seriously. "Aarti worked closely with you. Besides, since the time she left your behaviour has been hostile..."   
  Still weak after the recent blackout, Mini felt exhausted. But responding to Moitra's allegations seemed to be the most important thing now. "Vilas, this is a serious allegation you are making and it disturbs me very much. Please remember, we both began our careers at Benchmark with a pledge to nurse it back to health. I don't know about you, but I still have the same fervor and dedication. Before we go on a witch-hunt for Aarti's informer, it's important that we resolve our differences, or better still, talk about our goals and Benchmark."

When a magazine article on the corrupt practices at Benchmark came out, Mini had a long ltalk with Moitra, but he didn't admit his failings and accused Mini of leaking the information.

  They talked. About his bills, his tampering with production charges to clients, Thomas, Golden Gate... "I know I do not have the ability to be 100% honest. Running a business, specially one that challenges your career, is not easy. My marriage has been troubled for over a year, this story is not going to make it easy. Yes, Thomas threatens my peace. There are rumours that he will replace me..."
  Mini heard him out patiently. But nowhere was she hearing in Moitra's voice any sign of regret. At every stage he was justifying what he had done. But at least the misunderstandings were now out in the open... Then he asked: "Tell me Mini, how much did you tell Aarti?"
  Mini was taken aback. All that talking had not restored his faith in her integrity. "Not a word, Vilas," she said. Mini could see that Moitra did not believe her. As if in confirmation, he said: "Mini, I am not sure I trust you."      
  That night Mini sat in her room watching TV, aimlessly surfing channels. Towards midnight, she decided she would chat with her husband in Daman. It would take her mind off the problems at work, she thought. As she reached out for the phone, there was an agonising pain in her chest. A gasp left her lips as she pressed the buzzer which rang in her maid's room.
  Mini had suffered a massive cardiac arrest. Her sugar level had dropped to an all-time low. The doctors worked on her frantically and managed to revive her. Her husband arrived the next noon, which speeded her recovery. Mini had four blocked arteries, said the doctors. They would have to wait for her sugar level to return to normal before they performed bypass surgery, they said. The hospital psychologist met Mini. "You have been working too hard," he said gently. "Hardly," said Mini. "But I have been neglecting my diabetes."
  "That is evident," he said. "But what happened? Haven't you been taking your insulin regularly?" he asked.
  "It's her diet," said her husband. "The maid tells me she rarely eats a proper dinner. It's either a piece of cake and coffee or rasgollas and tea. Where does her sugar level stand a chance?"
The maid in turn blamed it on overwork. Guha flew down from Bangalore and threw in his bit: "Ever since she lost her father, she has been very different," he offered. Moitra had the last word. "She is very sensitive. She absorbs the problems of the people around her." Mini shut her eyes; she could not bear this dishonesty any more, she felt.

ACCEPT THE PARADOXES
Inder Walia is director (human resources) at ModiCorp.

  LIVE as if you will die tomorrow, but plan to live forever." It is a useful maxim because our responses to situations change over time. Work is not what it used to be inside or outside organisations. The paradox of time, combined with the changing nature of work, forces us to rethink our whole idea of work and time -- when we work, where we work, how we work and why we work.
  What Mini has been through typifies the experience of many professionals. All organisations have Guhas, Rais and Moitras in some measure -- some more and some less. It is not uncommon to witness power plays and politics in an organisation manifesting themselves in several ways as in the case of Benchmark.

In the prevailing business environment, while organisations are experiencing discontinuity and uncertainty on the one hand, professionals are coping with heightened aspirations and growing insecurities on the other. This is leading to enhanced and sustained stress levels. Benchmark and Mini are no exception.
