  All of us, along with the demands of the various roles that we play, need to focus on many more fundamental issues of life like: What are my aspirations for both my personal and professional life? Why do I work? How do I deal with emotions, both in personal and professional life? What is my responsibility towards myself? What are the values I want to live by?

We need to be able to strike a balance between our professional and personal lives without allowing either to impact the other.

  A clear vision for ourselves is what Mini and most of us miss out on. Mini's vision for herself and her life seemed to be limited to Benchmark Advertising. She seemed to be reacting to whatever was urgent at work based on the impulse of the moment, her feelings or moods. Thus, she operated from a limited awareness of other options and responsibilities that she might have. In effect, her vision was partial and the choices she made led to the imbalance. Her vision was based on the choices and expectations of others. This, in turn, led to Mini disconnecting from her inner self and finding herself at odds with herself.
  Each one of us needs to step aside from our lives and evaluate the course that we are taking. Getting into activities is a very comfortable state of existence. But reviewing whether these activities fall in the overall pattern and expectations from one's life is what will ultimately determine happiness and fulfillment. Mini moves from Hyderabad to Bangalore to Mumbai, changes agencies, does not go to Daman, takes it upon herself to change Benchmark single-handedly without focusing on "what do I want to do and why?" Such high involvement in activities without an aim is bound to lead to stress.
  We rarely experience intense feeling. And for most of us, our feelings fall into the grey zone, with mild bumps in our emotional roller-coaster.

  Managing our emotions is a full-time job. Much of what we do -- especially in our leisure time -- is in fact an attempt to manage our feelings and emotions. The art of taking care of and nurturing ourselves is a fundamental life skill and is one of the most essential ones. Each one of us is actually left to invent our own devices to not only vanquish bad moods but also to balance our emotions. Life has its ups and downs, but they need to be balanced. Like Mini, each one of us needs to develop coping mechanisms for dealing with intense emotions.
  Life has many facets: a profession is just one of them. All of us need to have worthy goals in life, both personal and professional. Success in life encompasses not only professional excellence but also sound health, high energy and zeal for life, satisfying relationships with people, creatively crafting a future life path, emotional stability, happiness and, above all, peace of mind.
  We need to be able to strike a balance between our professional and personal lives without allowing either to impact the other. Mini seemed to be unable to give herself a focus. She actually made her personal and work lives fuzzy and procrastinated in taking decisions in both these areas, which worked to her disadvantage. It is, therefore, important to continuously hone our ability to consciously delink ourselves from professional worries and avoid carrying them into our personal life. These two lives are somewhat mutually exclusive and our ability to effectively manage this aspect will help strike an appropriate balance in our lives.
  Another important emerging issue is: how does an employee deal with a corrupt superior? One could be courageous enough to report the boss to his superior or confront the boss and deal with the outcome or response in a mature manner. One could even deflect such issues and stay focused on the task on hand. Not being able to manage/deal with any of the above would result in severe stress to the individual, leaving him/her wondering as to why he/she is still with the organisation. Mini, even after confronting Moitra, found herself in this situation. Many of us do not help ourselves in understanding our own values about discipline, time boundaries, the kind of work we want to do, ethics etc. The courage of conviction to stand up for and deal with situations/issues leading to harmonising with our inner self is indeed important.
  We need a new way of thinking about our problems and our future. If the contradictions and surprises of paradox are going to be part of that future, we should not be dismayed. The acceptance of paradox as a feature of our life is the first step towards living with it and managing it.
  Earlier, one thought that paradoxes were the visible signs of an imperfect world. Paradox has to be accepted, coped with and made sense of, in life, in work and the society we live in.
APPROVAL ADDICTION
Achal Bhagat is an organisational psychologist trained at Oxford. He has an established psychiatric and psychotherapy practice at the Indraprastha Apollo Hospital in Delhi.

Let us imagine I met Mini. Let us try and recreate a possible conversation.    
  Mini: Life is stressful.
  AB: What makes life stressful?
  Mini: Oh! I do not even know where to begin. Everything is bad.
  AB: What makes it stressful today?
  Mini: Oh! I feel there is so much conflict. I cannot take it anymore.
  AB: Do you want to talk about the conflict you faced today?

  Mini: Well! There is this person Moitra. I have known him for more than eight years now. We were good friends. He suddenly turns around and tells me that he doesn't trust me anymore. It makes me feel so bad. To think that I moved with him to this company I work for! He said we would change the world, I was almost a partner in whatever he did. It is so painful!
  AB: So, you feel upset that Moitra doesn't trust you anymore. It must make you feel bad. Has your opinion of Moitra changed in the last eight years?
  Mini: Oh, absolutely. He was such a nice guy, you know, middle-class, never one to run after glitzy things, zealously committed to what he was doing. He was a good man. But now, he is what I would call a bad egg. You know there is this person Aarti who has even written a story about his exploits.
  AB: How does Aarti know him?
  Mini: She used to work with us for a few years. AB: Do you agree with what Aarti has written about him?
  Mini: There is no doubt about it. Aarti is an honest person. In fact, that is the problem. Moitra thinks I told Aarti all that she wrote. How can he think like that of me? I would never do such a thing.
  AB: If what Aarti is saying is true and you have known Moitra so well for so long, did you not see what was happening?
  Mini: That is the problem. I saw it happening and I did not see it happening. If I look back he always was up to something. But I believed in him so blindly that I could not see anything. I was sure he would help the company change.
  AB: So, he seemed so good at that time that it was difficult to focus on the wrong things that he did.    
  Mini: Yes.
  AB: And he is so bad now that it is difficult to focus on anything right he may be doing.
  Mini: Yes.
  AB: What you seem to be saying is that though you do not trust him anymore he should continue to trust you? He owes you that degree of trust.
  Mini: He has no right to mistrust me, it hurts me so much.
  AB: It seems to be an important feeling for you that everyone should trust you, whatever you think of them. If everyone did not trust you, would you believe that you are not a good person.
  Mini: Maybe. IT WAS not advertising, not the mistrusting Moitra, the entrusting Guha, the value-strapped and cash-strapped Benchmark that caused her to have a heart attack. It was perhaps her thoughts.
  Mini is an intelligent person who knows how to care for people. She seems to be diligent, meticulous and honest in her work. Then what makes her neglectful, sloppy and uncaring when she deals with her illness? Her personality traits should actually lead for her to be extremely cautious about herself -- unless she has a stronger need that is met by not looking after herself.
  What is that need? Let me highlight some ways of thinking that Mini demonstrates that made life stressful for her. These ways of thinking contributed to her heart attack.
  There are assumptions that Mini seemed to be working on the basis of.
  All people have to be approving of her all the time. Whatever the cost. Moitra has to like her. Guha has to like her, the man whose increments got stopped has to like her. Such an addiction for approval makes one strive for unrealistic self-neglecting altruism.

Mini, unlike many others, believed that the organisation came first. But how she coped with this assumption was stressful to her.

  Mini, unlike many others at Benchmark, believed that the organisation comes first. Rightly so, perhaps. But how she coped with this assumption was stressful for her. Whenever she saw people not adhering to this principle she felt hurt and worked harder to bring in the euphemistic warmth of Caption to this organisation.
  Her hurt and anger at people who did not care for the organisation became a starting point for a messianic mission. Each Rai, Mallar or Moitra incident made her personalise the fight for the organisation. Such personal battles without looking at systemic changes are like pushing a wall. They create only clogged arteries, not changes in organisation.
  She did not look after herself, for she wanted the organisation to look after her. She wanted the company to understand her needs and take care of her because she had taken care of the organisation. Organisations are unfortunately unfair. They view matters with a broad brush. They overlook potential crises. Benchmark was adept at overlooking potential crises; that it missed the fact that Mini was carrying the load of the organisation's conscience was to be expected. An organisation that does not put in place systems to build values even when two consecutive heads have had to go because of integrity issues must be a sleepy organisation. It is unsafe then for people like Mini who make their work their life goal. There is more to life than the organisation you work for and there is more to an organisation than a personal battle to bring about change.

Designed by: Epsilon Systems - SQ-80, Hauz Khas Appts, New Delhi-16. Phone: 685-3301, 685-0878, 98100-06431

Case Study 4


5 Jul 1999		 A Cosmetic solution?
MEERA SETH
Liberalisation posed a dilemma for Remee India. Should it take on the multinationals in the cosmetics market? Or should it be a distributor for the multinationals?
  DINESH Vira arranged the jars of Remee lotions and creams on the top of the semi-circular shelf, keeping Besille's products on the second and Cover on the third. Stepping back, he eyed the display proudly. 
  Only two years ago, this showcase in his outer office had looked sparse and empty. In fact, the tea boy used the last shelf to keep used teacups which he collected on his way out. 
  Today the three large shelves were bursting with jars and bottles of varied skin and hair care products. In short, Remee India, where Dinesh was the marketing director, was back in the reckoning.
  Three years ago, Manu Pal, the chairman & managing director of Remee India and group chairman of MP Industries, whose businesses included paper, oil, pharmaceuticals, agro-chemicals and skin care products, announced the sale of the pharmaceutical and agro-chemicals businesses. The decision came after months of debate with his board members who concluded that MP Industries should streamline its business focus. Remee India, which held the dental, agro-chemicals and skin care businesses, was a Rs 30 crore company.    The skin care business, which showed no signs of growing, seemed to be next on the firing line. In the pre-liberalisation days, Remee's skin care business coasted along, happy with its small product base and protected markets. The company did not do anything dramatic to grow nor was competition too tough. There were Pond's, Lakme, Charmis, Nivea and some herbal products in the market. All of them had similar portfolios, and there were hardly anything to differentiate the brands.
  Remee's skin care business included a talcum powder, a skin care cream, a shaving cream and a heel crack healer. But over 60% of its sales was accounted for by Remee cream, a multipurpose, all-weather cream. That was dangerous, because, despite being an all-purpose cream, most of Remee's sales took place only during three months of the year, in winter. "
  Remee had launched summer products like deodorants and freshness soaps only recently and tentatively. During the lean summer months, the company lost touch with its consumers. Yet when winter came, consumers queued up at the shops for Remee. In a good winter, sales targets were met. But if it was a humid winter when nobody needed heavy moisturising creams, stocks began to pile up. During those lean times, its pharma range of colds and flu prescriptions kept Remee busy.
  The skin care business required a turnover of at least Rs 100 crore to be viable in the new market -- a far cry from Remee's Rs 17 crore. It was not a viable size by any stretch of the imagination. Even reaching the Rs 100 crore mark was unimaginable. When liberalisation opened the floodgates, Lever bought Lakme and merged sister concern Pond's, creating a mammoth skin care conglomerate, with a presence in every segment and a distribution clout that was terrifying. 
  The rules of the game changed for ever, putting a question mark on the survival of Remee's skin care business.
 Dinesh and his sales & marketing vice-president Raghu Iyer knew it was a matter of time before Manu closed down Remee skin care. While Dinesh prayed silently for a miracle, Raghu kept his cool. Said Raghu: "I know many companies are hiving off non-core businesses. Our skin care business, even if not core, is certainly the business of the future. No doubt it is small, but just look at the opportunities. We have been in this business for over 30 years. We have already covered the learning hub to some extent. Then there is our distribution set-up, which is a gold mine. Today distribution determines success. When there is so much in the bag, why don't we try grow this business seriously for a year? After all, new players are entering this field... We can make this work."
  Dinesh heard him out keenly. Theoretically, liberalisation offered new opportunities which were not available earlier like the freedom to import raw materials or even finished goods, to tie up with a foreign player for technology or marketing skills, or for leveraging distribution strengths. "We could, in fact, become a distribution company, take on the brands of large companies and sell and distribute them," he thought.
  Dinesh, for all his enthusiasm, was not sure if they had what it took for success. But Raghu egged him on. They had to put the past behind them, he said, for the future beckoned and Raghu did not want to give up yet.
  "There was no compelling need then to prove one's might," he reasoned with Dinesh. "In those days of protectionism, one could go on for years doing barely anything at all. Then again, when were the skin care companies so savvy in India? For years we coasted along with cold creams."
  Remee could capitalise on its strong distribution network, but at no time in the past was it challenged. "The people and markets were always there, even the demand we can say was there, if latent, but we did not push our distributive strengths to its limits. But today, just look at what our competitors are doing. Lever has capitalised on its distribution strength," said Raghu.
  Dinesh shook his head. "It won't work Raghu. What are we trying to do? Put in the money only to have them wipe us out?"

In the pre-liberalisation days, Remee's skin care business coasted along, happy with its small product base, protected markets and little advertising. It did not do anything dramatic to grow.

At the next stage, I would look for answers to the following key issues: In this highly competitive skin care industry, what is the 'niche' that Remee India can dominate? How fast and how soon would Remee India be able to enter this segment? What innovative and unique business processes can be created to be a successful player in this chosen 'niche'? This analysis is critical because business is all about focus -- it is very easy to get into several areas, trying to be everything to everybody. But you can end up being nothing to anybody.
  Several companies followed this focused approach and came up with success stories. One that comes readily to my mind is Velvette shampoo. In a market dominated by the likes of Lever and Procter & Gamble, Velvette became a formidable brand in a very short time (obviously capitalising on the excise laws at that time) by choosing the sachet route. Velvette was one of the first to sell shampoo in sachets, rewriting the rules to become a significant player. Had it taken Lever head-on in the bottle segment for shampoos, it would probably have had no success.

  Even so, Dinesh met Manu and put forth his case. "There is a future if you believe there is one," Dinesh told Manu. "I believe Indian companies can survive liberalisation provided they believe there is a future for them. It just requires vision and optimism. We only need to be persistent, innovative, creatively dynamic... Leave no stone unturned."
  But Manu was wary. "Dinesh, you are suggesting that I put in more money so that you can experiment with your ideology," said Manu. "That's unfair Manu, there is my career too," said Dinesh. "Besides, when so many new entrants are coming in, wouldn't it be easier for me to thank you for the wonderful times and move on to an American or French major and mint my millions? I can go there and peddle this very mind and intelligence to strategise for them, build their millions and their brands. Why can't I use that very intelligence here?"
  Manu was cynical about the opportunities that liberalisation offered. Yet he didn't want to discourage Dinesh. "All right," said Manu finally. "But if I don't see results in a year, I go ahead with my plan," he said. Then he asked: "What is your plan?"
  Dinesh felt Remee needed to bring in far more round-the-year products; products that were relevant and which had a mass base. Then again, he said, Remee alone could not drive growth. To survive in the fast changing market, the company needed to grow quickly and hence required more products. "Therefore, we need to launch shampoos, after-shave products, hand washes, face washes, maybe a good Remee soap which uses the Remee cream base..."
  The soap idea interested Manu. Why not, he asked himself. Soap was the biggest skin care tool in India, and if he was saying that Remee was in the business of skin care how could he not be in soap? Even so, the prospect of entering the soap market worried him.
  But Dinesh was upbeat. "The soap idea is good," he said confidently. "We can use Remee soap to reach out to a much wider audience, bring it into the Remee fold and slowly graduate from there. We can get into body lotions, which is an area that is just beginning to grow; it's the right time to enter it, and it is a round-the-year product. We need new products, more competitive pricing and more of a mass reach."
  Over the next few days Dinesh decided to redefine Remee's business. Along with Raghu, he looked at the distribution network Remee had built, and agreed that it was sheer neglect that had weakened the system. Today when new companies were desperately seeking ready-to-use distribution networks, Remee was sitting on a gold mine.

  Dinesh then got on to the serious task of strengthening the sales and distribution teams. To the sales team he said: "We have stopped thinking differently, daringly, innovatively. We need to have the guts to try out the craziest of ideas in the marketplace. It's the only way for a small outfit with little money, like us, to grab the consumer's attention."
  The next few months were hectic for Dinesh and his team. The timing was unusually right. Summer was six months away and if they could get the products out by February, they would catch the market at the beginning of tbut he season, he reckoned.
  Simultaneously, he set to work on Remee's image. Dinesh spent long hours brainstorming with Raghu. Two things emerged from the discussions: one, Remee had to become more visible in the marketplace. 
  "Everybody knows the brand, but nobody remembers it," said Dinesh, "and somehow that knowledge does not seem enough to translate into purchases."
  Raghu agreed. "Most of the time, people don't remember it. If they do, the chances of their buying it will be high," he said. The brand enjoyed a lot of goodwill and that was a card they had to play. "It's a brand that won't let you down," said Raghu, not realising he had gone on to create the stunning new ad line for Remee. For that summed up the brand's image in the market. The whole platform on which Remee stood was nurturing care, and that is what they all now realised. So now the entire focus of their strategy was to make Remee visible. Dinesh and Raghu decided to test the idea at Goodsland, a supermarket in the suburbs. They installed a dramatic boot-shaped trolley using the Remee colours, layered with shelves and decorated with purple and white festoons and filled it with Remee products of every size and packing. Goodsland, which had for years given them sales of Rs 9,000 a month, now gave them an eye-popping Rs 2 lakh! Manu was astounded. "What did you do?" he asked Dinesh. "Just reminded the consumer that Old Faithful is still there," said Dinesh. The storekeeper agreed. "I have been running this supermarket for nine years and I have watched the performance of so many brands here. Even the new brands that came in didn't attract so much interest. This boat full of Remee somehow touched the consumers' hearts." By the following month, Dinesh and Raghu acquired strategic spots in over 5,000 outlets, decked up the shelves with ribbons and dew-drops, increased the point-of-purchase display and the advertising.
  The winter of 1997 saw hectic activity at Remee, with preparations on for the launch of a soap in the coming summer. That was when Dinesh got a call from a friend in Delaware's joint venture, Progg. "Have you sensed any drop in market movements? We are feeling a dramatic slowing down..!"

Even though Remee's tieups for distribution products of other companies had worked well, this strategy had its pitfalls. What would it do if its partners decided to set up their own outfits?

  But Dinesh had not felt anything. Even so, he took note. After all, Delaware was a huge company and was therefore more likely to be correct in predicting or sensing market pulse changes. "This winter has been very humid," said the friend, "so moisturising creams and lotions are not selling well. The No. 2 in the market, Dewy, has lost almost 50% of its share in this one season!"
  In such a season, Remee, which was a distant No. 6, had actually turned around and moved way up the charts! Dinesh didn't know what to make of this trend. "Clearly, Remee has a consumer value which is unaffected by seasonal distortions," said Manu. Dinesh didn't let himself get carried away by this performance. As he said to Raghu: "Let us not get overoptimistic. None of us has clear reasons for this behaviour. If we believe a maverick market, we will be digging our grave."
  But Dinesh had mixed feelings. On the one hand, he needed the optimism and would have loved to believe that Remee was coming alive. That was the sort of news he needed to reassure Manu that the skin care business could survive. Yet, one stupid mistake and the shutters would be drawn on the division.
  Remee's marketshare did not grow, but neither did it lose marketshare in a scenario that was destroying the bigger players. Dinesh now prepared to build a strong position in the skin care market. As long as Remee remained outside the C class markets, there wasn't much scope for building volumes. But going into the C class market required a product suited to the category, and the recession didn't encourage such moves. To complicate matters, Manu received an offer for a buyout from a consumer products major. Dinesh naturally cringed. Recent events in the industry provided no encouragement to stay on, unless he hung on to the idea that Remee had survived the bad winter... Surprisingly, Manu put the offer on hold, preferring to wait the one year he had promised Dinesh. But the doomsayers were aplenty. "The next millennium is for those who cross the Rs 100 crore mark. You don't stand a chance, sell out while the offers are still coming," they said. The summer of 1998 saw the launch of Remee soap, which was successful, much to everyone's surprise. But Dinesh knew that the soap was not enough. Remee's creams and lotions too had to perk up, for their survival determined the soap's future. The synergy would help both product groups, he realised.
  Even as sales picked up, Dinesh thought: "Just Remee is not going to be enough. Neither can we keep doing this `visibility' act for too long. We need to do something else to sustain the memory. Besides, the fate of the division cannot hang on one brand. Since we have this very driven distribution network, we need to bring in more products of the same quality, going to the same outlets, which have some synergy. We must use the existing infrastructure to service a far better throughput."
  Dinesh looked at the horizon keenly for new products. In six weeks he had examined 12 products, but none was inspiring. That was when he came across Besille, a hair care company that was looking for a distribution tieup in India. The product category suited Dinesh, who did not want any product that was in conflict with Remee's range. Besides, hair care also gave him the width of range he needed.
  Dinesh realised how much liberalisation had benefited them. "Today we can import just about anything and put it into national distribution in as short a time as six weeks! And we're offering terrific reach as well, if you consider the networks we have developed."

  Remee was one of the companies that the market had written off in the wake of liberalisation. It fell in the category of companies that appeared big, strong and active, and had one or two products that stayed on shop shelves simply because there was space. These companies just stayed where they were, the people stayed where they were; new people joined, but the older ones didn't go. They had high costs and low revenues. But they also had manufacturing facilities, selling strengths and product management teams. They were sitting ducks for takeovers, which is what the market predicted for Remee too.
  Now Dinesh had reason to feel hopeful. Remee had gained visibility and had survived one very bad winter. Sales had increased, but he still had the task of pushing the Remee brand to a younger segment and to year-round usage. 
  But that needed money, and whatever they had was used to deliver extra mileage through the new innovative marketing ploys."
  Over the next six months, Besille's success at Remee India triggered off a chain of new business opportunities from various prospective new entrants seeking distribution tieups. One such was Cover, a foot care and treatment range of sloughing grains, corn treatment, rough skin vitaliser and nail toner. It suited Remee. It did not compete with its other ranges and was a product which went through the channels that Remee was servicing, through the normal route it had. Remee wouldn't have to make any additional investment.
  The overall gameplan had worked so far. Remee had survived and gained visibility. It had increased its range with a soap and a lotion. Besille and Cover had come at the right time to leverage on its distribution strengths.    But at the end of the day it was just trading, felt Raghu. "How long can we sustain it? I feel if the ownership is not there, the stakes will be low and our interest may wane. Don't forget, even if we are great joint venture partners, we can't be sure about our partners and what demands they might make. Once they get a foothold in India, they may set up 100% subsidiaries and terminate the contract. What then?"
  Dinesh now wondered whether he was making a short-term decision just to stay afloat. "As a business opportunity it may be interesting and useful for starters," he said to Manu. "But we will always be vulnerable as partners."
  The question that plagued both Manu and Dinesh was: how viable was this gameplan? What other strengths did they need to develop? What, if any, pitfalls were they overlooking? How should they leverage this distribution strength? Could they continue being a mere trading company for all these brands? No doubt they manufactured Remee, but how successful was it going to be when `only distribution' businesses did not allow them brand ownership?
  Where does Remee go from here? Dinesh was happy that their gamble had paid off. Remee was today soaring high with a turnover that had more than trebled. But change was so endemic to corporate life these days that he was not willing to rest on his laurels. No doubt he had a couple of winning formulae to change the direction of growth at Remee. These formulae would see him over the hub of failure in the short term, but he needed to innovate and introspect every day so that Remee would keep pace with the changes in the marketplace.
  Today distribution was Remee's perceived advantage. But Dinesh needed to evolve a system for continuously inventing new advantages. As he said to Raghu: "I can't really call all this `strategy'. It is at best an operational optimisation effort. We have put together a gameplan that helps us do profitable things with our distribution resources. But what we need to do is examine what else we are capable of within the dimensions of our learning and then examine what new resources and learning we need to acquire. In short, this survival is to be seen as merely an event in our lives. Do we know where we go from here?
QUE SERA SERA?
Shunu Sen is the chief executive officer of Quadra Advisory, a strategic marketing consultancy organisation in partnership with WPP plc of the UK.

TO PARAPHRASE Charlie Brown, Dinesh must feel that happiness is not knowing what to do next. As the marketing director of Remee India's personal products business, Dinesh Vira has a stable containing a range of products for skin care, hair care and even foot care. Indeed, a showcase in his outer office which looked sparse and empty just two years ago is now full; full with three different ranges of products in three different categories. Looks like good work, well done. 
  However, a close look at the business seems to show that all is not well. Remee is still a skin care category business, very much in the basic skin care business, marketing talcum powder and a multi-purpose, all-weather cream and some other minor products.

  New launches, such as deodorants and freshness soap, haven't really been successful; at best (to quote the case) a tentative success.
  On the other hand, Remee has certainly met success as a distributor for a range of imported products such as Besille hair care and Cover foot care products. Both imported brands have done well in the market, and though the case does not really amplify Remee's role, I am pretty sure that Remee provided more than just distribution.
  But the main question continues to be what should Remee do in the future. In the post-liberalisation world, being a distribution house is certainly not enough; indeed, all the distribution houses in India -- Voltas, TTK, Muellar and Phipps and all the others have long disappeared.
  In addition, building foreign brands in India carries with it the risk that the owners of the brandname can -- indeed most certainly will -- build their own business once the brand is well established in the country. As Dinesh has correctly concluded, making a series of short-term decisions, just to stay afloat, does not necessarily result in a long-term business vision.

In the post-liberalisation world, being a distribution company is not enough; Indeed, all the distribution houses in India have disappeared.

  Recently I have been speaking to some young brand managers from the area of understanding consumer behaviour as being an essential prerequisite in developing marketing strategies. In that context, I found this quotation from Alice in Wonderland most appropriate; indeed, it is so even in the context of this case study:
  "Would you tell me, please, which way I ought to go from here?" asked Alice.  
  "That depends a good deal on where you want to get to," said the Cat.
  The key question that has to be answered by Manu Pal is whether the skin care business is a business in which Remee India and MP Industries are interested. If they are interested in the business, they require a lot more than being a good distribution house and have a small set of nondescript, rather old-fashioned brands.
  Indeed, the need is to develop a skin care culture within the business and understand the market, major competitors, consumers and the overall environment in which the market is operating. Unless this is done, Remee India's skin care business has no future.
  Take the example of Pond's in India during the pre-liberalisation years. Around 80% of its profits came from its export business, which accounted for just 20% of its turnover.

  Though a market leader in some segments of the skin care market, such as talcum powder and basic skin creams, its record in new product development, was, to put it bluntly, miserable and margins earned in its traditional business were poor.
  In the post-liberalisation period, with the help of Unilever technology and having developed a significantly better understanding of consumer needs, Pond's has metamorphosed itself into a high-quality, mass-market, skin care business.
  A leader in its basic products, talcum powder and skin creams, Pond's made an enormous effort to understand consumer needs within its category and has leveraged the Unilever technical research capability to produce a series of products which provide skin care and skin improvement benefits relevant to the Indian consumer's needs. At the time of the business amalgamation with Hindustan Lever, Pond's was a profitable operation on the basis of its domestic business.
  However, the post-liberalisation years have seen the demise of several skin care businesses which, at one point of time, were considered to be quite successful. Brands such as Vicco, Himani, Emami and many others have fallen by the wayside. Compared to these brands Remee has, indeed, managed to do well.
  However, the question for the future: what can Remee do now? The company has got time, but time, like everything else, will run out.
  Dinesh knows this very well. And, more importantly, Manu knows it. Remee must now take its first steps down the long arduous path of changing itself from being a distribution company to becoming a marketing company.
  It must now begin to understand, with the help of research, where necessary, who its potential consumers are, what their needs are, and how Remee can make products to best satisfy these needs. This does not cost a lot of money, nor does it need great marketing capability: merely the will to succeed and the ability to get ahead.
  It will just be a matter of time before we know whether Dinesh and Manu can actually do what is required; create a real marketing business out of a distribution company.
FIND YOUR NICHE..
Ram S. Ramasundar is CEO of the Electrolux group of companies in India.

  THE liberalisation process, which began in 1991, presents opportunities as well as threats -- it all depends on how a business is able to handle the micro and macro scenarios that emerged in its wake. Unfortunately, many businesses have not been able to get a good grip on the new situation, causing heartburn and other problems.
  Remee India seems to be no exception. The problem is its lack of clear business vision, business strategy and an inadequate preparation for the new challenges in the changed scenario. Before proceeding further, let's do a brief SWOT analysis of Remee India.

  Strengths: Its distribution network is Remee India's single biggest strength. And it also appears that the company has a loyal customer base and thus, one can presume, a reasonably good brand strength. 
  Weaknesses: Although it has a strong brand, it appears that it is no longer relevant in the modern context, suffering as it does from the lack of a contemporary image for the emerging youth market. In addition, it has no significant product range, with nearly 60% of its sales coming from a single multi-purpose all-weather cream. Also, it doesn't have much money.
  Opportunities: Like in the case of everybody else, the liberalised environment offers potential market opportunities and there is also the prospect of a major emerging youth segment.
  Threats: The financial constraints, lack of a broad product range and dependence on a single brand/product renders Remee very vulnerable and makes it a prime takeover target.

Remee's problem is its lack of business vision. Business strategy and inadequate preparation for the new challenges in the changed scenario.

  In this background, chairman Manu Pal and marketing director Dinesh Vira do not seem to have a very clear idea on the future direction of the business. You cannot manage a business on a day-to-day basis with survival in the short-term as the single aim, because that is not going to get the business anywhere. To stay in any business, one needs to have a very clear vision for the business from a medium- to long-term perspective.     Both Pal and Vira place too much emphasis on their distribution strength. Unfortunately, they're missing the wood for the trees, since a distribution system can be effective only if you have relevant products to offer to the end consumer, that is, the product which the consumer wants.
  Being a very small player (Rs 30 crore turnover), Remee India can never hope to match the deep pockets of its competitors; it can't spend so much on marketing, new product development or technology upgradation. In this scenario, if I were Pal, I would go back to the drawing board, jot down the following key questions and try to find the answers:
  What is Remee India's core competence?
  What sort of competitive advantages can Remee India build vis-a-vis the competition?
  What is the salient brand strength (promise) which can be leveraged and extended to other product categories?
  Finding the answers would point the direction for the future. The skin care business, to my knowledge, is extremely competitive, dominated by powerful players with huge financial resources, mega-brands and huge distribution networks.

  At the next stage, I would look for answers to the following key issues: In this highly competitive skin care industry, what is the 'niche' that Remee India can dominate? How fast and how soon would Remee India be able to enter this segment? What innovative and unique business processes can be created to be a successful player in this chosen 'niche'? This analysis is critical because business is all about focus -- it is very easy to get into several areas, trying to be everything to everybody. But you can end up being nothing to anybody.
  Several companies followed this focused approach and came up with success stories. One that comes readily to my mind is Velvette shampoo. In a market dominated by the likes of Lever and Procter & Gamble, Velvette became a formidable brand in a very short time (obviously capitalising on the excise laws at that time) by choosing the sachet route. Velvette was one of the first to sell shampoo in sachets, rewriting the rules to become a significant player. Had it taken Lever head-on in the bottle segment for shampoos, it would probably have had no success.
  Having decided to give the skin care business one last shot, I would suggest this five-step approach to Manu and Dinesh:
  Identify your core competencies.
  Identify a specific niche which can be created in the skin care segment -- surely this cannot be soaps.
  Create a business process which gives a competitive advantage -- this could be in the form of superior customer service, for example.
  Set up clear milestones, action standards and benchmarks, along with a time plan.
  Examine whether you can create some presence programmes (like the Goodsland experiment) which have a lot of impact within the resource constraints.
  At the end of the time frame, if Remee is unable to come up with a viable strategy, it would be better off selling the business.
  There are certain never-ending debates in the world of business such as economic return on investment versus volumes and marketshares; core versus non-core; short-term versus long-term. The trick is to identify the right balance between these polarities. Successful businesses are those that are able to find the correct balance.
  Dinesh, develop a proper plan on the lines suggested above, and give it all you have. There's enough room for big as well as small players in an industry, provided the small player knows his strengths and limitations and acts according to them.
  Good luck!
Designed by: Epsilon Systems - SQ-80, Hauz Khas Appts, New Delhi-16. Phone: 685-3301, 685-0878, 98100-06431

Case Study 5


 19 Jul 1999		 OF ETHICS AND EFFICIENCY
MEERA SETH
Steven Fraser was in a fix. His efficient man friday Dilip Khare was found to be corrupt. Should Khare be asked to go, or should he be given another chance? .
  STEVEN Fraser read the fax message as it rolled out of the machine. "We have discussed Tapas Roy's case amongst ourselves, with our legal advisors and HR director Maya Suri. We have also given due consideration to your feelings on the matter. 
  However, we feel Roy's involvement in the accident was marginal, unpremeditated and his subsequent actions a result of panic. We feel it would be too harsh and unnecessary to terminate his service. The matter has been recorded in his personal dossier...."
   Steven, the country manager of Pemento India, shook his head. "Very well, so be it..," he muttered, not entirely happy. Personally, he had nothing against Roy, but the entire episode was fraught with callousness and insensitivity, he felt. 
  Four months ago, Roy, a salesman with Pemento, had hitched a ride with a friend on his bike. Roy was attending to a sales call at Masjid Bunder in Mumbai and since he was late for a post-lunch area review meeting, he jumped on to a friend's bike to make it to Flora Fountain before 3 p.m. In the rush to get Roy to his office, the friend jumped a red light at Mohammed Ali Road and that was when he hit a pedestrian. 
  Intending to help the lady, Roy got off the bike, but his friend sped off. Rattled and fearing mob fury, Roy got into a taxi and fled too, but not before the public got the cab number. The lady, it turned out, was a school teacher whose husband was a lawyer" he filed a hit and run case. 
  Unable to find the bike rider, the police traced Roy through the cabby to the office of Pemento India and what followed was a nightmare. Roy claimed he did not know the person who drove the bike, that he had simply hitched a ride. That did not wash with the police or the lawyer who threatened to file a case of negligence. Eventually Roy was forced to identify his friend.  
  Steven was hopping mad. "This is absurd," he yelled. "It is a matter of a serious human rights violation and at Pemento we take a serious view of these issues. Roy is guilty on two counts: one, neglecting to help an injured party when he was partly responsible for the injury, and two, lying that he didn't know the driver of the bike. Clearly, he will have to leave Pemento.;" 
  HR director Maya Suri tried hard to defend Roy. "There is no court proceeding against him" the law has declared that his responsibility was minimal because he was not the driver. Roy simply happened to be there," said Maya. "Nonsense!" said Steven. "What view the law takes has nothing to do with human rights. That a responsible member of the company, on duty, acts in a manner not befitting the image of the company is the issue. 
  How can we take a lenient view of his lying, of ignoring a pedestrian who has been hurt by the very friend he was riding with? If he could act thus to save himself, could he not similarly lie to the detriment of the company to benefit himself? Can we be seen as a company that protects such a person?"
   "Steven, Roy is young and ignorant," said Maya. "He `lied' as you say, but that was because he panicked. He had intended to help the victim, but fearing public ire, he fled. This is India, Steven, where mob fury is unmitigated and lethal. Then again, just because a friend was in the wrong does not make Roy an accomplice. Roy was in a fix and he was trying to manage it somehow. The fact that he came straight to the office and didn't disappear is proof enough of his honest intentions. To now say that Roy was also guilty of negligent behaviour appears unfair to me. We must stand by him." 
  "People must learn to accept responsibility even for their ignorance and for their inability to act responsibly," said Steven. "Social responsibility is an integral part of being a corporate citizen." Maya had then taken up the matter with the board, whose verdict was now lying in print on Steven's table. 
  Steven shrugged his shoulders and put the fax into a tray. Looking up he saw Dilip Khare, his personal assistant, standing before him with a pile of papers. "Dilip, Roy stays, that's the board's decision," he said. "What do you think?" "It's best to forgive and forget," said Dilip. 
  Steven considered the remark as he watched Dilip potter around the room putting away papers and files, pulling out folders and setting the alarm for a meeting at 4.30 p.m. As he put a post-it sticker which read `Stockist Mathur' on the clock, Steven smiled. "What would I do without you, Dilip?;" he asked. 
  Dilip was more than a personal assistant. He did much more than his job called for, which meant managing the life of his boss too. When the Frasers came to India, it was Dilip who arranged their stay, accompanied them on shopping sprees for linen and upholstery, cutlery and gadgets, even helped with their house lease, bought the car chosen by Mrs Fraser, organised its servicing, the petrol account, a driver... In short, he was the one person who had made their India posting manageable. 
  Steven was enormously overworked. If he left the workplace at 7 p.m., it was only to return to his study at 10 p.m. and slave away at market reports, plans, etc. Often he would call Dilip at 7 in the morning to dictate the tasks for the day, before he left for the plant. To make matters easy he had given Dilip a cellular phone. Later in the day they would talk and check on work done and new jobs to be done. 
  Dilip in turn managed Steven's deadlines, meetings, follow-ups on action plans etc. He was very efficient and Steven, who had come to India wondering about the quality of assistance he might have, was relieved. He encouraged Dilip to study further -- a course in sales management -- and promised him that his dream of getting into core operations would be fulfilled before Steven left India. 
After 18 months in the country, Steven had come to rely on Dilip Completely. He was his coping mechanism in the hectic life that the indian operation entailed. Now Dilip managed a lot more independently
  At 26, Dilip was very ambitious with dreams of making it big, even to step into Steven's shoes if he could. So enamoured was he of Steven's style and demeanour that, within a few months, he took to growing a small French beard like his boss. Steven had no class biases, shared his cigars with Dilip and often took him out to lunch at the various five-star restaurants he dined at. It was Steven's desire to treat him as an equal, socially.
   By the end of 18 months, Steven had come to rely on Dilip completely. He was Steven's means of coping with the hectic life that the Indian operation entailed. Now Dilip managed a lot more independently, like collecting sales reports, collating them and e-mailing them to Steven wherever he was. Or issuing print orders for new stationery, buying supplies and even deciding on the supplier. Steven did not have the time, or the inclination, to look into such detail. He had trained Dilip adequately so that Dilip knew what was needed, anticipate them and execute them as necessary. The odd query that came directly to Steven from the market was efficiently passed on to the functionaries concerned for action. Pemento was now on the threshold of bigger things and the routine had to fall into place
  But Dilip's airs and his demeanour irked the managers. It was common to see him, feet on the table, a cigar stuck between his lips, talking on the phone when Steven was not around. At such moments if any of the managers walked in, Dilip would not correct his posture, but gesture to the visitor to sit, which was rather unbecoming of an personal assistant, it was felt.
  Dilip's growing irreverence towards his seniors did not escape Maya Suri's eye. "When you are working with a senior person like the country manager, your conduct comes under scrutiny. It is very important for you to be respectful and courteous, no matter what pressures you may be under," she had said to Dilip. "For instance," she said, "I wouldn't walk into Steven's room with a cigar stuck between my lips, as you are now doing." Dilip grinned and readily pulled the cigar off his mouth and stubbed it out on the floor. 
  Tough though she was, Maya knew that Dilip carried an efficient head on very young shoulders. At 26, he was like any youngster, full of cola and MTV and an attitude to match. But when it came to work, Dilip was the personification of efficiency and agility. 
  Three weeks later something was brought to Maya's notice. Her assistant, who was examining the medical bills, noticed a discrepancy in Dilip's bills. A doctor's bill for Rs 700 appeared to have been altered -- the number `7' was actually a `1' which was creatively changed to `7'. Of course, where the amount was written in words, the alteration was apparent. 
  Maya decided to investigate. Going up to the accountant, she asked to see some printing bills for Steven's jobs and found more evidence of hanky panky: a bill from a printer for visiting cards where the numbers had been changed, resulting in a premium of Rs 2,100. The bill was handwritten and there was no telephone number of the printer. But a fading rubber stamp revealed the address. 
   On her way home that evening, Maya drove to the printer and met a Singhal who confirmed he had printed some cards for the company. She asked for a quotation for a set of 250 cards and 1,000 letterheads" the figures were much lower than the amount mentioned on the bill she had seen that noon. Maya looked around and felt that this was hardly the kind of printer her company was likely to use. 
  "What other work have you done for us?" she asked. "I will be doing a big job this month for Dilip saab,;" he said. "And how much is it worth?" she asked. "Difficult to say, but I suppose it will be around Rs 15,000-20,000." "Have you quoted for it?" she asked. 
  "Dilip saab said he would decide. I have even left blank copies of my bills which he said he would fill himself," said the man. 
  As Maya probed more firmly and with authority, the story tumbled out. Dilip would bill as he decided and when the company paid, the printer was to return 40% of the bill amount in cash to Dilip and be assured of more jobs in the future. 
  Maya decided to mention her fears to Steven. He was naturally very surprised. "No, no, it can't be, not Dilip. Not that boy. You may be making a mistake, or that printer probably has a grouse against Dilip,;" he said. 
  But when Maya mentioned the medical bills, it was too much for Steven. "Good Lord!" he said. "I wish this is not true." But it was very true. Steven confronted Dilip. 
  "Do you have anything to say about this?" he asked. 
  "I don't know what you are talking about," said Dilip. Maya, who until now had decided to keep the printer out, said: "The printer tells me you have blank bills which you fill..." 
  "That's a lot of rubbish," said Dilip. "Why would I do anything like that?"
  "That's what I thought too," said Maya, "but then I saw these bills for visiting cards and letterheads and felt that Rs 4,500 was far too expensive!" 
  "That's what I thought too, but the job was done and I decided not to use the printer again," lied Dilip. 
  But Maya kept the pressure on. She mentioned the medical bills and other lapses and eventually Dilip confessed he was in dire need of money. "It won't happen again," he promised. 
  For both Maya and Steven, Dilip's dishonesty was too much to take. Later Maya said: "Dilip cannot stay with us any more, he will have to go." 
  Steven was half expecting that, yet he was taken aback by the finality of her words. "He is a young person, ambitious, aspiring to go places, stars in his eyes and so full of life. Does he really have to be sacked for this?" 
  "It is certainly improper to keep him on," replied Maya.
After Dilip's dishonest practices came to light, steven was shocked, but reluctant to let him go. "He is a young person, ambitious and so full of life. Does he have to be sacked for this?" He asked
  What would we achieve?" asked Steven. "Understand what I am saying. We need to also take corrective steps for his own sake. I am sure Dilip is mesmerised by the enchanting world he is trying to conquer. Can't we help him change? After all he is from a nice family, we could help him move forward rather than get stuck with what I see as a moral problem, which certainly can be overcome." 
  Maya was taken aback by Steven's attitude. "How can we condone his actions?" she asked. 
  "Look at the situation, Maya," entreated Steven, confused and overwhelmed by what had happened. "The amount is too small, the person too young... a junior who is on a learning curve, not just professionally, but personally too." 
   Maya was amazed. "Steven, this is an integrity issue and has nothing to do with seniority or the magnitude!" 
  "The issue certainly is of seniority and magnitude," said Steven. "If a senior manager had defrauded the company, my stand would have been that he had the opportunity and the responsibility to act in an ethically proper manner. His seniority indicates that the company has spent a lot of time with him grooming him, imparting corporate values and also given him a chance to lead a noble life. And by virtue of his qualities he was given charge of a responsible portfolio for which he was expected to behave soundly and have moral qualities. Therefore, when such a senior manager is found guilty of misdemeanour, there can be no doubt that he can't continue in the firm.   
 "But in the case of Dilip, I see two things: he is young and as such inexperienced in the ways of the world" he is still learning about the need for sound conduct and the need for ethical behaviour. Besides, he is beginning his career, on the learning curve, which covers not only functional skills but ethical skills as well. Therefore, we should give him a chance."
  But Maya felt his retention would be seen as `overlooking' an impropriety and not as `forgiveness' which in any case was out of place in an organisation. Were organisations to now dabble in reforming people, when their sole purpose was business?
  And this was the same Steven who had argued ceaselessly over Roy's misdemeanour, insisting he had no place in the organisation. Yet now, Steven was seeking lighter treatment for an obviously dishonest Dilip, felt Maya.
  "I am not condoning what Dilip has done," said Steven, "but I can also see his other side -- he is hardworking and efficient. I am keen to rescue Dilip from the abyss of dishonesty."
  Steven felt that sacking a person from his job could damage his career and life. Should a company exercise such a right irresponsibly, he asked. "He has always done a great job and been an example of efficiency. What has happened now is an aberration. What is our social or corporate responsibility in this situation? Declare he is a bad egg and sack him, or do we say, okay he was dishonest, but we are going to help him out of what could become a serious behavioural issue?" 
 "If I let him go, I will be setting a precedent whereby I will not be able to challenge any integrity issue in the future with credibility or authority," said Maya.
  "Not at all," said Steven. "You must challenge and question each integrity issue whenever it crops up. But I am also saying that every time it occurs we must contribute to reform."
  "Steven, at the end of the day, we are running a business in the expectation that our people will act with responsibility and integrity. If I have a doubt about even five people in my organisation, whether I like it or not, it will be reflected in my behaviour with them. And it will be impossible to work with them as a team. For, each time I ask them to do something, I will find myself triple-checking their motives and actions. I will not be able to ensure their commitment. What is to stop a person who rationalises wrong behaviour once from repeating it? So, I have a problem. And if my team realises that the consequence is only going to be counselling, there will be havoc! How am I then to take action against persons who travel economy and charge business class fares? Or even a peon who takes Rs 10 for an errand? If the organisation is seen as one that does not punish dishonest behaviour, it will become corrupt. Then what?"
  Did we punish Roy's misdemeanour?" asked Steven. "Why wasn't that an integrity issue? Please do not think I am being biased. I really am trying to understand what your reasoning is," he said honestly. "Was it because Dilip's actions are quantifiable in money terms while Roy's is not? Why was Roy's dishonesty different, Maya?" he asked.
  "Stealing is different from what happened to Roy," explained Maya. "Stealing or defrauding has a certain planning and deliberation attached to it. If you get away with it once, the chances are high that you will repeat it. Which is what Dilip did. He played with bills not once but thrice. But Roy is going to be doubly careful that he does not get into the same situation. What happened to him was an accident, there was no premeditation. There was not even the intention to hurt."
  "But Roy lied too, remember?" said Steven. "He said he did not know the person who gave him a ride on the bike."
  "Let's face it Steven, it is not my business to be a watchdog of integrity in the manner you are saying. In Dilip's case, there has been a deviation from accepted behaviour, or behaviour expected of a person holding a job such as his. Roy's situation was not `on the job'. The maximum I am willing to do is not give him a bad certificate. If it is reform that you are seeking, we could ask him to resign rather than sack him."
  Steven thought for a while. Then he said: "In doing so are you being fair and honest to the next organisation that he will join? Now what happens to your ethics? You know what will happen? Angered by the whole episode, he will go to another organisation and say he left Pemento because Steven and Maya were terrible people to work with."
  Maya, as head of HR, was in a dilemma. Should Pemento sack Dilip or reform him? As far as she saw it, what Dilip did was blatantly dishonest and age had nothing to do with it. It was an integrity issue. And it had to do with misdemeanour on the job. Dilip was handling a very senior manager's portfolio and the derived authority and power such a position gave him was abused, she felt.

DILIP HAS TO GO

  Zahid Gangee heads a Calcutta-based human resource and organisation development consultancy firm. He heads the manpower development group at ITC.
   DILIP Khare's case is a tragic one. An efficient quick learner with a supportive boss investing in his growth, he is a victim of the `now' culture which urges people to attain immediate, personal gratification. Dilip is also a victim of accelerated growth and responsibility which was badly managed and which he could not cope with on his own. But ultimately, he is a victim of his own vaulting ambitions and values.
 Steven Fraser arrived in India with his own fears of not being able to manage in a new culture and worried about the `quality of assistance he might have'. He was relieved to find in Dilip a hard-working, efficient and agile resource who also helped him settle in (`linen, upholstery, cutlery and gadgets... house lease, car, petrol account, driver...).
  At work, Steven found Dilip a dependable resource who managed his `deadlines, meetings, follow-ups on action plans etc'. Within 18 months Steven had come to `rely on Dilip completely'.
  Their relationship also became a dependent one. Steven treated Dilip as an equal at work and socially -- taking him out for lunch, sharing his cigars with him, etc. These twin forces of task and emotional dependencies made Steven delegate more and more authority to Dilip. There are no data in the case to suggest that there were checks and balances.
  The first evidence that Dilip was not being able to manage his new-found powers came in the form of aberrant behaviour. Sitting with his feet up on the table, cigar clutched in teeth and gesturing to visitors to sit while talking on the phone -- this behaviour would not be tolerated in any Indian organisation, no matter how senior the manager. That Dilip -- who was 26 years old and very junior -- displayed such behaviour is evidence that he had begun seeing himself as above the organisation or larger than the institution.
HR Head Maya Suri was not willing to send out the message at pemento that as long as people were efficient they could be dishonest
 Every organisation runs on basic beliefs and values that express themselves as norms -- unwritten rules about what people can and cannot do. Dilip had neither the power nor the credibility to unilaterally change the rules of `expected behaviour'. Yet he persisted -- removing the cigar from his mouth only after Maya Suri, the HR head, pointed it out to him, and without an apology. He seemed to really believe he could live by his own rules.
  At this stage it becomes necessary to compare Dilip's case with that of Tapas Roy's. Roy was a frightened coward who fled from the scene of the accident `fearing mob fury' (very real in India) and did lie -- to protect his friend who had sped to ensure that Roy was in time for his area review meeting. His behaviour was a reaction to the situation he found himself in and the feeling of panic within him.
  Dilip's case was totally different. He altered the amounts in his medical bill. He did the same with the printer's bill and then set up a system where he had blank bills which he could fill at will. This is very cold-blooded and planned behaviour -- very different from that exhibited by Roy who was reacting in panic. Though, after trying to bluster his way through Maya Suri's confrontation, Dilip did say that he was in dire need of money, there is no case data to tell us if he had a sick parent or gambling debts that created internal pressures that drove him to cheat Pemento. It seems more likely that Dilip had begun believing that he was the cleverest around and that normal rules did not apply to him.
  In Roy's case Steven had said: "People must learn to accept responsibility even for their ignorance and for their inability to act responsibly." Which is very true. It is sad to see him pleading for Dilip because of his own emotional dependency on him ("the amount is too small", "he is too young... on a learning curve", "inexperienced in the ways of the world", etc.) He is even concerned about what Dilip will say about them to his next employer (`Steven and Maya were terrible people to work with'). 
  No matter how personally painful it is, the head of HR in a company has to keep at least two parameters in mind when taking decisions regarding individuals: How is it going to impact all the other employees? Will it be fair to them? Do our current systems and norms allow this decision to be taken? Is this such an exceptional case that it warrants disrupting the current ways?
  What message will this decision send out regarding what we consider `valuable' in our organisation -- what we believe in and want practised? What impact will it have on the organisational culture?
  Maya Suri is absolutely right. It is `an integrity issue and has nothing to do with seniority or the magnitude'. She was not willing for a message to go out in Pemento that as long as people were task-successful they could be dishonest. She is again correct when she says that in today's fast-paced work life, people cannot work effectively with others if they have to spend time and energy `triple-checking their motives, their intentions and actions'.
  Maya has to ask Dilip to go. At best she can ask him to resign. She does not need to give him any letter of recommendation and Dilip is not stupid enough to give her name as a reference. It does not matter what Dilip tells his future employers regarding Pemento. What should matter to Dilip is that he got away lightly.
  If he is really intelligent, he will use this experience to change his framework of values. No matter what interpretation he had earlier made of `cola and MTV and attitude', he should understand that honesty is the best policy in the long run.

DON'T COMPROMISE ON VALUES

  Ashoke Bijapurkar is a fellow of the Indian Institute of Management, Ahmedabad. He has over 22 years' experience in advertising. Bijapurkar is currently group president of the Rediffusion-Dentsu Young & Rubicam group of companies.
  I WANT to comment on three aspects of this case. First, about the role that Steven was playing versus what I think his role ought to be. Secondly, my view on what condoning defalcation in an organisation actually does to the organisation. Finally, my idea of what an organisation value system is, and how organisations can build and reinforce it.
  Steven Fraser needs to understand that as head of the organisation, he is being called upon to be `judge and jury', and dispense justice from the standpoint of what is right and wrong for the organisation. From this standpoint, the rules are not for him to make.
  Pardon or redemption of sinners should not be in his charter. Nor can he use the organisation as a platform to fight the larger cause of humanity. Steven's role entails dispensing justice objectively, firmly wearing his organisation hat, with total impartiality.
  Steven should arrive at a judgment that is based on the facts available before him, and not on what they could have been or where they could end up going.
  Steven did not perform his role well. He was not objective -- he viewed both situations through the filters of his personal views and personal comfort, rather than as a custodian of the organisation's well-being.
 Thus, Roy was to be indicted because of Steven's passion for, and particular perspective on, human rights (what is the organisation's stand on this matter?). Dilip, on the other hand, was to be let off because he happened to be an asset to Steven (and his wife as well!) personally.
  Steven was not impartial -- that is, he did not use the same yardstick for everyone. Roy's youth and stars in his eyes were not to be used as an excuse for Roy's behaviour, but Dilip's action was sought to be condoned by Steven for the very same reason.
  Steven chose to look beyond the evidence in both cases -- there is hard evidence in one case (Dilip's) of proven premeditated cheating and an intention to continue and in the other (Roy's) there is hard evidence that there is no court case pending against him, and the law had declared that his responsibility was minimal.  
Leaders must make sure that they treat the value system as non-negotiable. Because once you start compromising, there's no end to it
  Maya Suri was all that Steven was not -- she was objective, looked at the cases from the standpoint of the organisation, and dispensed justice impartially based on the facts.
  Let's talk about defalcation as an issue. Unfortunately, cheating an organisation of money is not an isolated case, and most of us in leadership positions are confronted with it at some time or another.
  In my view, an employee cheating an organisation is no different from your son stealing from his classmate, or an organisation cheating customers. It involves taking away wealth that belongs to someone else (the stakeholders), and is not rightfully yours. If this kind of behaviour is allowed to go unchecked, it sends a signal to the entire organisation that this is dacoit land, and you can get away with it.
  Yet often, and rather distressingly, many organisations do not look at cheating in such black and white terms. They respond with greater severity towards those who are easy to replace in the organisation, and show a surprising amount of tolerance towards those that are difficult to dispense with.
  Further, the more senior a person is, the more of a blind eye is turned, and excuses are made for his behaviour. It is not uncommon for A to be found cheating by his boss B and for B's boss C to say: "Forget it, he is an asset to the company." What does A do in this situation? If Steven's view had prevailed (as it may have, had Steven owned a large chunk of the organisation), what should Maya Suri do? Be pragmatic and say she did her best, but she is merely the professional manager; or be idealistic and leave the organisation?
  If she chooses the former route, one thing is certain -- Maya will become a less effective personnel manager and a diminished leader in the eyes of many in the organisation. And once the erosion begins in the organisation, there's no way of stopping it. 
   I firmly believe that organisations must have clear and well understood value systems. Just as many of us tell our children "in this family, this will not be condoned", so too should it be in organisations.
  Organisations cannot be governed by rules alone -- they need to have a value system that is shared by everybody. People with a dissonant personal value system are then welcome to not join, or to leave.
  The biggest challenge for the leader is to establish and disseminate a value system throughout the organisation. This then often obviates the need for him to take on the roles of judge and jury.
  However, having an organisational value system in place is not good enough -- it needs to be constantly reinforced, because there will always be compelling business or personal reasons which make it tempting to follow another path.
  And finally, leaders in organisations must make sure that they treat the value system as non-negotiable. Because once you start negotiating on it, there's no stopping. The first step, as always is the hardest to take.
  Does all this sound utopian, idealistic? Is it the ramblings of someone who belongs to an older generation? Or does it have something to do with good business sense? I would like to think it does!
Designed by: Epsilon Systems - SQ-80, Hauz Khas Appts, New Delhi-16. Phone: 685-3301, 685-0878, 98100-06431

Case Study 6


2 Aug 1999		 NO ROOM FOR THE INN.
MEERA SETH
NRI Alok Aggarwal's dreams of setting up a hotel in India came a cropper in the face of red tape at the lower levels of the Government Machinery.
  ALOK Aggarwal looked out of the aircraft window as it taxied down the runway. "I hope this visit to India is going to be successful," he mused. He was pinning his hopes on getting the land purchase through for his hotel project.  
  His last visit had left him with a lot of anxiety and if he was returning, it was at the behest of Sanat Dani, the IAS officer friend who was full of optimism.
  Alok heard the man next to him, a Swede, hum a happy tune. Happily, he told Alok: "I am really looking forward to this visit to India. I have been waiting a whole year for it! I must see the Taj Mahal." He went on say that he was booked on the Palace on Wheels, an experience he was looking forward to. "Rajasthan is a beautiful state and the Taj Mahal is a fitting tribute to its beauty," he added. 
  Startled, Alok said: "The Taj is not in Rajasthan. It is in Uttar Pradesh." 
  "Oh? But it can't be, my travel agent told me it is in Rajasthan," said the Swede, a trifle confused by what Alok, an Indian, was saying. 
  Alok could see from where he had obtained that impression. The Palace on Wheels was a Rajasthan train which ended its journey in Agra, doing the golden triangle of Jaipur, Delhi and Agra. Naturally the Swede thought that all that it covered was Rajasthan, Alok thought.
  The Swede's remark was on Alok's mind as he alighted from the aircraft. "What a pity," he thought, "70% of the tourists who visit Agra do so without realising that they have been in UP. We have an industry which we can build into something terrific, it offers the basic opportunities, but we have such a poor image, that even the tour operators believe that the Taj is in Rajasthan! Obviously, people at the ground level have not done the job of promoting the Taj as a UP destination. In the process, the state does not get mileage or the tourists," he mused.
  And that was precisely what he had told Sanat on his last visit. "I think India is not being marketed properly to the world," he had said. Alok had come to India after 20 years, to explore a location for his hotel project. A wealthy UK-based businessman, he was keen on participating in the growing, booming recreation and tourism industry. And it was Sanat who was playing guide and advisor.
  Sanat had sounded optimistic. He had done his homework and identified a place called Kinno Valley, a beautiful stretch dotted with historic monuments. "You will love it," he had told Alok. He described its beautiful lakes and open stretches of land, which appeared to Alok ideal for sailing and paragliding. This central Indian state had scenic places ideal for a hotel.
   Sanat had sat with a map and showed Alok the tourist spots; they all looked well-connected on the map, appearing as if a simple train ride could take you through them.
  But the reality was that there was no servicing support. There was one historic site, Bilbit Point, which was visited by many tourists. But this lay near the state's border and tourists dropped in and out, giving the rest of the state the go-by.
  Alok had been worried. It appeared to him that many states were similarly afflicted. Alok had found that Kinno Valley did not ring a bell with tour operators. Even the largest tour operator hadn't been interested in going beyond Bilbit Point. A hotel would need commitment from local operators, connections with foreign operators, other tourism projects to come up... only then did it stand a chance of succeeding, he had felt.
  But Sanat had been optimistic. "These are image issues and you can easily work them into your marketing plan," he had urged. Kinno Valley was breathtaking. An old fort of stunning architectural beauty stood in the middle of lush green landscape, the slopes on one side were ideal for hand-gliding, and on the other side there was a river for para-sailing. It was an ideal interim destination between Bilbit and the city. Alok had been thrilled with the place, but the fact that there was no reliable transport from Bilbit bothered him.
  He had spoken to the railways. The spot he had chosen was slightly off the main line, an hour's drive from the nearest station. To make the circuit meaningful to the tourist the timings of trains connecting to the main gateway points like Delhi, Mumbai and so on needed to be adjusted. But the railways told him that that was not possible, at least not soon. "Can you run a rail bus to my destination, since there is a branch line running to Kinno Valley?" he had asked. He had been willing to guarantee traffic, but Alok was told by discouraging tour operators that it would take the railways five years to decide on this.
  Then he had gone to the transport operators to find out how many air-conditioned buses plied in the region. The transporter said: "First get the tourists, then we will see about the buses." Sanat had led a distraught Alok away. "Talking to tour operators is the wrong point to start," he had said. "Right now Kinno Valley is a dead site for them. Naturally they will sound discouraging. Why don't we meet the land development agencies and check what plots are available? Even if you get a plot within 3 km of Kinno Valley, you are in business."
After months of searching, a plot was found for the hotel. But it was far from perfect: It was in a marshy area, there was no regular water supply, power or even sewage disposal facilities
   That was where his last trip to India had ended. Alok returned to the UK with a dream of Kinno Valley and a promise from Sanat that he would identify a plot for him.
  And Sanat had done his homework, much to Alok's delight when they met the next day. "I have managed to earmark the perfect site for you from where you get a beautiful view of the fort and the lake. What's more, it is under the state development corporation, so you will get all the facilities," he said. "You must finalise the project site on this visit."
  The next day as they drove to Kinno Valley, Sanat spoke eloquently about the site. "It's perfect, the adjoining areas are also under development, which means all your ancillary needs are also taken care of," said Sanat.
   Yet when they reached the land development office and asked to see the plot, Alok was told: "Not this one but you can take the third on the left... 301-C." Alok was alarmed. The piece of land he had seen and on which his entire dream was based was now not to be his. Worse, Sanat told him that the plot he was now being offered overlooked a slum and its location took the approach 3 km away from the main line.
  There was worse to come. It appeared that no one had done a detailed evaluation of the levelling requirement: it was a low-lying plot and needed to be developed. "Can you develop the land and make it ready for purchase?" asked Alok. Yes they could, but the extra charges of development would be added to the land cost -- a shocking Rs 6 lakh!
  The reality was that no development could take place till an advance was collected from the buyer. In fact, the land hadn't even been acquired from the farmer-owner until a month ago, a fact Alok learnt from a certain Jarnail Singh, whom he met outside the land agency's office. "Don't touch 301-C," said Singh. "They are still negotiating with the owner on that, and he has gone to court on the acquisition price."
  Alok thought about 301-C. His entire project proposal was going out of the window, but he decided not to give up yet.
 Then the horror stories unfolded one by one. Yes, there were water and sewerage facilities, the authorities said, but he found no connections were laid out to 301-C, although it was a developed plot. The local water board, which had been asked to lay the connections, had said that it did not have enough pressure to support new connections, nor enough water.
  "When will it be done?" Sanat asked the water engineer.
