  "See, it is not in our priority list for the next five years, because we have other pressures. Some Janata colony has to come up super-fast so I can't give any connections for some time," the engineer said.
  "But funds were earmarked for the water supply by the state agencies," argued Sanat.
  "Yes, but we have so many expenses, how long can such a small amount of money last?" said the man.
  Jarnail Singh, who was following Alok's inquiries closely, led him out of the agency office gently. "Don't waste your time, let me show you 301-C and you will understand why," he said.
  They drove 3 km and arrived at a huge stretch of water and marsh under. "Where's the land?" asked Alok tentatively.
  Singh laughed heartily. "That's the beauty of it," he said. "It is cender the water."
  Alok was shocked. Singh went on to explain: "There is something wrong with the drainage connection on the adjoining plot. The back pressure builds up and it overflows... and this is what you get. And don't think the development institution is going to do it; you will have to run around and get it sorted out."
  "There are more complications," added Singh. "You will have to get certificates to prove that your effluents are okay, your drainage is fine etc. Now do you see? It can overflow onto your land, but you have to get the certificate to get it out! You have to set up solid waste management systems on your land, prove to the authorities that you have the capacity to do it..." Singh clearly had done a lot of research on 301-C. "The sewage capacity of that main drain going the next 15 km is enough to handle the discharge from a 30-room establishment," he explained. Alok's hotel would be viable only if it had 70 rooms, but now it appeared that he would be able to construct only a 30-room hotel! He would not get permission to set up a evacuation system for a 70-room hotel because such a hotel would discharge more solid waste than the pipe could bear.
  "That straightaway affects the project's viability," thought Alok.
  Yet, Alok decided he was going to see this thing through to its logical conclusion. To Sanat he said: "I remain hopeful. Let us meet the development agencies again and evaluate the possibility of providing the sewage and water connection and the certificate."
  But Alok's problems were not over. The developing agency said: "It will be difficult... we do not have the funds to build an additional evacuation facility."
   Now that stumped Sanat. "But this land has 50 plots. Surely you have permission to create the necessary connections!" he said.
  "I understand," said the sewage officer. "We built 50 plots for various commercial activities, but we built the evacuation capacity for only 25... We will be unable to give the approval. We will have to wait for the upgradation budget."
  At the power department, Alok was told that he would have to buy the transformer himself and the department would help in the installation. That meant another Rs 20-30 lakh, excluding the last mile of wiring for which he would have to pay himself. Alok's project was slowly becoming unviable and unbearable.
The problem was that even though the economy was liberalised, things remained more or less the same at the grassroots level of government. There was no urgency in pushing through projects
  Singh said: "Bhaisaab, they may have sanctioned 5 kva power supply, but you will have to run around to get the connections. That can take any amount of time. Meanwhile you will have to invest in a power back-up and start business with your own generator. You can't wait for them."
  By now, Alok was thoroughly disappointed. "To set up a hotel I not only have to sell the hotel service, but much before I get there I have to sell the state, the destination, build the related services and the service culture. I have to set up the industry," he thought. His project's success did not appear to lie in investment management as much as `environment' management. "Do I manage the environment or do I manage my core skill of running hotels?" he asked Sanat.
   Sanat wished Alok wouldn't despair. He had targeted foreign tourism investments of Rs 60 crore. If Alok agreed to set up the project, Rs 40 crore would be taken care of. It was a great idea to have a sophisticated hotel in his state. But Alok was talking about law and order, about water supply, effluent management... things which he had no answers to.
  "I need to interact with the local panchayats and make sure they accept a stake in this. I will also have to rope in the public works department (PWD), the sanitation department, the electricity board. In short, I will have to market the hotel to a plethora of colleagues in different departments -- half in the central government, half in the state government -- who will have to deliver. And I have to also play the catalyst! What a job!" rued Sanat.
  The following day Sanat called Vijay Phadnis, a friend and batchmate, in faraway Patna. "Liberalisation may have come in, but the opportunities have not permeated down the line," said Phadnis. "Only when it reaches and impacts the man at the grassroots level will there be a change. We all generate statements of good intentions without taking any action to make it happen. All the men from the PWD, water supply and other departments, you will find, are unable to act because they do not have the funds. Somebody is sitting on a file, or somebody is on leave! The criticality of the project is lost in the confusion. Then again, each department officer sees it from his own point of view, in terms of timing. One will say: `This thing must be done six months later, not today' because he has other priorities. Or he simply says, `Karengey', which is neither here nor there."
  "Take what happened to another investor recently," continued Phadnis. "The electricity department had sanctioned a transformer, but the linesman would not bring it. What do you do? Your problems are going to be of this nature. 
  Of course the government will woo you, the investor. But thereafter you are at the mercy of operating staff like the local inspector and the linesman! And they will have many reasons for delay or procrastination! The sad bit is none of them even realises how it is affecting the local economy.
  "Meanwhile, Alok was on the verge of abandoning his project, just like the way his cousin Raghav had given up his plastics project. In fact Raghav had cautioned him, having spent Rs 44 lakh and eight months on his project, only to find that he could not get the clearance of the environment department, which claimed there was a small bird sanctuary 20 km from his site.
  The fact was, the so-called sanctuary was virtually defunct, since no bird had stayed there in the past 19 years. But what had irked him the most was that neither the land agency nor anybody else knew about it, or had warned him.
  "Mind you, when I spoke to the chairman of the local electricity board, the head of the PWD and the water supply department... they all wanted the project. They were also willing to earmark funds for it. Still it wasn't getting done. Why? I will tell you," he had said.
  "The local junior engineer (JE) sitting there doesn't know the importance of delivering for that particular project. I felt that it was not communicated to him in his language. These very heads of departments later threw up their hands and despaired, blaming the lethargy of the junior staff," he said. "I even went to the extent of telling them: `This is a Rs 25 crore project. If your department spends even Rs 20 lakh inviting these operating officers to half-day seminars at a hotel and explains the value of this investment to the economy of the region and the importance of their involvement, you will find that the responses of 70-80% of the officers will become a lot better'."
  You know," said Raghav's father, "it has nothing to do with lethargy. There are many intermediary departments whose roles are critical to a project. Yet, no one department sees it as critical enough for it to complete its portion of the work. Each one will do it when it is convenient for it to do the job. There is no joint feeling of ownership of the project."
  "For that," said Raghav, "every officer in every department must feel he is a prominent part of the investment execution process. He has to feel that he is the one who is enabling the investment to take shape and reap the harvest. He has to see his contribution as a critical part of the investment.
  "So what is really required is a catalyst's role which sensitises the lower-level officials to their importance in economic development," continued Raghav animatedly. "Economic development is a big word with big ramifications. People like your JE feel it concerns ministers and economists and highly placed bureaucrats. No! Economic development should take place at the grassroots and only then can it travel upwards."
  Then it was a mere drawing room debate on India that was far away. Today it was real and happening to Alok. "How do I make an impact on these various departments and make them `want' to enable my project?" wondered Alok. "If I can do so, I will have begun the process of economic development. These catalysts of development, these IAS officers, the heads of departments, see their role as primarily interfacing with external investors. But they have a bigger role as internal marketers to the government system without asking for greater powers and authority. Just be trainers, motivators and enablers. More than interfacing with external investors, their customers are the men down the line."
  Sanat was very keen to see Alok proceed with his investment. But he could see that Alok was getting put off by what he thought were the softer issues. "I can bring in the money, the support, the equipment, I can train and develop people for the jobs, but can you ensure the security, the permissions, the lines, the connections?" Alok was asking. "I need a rail service, rail bus, security, land clearances and the electricity. How are you going to assure me that all this is possible?" Alok said.
  Sanat had thought he had a well-intentioned investment in place, but he now realised that he had not bargained for the state machinery's complex relationships. "I know we have to change it, but I don't know where to start. Is the issue planning or is it execution? Or something else?" he wondered.
  "How do you get industry to perform, the efforts and the revenues to percolate down the line?," he wondered.

REFORMS AT THE GRASSROOTS

  Duvvuri Subbarao is an IAS officer and was joint secretary in the ministry of finance in Delhi in the early nineties when economic reforms were initiated. He has been principal finance secretary in the government of Andhra Pradesh for the past five years. Currently,he is on assignment with the World Bank.

NRI comes. NRI despairs. NRI goes back. The details varied, but the gist was all the same. Here were educated, enterprising Indians who, having proved their success at the world level, now wanted to return home, and with their money and expertise, do something in the country of their birth. 

  But the pernicious regime of licences and controls combined with the delays, lethargy and corruption so deeply ingrained in the system sapped all their energy and enthusiasm. Chastened, if also a bit saddened, they returned to the seclusion and comfort of their first world endeavours
  Not to worry. That was back in the eighties. Post-reforms, there is a dramatic difference. No more running around for licences and permits. No more endless waiting in the corridors of power. India has unleashed the magic of the marketplace. The country is now a haven of opportunity for get-up-and-go type of entrepreneurship.
  Is it? Decidedly no, if Alok Aggarwal's experience is any guide. Between his two trips, 20 years apart, nothing much has changed -- not at the grassroots, anyway. Surely, there is no need any longer to negotiate through the complex and frustrating bureaucracy in Delhi or to lobby underhand for loans from financial institutions. But that is small change. At the cutting-edge level, it is business as usual with no coordination among agencies, no motivation among staff and no accountability.
  Therein lies a powerful lesson for our political leaders and top bureaucrats. Championing reforms at the top is necessary, but not sufficient. There is a need for understanding, commitment and ownership of the reform ethos down the line.
Championing reforms at the top is necessary, but not sufficint. There is a need for commitment and ownership of the reform ethos down the line  
  So what can be done about it? Several things. First and foremost, within the government, the constituency for reforms must be deepened and broadened. As of now, our reform process resembles guerrilla warfare, with the aggressors, a small band of ministers and technocrats, fighting the rest of the system. Such blitzkrieg type of manoeuvres with secrecy may have been necessary in the initial stabilisation phase of the reforms process, but are certainly inimical to the longer-drawn structural adjustment phase. Early on in the reforms process, it was fashionable to say that reforms have not travelled beyond North Block. Nearly a decade down the line, they do not seem to have gone much farther. Many ministers and civil servants in peripheral economic ministries and state governments have often complained that they have not been taken on board on the logic, agenda or sequencing of reforms, even as their participation is vital to the success of the exercise.
  If that be the situation at the very top, is it any surprise that the reform ethos has not percolated down the hierarchy? The indifference, apathy and lack of motivation that Alok confronted are the result of weak, if also halfhearted, political management of reforms within the government itself. As his cousin Raghav tells Alok, these people at the operating level have never been told the importance of their respective inputs in the overall scheme of things or how their non-performance can be costly not just to a project or to an individual but to the whole gamut of development. But who is to exhort and encourage them when their bosses themselves do not feel a sense of ownership or commitment?
Motivating employees at all levels of the delivery system, right down to the cutting-edge level, as pointed out by Raghav, is important, but again not sufficient. We need deep-rooted structural reform of our civil service. The public sector is such a secure haven of employment that there is just no danger in not performing. Nobody in the government gets sacked. Everyone gets promoted by the mere passage of time. The committed and the efficient do not get any rewards or recognition. They only get saddled with additional work. The corrupt and the inefficient not only survive, but even thrive. This generous, inclusive and non-discriminating system that we have perfected is a sure recipe for sapping motivation and breeding corruption.At the very least, we need to make our government employees accountable for results through a ruthless system of rewards and penalties.
  A 'soft' state is hardly compatible with hard-nosed reforms.
  Alok was understandably despondent that he had to fritter away his energy on managing the 'environment' rather than building on his core competence. He mused about a system that would 'demand' the enterprise that he was willing and able to 'supply.' This should be a pointer to government agencies on where their priorities should lie -- in operational level planning and in coordination and monitoring.
  Economic reforms have unleashed competition among states for investment. Globe-trotting chief ministers, glossy brochures, slick presentations -- all promising sops and subsidies to potential investors -- are the staple of this competition. Alok's case suggests that what potential investors value much more than sops is an environment of rule of law and adequate and good quality physical and social infrastructure. In short, the state should concentrate on reducing the quantity and increasing the quality of governance.
  Finally, a word of advice to Alok and his ilk. Do not despair. India is changing, albeit slowly. Remember that we also have an enviable record here of never having retracted on reforms, notwithstanding the varying ideologies of the frequently changing governments. Do not look for the quick buck. Come here for the long haul. You will find reward and fulfillment.

A PARADISM SHIFT IS NEEDED

 Rajan Chhibba is associate director at A.F. Ferguson & Co

ALOK Aggarwal is experiencing the classic clash between entrepreneurs and the bureaucracy. It is a well-known fact that unless the bureaucracy itself turns entrepreneurial, development does not occur. Governments and entrepreneurship may seem a contradiction in terms, but if one goes by the definition of entrepreneurship -- "the process of shifting resources from areas of low yield to areas of high yield" -- the government, whether at the state or central level, has displayed varying degrees of entrepreneurial spirit in the past. Take, for instance, the green revolution or the basic industrial infrastructure which has been created in the country.

  Just as the private sector has had to learn new paradigms of successful entrepreneurship, the paradigms of entrepreneurship within the government need to change. Resources for development are more efficiently deployed if the central and state governments act as catalysts and regulators -- and not as executors -- of development. Like in the case of the green revolution, where the government mercifully left the execution to the private initiative of the farmers, but was an important catalyst. The problem, as experienced by Alok, is that this message of paradigm change has not reached the operating level within governments.
  The local junior engineer, the sewerage department, the power department and so on are used to an environment where anything the government gives out is considered "largesse" which the private entrepreneur has to consider himself lucky to get at all. This has led them to believe that since beggars can't be choosers, private entrepreneurs should either take it or leave it. Therefore, the junior engineer actually sees nothing wrong in offering another plot instead of the one that was promised to Alok.
  Alok's experience shows how Indian entrepreneurs have to take on an additional management burden called "managing the hassle factor". It is important to get to the root cause of this hassle factor.
Resources for development are more efficiently deployed if the government acts as a catalyst and regulator, and not as an executor
In the past, entrepreneurs had learnt to be prepared for hassles like undeveloped land sites, no sewerage and other problems. But now, with the top echelons of government promoting their states as havens of entrepreneurship, the expectations of entrepreneurs are much higher. Unfortunately no one has seen the need to sensitise the lower operating rungs of the government on the need for change. Hence the issue boils down to one of training.
  The pity is that although the top officers constitute less than 2% of the workforce, they get 100% of the training. And we are not talking about sophisticated knowledge-based training sessions, since most government departments have adequate technical knowledge of their areas, but really about awareness sessions -- awareness of the meaning of the role of the government as a catalyst.
  To illustrate, if 1-2% of all budgets for development programmes are earmarked for half-a-day or one-day sessions of awareness training for the operating officers of the relevant government departments in that area, each junior engineer -- whether in the electricity department or in the sewerage department -- will see himself as a catalyst of economic development, thereby ushering in change necessary to support government programmes. This is precisely what Raghav has highlighted, though in his exasperation. The content of the awareness programmes should focus on selling the benefits of local development to the local people (perhaps a job opportunity for the junior engineer's son tomorrow!)   
  Just bridging this training gap will go a long way in converting the state and central governments -- and their laid-back bureaucracies -- into entrepreneurs which can propel this country to the forefront of economic well-being.
  It is perhaps wishful thinking to hope that to set up a hotel, Alok will not have to deal with 15-20 different authorities -- it happens even in developed countries. But if the operative officers of each of the departments are on the same wavelength as him, the task seems almost like a single window approach to the entrepreneur.
  Another relevant issue in most governments is the issue of the senior officers' (IAS) tenure. Since transfers are frequent, even if officers get down to the task of orienting their departments to be customer service oriented, they are transferred within a year or two.  
  The resultant change disrupts the process of change management. By the time the new incumbent settles in, it's time for him to move too.
  The need is not so much to reorganise governments' structure as to ensure that heads of relevant departments have long enough tenures to be accountable for their departments' actions. And accountability is the cornerstone of running any entrepreneurial organisation.
  Alok Aggarwal can practically decide which state to invest in on the basis of one simple rule. How long does an average IAS officer stay in his position? The longer he stays, the more responsive his department.
  Alok need not be disheartened. He needs to recognise that change in the government is not going to happen by internal processes alone. Alok himself can, as an entrepreneur, push the change process through by evolving a government management strategy. If he allows himself to be a tool with which Sanat and his friends can change governments, he will reap the benefits of being a pioneer.
  Alok has to realise that in the Indian context, a bit of the `frontier spirit' that made America great in the last century is still a key success factor in business. This is his opportunity to display the same spirit.
Designed by: Epsilon Systems - SQ-80, Hauz Khas Appts, New Delhi-16. Phone: 685-3301, 685-0878, 98100-06431

Case Study 7


16 Aug 1999		 COSMETIC STRATEGY.
MEERA SETH
Panleaf, a multinational in household hygiene and coloured cosmetics, found that succeeding in the Indian market wasn`t easy as it thought..
  ANAND Rai examined the charts he had drawn up. A variety of graphs and bar charts were spread out on his desk, plotting growth, volumes, seasonal trends, marketshares and profitability in India, China and eastern Europe. 
  The trend was clear -- but for eastern Europe and some parts of China, PanLeaf Inc's performance had been dismal.Rai was the director, market operations, Asia-Pacific Region, of PanLeaf, a multinational in household hygiene and coloured cosmetics. He was currently on a troubleshooting mission to India to examine and review the state of PanLeaf's operations, as part of his new markets audit.
  Traditionally a household products company, PanLeaf had carved a comfortable niche for itself in Europe and the US until its position was threatened and diluted in the early eighties. By the mid-eighties, PanLeaf had realigned its business focus, fine-tuned its product portfolio in household care and also rejuvenated its coloured cosmetics businesses.
  Until then, its cosmetics business had been tiny but had showed a lot of promise. PanLeaf brought in the best consultants and went on an acquisition spree on their advice, collecting some 18 cosmetic brands all over the world. That gave its cosmetics business the much-needed shot in the arm.
  PanLeaf now wanted to also expand geographically since its interests were largely in the US and Europe and the company's management did not believe that was sustainable. They believed that the 300 million-strong Indian middle class could be leveraged for mass-market products and if they developed a good cosmetics business they could develop a good portfolio at the mass level which could be leveraged anywhere in the world.
  There were two possibilities in India: household care or skin care and coloured cosmetics. The international board strongly mooted the fragrances idea, since research showed this market was virtually untouched and global brands had a far greater possibility of succeeding.  
  The Indian operation was headed by Jim Kura, a Korean with vast experience in new market development. Kura quickly built a sound sales and marketing team, drawn from Indian companies with strong distribution skills. PanLeaf did not want an Indian heading strategy and decision-making, a strategy it had stayed with everywhere else in the world. Kura spent six months studying the market before he rejected the international board's idea of entering the detergents market, despite the consultants' advice which said "...the detergent market is very large, over 20 times the size of the cosmetics market... it is possible to build a strong niche in a large market with speciality detergents like top-end laundry detergent, stain removers and hard surface cleaners."
  "No, we cannot take on Procter & Gamble or Unilever," Kura had said. "Though the market is large it will be difficult to set up a company from scratch, given the intensive distribution required and the huge entry cost required to create a reasonable base of production, logistics and sales."
  Instead, Kura pushed for an entry into skin care, which seemed more viable to him given that there were just two national organised players in the Indian market. But he rejected an entry into coloured cosmetics. "Skin care products are relatively easier to produce and manage than nail polishes and lipsticks where the range is large and dynamic," he had said.
  But the parent company was more keen on launching colour cosmetics before expanding to skin care, as research had indicated that other than one large organised player, Lakme, colour cosmetics in India were produced largely in the unorganised sector. "The scope for market growth and expansion is high," went the logic. "With the lowering of excise duties and the consumer's preference for organised sector brands, there was scope to build a new brand. In any case, consumers are known to experiment with a variety of global brands bought on visits abroad or from the local grey market. Lastly, of course, the brand awareness of PanLeaf is already high in India," it said.
  Kura, who until then was preparing for an entry into the mass market, now had to shift gear to the premium end where PanLeaf's coloured cosmetics were positioned. Even as he went about creating the organisation structure, the international board declared: "We will budget for some losses in year one and two, but we must break even in year three."
  By this time Kura decided to create a company-owned distribution setup, because brand focus in retail could not be sustained otherwise. The teams were put in place and so was the distribution network, across major and mini metros. 
  PanLeaf then launched three colour cosmetics products -- nail varnishes, lipsticks and eye make-up. They were engineered to suit a variety of cultures and climates. A vegetable extract in lipsticks helped to maintain the correct moisture level and protected lips from ultraviolet rays. They were also smear-proof, long-lasting and hypollergenic-certified -- attributes which had developed a huge consumer following for PanLeaf in most markets. Likewise, the nail varnishes were chip-resistant with built-in nail strengtheners. With such product benefits, the nascent Indian market will boom, since the attitude of Indian women to grooming and appearance had evolved a lot, it felt.
  The marketing team conducted a series of pre-launch blind product tests in many cities. It was found that the consumer rated the PanLeaf sample very highly and was even willing to pay a premium of 25-50% over her current brand. Encouraged by the evaluation, the company went ahead with its plans.
When coloured cosmetics failed to take off in the indian market, panleaf's brass decided to go into skin care and fragrances, but eventually discovered that even these didn't show much promise
  But when it finally developed the products, PanLeaf found that they were two times more expensive than those offered by its closest competitor, Lakme. PanLeaf, which was hoping to take a 15-20% share of the market in volume terms, now suddenly faced a pricing dilemma. On the one hand it expected to sell its products at a price premium of 50-75%, yet on the other, it needed high contribution to support marketing costs. A lower price would lead to higher volumes, but that would upset its break-even calculations.
  Kura had watched the launch of newer brands like Revlon and Oriflame and new launches from Lakme. He also had as benchmarks other fast moving consumer goods (FMCG) in the Indian market, specifically in foods. He was reassured by the presence of a discerning class, which would pay a premium price for superior quality and product performance.
  But when the coloured cosmetic products were launched, the initial trial purchases were very good but repeat purchases were very low. Though they were satisfied with the product, consumers found the lipsticks and nail varnishes not offering enough value for money. PanLeaf quickly went back to the research table and assessed the consumer feedback. It found that the consumer felt that the brand's value was almost as high as that of the Indian brand, Lakme. Whenever they put a Lakme product in a non-Lakme packaging, that product scored low. But when they took a better product from the market and put it into Lakme packaging, it was rated high. So there was a score which the consumer was willing to give Lakme which she didn't give to any other product. PanLeaf came close to Lakme; just its product delivery and positioning, it seemed, was strong enough to take Lakme head-on. But the trouble was that it was two times more expensive. The consumer was satisfied with the product quality, but she was not willing to pay a price which was twice that of Lakme's for it.
  Kura went back to the drawing board, and ran a fine tooth comb over his costs. But the international board reiterated its averseness to lowering the prices, for that meant a squeeze on margins and a delay in breaking even.
  Besides, a lower price also meant that that Kura would have to sell 20 times as much, and he wasn't sure if there was enough demand to justify such volumes. Then again, when he went back and studied the research findings he wasn't sure if a small drop in price was going to shift consumer loyalty too much. He felt the consumer expected imagery and variety in nail varnishes and lipsticks rather than safety, durability and protection. Maybe Lakme was safe and all that, but those were not the attributes on which it was positioned. For instance, Lakme was not saying 'good company, sound brands', which the consumer knew anyway, but was saying `we will make you beautiful...' and that was what the consumer was paying for. Kura could see that Lakme met her expectations, so she had no reason to pay twice as much for a brand that was also global.
 Naturally, for PanLeaf's attributes to gain consumer saliency, it required huge investments in need-building, awareness and habit development. It was therefore clear it would be difficult to break even in three years. Lowering the price and pitching for volumes was against the company philosophy as it would lead to sustained marketing costs.
  It was then that Fred Miller, the corporate marketing man in PanLeaf International, decided to restate the India strategy. He now suggested launching the premium range of skin care products and fragrances. "The current distribution setup is capable of leveraging a limited premium range across coloured cosmetics, skin care and fragrances," he said. 
  "Your incremental costs will be just the launch phase, but your consumer set is the same for all three categories. By adding width to your portfolio you will get a bigger bang for your buck as your costs will now be borne by a wider portfolio, and this will heighten brand interest."   
  Extending that logic, the international board suggested that PanLeaf India launch a series of speciality skin care products -- peel-off masks, day creams with vegetable ceramides and enzymes and skin exfoliators. Even if these were more expensive, the research finding was that consumers were less price-sensitive to speciality performance products. Kura had seen that in the performance of Complex, a recent entrant in the Indian market. A global brand too, its speciality skin care products were faring well while its mass products were not.
  So within eight months Kura brought in Panleaf's Plus range of treatment products, preceded by a huge ad splurge. The market got off to a good start. But soon after, the offtakes dipped sharply.
  More research and analysis followed. PanLeaf India discovered that the positioning and the imagery needed for a skin care range were vastly different from those for coloured cosmetics. Skin care was serious, technology and testimony-driven; coloured cosmetics were about fun, beauty and fashion. PanLeaf had assumed that a consumer would respond to the umbrella brand `PanLeaf', that the umbrella brand would sell, and other than launch costs the only things needed were to build awareness and trial for the skin care range. But in reality, awareness and trial did not build up.
  Around the same time PanLeaf kicked off its fragrance business too, to leverage its premium image. Kura had been wary of entering fragrances. Skin care was not new to Indian usage habits, but as far as fragrances went, he had noticed, even the deodorant habit did not exist in India. Perfumes were associated with expensive occasions and locations, and were not a part of the daily dressing habit. Given the climate, it was talcum powder that was most used. To upgrade such a consumer to deodorants or perfumes required habit development, Kura felt.
  Then again, if PanLeaf entered fragrances, it had to do so with many variants, for a consumer would demand a battery of fragrances to choose from, not just a lavender or a musk. "The entry into fragrances cannot and should not be arbitrary," he had warned. "This is a country where the usage habit for perfumes is at a nascent stage. We must research usage habits and needs and launch the appropriate fragrances," he had said.
After trying all the combinations, Rai felt that the products could be profitable if there were no advertising costs. Could direct marketing help in reversing the company's fortunes?
  Even so, PanLeaf India went ahead and launched four varieties of deodorants and perfumes. Pushed into accepting fragrances in his portfolio, Kura manoeuvered the launch of talcum powders, which were adaptations of their deodorants. Fred resisted, for nowhere in the world did PanLeaf market talcum powders. So why in India, he asked. But Kura bullied his way through. His maverick ploy did not have the blessing of his bosses, nor did he get global assistance on product development, since talcum powder was not part of the global stable. Kura then priced his talcs at only a 20% premium over the market, defying the internal contribution requirement.
  Three months after the launch, PanLeaf found that retailers had a huge stock of unsold deodorants. The talcum range had medium levels of trial, but repeat purchases were high. From a sales perspective, it could be said that the volumes existed but given the pricing there was hardly any contribution left!  

  End-1998, when Kura looked at the performance of the speciality skin care range and the fragrances, he could see that neither held promise. And that was what he told Rai in Singapore. "Whether it is cosmetics or skin care, it is about researching and understanding consumer habits and usage, and then about habit development. But in India the consumer is not upgrading. The attitude continues to be `mass' and low price. She is not looking for long-lasting lipstick, or ultraviolet protection for lips," said Kura. "That will require investments in habit and market development, which means higher breakeven levels. Secondly, the imagery in colour, skin and fragrances is so different that each category will require its own investment."
  But PanLeaf India did not have that kind of spending stamina, emotional readiness or the encouragement of its parent which had set its sights on a three-year breakeven period. "We came into the country thinking there were 300 million consumers, but the fact is that there aren't so many consumers -- at least not at the prices at which we are selling. We are neither in a position to lower prices nor are we investing in market development," Kura had said abjectly.
  Rai, who had been called in to sort out the problems in India, heard out Kura's woes. "Naturally, the local management team was at a loss to make the operations viable," continued Kura. "Every product it wanted launched or developed straightaway meant investments in market development. The corporate marketing team in head office would not make a commitment or decide. The business plans that were drawn out were not implemented. When the company is new and you want to break even in so short a time, how can I develop resilience?" asked Kura.
  Yet at the same time, new entrants like Complex had demystified the high price myth, felt Rai. "Naturally," said Kura. "Complex was less concerned about breaking even than with getting the brand accepted. "The singular roadblock we faced was the dictat of early breakeven periods. And it was amazing, because we were not the only new boys sitting in India. Every other FMCG category -- be it shoes, cola or cereal -- was similarly tackling costs and breakevens and providing for start-up losses or non-profits," he said.

Now as he studied the charts before him, Rai was intrigued by the emerging patterns. PanLeaf's approach had been the same in China, eastern Europe and India. It had came to the eastern European markets around the same time it entered India. Yet, eastern Europe was profitable from year one. In eastern Europe, the prices that consumers were willing to pay for products were almost similar to those in the western world. Skin care products in the European world were priced anywhere between $5 and $10. Even when eastern Europe was part of the Soviet Bloc, it was influenced by the goods and services in western Europe. When these countries became independent, the first thing they did was to allow imports.
  Naturally, when we launched in eastern Europe, we were amongst the first few to grab consumer attention by spending money on ad support, thought Rai. We had innumerable product ranges and variants to choose from. The prices quoted were high, ad support high, and in the first year we were successful.
  But PanLeaf had met with minimum success in China and India. It took the company five years to succeed in China, but that was because of the takeover of another large local cosmetic brand. And that struck Rai as unique: it seemed to do well wherever it acquired new brands. Taking on an ongoing business always gives you the energy to build it and take it forward. Even historically, the rapid growth that it saw in its first 10 years was due to acquisitions. Creating new brands in new markets would take a long time -- it would mean changing habits, building brand saliency and growing the market.
  Rai ran a tired hand over his unshaven jaw. The approach of trying to get price and contribution did not work, he thought. Or, our managerial style and marketing strategy which had worked for us worldwide did not work in developing countries, he mused. Or, we had no decisive, well-formulated category entry strategy for India. Or, should we have tackled the mass market before the premium segment, he wondered.
  Rai tossed the issues over and over in his mind. Exiting India was just not acceptable to him. It was a good market, of course. The margins were healthy, but the volumes PanLeaf sold were not enough to generate healthy contributions that would support advertising in Indian markets. If there was no advertising, the business would be very successful at whatever volumes it could get, he felt. And the only way you could operate without advertising was direct marketing. Mass advertising worked if volumes were large. PanLeaf needed to break even quickly and the volumes were simply not there.
  The direct marketing idea grabbed Rai. In the last three years they had altered a lot across product categories. None of these had worked. Should he give India one more chance and invest in direct marketing, he wondered.

A BLINKERED MINDSET

Rama Bijapurkar is a Mumbai-based strategic marketing consultant.

THIS case made excellent reading. It reminded me of Gulliver's Travels, where the Giant lay helpless among the Lilliputians. In fact, it reminded me of all the stories that I have read about the follies and foibles of the unsuspecting foreigner who arrives lured by the magic of the Orient, only to find a maze of minefields and swamps, inhabited by the barbarians and the heathen. What is he to do now? Where is the glass slipper that can make up for the horrific discovery, at the midnight hour when strategy turns to implementation, that the coaches were actually pumpkins and footmen lizards?

This case tempts one to take each decision made by the PanLeaf team and analyse where it went wrong and how it could have avoided the mistakes -- be it in assessing the size of the consumer base, or in resolving the "mass or class" dilemma, or in understanding the power of the grey market, or appreciating the cultural aspects that inhibit the use of make-up or deodorants. Most of these answers are by now wellknown, and I would like to address a more fundamental question: why do companies make such mistakes?
  The Indian market has now seen enough PanLeafs to know that the brightest and the best fall prey to the Emerging Market Disease. Why do they succumb? It definitely isn't because of a lack of market understanding skills. Or poor strategic thinking. Or an inability to innovate products to suit country-specific needs. They often have a formidable reputation of doing all this in their home markets.
  My hypothesis is that this happens because of the mindset with which multinational companies approach new markets, particularly emerging ones, which makes them very vulnerable. I would like to discuss three manifestations of this mindset -- discovery-inhibiting mental models of the market, value arrogance and a lack of alignment of the head, the heart and the purse. Let me illustrate all of them with reference to PanLeaf.
  There appear to be some dominant mental models about the Indian market, all of which have proved to be hazardous in different cases.
The indian market has now seen enough panleafs to know that the brightest and best seem to fall prey to the emerging market disease 
  Mental Model 1: "Just like somewhere else". In this model, the assumption is that the market is "exactly like the US was 20 years ago", or "If it worked in Thailand and Mexico and Vietnam, it will work here". This makes the marketer skip a very important stage -- developing a fundamental understanding of the market. The notion that developing markets are like baby developed markets or that differing stages of evolution mapped by per capita gross domestic product is the great leveller is patently incorrect.
  Mental Model 2: It is just a question of numbers. It is. But this is how it works. Not as in "if every Indian woman consumed one lipstick, one eye-liner and one bottle of perfume every year..." But as in "affordability limits the market to this number. Accessibility to this number. And acceptability by this number."
  Mental Model 3: "Who can resist progress? People don't know what they don't know -- all we need to do is show them." But what is aspirational in their cultural lexicon? Being able to have a cool shower in running water twice a day or using deodorant twice a day? Having glowing skin or sporting a splash of colour? A good hard look at Hindi films would answer that question.
  Mental Model 4: "Ready for my global strategy? Gosh this is really underdeveloped -- I'll just come back in 10 years." I guess the basic question is: "Do you want to be a shaper? Or merely an adapter?" The answer to this depends on the pain and the gain of doing each. PanLeaf has subscribed to all these mental models at some time or another -- perhaps it diminished its need to take a good hard zero-based look at the market. 
 Value arrogance is a deadly sin that many new entrants seem to fall prey to. Value, as the case points out, is the net effect of benefit minus price. That consumers pick the best value offering is universally true, whether they are in Poland or in Pune.
 However, how this value is processed is different across different cultures. Hypoallergenic and protection from ultraviolet rays are nice things to have -- but are they hugely valuable benefits? What proportion of Indian skins are as sensitive as European ones? What are the cultural labels of cosmetics? And where do they stand in the wish-list hierarchy of the Indian woman? PanLeaf, if it hadn't been value-arrogant, would have started from scratch to discover how value for its products is processed in this country.
  Its research would have yielded better results, since it would have done some naive consumer listening to begin with.
  Another side effect of a blinkered mindset at PanLeaf seems to have been that even fundamental assumptions that formed the pillars of strategy like "who uses what -- the same people or different people, and why" were not tested out. The questioning seemed to be a bit in the realm of "would you like a holiday in the Bahamas?" Who doesn't?
  Finally, the only way to win in an emerging market is for the Head, the Heart and the Purse to be in perfect alignment. What happened at PanLeaf was that the head was looking at developing a new market as a mega growth opportunity of the kind that no developed market could offer. The heart was however unwilling to accept that best practice in developed markets must give way to next practice in emerging markets. And the purse was unwilling to support what was needed for market development and learning a whole new game.
  As C.K. Prahalad said in a recent Harvard Business Review article, multinationals may change emerging markets forever -- but the reverse is also true.
KNEE-JERK DECISIONS
 
 SUDERSHAN Banerjee is managing director & chief executive officer, India, of amway Corp, the world's largest direct selling company with sales of $6 billion.

  THE case of PanLeaf highlights several interesting things. It presents essential attributes of the Indian market in the context of a multinational brand, while at the same time underlining the need for strategic common sense. It drives home the fact that while chalk is very different from cheese, both have a market.
  PanLeaf had the right corporate credentials: it was an acknowledged international brand, synonymous with home care and cosmetics. Over the years, it had acquired as many as 18 cosmetics brands from around the world and reinforced its status as a major cosmetics player. 

    While India was new to PanLeaf, PanLeaf was not new to the Indian consumer. In the cosmetics segment, the brand awareness of PanLeaf was already high in India.
  But PanLeaf read India wrong, and that was its undoing. It believed that the 300 million consumer base of the Indian middle class could be leveraged for mass market products. It was naive to see this 300 million as one homogeneous whole: it actually has several layers, sets and subsets, each defined by its own peculiarities of spending power, 'value for money' notions and consumption habits. The Indian consumer wants a quality product, but does not want to pay premium prices for it. This is the paradox most marketers face. PanLeaf was never able to balance this equation.
  The selection of a foreigner who did not have an adequate understanding or appreciation of the Indian market as country head compromised PanLeaf. Not only did Jim Kura lack knowledge about the market's nuances, he didn't have much influence at the company's headquarters too. One weakness was bad enough; two spelled disaster.
  What surprises me is that while PanLeaf undertook research, it did not abide by its recommendations. We know that market research favoured the introduction of home care products (detergents), and promised space at the top end of the market. Jim Kura's U-turn from home care (detergents) and into skin care seems to be whimsical rather than based on facts. He turned away from detergents due to the presence of two major market players. Deciding a marketing venture purely on the basis of the number of players is naive.
 
Panleaf read India wrong, and that was its undoing. It is naive to see tha 300million strong middle class market as a homogeneous whole
   With its core competency and implied quality in detergents, PanLeaf could have carved a niche at the premium end. But it abandoned this tack.
  Another serious lacuna was PanLeaf's habit of not making consistent decisions. Like detergents, it planned to move into skin care, but abandoned it in favour of cosmetics. Nevertheless, the cosmetics segment could have been exploited successfully. PanLeaf had a well-formulated category strategy in cosmetics. The premium cosmetic products market was small, but fast expanding. There was no major established player in this market and the nearest competitor was not positioned as a premium brand; it was a "good brand at a fair price", leaving the premium end unexploited. PanLeaf also had global competencies in premium cosmetics brands. This meant that it had an advantage over other Indian players in terms of technology. The local Indian brand was the only serious competitor in the organised sector. PanLeaf had a high degree of brand recall in India for cosmetics. It owned several global brands too, and had a core competency in the segment. The three colour cosmetics PanLeaf launched in the country rested on solid ground. They were technically researched, and had USPs: lipsticks which maintained the correct moisture levels and protected lips from ultraviolet rays, chip-resistant nail varnishes and so on. The competitors' products had none of these attributes. 
  PanLeaf gave up on cosmetics too easily.  The move into skin care was unwise. As was clubbing cosmetics and skin care products under an umbrella brand. This led to a diffusion of brand personality and took away the distinction between the two. Like everything else that PanLeaf did in India, it was more of a knee-jerk reaction rather than a studied decision. The market responded initially, but in a country with an age-old system of skin care treatments, popular in every home, skin care was not a hot proposition.  
    The addition of fragrances was not prudent either. Consumers see fragrances to be for occasional use, slightly flippant, and definitely expensive.
   No wonder they did not take off. His hunch on talcum powder paid off: repeat purchases were high. But progress in this case also was doomed by the now characteristic impulsive and inconsistent style of PanLeaf India.
  At the end of the day, Rai was called to fire-fight. "We have tried everything else and nothing has worked, so we might as well consider direct selling." But if the products are rejected by the market, direct selling will not help. PanLeaf believes that it can save on advertising and promotion costs by selling directly, but it is ignoring the hidden costs of a direct selling operation. Direct selling calls for strong competencies, global or local. PanLeaf doesn't have these. Direct selling depends on a strong relationship with distributors/members. It is a dual exercise: core marketing for brand building, and relationship marketing for a strong relationship with distributors/members. It takes years to acquire these. Direct selling also involves significant investments in IS/IT systems.
  PanLeaf has already made massive investments in its distribution setup (retail model). It should focus on retail, but limit its presence to major markets/stores which cater to the high-end consumer. But before it does that, PanLeaf must take a long, hard look at its basic failures and rectify them. It must develop long-term, well thought out strategies in sync with market nuances. Above all, it must adapt to India. And adapt imaginatively, with patience.
Designed by: Epsilon Systems - SQ-80, Hauz Khas Appts, New Delhi-16. Phone: 685-3301, 685-0878, 98100-06431

Case Study 8


30 Aug 1999		 TRANSITION TRAUMA.
MEERA SETH
With no competition worth the name, Tarmann could sell all it wanted of its product, Solo. But liberalisation proved to be its downfall .
  GANGADHARAN Raman studied the headlines in the business section of a daily: "Richmond India undisputed number one... unbeaten record..." he read out aloud, as he looked across at his son. 
  Sundar, who was ploughing through a bowl full of boiled peas and a shoulder-high pile of newspapers, waved his head -- half in rhythm with the beats of Umayalpuram's mridangam wafting from the music system and half in proud acknowledgement of his company's continued spectacular performance.
  Sundar was the country manager of Richmond India whose performance reports were covered by every newspaper. "Richmond India will always fly high; its systems are engineered to deliver only success," he said.  
  "You know," said Raman, "attaining success is one thing, but staying successful is a different matter altogether."
  "Appa, we have stayed at the top for six years now. That's no mean achievement. It also means that we have worked at sustaining the leadership position," said Sundar.
  But the senior Raman had a different viewpoint. "It is when you are tremendously successful that you should stop and take stock of your environment. You must examine the success factors and study what you can do differently now, what others are learning to do differently and, therefore, what you need to do even better than them," elaborated Raman.
    Noticing the tenor of his father's voice, Sundar asked: "You think somebody will pull the rug from under our feet?"
  "It's not entirely impossible, you know," said Raman, folding the newspaper. "It has happened to so many companies in the past. Why shouldn't it happen to Richmond?"
  "We are a cautious and conservative company, Appa, not one looking for a quick buck on an Indian tour," said Sundar.
  "Neither was Hindustan Lever Ltd (HLL)," pointed Raman. "It rode high with Surf, believing in its invincibility, until Nirma challenged its marketing wisdom. Nor Coca-Cola. In its 95th year, even as it stood tall in the soft drinks market, a virtual Goliath, Coke took its invincibility for granted and launched New Coke. The crash can still be heard."
  "These are rare marketing occurrences, Appa," Sundar replied. But Raman had more examples. "Take Society Tea. It stormed into the market in 1991 and within four years cornered 30% of the packaged tea market in Mumbai, overcoming Red Label, to assume the No.1 slot. HLL is also a cautious, conservative company, but Nirma and Society happened to it. The examples are many, Sundar. Robin Blue challenged by Ujala, Stayfree taken on by Whisper, Colgate by Close-up, Cadbury by Nestle... Each is a setback of a different kind, but at the core, it is all about a leader who underestimated his turf."
 "What about your own company?" asked Sundar. "After keeping an iron grip on the market for 30 years, you lost out to Delite in one year! Wasn't that complacency?"
  There was a pained expression on the senior Raman's face. Two years ago, he had retired from Tarmann India where he was a sales manager. "Tarmann's story is not about complacency," he said. "What happened to Tarmann was because of time, circumstances, evolution and mindsets in transition.
  "In the late sixties, Tarmann was a single-product company that relied heavily on its moneyspinner, Solo. 
   In 1977 when the Foreign Exchange Regulation Act (Fera) forced multinationals to dilute their stakes in their subsidiaries, the British parent company lost interest in India and whatever little investment was being made in Solo also stopped.
  The parent company now focused more on getting a healthy dividend for its residual stake. Naturally, the Indian subsidiary concentrated on profits rather than market growth and sales. In any case, those days there was hardly any competition in Solo's product category.
  "The Indian managing director, Prabhu Pal, who took over the reins, had a tough brief: make good profits and declare the highest return on capital in the region. There was a trade-off between capitalising on emerging opportunities and being an exceptionally profitable organisation. Tarmann chose profits.
  "Tarmann's share prices were five-six times those of other multinationals during that era. The company's market capitalistation was the highest in the stockmarket, and it was paying dividends of 70-80%. Everybody thought Tarmann was doing very well, and soon the management also believed that the brands could survive without new investments.
  "A chicken and egg situation followed. Post-dilution, Tarmann India had entered new product categories like basic skin care, creams and talcum powder. Even as these products took off in a market that was starved of variety, rather than invest in these growth products, the approach was: milk them for profits. Naturally, their performance dropped.
  "Then there were some glitches," went on Raman. "In the early eighties, Great India Products launched Shine, which competed directly with Solo. It nipped off marketshares here and there, but Tarmann had tremendous distribution strength, which helped. It sustained such a large distribution network on Solo alone, can you imagine? It says a lot. Companies with a portfolio of 10 products could not sustain those levels.
  "But no investments were forthcoming. The unwritten strategy was one of low fixed investments and low advertising support -- the advertising to sales ratio (A:S) was only two! Despite all that, sales boomed and the product was in perennial short supply.
  "Pal's overall approach was low-cost management. Wherever he could, he cut costs and that went for advertising too," said Raman. Sundar was taken aback.
  "Look at it from Pal's viewpoint and situation then," explained Raman. "He was running a company whose sales were growing by 15-20% annually. Its product was in short supply. So he felt no need to invest in advertising or promotion. And there was no serious competition! Geckman's Spark was not making any waves. There was MabelWhite which didn't do much. Then there was Brite from Delite Products, sitting somewhere on the shelves, not making any great moves. In the early eighties Delite brought in another new brand, Beam, which floundered, withdrew it, launched another, Glow, which met the same fate... Where was the incentive for Pal to change the way he was doing things?  
  "In any case, despite the low support from the parent company, Tarmann India's skin care category was faring well. Pal was setting the phase for the company within the philosophy of no investments behind the brands, because he was getting sales growth anyway.   And Solo was in short supply! We sales managers were taught to manage shortages. We did not have to mess with supply chain management, logistics, nothing. We only had to master allocation!
  "Around that time we began to build capacities to increase production. Then something unique happened. Beam was reintroduced, and this time met with unexpected success when it was testmarketed in Tamil Nadu. Much to our surprise, Delite also relaunched Glow, which didn't do too badly either."
  "Yet, your management decided to do nothing, why? There must have been some flaw in its thinking!" said Sundar.
  "No, it wasn't a flaw," corrected Raman. "Its thinking was influenced by what had happened until then and what was happening. A Beam from Delite did not mean Tarmann was going to close down. In any case, Delite was small, and this was not the first time it was flirting with the category. It had done so four times in the past, and none of those attempts had borne fruit. The relaunch of Beam this time too did not come as earth-shattering news. It was not any different from the earlier launches. Where was the cause for panic or even anxiety?
  "It is very easy to blame the management for what happened. The fact is, Pal was so successful. Success does make you myopic. HLL itself got its first shakeup with Nirma, another upstart like Delite. And it took the company five years to wake up to the truth. Beam was the big event which had to happen to Tarmann. Every successful organisation goes through one trauma in the market and comes face to face with the vividness of its `emperor's new clothes' world view of life. 
  "For an organisation, managing that trauma is tough. The fact is, you have to accept the trauma. Those who had been there in the past also had to battle the old reality. They were present during that phase of rationalisation during which they declared: `Delite is not a big threat... we don't need to invest in advertising...' Now the very same people had to go back to the parent company and say: `But all that was wrong.. Actually, Delite is posing a threat. Then I told you it was all right, now I am saying it was all wrong..."
  "It's not easy doing that, believe me. It is devastating. At one level Solo's sustained success seemed too good to be true. And even in those days when the best management advice came only from a Drucker, one looked for `ifs and buts' that would dampen runaway success. There weren't any. But on another level, there were all those reasons for its continued success -- failed competitive efforts, booming growth, no need for advertising, a distribution system that was effective, a market that was in perpetual shortage.... Clearly, it was foolish to put money into areas that were simply not required by the market.
  "The other issue was: the organisation hadn't been used to getting kicked around in the marketplace. I do believe that every organisation must periodically get kicked in the pants by circumstances or competition if it wants to stay successful. When you have a proud set of sales managers who have never faced trauma, who have never failed to meet targets even for a single week, who have never once been rejected by the consumer, it is time to create artificial anxiety. They never had to face the following kind of arithmetic: 'Out of every 10 units bought in the market, six are yours, three are his and one is hers. What are you doing to increase that six to seven or nine?' It never happened, not even once in 30 years!
 "And this was exactly the kind of market Tarmann India was entering. And suddenly the problems were manifold. Suddenly, everything began to fall apart. A plant we had acquired at a very high price turned out to be unsuitable. The skin cream segment started floundering because of a war between the number one and the number two brands in the category. 
  Since Tarmann had not invested much in the brand, skin care got annihilated by the war next door. 
  "The sins of the past were now visiting us. The market reality was: our core category was under threat, the only capacity investments we made had gone wrong. Worse, we were in the midst of a war between our competitors in skin care and just when we needed to throw money into our creams, we found that we needed those funds to defend Solo! So we took a conscious decision to protect our core category, and in the process both the cream and the talcum powder lost marketshare.    
  A virtual domino effect followed. The surplus investible funds which we could have got out of our creams and powders were now next to nothing. And with the core category under threat, morale took a beating in another way.
  Until then we sales people never went to the distributor to sell; goods were simply picked up by them. We interacted with the retailers. Now we had to woo the distributor with schemes and offers, to get them to stock our products. But now he was talking tough. "Where were you the during the past 40 years?" he asked.
  "Nothing made sense as Beam picked up marketshare fast. Poor Solo sales two months in a row led to panic. Everyone knew why sales were falling, but it wasn't only because of Beam. It was falling also because we had never learnt how to manage crises, because we had always only trained for managing shortages. Now, when a market was being supplied by a competitor, we didn't know how to sell.
  "Meanwhile, the economy was opened, and the parent company upped its stake to 51%. It was now demanding performance, volumes, sales... something it had never done all these years when it was a mere associate. It hadn't considered putting money into the brands, investing in marketing or even getting consumer feedback. From such a situation, the focus shifted from profits to volumes and marketshares and leadership. `We were leaders all along, what happened?' it cried. 
  "Very simply, the system was not geared to a demand-led market. It was not geared to competition, marketing, planning, logistics or supply chain management. The supply chain was not needed. Solo was a shortage product, we didn't need to manage distribution; selling was about allocation, mind you.
  "Everything was changing now. You had a parent company which was getting impatient, a market which was getting belligerent, you had skin care products which were getting into skirmishes with the competition, you had a field force which was feeling down and out and a trade which was talking tough. Worse, you had a top management team which was feeling peeved by its own past actions and was now on a witch-hunt.
  "It was a tough situation. The local management was finding it difficult to admit that what it had been conjecturing all these years -- 'The market cannot threaten me, I am the strongest, repeated trials by my neighbour will only result in repeated failures, I am invincible' -- was fiction.
  "The point is, Pal's corporate upbringing was very different. He believed tremendously in the wisdom of the parent company. Also, the reality around him didn't really point to anything more serious. He was being nurtured and brought up by a parent company which felt that profits were more important.
  "Consequently, related issues like environmental threats and weaknesses were never examined. After all, sales were taking place, and the competition was fumbling.   And in the process, Pal never got round to building a strong organisation that was also capable of managing a crisis in the marketplace.
  "Frankly that happens only during a crisis. Like I said, every successful company must get a kick in the rear periodically to stay in shape. Why has HLL been able to handle crises so well? Look at its portfolio: it is so vast that at no point in time can it get comfortable with the success of any one division. There is always one division or another falling apart. Naturally, with large portfolios you are constantly moving people around, you build credible human resource systems, and in the process every manager in your stable has been trained in crisis management.
 "HLL has built this through a culture of paranoia. Paranoia is good and important. At its heart is the fear of: `Is there something I am not doing well enough? Is there somebody who is going to do it better?' Which leads to: `What do I need to do better and how do I do it before the guy next door does it?' And that is precisely my advice to you. Paranoia makes you very outward-looking and ever willing to learn.
  "So, in the process, we didn't build an organisation. At the end of 30-35 years we found that we had not evolved, because there was no change, no need to change, no threats, not even self- appraisal. We built no marketing skills. So when the crisis came, no one knew what to do.
  Even as Beam came in, Pal retired and the technical director, Sukhbir Singh, took over as MD. Now look at what he inherited. Here was a man who had been party to all the decisions made during the tenure of his predecessor. Now he and his generation had to carry the sins of the past and, worse, justify it to the team, and take on the parent company. Not a nice thing to happen. So he looked at operations, plants, beefed up advertising, set up supply chain, logistics and so forth.
  "Sukhbir Singh's dictat was to fight competition. And this was a soldier who was there during the quota regime, during the Pal regime; suddenly he was being asked to go to the battlefront and take it in the teeth. I don't think he was mentally prepared for it. But Singh was an exceptionally competent man, very aware and very intelligent.
  "Unfortunately, Singh believed it was a sales failure, that it had nothing to do with marketing. But then everybody had their own story and when things are as bad as they were at that point for Tarmann, the buck kept on being passed from here to there. Around the time I retired, he began to bring about changes, but all this was caught in the vicious witch-hunt that continued to plague Tarmann.
  "The fact was, the issues were too set to allow for any quantum change to take place. It can improve particular functions, particular competencies, but it certainly is not adequate to bring about quick or urgent changes.
  "So you see, Tarmann's story is not about complacency, it's something else. It was a victim of transition -- transition of the economy, of consumer power, of management changes, of economic drivers. And it happened because it was vulnerable, because it depended too much on a single product."
  Sundar listened keenly. There was a shadow of complacency now and then in Tarmann's saga, but he reckoned his father had a point too. There were far too many issues which caused the myopia.
FOCUS ON GROWTH
 
   K. Venkatachalam is managing director of Kellogg India Ltd. He joined Kellogg India as manufacturing director in 1994 and was responsible for setting up Kellogg India's plant in Teloja. Earlier he was with Hindustan Lever Ltd and Unilever for 12 years in the technical and operation management area.
 
SUKHBIR Singh has a difficult task ahead. The future of the organisation depends on how well he perceives and handles the situation. His initial response has not been very encouraging. 
   Laying the blame on the sales function for past performance is likely to take Tarmann further on the road to disaster.Singh should instead focus on restoring self-esteem and organisational pride and channeling the energy towards a common goal.
  Singh should immediately:
  Understand the aspirations of the parent company and agree on realistic, medium and long-term objectives with it. 
   Redirect the company's focus from 'managing for profit', to `managing for profitable growth'.
  By increasing its stake in the Indian operations, the parent company has now indicated its long-term commitment to the subsidiary. Singh will need to work with the principals to establish a business agenda for the future. He has to ensure their support in all areas of operations to regain Tarmann's position in the market.
  However, the major challenge for Singh is changing the orientation of the organisation, to focus externally on the marketplace and bring about a culture of learning and innovation. Over the last 30-35 years, the company failed to evolve, never felt any need to change. Now it is being compelled to transform itself quickly for survival. Overall, as Raman rightly says, they have to have a mindset that they can handle a crisis and emerge a winner. 
The major challenge for tarmann is to focus externally on the market and to bring about a culture of learning and innovation 
   Tarmann thrived in a period of scarcity, completely disregarding the changing environment. In the post Fera-era and after the dilution of the parent company's stock holding, the subsidiary pulled back even the little investments it was making in its single flagship brand. The company chose to manage the business to earn in an emerging market, oblivious to the huge opportunities. Its portfolio comprised a single low-tech product that was partly outsourced. Its efforts were internally focused, to take the cost out of the system, losing sight of avenues to strengthen its position in the marketplace. It believed that it could launch new categories, and manage them profitably without making any investment.  
  The company had a big opportunity to develop other product categories such as skin care, given that it had such a great start after they were launched. Over a period of time, it could have emerged as a multi-category company and become stronger to fight competition. Tarmann chose not to build on its competitive strength. 
  It could have further consolidated its position by segregating the category and introducing appropriate product extensions or another brand at different price points to pre-empt the competition. It failed to anticipate or even react to changes in the marketplace, consumer preferences, technological advancements or regulatory/environmental issues.  Despite all the warnings, it failed to counter the entry of competitors in Tamil Nadu. Not only did it under-invest in brands and under-performed vis-a-vis market potential, Tarmann never stretched its systems to deliver to the full potential. 
  At the same time the company invested in building manufacturing capacities for low-technology products and acquired facilities at high prices. While the going was good, it never chose to build a healthy relationship with the trade. In a way it silently watched as the crisis developed, ignoring repeated warnings from the marketplace.
   Singh has to build on the strengths of the existing organisation without pinpointing the responsibility on one function or the other. Certainly there will be pockets of incompetence or people who fail to adapt to the changing environment, but Singh needs to set up the challenge, expose the weaknesses of the system or people, and then take remedial action. He may have to bring in change leaders from outside with the right mindset, sense of purpose and urgency. The mandate to its employees was to manage for profit and they did a good job of it by achieving the highest return on investments and earnings per share.Now he has to change gear and work aggressively a growth agenda, building the brand and portfolio, with adequate advertising support. Singh needs to infuse confidence and competitive spirit in his employees, which would be the key to future success. Singh has the equally important task of restoring Tarmann's good relationship with the trade and regaining its confidence in the company. It is quite possible that it may have to rebuild its relationship with other external agencies as well.
  A key area of action for Tarmann is the optimisation of product portfolio. Given past experience, Singh probably would not like Tarmann to be a single-product company in future. Singh would do well to research and gain key insights about market trends, demographics and competitive offerings before drawing up marketing plans.
  The company needs to invest in Solo and take it back to its leadership position. It should also create a second flank brand to counterattack Beam.

COLLAPSING IN A CRISIS

 John Pinto is the chief financial officer of Whirlpool of India and a director on its board for the last two years.

 THE setting: the quarter-final of the Cricket World Cup 1996. The two opposing teams: South Africa and the West Indies. Pre-match reviews: South Africa is a sure-shot favourite for winning the cup. However, the final result -- mighty South Africa suffer a humiliating defeat. After much deliberation the key issues: complacency and inability to handle pressure.
 South Africa's continuous string of victories created a sense of overconfidence and the West Indies match was regarded as just another regulation match. Another major factor -- the middle order rarely had had a chance to bat at all in previous fixtures and was unaccustomed to pressure situations. The combination of these factors led to inevitable failure.
  As in sport, so in business. Tarmann, carried away by unopposed success, became overconfident, complacent and so myopic that it could neither see nor respond to the real competitive threat when it came. Failure to properly address long-term issues in its quest for short-term profits drove Tarmann in a direction it could not easily and quickly reverse, leading eventually to its downfall.
Tarmann was too leathargic in waling up to the really and eventually failed to protect its brand equity from attack
  Tarmann failed to realise that volumes in the industry were purely attributable to population growth rather than its own initiatives. The company made no effort to proactively grow the category or increase marketshare. Investment in the brand was minuscule and the consumer was taken for granted with no attempt at innovation. Long-term needs were sacrificed to achieve short-term goals. The only reason for the brand's success was the lack of alternatives for the consumer at that time. As soon as a credible alternative appeared in the market, Solo's brand franchise was not strong enough to command adequate consumer loyalty.
  A company must constantly be in a state of preparedness to deal with all eventualities. What this means is that a brand must continuously be energised through new, innovative products that excite the consumer and create entry barriers for competitors.
  And, of course, powerful communication is essential to make the consumer aware of the latest developments. The best time for a company to take these steps is when it is on top because resources, especially cash, are not a constraint. Structural change in all aspects of operation must continuously be pursued even in times of plenty in order to derive quantum benefits. To start thinking about corrective action on the downturn is too expensive, too difficult to implement and much too late to secure management buy-in. Tarmann was too lethargic in waking up to the reality and eventually failed to protect its brand equity from attack by Delite. 
  In addition to a consumer focus, a company must always keep a watchful eye on competitors. Success often leads to arrogance, creating an inward-looking management without an external focus.
  Tarmann did so by not analysing closely enough what competitors were doing and what lessons could be learned and what remedial action was essential.
  All too often, when there is no challenge an organisation can lapse into complacency and not sharpen its skills. It is a key leadership role to keep an organisation in a state of constant "red alert" for the economic equivalent of war -- competition. An organisation which is not continuously challenged from within will not develop the ability to handle external threat.
  Tarmann was remiss in doing none of these. When the real threat struck, the organisation was thrown into disarray, internal bickering replaced teamwork and the company was unable to respond as one man to the crisis.
  Tarmann is clearly a case of serious leadership failure.
