Designed by: Epsilon Systems - SQ-80, Hauz Khas Appts, New Delhi-16. Phone: 685-3301, 685-0878, 98100-06431

Case Study 9


13 Sep 1999		 FROM HIRING TO PARTNERSHIP.
MEERA SETH
Giridhar Nemani, promoter of loss-making Kellapp Industries, faced a question: can a new chief operaing officer ask for a share of future profits?.
  Girdhar Nemani watched the hour glass fill with the glitter, drop by drop. When it was done, he turned it around and let it start all over again. 
 He had been at it for the last 45 minutes, while his mind brimmed with the meeting he had just concluded with Adip Rai, partner at headhunter firm Cornwell Search. Rai's recommendation had shaken up his restructuring plans. 
  Nemani was the promoter and managing director of Kellapp Industries, which manufactured an industrial speciality product, Sirrug-32. The product was recently had been deregulated by the Indian government. Due to intense competition at home and surplus capacities in the global market, the last two years had seen unprecedented losses at Kellapp. The company was sound on technology and clued into the global market, but Sirrug-32 was a commodity product range and was affected by global pricing. When he set up his business 15 years ago, little did Nemani realise that it would grow the way it did. 'You can't be in this category and remain small. Success lies in big capacities, volumes and investments,; he told his advisors, Bright & Thakur, which he had hired last year to examine the company's losses. But Nemani was optimistic since the market was huge and the demand for Sirrug-32 variants high. He proposed additional capacities that would take Kellapp's turnover from Rs 1,600 crore to Rs 6,000 crore.
  Bright & Thakur's diagnosis was tough on Nemani: Kellapp had not invested in professional management, had suffered from a lack of systems and processes and had mismanaged margins in the international markets. Sirrug-32 was competitive in quality, but quality was not the only determinant. There were other values that Kellapp needed to deliver.
  Another grey area was buying. Kellapp's key raw material was also a commodity, so buying was large and speculative. The company did not have a clear positioning for Sirrug-32, or a plan to increase value delivery. ;When you are in industrial products, you are actually a service-led company,; said Bright & Thakur. ;Products require different dimensions of servicing, in packaging, distribution, logistics, availability. It is a customer service-led market and you need strategic business units (SBUs).;
 Nemani had agreed, and Bright & Thakur created SBUs under the managing director. Kellapp now needed to hire business heads to manage the business units, since people of the calibre needed to drive the business were not available in-house. ;I am not a professional, I am the owner, I understand the business in the international context, but I do not have the management capability to drive that in the organisational sense. The functional heads are professionals but are not people of the quality to push the business beyond a point. They come from within the industry but the industry itself, if you see how it has evolved, has not harboured quality people.; That was how Nemani hired Cornwell Search, known to have hired the best CEOs in the country.  
   Nemani needed two business heads, one of whom he wanted to groom as the managing director. ;I do wish to retire,; Nemani had confided to Adip Puri of Cornwell, ;though that is just a dream and with the business going great guns, I certainly don't see myself being able to spearhead all that. It is time I looked around for a successor, but there is time for that, at least three years, for that is when the capacities come up in full swing.;
  But Cornwell did not think three years was enough time. Hiring business heads would only address the short-term mandate of leading the company into a profit situation. The long-term mandate was to build the organisation to manage a growth of Rs 6,000 crore. Rai asked: ;Having hired the business heads, how are you going to move the business forward? The business heads will change the capability of these jobs, and the drive of the other functions. So, let's say you will soon have a well run financial management, marketing management, buying management. But does all this add up to a total organisation which is now to make a quantum leap from Rs 1,600 crore to Rs 6,000 crore? ;
  Putting the capacities in place is part of the process, but going out there and developing the market and selling is another. The traditional processes of market development are not going to lead you there. There are some very cutting-edge management issues which I can see will impact the success of your business. And that can happen only if you create a layer in between the managing director and the rest of the organisation today.;
  Ideally, as Cornwell saw it, there was a job at the top, above the business and functional heads. A chief operating officer. And that job had to be manned today, not three years hence. ;The business head you hire today has a long way to go,; said Adip. ;First he will have to prove himself on the job, then he will have to prove himself in the managing director's job. And you are talking of a time-frame of 3-4 years, but you don't have that kind of time! The next 3-4 years are very critical. Your capacities will be up and ready by 2001; by that time, the business needs to be of a particular capability to manage those volumes.;
  Adip saw a serious issue. The kind of person Kellapp needed as the managing director or even as the COO was not available within the industry, the protected environment had not allowed capabilities to develop. Therefore, such a person would have to be hired from outside the industry and he would be on the learning curve till he learnt the business.
  It was a complex situation. Nemani wanted to hire two business heads now, one of whom would move up as the managing director in three years. As far as Cornwell was concerned, the learning curve was a given, wherever the person was hired. But Nemani's plan was sub-optimal, it felt. ;The next three years are very critical for you. A business head hired today will be on the learning curve for 2-3 years, during which period the company is also growing, the capacities are being built and you will need somebody who can pick up the baton and run!;
 Nemani now came up with a new plan. ;Let us hire a COO as you suggest, and only one business head. I will rather hire two senior operating persons internally and run the SBU myself than hire a business head. Thus, at the end of three eyars, one of these senior managers moves up as the business head, the COO we hire will become the managing director and I will retire. That structure is very viable. You have done succession planning, you have built the organisational capability and the people's roles are in place.; That agreed on, Adip now brought up compensation. Nemani was willing to pay the business head Rs 35-40 lakh. ;The COO can be paid 25% more.
   How does that sound?; he asked. ;I can't tell you that you cannot hire a COO at Rs 50-60 lakh,; said Adip. ;But what are your expectations of the COO? What capabilities are you seeking? You want him to be a totally different person. He is not a person who is 20% better than the business head, right? Then don't relate the COO's compensation to the business head's. They are entirely different jobs, with different mindsets, approaches and capabilities.;
  Three weeks later, Adip placed before Nemani the resume of three stunning candidates for the post of business heads. Nemani was impressed by all of them, each of whom, he felt, had the potential to become the managing director. However, their current compensation was higher than what he was offering for the post of business head. ;If you hire any one of them, understand you will have to pay higher than what you are offering now. Which means between Rs 60-70 lakh,; said Adip.
  Nemani shuddered. The amount was far beyond his imagination. If that was the price for a business head, what would a COO cost, he wondered. ;You need to clarify today if you will be prepared to pay what it takes to hire a COO and in our assessment it looks like Rs 1 crore or higher,; said Adip, even as Nemani's jaw dropped. ;You may even find someone at Rs 60 lakh,; said Adip. ;It is possible to find someone who is earning Rs 45 lakh and offer him Rs 60 lakh. But do you want to hire cheap, or do you want to start by putting a value to this COO job? If you want my advice, you might want to hire the person at a lower fixed component today, because you are making losses today -- but you might like to build a large variable component and let him catch up, based on the success of the business and gradually attain the market value of this job. That approach tackles both attractiveness and retention issues,; said Adip.
  Nemani was perplexed. At that kind of salary, where was there any doubt about retention? Why would such a person want to leave Kellapp? ;People also build up their own market value,; said Adip. ;You will be helping him learn a commodity business, a skill not readily available in your industry,; explained Adip. ;With the commodity sector opening up, in three years, he could have good value in the market. We saw this happening in the telecom sector.; Nemani was unsure, but he was willing to play along. ;
  So, what kind of person do you want as the COO? Look at successful businesses, and at ones that were once where you are today and are already where you want to be. Look at the people who did all that. Let us not discuss compensation yet.; said Adip.
  Nemani was amused, but it sounded like a good way to talk about his dreams. ;Like someone who built Hindustan Lever Ltd's (HLL) detergents business, or Pepsi's bottling operations.  
I want someone like the person who built Citibank's credit card business, grew it and defined the market norms for quality, product expansion, who gave the category its width and recognition, changed marketing norms. Like someone who led SRF, slowly and steadily built its category leadership;, went on Nemani, slowly warming up to his prospective managing director. ;
   Wonderful,; said Adip. ;Now, before we decide on their compensation, let us put down the qualities in these people that attract you.; ;
  People who are strivers, who have the breadth of vision to know where they want to take their businesses and how to get there. People who will not settle for half measures. People who can haul a business up a mountain and have the emotional stamina to do so, who can chalk out independent gameplans and have the guts to achieve,; said Nemani. ;
   Now that we have benchmarked the quality of performance you are seeking, look at the remuneration that went with the job and look at the successful companies they belong to,; prompted Adip. For Adip knew he was not drawing an HLL manager for a Coke, or a Pepsi manager for a Dabur. First, he was hiring for Kellapp which was not known as the best of employers. Second, Kellapp was making losses. ;Third,; he said to Nemani, ;you have a full CEO agenda and you are asking the incumbent to build value in the business. How much of the value addition are you willing to share with him?; Gone are the days when an employer could say, this is the job, this is what I want, but we won't pay this much because the employment market says this is the going rate. Today, compensation had to be a variable, linked to what the manager can deliver to the business. As he said to Nemani: ;Today, if your share price and market capitalisation rise substantially, doesn't the incumbent have a role in attaining that? Shouldn't he get a share of that?;
   Nemani would have to loosen his mind a bit, felt Adip, he was still caught up somewhere in the early 1980s, viewing managers as employees, viewing value-sharing as compensation, viewing partnering as hiring. Today's market was no more about jobs, salaries or employment. The managers today saw the whole market as a huge business opportunity, almost like a consulting firm, where they steered your business and shared in your value addition.
  But Nemani was not in sync. Today, the biggest problem that Nemani had was that he was the owner of the business and he held the ownership. The COO would have to share the ownership and reciprocally Nemani would have to share his profits with him. Nemani said: "You are trying to drive the compensation higher and making it very expensive for me."
  "Its not a question of compensation," said Adip. "It's about sweat equity. People are adding value to your organisation." Nemani could see the point Adip was making. Even so, he was rattled by the asking price. Adip tried again: "A ceiling to your offer will cut off people above that, and limit the talent you can attract. In my opinion, the person you are looking for may not lie in this bracket of Rs 60-70 lakh, but, probably in the next level, Rs 80 lakh-Rs 1 crore."
  "Naturally," said Nemani, "you could also find someone in the level below." "May be," agreed Adip, "but should you be paying lower because the incumbent comes from a lower remuneration category? Please understand, compensation is a way to position a job, not compensate in the real sense of the word." Adip was trying hard to reach into Nemani's mind.
  "I am not willing to pay that kind of money," interjected Nemani conclusively. "It's really a lot and I don't even know if he will deliver. I am willing to let the guy earn the money as he goes along. Therefore, I would suggest a very high component of variables and a lower fixed compensation."
   "But we cannot drag the fixed below his current fixed, " said Adip, "Why should he come? You are giving him a loss-making business, which he has to turn around, change and expand. You must offer him a fixed component which is higher than what he is getting, and a small variable which can grow fast. But you can't change entry compensation."
  The tussle was now over two issues. One, how much to pay now, and two, how to keep the incumbent involved and growing over time. Adip felt the COO's compensation must be between Rs 80 lakh-Rs 1 crore, with a definite plan for growing the variable component over the next three years. Nemani, however, was insisting it should be between Rs 60-65 lakh with an assurance to build it up to between Rs 80 lakh-Rs 1 crore in the next three years.

   Adip said: "You are running the business, so you must take the risk. Why should an incumbent do so? You are saying, come to my parlour, never mind that you are selling toothpaste today, but do come and sell chemicals, make me profitable, grow my base to Rs 6,000 crore, make me globally competitive, my company is in a mess. The incumbent is risking his reputation by coming to a loss-making company which is not considered a premium employer, and it's style is neither proven nor successful. You must take the risk on compensation."
  "The value that you will place on the job will depend on whether your business is going to be in the big league in 2001. The COO has to come in today, not three years from now. The next few years are going to build the big league for you. Therefore, he cannot be 20% over the business head, he simply cannot be seen in relation to the business heads. You have to position him differently, price him differently and there's got to be this halo of exclusivity around him."
  "Am I paying Rs 1 crore or Rs 80 lakh for a trainee managing director?," asked Nemani.
  Adip replied: "Please understand, Kellapp is a Rs 2,000-crore company going up to Rs 6,000 crore. It's a different dimension altogether. This leads to other dimensions in functional and market services. The transition you are seeking is your mandate. His task is to deliver this mandate because you are not going to do it. You are going to invest and build the capacities. You are going to find the funds. Why, this man is going to play a very important role in that. Often, funds come because of the perceived quality of management. Today your company is a risk according to the financial institutions. When you have the right person, what you are communicating is, `we are gearing up and seriously.' Now, if this guy is to drive all that, how can he be priced at 20% over the business head?"
  "When your Coke and Pepsi and Kellogg's came in, they came with empty balance-sheets. No numbers. Just a forecast and a determination to reach those figures. Didn't they place the right man to take them to those heights, commensurate with the goals they sought? What defined their CEO's price was the value of their desire to take the brand to a certain level of performance and growth. That is what you have to do."
  For Nemani it was a mindset problem. He had never thought of paying employees' in tens of lakh. Neither had he imagined he would grow from Rs 750 crore to Rs 2,000 crore in two years. He was nervous coping with four-digit sales. But he was beginning to see how the business had suffered due to a lack of understanding of the needs of the organisation. He was in the red, and so he stood a greater risk of attrition which he could ill-afford. But whether he could make the transition depended a lot on his ability to shift gears. Compensation was his big hurdle which he still could not see as placing a value on a job and positioning it correctly. Instead, he now wondered, should he really hire a COO?

MANAGING THE TRANSITION

PREETY Kumar is the managing partner of Amrop India, a leading international executive search firm.

  Dear Mr Nemani,
  It is clear that you are now faced with a decision that is the key to the survival of Kellapp Industries in the face of global competitive pressures to grow or sell out. Your decision to hire a COO and business heads, as well as your compensation strategy, will be the key to managing this agenda.
  Kellapp needs to build on the work done by you, and consolidate its position over the next two years. Its survival is dependent on the ability to develop an organisation geared to working cohesively at higher corporate growth rates and at global scales of operation. As you said yourself, this task will not be run by you, but by professional managers who will manage the Rs 6,000-crore company that Kellapp aspires to be. The big question mark, however, is whether you are willing to let go of the belief that ownership implies management, and in the value you attach to the survival and success of Kellapp. This would answer the question that is nagging you -- the compensation for your successor. 
  Let me address the decision to hire a COO first. I agree with Bright & Thakur's suggestion to establish an SBU structure within the organisation -- this would drive the organisational focus and the customer focus the businesses need to break away from the commodity mindset. It would consolidate Kellapp's position and give impetus to organisation building. The dilemma is whether to hire multiple SBU heads with or without hiring a COO. I believe that hiring just business heads has its drawbacks. First, it will not be able to attract managers capable of leading a Rs 6,000-crore organisation. Second, the person would be driving his own learning curve in the initial years, and would, therefore, come to grips with his SBU at the end of three years, and may not have a full organisational perspective. Third, the assignment gives only partial control. The level of exposure will also be limited, and will not make you confident enough to hand over the baton.
Business failure is a risk the company would take on in any event. But the real issue is your ability to hand over control transparently 
  Hiring a COO is a good idea. He would be the second-in-command and be able to drive growth from day one. Also, a capable COO should be able to contribute to organisation-building early into his stint. Therefore, from the point of view of leading the company to multi-fold growth, a COO is essential for success. As for compensation, it is important to look at risk factors. As I see it, the company and the candidate are both taking risks which can be managed by cautious planning.
   From your perspective, the risk you bear is managing the transition of control without losing focus and key team members. Kellapp is highly leveraged, and runs the risk of collapsing mid-way through the transition. The COO may not be cut out for the task in the short term when he is still riding the learning curve nor in the long term (when he is realigning the company to perform at a higher order of magnitude). He may leave mid-way through the implementation of the plan, either due to failure or frustration at not getting the freedom to perform. From the candidate's perspective, the risks are personal. He could be moving from a high-growth career in a profitable firm, to a loss-making unit. 
  He's also looking at movement from a professionally-run set-up to a non-professional one, running the risk that the team may not be able to realign with new global ambitions.   Business failure is a risk the company would take on in any event. 
  But the real issue is your ability to hand over control transparently -- to ensure continued leadership, and to give the COO space to perform. This would be critical to the success of this plan and would prevent him leaving mid-way due to frustrations with your management style. The arrangement I would recommend is to hand-hold for the initial six months while the COO understands the business, and draws up a recast plan. The next six months would involve handing over charge to the COO, while he establishes himself. After this, the COO should be given full authority.
  It is clear that this assignment is crucial for Kellapp's survival. I suggest that you do not look at compensation as the cost you bear to hire a manager, but rather the value you attach to Kellapp's survival and growth. The compensation should build in the higher risk attached to this move from your side and his. Clearly, your intention would be to pay for success, while the candidate would look for a substantial attraction factor to take up the job. This leads to a three-level compensation plan, with a fixed and a variable component, and stock options. The fixed component would have to build in the candidate-end risks and attractiveness factors. The variable component should build in the company-side risk that the COO does not perform. The stock options would help manage the retention risk.
  My suggestion, therefore, would be to hire a COO in addition to the business heads, and to compensate him on the value he brings to the organisation. The key issues at this stage are whether you are willing to trade off management control with survival, or you would rather look at compensation as the "cost" to hire a manager.  
  Regards, Preety Kumar
  

HIRING THE RIGHT FIT

  ASHOK Wadhwa is the managing director of Ambit Corporate Finance which specialises in M&As & joint ventures.

  In July 1991, when the government started the process of deregulation, it unwittingly put in motion an irreversible process which would have a profound impact on India's business landscape. This was the gradual shift in the balance of power between the various factors of production. In the traditional model, the key factors of production are land, labour, capital and technology. Before reforms, the balance of power lay with capital. Anyone with capital could set up a business, the regulated environment would ensure success.  
   But, post-liberalisation, the scenario has changed. Setting up an industry was no longer a guarantee of success. You needed skilled managers to ensure the success of any business. They began to realise that good people were hard to come by. And that once found, they had to be adequately incentivised. It is no surprise, therefore, post-1991, there has been an unprecedented rise in real salary levels for managerial staff. In any industry, the challenges match the rewards. And it is no surprise that the commodity business faces a severe crunch as far as managerial talent is concerned. The best headed for the fast moving consumer goods industry where there was both challenge and profit.
Hiring the right person is probably the most crucial investment decision he will ever make because at stake is his wealth 
   Mr Nemani's situation is not unique. Kellapp is just one among the hundreds of Indian companies which paid little heed to managing their profitability in an era of high import margins. And, when the import duty protection vanished, their shortcomings were exposed. But, as Mr Nemani rightly says, there is also a vast opportunity for players who can achieve globally competitive cost, service and quality levels. The challenge lies in getting there. And what Mr Nemani still hasn't come to grips with is that the challenge cannot be met by throwing vast amounts of investment into plant and machinery. Only transformational leadership of a high calibre can make the difference.
  Mr Nemani is a true old-school entrepreneur who grew up in the days when the ability to take risks was disproportionately rewarded as compared to managerial skills. Consequently, the notion that managerial skills are as important as income-generating assets is alien to him. 
  It's a pity that someone who is willing to commit vast sums of money to expanding capacity is agonising over investing a much smaller amount in the right managerial talent.
  Mr Nemani needs to realise that this is no simple recruitment decision. It is in fact more akin to making an investment decision. Hiring the right person is probably the most crucial investment decision he will ever make. At stake is his wealth, because the right manager could multiply it while the wrong one could erode it. And if Mr Nemani wants to see if a model exists which would work for him, he can look at the West. It is an accepted fact in developed nations that if you want to attract super achievers you need to pay them handsomely. Also, it is not surprising for CEOs to make millions of dollars in salary and stock options.
   In Mr Nemani's situation, given that his resources are strained at present, Adip Rai's suggestion of a low fixed salary with a high variable component makes sense. The former's squeamishness at large numbers shows that Mr Nemani hasn't fully grasped the underlying logic that the more money the employee makes, the more money he will make.
  A corollary to "hiring the best" as a policy is "hiring as many of the best as you can find". Mr Nemani shouldn't shrink from the prospect of three highly paid employees. In fact, the best option would be to hire a COO and two business heads. This way, if one of them, or even two, don't work out as expected, he would have a viable option to fall back upon.
Designed by: Epsilon Systems - SQ-80, Hauz Khas Appts, New Delhi-16. Phone: 685-3301, 685-0878, 98100-06431

Case Study 10


27 Sep 1999		 TO HAVE AND TO HOLD ON.
MEERA SETH
Alert's chief executive Raj Mehta faces a dilemma -- retaining a high performer while toeing the management line. Can he walk the tightrope successfully .
  Abraham 'Abby' Kurien heard the taped music for the seventh time. He was on hold for his boss, Raj Mehta, who he had been told, was on the other line. ;Would you like to call back?; 
  Mehta's secretary had asked. ;I am not sure I will remember to do so,; said Abraham. ;I prefer to hold on, I have nothing else to do,; he said.
  Abraham doodled on the paper before him. He was going to take one thing at a time. His list read, Speak to Raj'; it was first on the priority list. So, he was going to do just that. Everything else could wait, not that there was much to do otherwise. ;
  Hi! Abby,; came Raj's cheerful voice. That was Raj, always cheerful, always patient, always accommodating, always negotiable. ;Sorry, I kept you waiting...; he said. ;
  "Raj, I am resigning on 31st December. I wanted to tell you now so that you have time to find a replacement,; said Abby. ;
  "Gosh!; was all Raj managed for the moment. Then after a pause, when, as Abby mused, he framed his next sentence, he asked the inevitable:;Why?;
  "I am bored, irrevocably bored," said Abby. "There is no stimulation, no desire, no drive."
  Raj laughed. "You don't have to resign for that," he said cheerily. "Everybody feels like that some time or another.'' While he waited for Abby to offer some explanation, Raj continued: "What is it? Are you unhappy with the increment? Look, I do realise that 10% is nowhere near the 176% growth in revenues you brought us. But do you know, even the best manager got around 8.5% this year? We are cash strapped, Abby! Business has been tight. The only department that raked in decent figures was yours -- training. Between you and me, if it wasn't for the revenues from your department, I wonder what we would have done. The rest have done as badly or as well as last year. But we have to look at the business as a whole. You will understand, after all you head training. Your research across the industry must have a given you a fair idea of how bleak the horizon is for placement firms. I thought you understood our position. I haven't given so detailed an explanation to anyone, not even to our star manager K.C. Of course, he didn't complain about the increments we gave him...
  "Yes, you could say: He can complain all he wants, but does he have a choice? He is so singular in his experience he can at best make a lateral job change,' but do you think anyone else pays better? K.C. has, tremendous potential. If he puts his mind to it, he can go places. He is a skilled human resources (HR) manager and can make a great HR head somewhere. I have often wondered why he has preferred to stay in placement. I think he realises he can grow here, just as you can too. But K.C. is a private person. There is no telling what goes on in his mind. The 8.5% increment speaks nothing for his prowess, but such is life. We have to take the high with the low. Yet, I have given you 10%. I know K.C. is dying to know what I gave you, I am sure he will extract the information from you somehow..."
  Raj went on: "Look, I know you are terrific. Isn't that important? And it isn't just me who thinks like that, even the clients are saying that. After every training session, I am inundated with praise for our training from every manager who attended your session and very soon will be signing up for another batch. You have so much going for you, why peg your growth to increments alone? Are you upset that they withdrew the car allowance this year?
   "Look, I know that was unfair, but as I explained to you, the organisation is free to make changes in its terms of reference. Certain things are not under my control. I am merely the CEO. That title hardly bestows absolute powers on me. It is just the company's way of designating yet taking away all powers of management control.
  I don't control, I only manage! Isn't that the paradox of the millennium? I run the business, I am responsible for profits, but I have no control over costs or how the revenues are spent. You know how family-managed companies are. After all you are the training manager and you have 15 years of experience in HR management.
  "So, they make the rules. Even so, let me tell you, I fought hard to convince them that your loss should be restored. But they were adamant. You can't fault me for not trying. Look, it's just Rs 5,000, you will more than make up for it in the future. In any case, your wife earns a decent packet as media head at PanLeaf India. Double-income families have no cause for complaint about allowances! That was a joke, but I do think you must be rewarded for your efforts and I have always striven for that. Surely, that is not the reason for you to quit? What is it?
  "Is it because the decision to restructure the training department was not discussed with you? But I can't see how that can make you unhappy. Bringing in Markandey as profit-centre head was simply an operational convenience. You have so much to do in terms of developing the training software and training itself that it would have been foolhardy to dump administrative responsibilities on you! To be honest, I did want them to take you into confidence. But they were adamant. It's a family-managed company Abby, you know how they operate. Unilateral decisions are their prerogative. Maybe, you are annoyed that I did not discuss it with you. I told Brigadier Pant, our venerable owner, that considering how much you have contributed to conceiving the department, setting it up, marketing for clients and now the millions flowing in, you must be called in to contribute to the decision. You know what Pant said? I haven't ever met the man, I don't know if he has the capability to make judicious decisions. In any case, we cannot go about calling in all and sundry to craft decisions.'
  "But you know, that is Pant -- a maverick and cranky. You haven't missed anything if you haven't met him. The three of us at the helm simply endure him and humour him. He comes once in two months to rap our knuckles, a habit he probably picked up in the army, and to eat the dhansak from Brighton's. Do you want to know how his arrival fax reads? 
  Brigadier arriving on the 20th for the review meeting over lunch. Please call Mahrukh at Brighton for his special dhansak and three lagan nu custards. Cold pack two dhansak's for return... '' Raj laughed deeply.
   Then he continued: "Anyway, so you know I did try. Pant is averse to participative management. Fortunately, his participation is restricted to rapping us on the knuckles and examining profits. He never looks at who we have hired for what, nor does he get excited by new business. Naturally, he is not interested in who is performing well or otherwise. Names don't register with him. Profits do. That is how he came up with the idea of floating the training business as a separate profit centre. Actually Prasanna, our operations head suggested it. Thereafter, Pant got feverish about the idea and wanted it wrapped up before his dhansak was finished.
  "In any case, I don't see Markandey as a stumbling block. He loves designations; he will have no time to interfere with your turf. Or has he? You must tell me. I heard that lately he has been playing emcee at your sessions. Don't let it bother you. How does it matter if he wants to come in during the first five minutes and say what a great firm Alert is? I know, he also goes on to say how committed he is to the cause of training and that if he is present during these sessions it is only to make sure that the client gets value for money. Look, all that is a public relations (PR) tack. Do you actually believe he is auditing your performance? What does he know about training, anyway? These days good PR alone works. You've got to lay it on thick so that the clients come back in hordes like moths through a crack in the window. This is good for business. We must let processes evolve with the times.
  "Frankly, I would be loathe to have a Markandey thumping his chest and declaring the training department as the raison d'etre of his existence. That's 15 minutes only. Do you think it eats into your session? Or, maybe, takes away the seriousness of what you originally conceived? Hey, it's alright! You think those managers in your class don't realise that? They are in the business of selling too! Okay, so Markandey's method is not Druckerian, but what can be done? He is Pant's blue-eyed boy.
  "Look, you are concerned about quality and focus. All this thing about PR does not wash with you. I remember what you told K.C. once: If you are good it will speak for itself, you don't have to do the chest-thumping act'. Wonderful! I know how passionately you feel about training. Maybe you wish Markandey would not come in like a commercial and break the continuity. I believe last week, in the midst of a session on succession planning, he got up to talk about his own succession to the post of department head at the State Bank of India. Did that bother you? Did the managers from Duvell say something? I believe they exchanged glances and were restless. Don't you think you should alleviate those feelings by subtly corroborating Markandey's role? Look, you can't be silent and lead the client to believe that you are some suffering soul. This is an organisation, Abby, a family. We have been together through thick and thin, remember? Alright, that was too heavy. What I mean is that you need to develop strategies to pre-empt these interruptions, or if they occur all the same, then to subtly thank Markandey for his wisdom and get on! By keeping quiet, you encourage him to go on and in turn waste valuable client time. 
  "Last week I got a call from Duvell's HR head, Dasgupta. He laid out an analysis of loss of return per hour! For God's sake! What are a couple of 15-minute breaks going to do to his return on investment?  Okay, it's more like one hour, but we are trying to make profits. We will use our products to market ourselves.
  "These clients are unreasonable. They will not hesitate to announce in the middle of the World Cup that their pain balm is what makes Tendulkar a fine batsman. But if we take a short break during a session to announce how much other clients have benefited, they see that as loss of continuity. Why, Dasgupta even went so far as to allude that the last module, the syndicated group discussion, was cut short because of those interruptions. I know you loathe these commercial breaks' as you call them, but Markandey is adding value.
  "How else do we advertise our products? In training, the real advertising is on the spot, while the product is being delivered. Like that!; (Abby could hear him snap his fingers to drive home his point.) ;Then it gets carried by word-of-mouth. Not just the quality of the session, but also like, Did you know Alert has trained managers from Delaware in supply chain management? We must get that too...' and thus we get new business. But if we continue to follow your model of focused training and no breaks, the client takes away a lot for himself and we get nothing on an enduring basis. We want Duvell to come back for more, and that will happen with Markandey's methods. ;
  You probably feel that the original vision is being eroded. No, I say. Isn't our training revenue growing by leaps and bounds? Why else would a publisher like Gavin Delloit ask us to author a book on Training breakthroughs in India'? It's no mean offer, let me tell you, although I know you have declined it. You amaze me, Abby. Anyone would give an arm and a leg to get his name on a book like that. The trouble is you don't care enough. Pant is annoyed. In fact, that was the primary reason for his acidic remark I don't think he has the capability to make judicious decisions...' That's my reading, of course, not that he used the actual words. But it is easy to decipher what he means. ;
  I really think you should not have declined the offer. Because you know what happened next... Markandey is writing that book. I believe he has been video recording all the training sessions and using your wisdom to frame his script. Is that why you are going? Now really, Abby, I don't expect that sort of reaction from you. After all, you are the one who always says: 'It doesn't matter who gets the credit so long as the job is done'. Of course, I am going to make sure you get the credit somewhere. Maybe on the cover itself... Yes! That's what I will pitch for. I will speak to Gavin today and ask for the cover jacket to say, Drawn from the training lectures by Abby Kurien'. How's that? ;
  But you won't write that book and someone has to. After all, that is going to be the best PR and advertising for the firm. Just think about it. Hardbound, bold embossed title, glossy jacket and on every book-shelf! The Alert Principle Of Training And Development'. Can't you see that will brand our product as the country's best? They will come to us in hordes.; He continued: ''Frankly, I think you should, even at this stage, collaborate with Markandey. You must set aside all differences and take over the reins of that book before Markandey makes a mess of it. It will please Pant and, importantly, restore your equation with him. 
Maybe next year he will be more open-fisted during increments! Now, that was meant to be a joke, but it is a thought.  You know how family-managed firms work. It is all based on personal equations, not merit. ;That was how Shakti Pulin got to be the HR head of Alert. Personal equation.  I know you are still smarting over his move out of training at a time when you need him badly. What role is there for him as head of HR?' you ask. Look, that's our decision. People can't be stuck in one job for ever. Ask your friends in Hindustan Lever. Just because X sold Surf very well, do you think they won't move him to Close-Up? Of course they will. Good companies do that. And we are a good company. Shakti has to move too. I know you are angry about that. You feel Terrest did not sign up for winter because Shakti is not going to be on the panel. 
  That's rubbish. You are there. Yes, I realise you have 15 other clients to handle, what is a 16th? You fear there will be loss of quality? Rubbish! Not with you around.  
  Oh, now I know, you wanted to start an account management model in training and Markandey stymied that. I really think that was a brilliant suggestion. But Markandey is a business head, he must be empowered to make decisions. He feels there will be costly duplication of effort. In any case, we are not so large, we have just 16 clients. Once there are 30 or 40 clients, it might work. You said something about creating industry-specific training database centres -- what was that? I did not understand anything. Even so, I pushed, you know. Markandey was adamant. I wish you would talk to him and convince him. You must. I think you must accept Markandey as a power to reckon with. He has great depth. Spend some time with him, he is a virtual storehouse of information. You will like him.;
  Raj continued: ;I still don't see why you are being difficult. I am not accepting your resignation, you must know that. No way. Not by the hair of my chinny chin chin', as my daughter would say. Never. You must tell me the reason for your boredom. How can you be bored, with so much work? I can understand Markandey saying that. He doesn't have much to do. But he is getting involved. He is a quick learner even at his age. I would have thought some kind of lethargy would have set in. But no, he is raring to go. For instance, he has insisted on taking the training session next week on team building for Camylla Pharma. I saw his brief, his notes ran into 47 pages. And he spent the entire Saturday hearing and re-hearing your team sessions for Ribbifar Shoes. I heard you were devastated. He has no HR background, you say. What has that got to do with lecturing? At least, he's making an effort. ;
  Don't think I don't have my ears to the ground. I heard about the exchange of words you had with Markandey over this. You told him that team building for shoes and team building for the pharmaceuticals industry are as different as Superman's cape and the Cape of Good Hope. Now, why did you say that to Markandey? Look, team building is an art. The principles are the same, whether you are selling shoes or shower gel. You feel that the marketplace nuances determine the thrust and drive of team building. Now really, Abby, you must credit Markandey with some intelligence. After all, this is his maiden session. You must encourage him. How else will he learn? And to make matters worse, you tell him we have to respect the faith the client places in our zero-based approach to research-based training'. What research base, Abby? How can Markandey possibly go out and research 10 pharmaceutical firms before he structures his session? I believe you told him he must use your researchers. That was nice of you, but you know he was furious because you referred to them as my research staff' and not our research staff'. ;
  Actually, the research staff' would have fitted nicely. You must be careful about what you say. You cannot go about stepping on the corns of people like Markandey. The next thing you know he will be on the phone long-distance to Pant. ;
  Abby, I realise you are doing tremendous work. I appreciate that. But you must not let these things bother you. Look at the fine work you have put in. You have created a virtual revolution in training by getting managers of different companies to collaborate in training. If you leave, I can't see us being able to sustain the department. I am not letting you go, not by the...; ;
  Raj?; said Abby. ;As I was saying earlier, 31st December is my last day.;

PERSONALITY DYNAMICS

  ACHAL Bhagat is an organisational psychologist trained at Oxford. He has an established psychiatric and psychotherapy practice at the Indraprastha Apollo Hospital in Delhi.

  What a dhansak! When you try to understand situations like these it is important to focus on patterns that exist. There are patterns in what people do and in what organisations do. Some patterns are inherent and some arise out of the interaction the inherent patterns have with the context they are placed in. These inherent patterns and their interactions are broadly called personality. Let us look at the inherent patterns in the key players at Alert. 
  Raj is a communicative person who tries to be all things to all people. He has a fixed set of beliefs. He would rather project his belief as a consensus view or the view of a person who cannot be questioned. At one level, he creates a sense of camaraderie by agreeing with Abby and portrays himself as helpless and only a cog in the system. At another level, he almost delivers the sense of ultimate power with veiled threats of consequences.
  He derives authority from evoking myths and stereotypes about the family management concern. Raj's style of leadership is reminiscent of the proverbial divide and rule. He puts forth strategic decisions as decisions based on personal equations. He is a strategist and a political person. He has complete hold over the pulse of the organisation. He has informal channels of communication which tell him what is happening in the company and who is saying what. He also knows who feels what but chooses to ignore the emotional impact of the processes put in place by him. He is a person who is likely to bloat the problem to seem more successful when solved. 
Organisation that do not look out for patterns in their decsion-making or their performance end up as myopic entities
   He is unlikely to be able to listen to other people because he is strategising his next move even when he is trying to be helpful. He is mentally arguing with the person when they are expressing what they feel. Whenever Raj is asked about Abby, he is likely to say: ;Wonderful person, very clever.; But do not the miss the curl of the lip, the half smile and the half wince that is likely to go with it. 
  Abby is a man with ideas and a great belief in fairness. He tries to be patient with people, allows them to have a say and tries to understand them. Raj is aware of his empathetic capacity and uses it as a tool to disarm him. Raj tries to get Abby to empathise with him, with Markandey, with Pant and with the organisation at various stages in the conversation. This is perhaps not the first time Abby has gathered courage to let Raj know about how he feels but in the past Raj managed to evoke Abby's empathy on a number of occasions. Abby's ability to see things from other people's point of view is his greatest strength, and his greatest limitation. He understands his clients' needs and, hence, can formulate training that people find usable. He tries to understand other people in his team and in the process he becomes unassertive. He then gives up what is genuinely his to accommodate other people.
  He has given up so many times that the lack of control makes him helpless and rudderless. He sees no option now except leaving and starting afresh.    He comes across as a person who wants to pay attention to quality and detail. He is a person who believes passionately in what he is doing. He likes variation and unless the work can contribute to his growth as a person he would not do it.
  The book offer may have been rejected because it is only a repetition of what I have done'. Abby likes people to be aware and sensitive and is unlikely to voice his needs. He would like his work to speak for itself. In short, a quiet, introspective person, someone who would find it tough to survive in the corridors of politics.
  A word about Alert. An organisation that tries to promote people on what they say and not on what they do has a problem on its hands. If there is only one division that is making profit the need should be to learn from it and see what is working. If I were Pant, rather than saying that I do not know the man making the most money for me, I would immediately call him and ask: ;What do you think we could do differently to turn around the organisation?' Rather than creating notional power centres like the head of human resources or profit-centre head for training, there is a need to let that person who knows the customer make the decisions. This would only happen if the gossip and backbiting channels that exist were not nurtured.
  Successful organisations cannot be run as political parties or personal fiefdoms. There has to be a feedback system that allows it to correct its course. Everyone at Alert feels the need to perpetuate a communication pattern that validates his or her own viewpoint. Raj does not listen to Abby but knows what he might have to say from other sources' and tries to counter his thoughts without hearing them. Similarly, Abby has developed a belief system that no one cares in the organisation and so it is best to keep what you feel to yourself. Pant feels that he knows best intuitively' but what is called intuition is only an incomplete information-gathering exercise -- information gathered by listening to people who tell you what to listen to. Such organisations that do not look out for patterns in their decision-making or their performance end up as myopic entities. Short-sighted in their vision and short-term in their existence.

THE LEADERSHIP LACUNAE

  Matangi Gowrishankar is vice-prisident(human resource at ICIL.
  
  Raj Mehta's monologue is a study in organisation dynamics and the dynamics within Alert. Interestingly, I believe that the case has less to do with being a family-managed company (FMC) and more to do with styles of leadership and how people respond to them. Alert appears to be managed by a tyrant in Pant. He makes all the decisions without involving the key managers who run the organisation.
  Not all FMCs are like that. I know of some where the chairman is involved in hiring every key manager and of companies with CEOs like Pant
  Mehta typifies those managers who leave their conscience at home and focus on getting their jobs right, delivering what the boss wants. 
  It is clear that he feels for the wrongs that have been heaped on Abby but does not want to correct them since he is not empowered to do so. His main concern is to get things right for Pant and keep his head above water. 
  He has, in fact, taken the solid, strong performer Abby for granted. It is apparent that significant changes that concerned Abby personally and professionally were neither discussed with him, nor intimated to him. This is unacceptable in any organisation.
The organisations that create helplessness in people can never hope to survive. You can't afford to take your people for granted
  Markandey is a different story altogether. He is capitalising on the fact that he is the chosen one. In his quest for self-aggrandisement, he does not really care that he is hurting the carefully cultivated client base and undermining the quality that Alert is acclaimed for. In such circumstances, what can a person like Abby do? His claim that there is no excitement, no drive, is just an expression of the frustration he feels at seeing his assiduously created training arm being demolished. Abby is a good corporate citizen, one who takes pride in his work and delivers value to the organisation he works for. For him reward is not necessarily monetary, it is in being involved, it comes from his knowing that he is valued by the organisation.
   Between Mehta's inability, or rather unwillingness, to present the right picture to Pant and Markandey's quest for the limelight, a good corporate citizen like Abby is lost and disillusioned. Abby expects Mehta's support, which is not forthcoming. He has been told that Markandey cannot be touched and he is unable to influence his tactics. So the only option left for him is to leave. Abby is a high performer but he belongs to this strange breed of people who will not trumpet their achievements or what they can deliver. In the end, such managers have to pay a heavy price. They become fiercely competitive or disrupt the organisation, or like Abby, become more introverted and opt out of the game.
  Caught up as they are in the fight for survival, organisations find it difficult to be sensitive to individual personalities and inclinations. While organisations have in the past represented surrogate homes where managers looked after their people, this is not possible in today's highly competitive environment. This, however, does not absolve an organisation of its responsibility to uphold propriety and build a culture where people are valued. Successful organisations have a well-defined culture of justice and fair play, where performance and contribution are recognised.
  Alert can be in deep trouble if its high performers leave. Pant will be surrounded by yes men' and no profits. Organisations that create helplessness in their people can never hope to survive. Just as it is felt that values like commitment, loyalty aren't virtues any longer, organisations can't afford to take people for granted. If Alert loses its standing in the market, it has only itself to blame for it forgot about the crucial people factor... Recognise, involve and appreciate people, or perish!
Designed by: Epsilon Systems - SQ-80, Hauz Khas Appts, New Delhi-16. Phone: 685-3301, 685-0878, 98100-06431

Case Study 11


11 Oct 1999		 KILL A RIVAL OR KEEP IT ALIVE
MEERA SETH
Prem Raghuvanshi's eyes are fixed on Rago's hot brand, Soz. But should he buy it at all? Or should Raghuvanshi simply live and let live?
  Prem Raghuvanshi felt dissipated. Eight months ago, when he started negotiating with Rago Foods to buy Soz, its ready-to-cook brand, Rago was excited, even enthusiastic about selling Soz to Bluehill Foods Inc. But now, the negotiations dragged on and on, laboured, leaving Prem completely disillusioned.
  Prem and Rago's directors were happy until a few weeks ago. For, Soz's distribution network and plant facilities in Haryana would cut short its market entry stages, besides giving it a readymade market share. Then Rago doubled the price. Prem was weary with the two warring factions at Rago -- one wanted to keep Soz, the other desired a handsome price and wanted to plough the money into Rago's booming dairy products business and had commenced fresh talks with another prospective buyer. Naturally, Prem was anxious.
  A tired and hopeless Prem sought the counsel of Atul Datta, friend and research professor at a leading management institute. Atul's advice: Bluehill should avoid the takeover route. "Indian corporate history is replete with takeover tragedies, whether they were intended to nurture the brand or kill it. In any case, killing competition is not good for business," he said. 
  That came as a surprise to Prem. "You will end up expending a lot more energy managing a newer set of variables, which very often can take away more than they can contribute," said Atul. "Look at what the purchase of Thums Up and Limca did for Coke. Coke too bought Thums Up for a readymade distribution network, and a sitting market share of 55%. And like you, it planned to slowly withdraw Thums Up and replace it with Coke. But everytime they tried killing Thums Up, Pepsi took away the market. It didn't go to Coke. So, Coke had to allow Thums Up to survive. Can you imagine the kind of energy that was wasted fighting the wrong battle? Having bought Thums Up, it was faced with competition in their own drawing room. Do you want that?"
  "Buying out competition to kill it, is marketing machismo," said Atul. "You should rethink the idea. Brand supremacy is to be proven in the market place, not in the morgue."  But the fact was Soz was ready to sell and this was Prem's point. "Forget for a moment the intention behind my purchasing Soz," he said. "Rago wants to sell Soz, it's not a covert takeover! "Neither was there pressure from Pepsi or Coke on Parle to sell," said Atul softly. "Parle felt that with both Pepsi and Coke in, it had no chance of saving Thums Up. That in the ensuing battle between the two giants, and given the kind of money the two would unleash, Thums Up would die like a poor man. So it is said, Parle offered Thums Up and Limca to Coke. It wasn't selling so that Coke should find the going easy in a new market!It was selling because it made sound business sense for Ramesh Chauhan to sell Thums Up. But did it make sound business sense for Coke to buy? That's my point!"
   "Likewise,'' asked Atul, "does it make sound business sense for you to buy Soz? It certainly makes a lot of sense for Rago to sell, after all you are a multinational, deep pockets, panic in your heart, great hurry for distribution clout, and Rago feels that Soz is a lost cause once you make an entry. After all, you have your own brand Tom Yum in that category. So, Rago will sell Soz. But what about you? Taking over competition may have its upside, but it will create a lot of stress for you.
  "Besides, Atul did not think Bluehill needed to adopt this route. "You have chosen this route, because it is much easier and a short cut to market supremacy in an evolving economy like India," he said. "And you can afford to do that because you have the power of money. If you want Tom Yum to gain, you need more than money power. You need energy. And that goes for any marketing situation. For example, you can have one stupid ad running 100 times on the World Cup or you can have a brilliant ad running 10 times. Both ads cost the same kind of money, but one of those ads is going to return vastly on the investment, the other could even take away from your investment," said Atul.
  Continuing his analysis of whether money energises marketing, Atul said: "Mere aggression is not indicative of energy. My favourite example has always been the Polo-Minto ad exchange. Nestle said, 'The mint with the hole' a hundred times, Minto came in unobtrusively and whispered, 'All mint, no hole'. That one ad was so effective, so packed with punch, it took the wind out of Polo. It is a different thing that Nestle countered it later, and aggressively too, but the point is, the delayed aggression was thanks to money power. If it had been energised aggression, the original situation wouldn't have gone on for so long. Very simply, money power doesn't always equal energy. "
  "It's like this Prem," continued Atul. "If you have less money, then a lot of thinking will go behind every ad spend because you know you have only one arrow and it must hit when it is launched. But if you have many arrows, then you will keep launching until one hits. That's the fundamental difference. And then you also have it drilled into your head, 'Let's think long-term'. But didn't Marico too think the haul is long when it bought Sil? When pockets are shallow, you say, the haul ends tomorrow, I must achieve my target sales now. It's not that the richer brands are laidback, but they have the wherewithal to try and err, and the errors don't show since there is already so much fanfare surrounding their brand. But what happens is the deep pockets also tends to dissipate creativity; it lends a certain lethargy to aggression."
  Atul pointed to a yellowing letter pasted on his pin board. It was from his daughter's school principal. "...if you give a child too many comforts, he does not learn how to struggle, to persevere, to create, to innovate..."
  "See that?" said Atul. "A simple 'ism' in child rearing. Father Bonaventure sent that to me when my daughter produced a history project out of Encarta, with colour printouts of the Jama Masjid. I thought it was a terrific effort, but Father wished she had visited the Masjid and researched first hand. There is a message for you and Bluehill there," he said with a smile. "Buying out Soz is a short cut, but it's tantamount to destroying competition which is a destructive strategy," he said.
  Atul now stated another theory of acquisitions: destroying competition stunts the market. "Take Walls," he said. "It bought out competition -- Dollops, Milk Food, Kwality, and but for parts of Kwality, the others were killed. The decision to kill Dollops was a horrific idea. Dollops was parlour business whereas worldwide Walls is a retail brand. Within six months, all the Dollops parlours were closed. So, where is the parlour habit today? Where are those wonderful flavours like litchee and melon and carrot cabaret? That is stunting the market.
   "Five years ago if you switched on the television, you usually saw an ice-cream ad. All these small brands were growing, the market was growing too, albeit slowly. Today, you see only soft drink ads.The ice-cream habit never picked up as it should have, instead consumers found a new recreation habit, colas and pizzas.
  "Question is, why did Walls take over competition? The market itself could have grown had there been some competition. But there was no competition left. If there had been competition in the industry, the synergies that your company gains from an acquisition naturally benefits the total industry."
  "These are highly debatable issues,'' cautions Prem, ''besides which all these brands which you talk of as being bought out, had declared they were unfit to continue in business."
  "Relatively speaking. Relative to competition, mind you. If Walls hadn't come in, Dollops would have gone on, long enough to prove its viability. Milkfood would have gone on too. So, who decided these brands were unfit and under what provocation?'' asks Atul. "The provocation was a mythical, imported management principle, which said, 'Core competency is what works. If you are selling chocolates, don't mess with ice-creams'. The core competency principle has been highly exaggerated to the point of abuse. But they averred, if you stray from your knitting, you will be unprofitable. So, everyone examined their knitting and started selling.
  "Thankfully, not everyone panicked. They decided to trust their marketing energies. Today, seven years down the line, we can see the kind of initiatives they made. Companies like Dabur, like Britannia. They responded to competition." Thus came Atul's next theory: competition causes performance.
  "What happens when you allow competition to survive?" he now asked. "It decreases prices, improves the total market performance, makes the consumer more demanding, which in itself triggers innovation, creativity and quality. How did Dabur go through an overhaul? Competition. How did Britannia step up its market presence and categories? Competition. Competition makes it imperative for every player to revamp his approach and change. Why is Surf continuously adding value? Because if it didn't, then someone else is going to offer that value addition and take away its consumer set. Competition is what allows for product improvement.
  "Therefore, competition also improves performance," said Atul. What is happening in the airline industry today? Once Sahara came in, Jet Airways reduced prices and so did Indian Airlines. The consumer electronics industry is a great example of how competition is offering the consumer value for money. No doubt the system will shed the unviable players, but Thums Up was not unviable, Dollops and Milkfood simply did not have the money to conquer the market in one go. But as brands, they were terrific. If Walls or a Pepsi or a Coke could not return profits in seven years, how could a Dollops have been expected to return profits in two years? 
   We are very forgiving of a foreign brand, Prem, but we do come down heavily on an Indian brand. And that is because as a people, as investors, as business managers, we have not evolved. We lack patience and awareness. Liberalisation requires patience, not predators." Atul was confident that if Bluehill steered clear of its current strategy to buy out Soz, it would end up with a better performance. "If it wasn't for Nirma, Lever's detergent business would have been a different story," he said. "Surf would have still been the leader, just as Coke will be in 10 years, but it would have been a long and boring story of we tried, tried and tried. Not, we thought, thought and thought, which is what led to Wheel, and the Rin variants. To Ariel and its variants. Simply, competition advances performance."
  Prem felt recharged by Atul's outbursts. He asked: "What would have happened if Coke hadn't bought Thums Up, that is, if Parle hadn't sold out?" Prem was really thinking about what Bluehill's fate would be without Soz.
  "I wonder myself," said Atul. "At least one thing is clear, Coke would have been in a better position. Can't you see? Today it has a 56% market share, but how much of that is pure Coke? If it hadn't bought Thums Up and Limca, chances are its current 56% market share would have been smaller, but it would be entirely made of the Coca-Cola brands. And it wouldn't be spending so much money fighting Thums Up. Its market warfare would have been more focused."
  Atul was emphatic Prem should not succumb to the pressure to buy his arch competitor, Soz. "Buying competition does not guarantee success, my friend," he said. "Because a lot of Coke's energies went into killing a brand and not so much in developing Coke, their energies got split. That I think made a lot of difference to what Coke did.
  "Yet look at what competition did for Pepsi," he said. "It's rival was just one, Coke. Not Thums Up and Limca. If Pepsi was lacklustre those initial years after its Indian entry, I think it was because Coke had not arrived. Life at Pepsi is about challenging Coke. When it came to India, that challenge was not there. Those early years were very pessimistic. It got weighed down by issues like the government, the export obligations, the bottlers, joint venture relationships... which were not doled out only to Pepsi. Most new entrants faced constraints of one kind or the other. All that fell into place the moment Coke came in. Just like that! How come? Some call it coincidence, I call it burst of energy. The energy came with the entry of Coke. Suddenly, there was a reason. They got energised with the news of Coke's entry. Suddenly, all other problems seemed small. Simply, competition caused performance.
  "Pepsi too had the option of buying out Thums Up. It didn't. Never mind the reasons. Simply examine the different turn of events for Goliath A who bought out competition and Goliath B who did not buy out competition and look at their market share composition. There is some food for thought there for you," said Atul. "And mind you," he continued, "it was competition that grew the soft drinks market. Competition increases consumption. If the cola market is growing, it's because of the continuous distraction and entertainment provided by the cola wars. What Sachin did, what Aamir did, what Pepsi said, what Coke said... it goes on. But in ice-creams there is no war. So consumption is not fun. Walls has no one to fight. The ice-cream message is coming from a single voice, Walls. Excitement is when five people scream ice-cream. There is no fun in the category." Thus came Atul's next theory: competition is good for business and market growth.
  "You need somebody to fight," he said, "That's what keeps your adrenaline charged and active. Take Cadbury. It did not grow the market aggressively until Kit Kat came. As a monopoly it had no competition and the market did not grow. Prices were going up. The chocolate industry was Cadbury, so it never examined the opportunities. It said: 'Cocoa is expensive. Excise is high. Sales tax is high. Indians don't eat chocolates -- it is a western concept. Indians eat mithai.  
  They don't eat chocolates in the rural areas. Only children eat chocolates...' It defined the market with its constraints. So whenever there was a problem, it increased the price. You didn't have a choice, you either ate chocolates or you didn't. By default, the Indian consumer chose mithai.
  "When Nestle entered chocolates, Cadbury didn't suffer, because Nestle's entry lacked aggression. Just examine the competitive responses of both players. Nestle resorted to being a me-too. If you have a Dairy Milk, I have Nestle Milk , you have a Five Star, I have a Bar One, till they came and said I have a Kit Kat. That was when the horse power at Cadbury shot up. Its sleeping giant Cadbury Dairy Milk (CDM), woke up.
   Kit Kat broke into a huge success. Coincidentally, Cadbury launched Perk at the same time, which was similar to Kit Kat in form. Now look at what competition did. The whole chocolate biscuit category flew wide and huge. It became so huge, that it became bigger than even CDM, the biggest category until then. Suddenly, grown ups were eating chocolates, rural areas became opportunities for growth, and no one wondered if Indians preferred mithai! That's how competition grew the market phenomenally. Now compare this with what Walls and Coke did with competition. See?" said Atul.
  "So," went on Atul, "don't try to kill competition. If you have the money to buy a company, use it to set up your own plant and distribution, just like Pepsi did. And innovate and energise your aggression. Let competition live, they will give you a reason to reach out for the best in you. Don't look at what Surf did after Ariel came. Look at what Surf did before Ariel and Nirma. Look at what Cadbury did before Kit Kat. And also look at what the ice-cream industry was and what it is today after the demise of competition. They are studies in the power of competitive forces.
  " Buying market share was easy, felt Atul. It does give readymade markets. But there is no study to show that this strategy works or does not, he said.
  And what about the cost of buying out competition? "Point is, did the acquisition money pay itself back?" he asked Prem. ''Did you fix a payback period? Did the returns come from Thums Up or from Coke? Real payback is if it came from Coke. For the Coca-Cola Company, revenue from Thums Up is not real earnings or growth. Revenue from Coke is.
  "Only then can it be said that buying competition worked for your brand," said Atul. "The other yardstick would be, how much did you contribute to market growth? If it was x%, then how much came from Thums Up and how much from Coke."
  Prem was confounded. Did Atul's theories stand on good ground, he wondered. No, he was not rethinking his buyout of Rago, but about letting Soz live.
   "Most learning is hindsight," said Atul. "Nobody knows at the time what the future holds. These acts are done assuming that events a, b, c will occur. Until these results unfold, it remains just an action. It's only five years since competition died in soft drinks and ice-creams. But what we see so far is that killing competition wasn't good business for the total industry," ended Atul.

Walking The Razor's Edge

KIRAN Khalap is a member of the chlorophyll Brand and Communication Consultancy.

"Coexistence or no existence"  --Anonymous

  Bluehill and Rago foods bring alive the pithy wisdom of the phrase "coexistence or no existence". In the absence of figures, costs and overall quantitative data, we are restricted to qualitatively studying here the hypotheses that Atul Datta is passionately presenting to Prem.  
Whether it is due to the physical tiredness that follows too much squash or the mental fatigue of a protracted takeover battle, poor Prem appears defenceless against this onslaught. Some are truisms that the bravest marketer in the subcontinent would be loathe to challenge, some are so fuzzy a sophist could quote corporate history to support either interpretation.
  Let's deal with them, one by one, and see where it leaves Prem: in a blind alley or a dark tunnel with a light at the end. Hypothesis One: In an evolving market like India, it is easier to gain market supremacy by using your cash to support your own brand than buying out rivals.
  True. In a market where penetration figures in most categories are single digit, the prime task of a marketer is conversion, attracting category non-users. In foods, the penetration figure for branded atta is about 4%, branded salt 15%. And despite the combined efforts of Pepsi and Coke, the penetration of soft drinks in India is around 14%, rising to a high of 40% in the higher socio-economic class segments. That means even at the upper end of the market, you have more non-category users than other brand users. Which is perhaps why Coke (correctly!) acknowledges its competition as water, nimbu pani, naariyal paani, not Pepsi.
  In Prem's category of ready-to-cook foods, the penetration would be pathetic enough not to show on the radar, which validates Atul's hypothesis. Unless, of course, like the oft-quoted Coca-Cola company, you have enough cash to do both: support your own brand and buy competing brands!
As far as Prem Raghuvanshi is concerned, soz alive is better than soz dead, whether he buys it out from rago or not  
  This means, if Prem can afford to buy Soz, he must. And just as Coca-Cola paid Mr Ramesh Chauhan an additional $20 million for the bottling plants, he should do that too. Hypothesis Two: Competition is good for market growth.
  Atul's second truism. Of course, the flip side of the competition coin is choice. The wider the range of choices for the consumer whether in kind or cost, the higher the likelihood of his entering a category, though, in India, it is the price that is the key to large volumes. The moment Cadbury's launches Dairy Milk cubes for Rs 2 each, as it declared recently, a vast number of consumers will finally be able to afford to lay their hands on a chip of an old dream: enjoying a vilayati sweet.
   Nirma was good for the laundry market. At Rs 7 a kilo on its launch date, it cost one-third the market leader's product and converted soap users, fuelling the growth of detergent powders. It was also good for Hindustan Lever, which responded by changing its paradigm, and launched Wheel, which in turn converted more soap users.
  Of course, the battle for market share is easier to win when you as a corporate own more than one brand in a category. Then you can assign roles, comparative, monadic, competitive, whatever. After all, Coca-Cola owns four of the top five soft drinks in the world. It bloods its brands when required, to engage in man-to-man combat, in different segments. So, as far as Prem is concerned, Soz alive is better than Soz dead, whether he buys it out from Rago or not. Hypothesis Three: Necessity is the mother of creativity.
  When Atul mentions that money blunts marketing aggression, he's opening up a debate that sophists would love. Supporters of size and economies of scale would argue that's the only way to succeed. 
When the Chinese juggernaut invades India with colour televisions (TVs) at Rs 6,000 apiece, churned out by manufacturing units with capacities equal to the entire TV industry in India, what creativity can you invoke to do battle?
  Supporters of skill and precision would cite Biotique, a brand that has carved a sizeable niche in personal care, without spending a rupee on advertising, the chief weapon of both the multinational and the local biggies in the fray. Remember, personal care is one of the ten biggest adspending categories.
  How should Prem walk this razor's edge? Does he have a choice? If he buys Soz, yes. He can juggle between his own Bluehill brands and Soz, hopefully at different price points. If Rago doesn't sell Soz to Prem, he is in a worse soup. He is committed to a category that is alien to Indian culinary practices. Even though 73% of families here are now nuclear, and there are more double-income households than before, the percentage of housewives who would give up the satisfaction of cooking a meal for the family is low indeed. Prem is yet to set up his distribution network. And in a low interest, low involvement category like ready-to-cook foods, his brand name Tom Yum would carry no resonance, unlike global brands like Nike or Coke. Prem's best chance is in pursuing Rago to part with Soz... and letting it thrive alongside his own. Remember, coexistence or no existence.

Don't Buy Blindly

G.BALAJI is professor of operations management at the Management Development Institute, Gurgaon.

  Acquiring incumbent firms is a valid entry strategy. The process of acquiring a firm, hostile or friendly, involves a bargaining phase where both players seek to maximise payoffs. In that sense, Rago's revision of the bid value seems to be a rational tactic. It would also not be surprising if it introduced another prospective buyer to increase bargaining strength, since at this point of time Bluehill has not started talks with alternative incumbents to strengthen its bargaining position.
  Although Prem's poser seemed to be on the bargaining process, Atul raises other issues. His discourse delves deep into fundamental aspects of conventional management wisdom. From public policy analysis, to agency problems in management of multinationals, to entry strategies, Atul convincingly argues for axiomatic changes in attitudes and actions.
  Atul's theses that "competition causes performance" and "destroying competition stunts markets" are fairly well-developed propositions in public policy analysis that economists and policy makers are sensitive to. The purchase of Thums Up by Coca-Cola brought this issue into sharper focus. Observers have pointed out the inadequacy of prevailing anti-trust laws in India. Whether Thums Up-Coke is comparable with Rago-Bluehill needs to be analysed. Prem's dilemma about whether to let Soz live or not clearly indicates that Atul's advice is being seriously considered. And this is, indeed, encouraging.
Reduced Incentives to innovate are detrimental to market growth as will as competence development within the company 
 While the erstwhile Monopolies & Restrictive Trade Practices Act was diluted earlier this decade, legislation that replaces it is yet to be passed. One can hardly disagree with Atul when he cites examples like airlines, ice-cream etc, to substantiate his theses. However, where Atul departs from conventional thought is in the application of these concepts to strategic management in general and entry strategies in particular.
  This is his argument: for firms planning to enter a market, takeovers have to be evaluated not only from the benefits obtained from readymade production facilities and a well-oiled supply chain, but also the long-term costs the entering firm may need to pay that arise out of a relatively "easy" entry into the market.
