  These costs could include the sub-optimalities arising out of a potential reduction in rivalry. These sub-optimalities arise out of the fewer incentives to innovate. This in the long run is detrimental to market growth as well as competence development within the firm. The negative externalities that arise from an aggressive and, perhaps, insensitive entrant, Atul hypothesises, is greater than the operational and strategic benefits he would acquire. This proposition, from a theoretical viewpoint, would definitely require further examination.
   Businessmen are usually sensitive to these points while planning strategies. However, Atul's plea is directed at CEOs of MNCs who, with deep pockets and an urge to quickly show results, tend to look at the "buy" option as an expedient solution, ignoring these negative externalities.
  This is the single most important message in his discourse.
  The balanced score card used to define a CEO's performance in MNC subsidiaries in less-developed markets may have to be incorporated. The absence of adequate legal mechanisms in India to block social welfare reducing takeovers puts an extra burden on the MNCs to ensure that CEOs are sensitive to these negative impacts. This is important to them not only from a strategic management viewpoint but also the extent to which an MNC is perceived to exhibit "good" behaviour worthy of a corporate citizen of the country. The experiences recounted by Atul definitely establish the prima facie for this prescription.
  Whether Thums Up-Coke is comparable with Rago-Blue hill needs to be analysed. Prem's dilemma about wheter to let Soz live or not clearly indicates that Atul's advice is being seriously considered. And  this is, indeed, encouraging.

Case Study 12


25 Oct 1999		 NEGLECTING THE CHILD
MEERA SETH
Rajdeep Kohli thought selling products to Children was something marketers like him knew all about. Then his 12-year-old son Pranav told him otherwise
  Rajdeep Kohli entered his house singing a Johnny Cash hit song, "These boots are made for walking..." with the same accent and an energetic shake of the hips. "Pannu baba," he called out cheerily, placing a shoe box on the dining table where his 12-year-old son Pranav sat playing solitaire.
  "What's this?" asked Pranav, touching the box. "The new, most spectacular shoe from Freedom, only for kids!" said Rajdeep, dramatically. "It rolled out today from the factory, I was dying to show it to you! Take a look..."   
  Pranav examined the shoe, turning it around. "Are these running shoes, sports shoes or just casuals?" he asked. "Sports shoes," said Rajdeep.
  "In that case, it should be turned upward at the toes, dad," said Pranav. "That gives great momentum."
  "These are for kids, Pannu," said Rajdeep. "Yeah, so?" asked Pranav. "Kids run and play sports, dad. You make that sound as if because it is for kids, it can be any old rubbish!"
  "Hey, that's not fair!" said Rajdeep, a little annoyed. "What I meant was... well, I guess you have a point. If these are sport shoes, then a little more needs to go into the design... Hey, you are good, man!"
  The shoes were visually terrific. Very dandy and very happening, just as his research team had advised. This was the first time Freedom was entering the sports shoes for kids' segment and the plan was to put out some visually appealing shoes. Except that Pranav was now saying: "It should also work, dad!"
  "You know, the minute we do that, the shoe becomes expensive and parents won't buy it," said Rajdeep.
  "If you are making shoes for children, where do parents come in?" asked Pranav.
  "It's like this, parents are the ones who finally decide what to buy and price is usually the consideration," explained Rajdeep.
  "For you grown-ups what is important is other grown-ups. Who cares what kids want!" said Pranav, now almost angry.
  "That's not true," said Rajdeep. "Companies that manufacture for kids, be it chewing gum or ice-cream, do care about what kids want and our entire marketing effort is aimed at appealing to the child."
"If that was so," said Pranav, "you would make shoes the way kids like them. If not, why pretend they are for kids? All grown-ups are the same."
   Sensing some distress, Rajdeep asked: "Something wrong today?" Pranav sat hunched up and made one more move on his solitaire board. "It's really sick, dad. I mean, it's really not fair," he said sounding very hurt and angry.
  As it happened, Pranav had enrolled for a four-day personality development workshop for 10-13-year-olds at India House. The letter said: "1.15 at the Marigold Room." Today, which was his third day, he and his friend, Kartik, had gone up to the Marigold Room, only to find a corporate lunch going on. There was no attendant in sight, no sign of their workshop. The two boys rushed down four floors to the reception, to find that the venue had been changed to the Jasmine Room.
  "I told the lady, India House should have at least bothered to put up a notice outside the Marigold Room about the changed venue," said Pranav to his dad. "You know what she said? You must not backchat with elders!' Now you see?" he asked. "Had it been a company event, for grown-ups, I am sure there would have been arrows and stickers all over the place telling grown-ups to turn right, then left and sorry for the inconvenience. But because it was for children, they probably felt, why bother!"
  "Coming from India House, that is downright rude, not to mention inefficient!" said Rajdeep.
  "It's got nothing to do with efficiency, dad, please!" said Pranav. "Kartik said to me: Children are for fun, not serious stuff.' And Kartik has a good reason to say that. You know that TV programme he took part in and won? It was a kids special. Guess what his team won? Exerciser cycles, saris, jewellery and a blood pressure monitor! See dad? Did any one of those organisers think of CD-Roms, Lego, BSA cycles or tennis racquets? No! Kartik got saris, dad, saris! It was the pits! You call that taking children seriously?" asked Pranav.
   Rajdeep heard his son out. Then he said: "That is rotten, I agree. But take it from me, there are many who do take children seriously. Companies are waking up to the truth that children are eager consumers and can influence their parents' buying decisions. We have a saying at Freedom: 'Work on kids today and, in turn, they will be devoted to you even at 60. How do you think Colgate did it?" he asked. "Did you know that of our vast population, over 35% are under 14? And what an impressionable lot! All of them chew gum, drink Coke and seek trendy shoes. They remember to tell mom 'Moov laga!' Why, you told me I should buy Electrolux's washing machine and get a Samsung TV free! That was the day I told Aniruddh Bari, my marketing head: You must work at moulding their psyches.' So, there are many marketers who really respect children and are making efforts to get reciprocal respect from them."
  "I am not sure," said Pranav, now warming up to a chat with his father. These were the chats he loved the most, the ones where Rajdeep discussed his work. "Yes, now they are making lots of products for children," continued Pranav. "But when I go out to buy the product, I don't see any of that respect. Like the workshop. India House even called you up to tell you how terrific it would be for me to enroll. All of us got excited. Then what happened? Just agreeing to buy is not enough. Do they respect me even after I have paid for it?" he asked.
  "I guess you're right," agreed Rajdeep. "But you know once he has sold the product to you, the marketer loses control over you, the buyer. Ideally, that shouldn't be, but in reality, thereafter, it is left to the retailer, or in this case, the service staff to ensure that the buyer continues to be delighted."
   "There are only two people who take children seriously, even respect them," said Pranav. "The boys and girls at McDonald's and Pizza Hut. You know how nicely they talk to us? As if we were grown-ups. Like this guy at Pizza Hut would come up to me and say Hi, you are back!' It makes me feel so good. Like he is being a buddy. I feel respected. Even when I give my order, he will listen to me attentively and say, may I repeat your order? If it had been any other restaurant, the man would look towards mom to check if what I ordered was okay by her. But not at Pizza Hut. Not at McDonald's. That's why we kids go there often, because they make us feel that we know what we want."
  Rajdeep was amazed at how much children noticed, imbibed and absorbed. Like most 12-year-olds, Pranav was attentive to his environment. "Some day Pannu, you will make a great marketer, I am sure," he now said. "So, listen to this, we say the act of consumption is not over after the product has been bought."
  "Doesn't apply to kids, dad," said Pranav. "During the cricket season, there were these chewing gums offering cricket cards. Chotu and I bought them using our pocket money, just to collect the cards. But very often we found the shopkeeper had given the best cards to the kids he knew and we were left with rubbish cards. Now, that's not fair! We are kids, dad, how can we go all over town finding the right shop?"
  Rajdeep could see Pranav's point. Was it enough to put cola and gum for kids on the shelf and believe you had served your target audience, wondered Rajdeep. There was a huge process between the retail and the child's stomach that played a role in the consumption process. Did marketers care to find out if their product left a happy after taste with the child consumer? "You know, in America, where Andy lives, they actually respect children," Pranav went on. "And you know, the kids are also quite bold and outspoken. No one tells them Don't backchat'!"
  Andy was Rajdeep's nephew who was visiting them. Pranav explained how they had been to a supermarket and Andy had said something clever. "Mom let us buy MilkyWay, the flavoured milk. She said, we should check the expiry date," narrated Pranav. "The pack said May 99' and the expiry message said, 'Use within 120 days of date of manufacture'. Andy told the manager: Why do you have the manufacturing date as a month when your expiry date is in days?' But he's allowed to speak his mind. In the US, they listen to kids. I wish I was like that." Rajdeep looked at his son's big innocent eyes and felt a certain sadness. What right did the economy have to unload so many goods on children when it did not empower them to demand? Worse, many of them were using children as a vehicle to market their products, communicating with them on family air time, because they knew that children imbibe advertising keenly. Even worse, many of them even proudly averred that children were the best advertisers for their products, even if it was shaving foam or mosquito repellent.
  He recalled what the head of a car company had once said: "The aspiration you see in a child's eyes speaks volumes for the brand attributes. You won't need anything else. Use children liberally. Put words in their mouths, build dreams in their hearts and put it all on TV if you want the profits rolling in."
  But what about sensitive advertising and marketing, wondered Rajdeep. It's splendid that you know that children imbibe advertising keenly. But that puts an added responsibility on you, the marketer, to be careful in your communication, specially when the target audience is children, he thought.
  Only last week Rajdeep had witnessed the power of advertising on his little boy. They had been to the club and after a swim the boys demanded an ice-cream. Rajdeep's seven-year-old, Chotu, his head barely reaching above the ice cream counter, hollered: "I will take an F-stick." Rajdeep's wife, Sarla was annoyed. "Not that one, it is not good, take Millie," she had said. "Why not?" protested Chotu.
  "Why not?" Rajdeep had echoed, surprised. "Because," she whispered, "it is indecent!"  
  "But he is just seven!" argued Rajdeep.
  "Yes," said Sarla, "but soon he'll be 14 and when he understands what else that could mean, he will think, these things are okay with mom'. No Raj, there has to be some limit." she said.
  But Chotu was adamant. As he looked towards his dad for help, Rajdeep said: "Chotu, we will let mom decide."   "No!" said an agitated Chotu. "I am going to eat the ice-cream, na? Shouldn't I decide? And who says it is a bad ice-cream? It comes on TV and all the big boys eat it, I have seen the ad! And big boys also drink Coke and so do I. So, what they can do, I can do too!"
  "Cool it, Chotu," said Andy, "you are not a big boy anyway."
  "Who says?" yelled Chotu bracing for a real fight. "I'm a big boy, my dad says so. He lets me choose his shaving cream!"
  Andy picked up Chotu on his back and said, "OK big guy, what shaving cream did you buy for dad?"
  Chotu, pleased with the attention said, "Gillette! No dad?" and broke into a toothy smile. "And what else?" asked Andy, deftly putting another stick of ice-cream into Chotu's hand and moving away from the battlefront. "I got mom her shampoo, Wah se Wow! No mama?" asked Chotu, reaching out for his mother's hair.
  That battle would rear its head again, Rajdeep knew. Marketers knew exactly how to mesmerise a child, because in business all was fair. Chotu, at seven, knew all the ads, frame by frame, lisping every ad along with the voice over -- even enacting many, like the Ice-Ice baby ad. This was a country which had no means to prevent damage to a child's psyche. Air time was not regulated, visual communication was not adequately censored. Usually, the realisation came after the damage was done. And when a child was like Pranav, the messy marketer had lost a consumer forever, he knew.
  So he said to Pranav: "Son, don't let advertising mislead you. If as a consumer you don't like what you get, if you don't feel respected, go right ahead and lodge your protest."
  "And who is going to hear?" asked Pranav. "When the consumer is a child, he can't protest. And what do you mean by respecting children'? When I go to a department store with you or mom, and I ask to see a Timex watch, the sales man says: 'See through the glass, that's enough' and he walks away. Would he say that to you? Children look good in ads, dad, not in real life. And in ads, they make kids say and do things which in real life they won't be caught dead doing. Remember that Indian ad for Cartoon Network which showed Indian kids making silly faces at the camera? Those kids looked like a bunch of idiots. Indian children are not like that.''  
  Rajdeep laughed. "Children are very effective in promoting a brand, Pannu," said Rajdeep. "When we use children in advertising, we know the brand is sold. Today, you have TV which is the greatest hypnotist, compelling children to choose Pepsodent because it is a dentist inside a toothpaste. You loved that ad, didn't you? You even told mom: 'You must get me Pepsodent'. Companies like that take the child consumer seriously."
  "What's the point?" asked Pranav. "You think because one kid goes on TV and says Use Pepsodent' all parents will let their kids buy Pepsodent? Never! Only if the parents believe in Pepsodent will they buy Pepsodent for the kid. Otherwise, we use what the parent uses. Why, you and mom use Colgate, so Chotu and I use Colgate too. C'mon dad!"
"That's different Pannu, mom is just wary of making a change," said Rajdeep. "See, it's like this, she has used Colgate all her life. And she makes most of the buying decisions, since she handles all that. Very often she places orders on the telephone. The point I am making is that the message has reached you, whether you buy Pepsodent or not. Isn't that correct? And that is what I mean, companies are aiming at children like you who watch TV two hours a day and they put their ads exactly on those programmes that kids are prone to watch. In short, they are out to make a good impression on children."
   Pranav grew thoughtful. "Yeahhh..." he said thoughtfully. "I remember one... L&T... remember? The one where three boys are travelling by train and each is boasting about his father? Then this kid points out to various things outside the window and says: My dad built those... and those and those...' " Then Pranav laughed. "You know until then I used to think Larsen & Toubro made tubelights!"
  Rajdeep joined in his son's laughter. "But you were also much younger then. Amazing how much you remember! And you noticed that ad because it had children in it. But that's the point! You, a child, noticed and remember the company. And companies strive to leave lasting impressions on young minds, because today's child is tomorrow's consumer."
  "Dad, you make it sound as if companies that sell to kids have kids on their mind all the time. I don't think so. Do they think of kids when they write to them?" asked Pranav.
  "I don't understand," said Rajdeep.
  "I will tell you," said Pranav. "Remember the time when Chotu wrote a joke for Bag O' Chips? They sent him a letter to say, we enjoyed your joke and all that and a surprise is enclosed for you. But there was no surprise. So, I wrote to the company and told them no surprise was enclosed. You know what they wrote back? You bought a pack of Bag O' Chips and that it did not contain the hologram stickers.' Can you imagine!" said Pranav sounding incredulous. 'So, we are sending you four hologram stickers...' I mean, that is a lie! All pretense! Mom said these were standard letters. Chotu was so annoyed. He said: 'God, man, these guys have got it all wrong!'
  '' Right! thought Rajdeep, they have got it all wrong! He now looked at his son with renewed interest. What idiots these marketers are, he thought. It is not enough to simply manufacture products for children if you are not going to see them through to their consumption. When you are targeting children you have to go all the way and ensure the quality of your targeting is 100%, in your dealings, in your responses, in your promotions, in your point-of-sale material... all that should be borne in mind is that it is the child you are addressing.
  From product design to product presentation to communication, to the way we feature a child in an ad, it must be linked to the fact that he is a serious target segment. Why don't they remember that children imbibe every motion, every act from the brand, and hence, are likely to misinterpret even body language? Do these marketers go to the retailers, watch them serve children, talk to children and find out what makes them unhappy about a product?
  These advertisers had just about got the mailing address right through their targeting. Having reached the consumer's doorstep, they were fumbling with their parcels. Some left it to innocuous middlemen to deliver the goods, some appointed bored grown-ups in the garb of service staff to unwrap the goods, while some didn't even bother if the child liked what they had sent, whether the child was delighted, whether the child was likely to come back for more.

  THE ADULT IN EVERY CHILD

  ANAND HALVE is a member of chlorophyll, a Mumbai-based brand and communications consultancy.
  The case raises several issues about marketing and communicating with children. Let me focus on the key issues, but first a note of explanation: Throughout the piece, I will use the definition of 'kids' as eight to 14-year-olds. (I believe that from 15, they are proto-adults, at least in larger cities.)
  If children are being considered a consumer segment in their own right, then all guidelines that apply to marketing to any segment should apply. Having said that, though, it is necessary to look at what makes this segment different from other segments. These differences exist because of the ways in which children process information and the weightages they assign to different variables. These are likely to be different from the ways in which adults act. Let's consider some of these aspects:
  Reference set: The first thing to appreciate about marketing to kids is that they hate being called kids! (There is some evidence that after the age of six or seven there is a significant change in the way they look at advertisements.) Therefore, the reference group that eight to 14-year-olds look at is not their peer group but their older siblings i.e. the next higher age band; perhaps, 16 to 19-year-olds. Enough signs of this are visible in 11-year-old girls trying on their older sister's lipstick, tottering about on mummy's stilettos and dressing up for parties, or in 12-year-old boys wearing jeans after school, talking cool' or looking in the mirror for the first hair on the chin. This desire to act grown-up and be accepted as such is accompanied by an acute consciousness of their inadequacy in terms of experience and knowledge. They are, therefore, sensitive to, and resentful about, any cues that suggest that they are just children. This is an underlying concern at all times. Marketers need to be aware of this in developing any aspect of the marketing mix.
Kids resent cues which suggest that they're just that--kids. Marketers need to be aware of this in developing any aspect of the marketing mix
 Product evaluation: The first point at which children get evidence of whether they are being taken seriously is in the product design itself. What they are looking for is " the same thing adults get, but in my size". Compromises on any features are reminders of a reality they wish to avoid. This explains Pranav's reaction to the design limitations of the sports shoes. But there is one more aspect of children as consumers which needs to be understood. This is the nature of their reactions . Rather than the continuum of reactions of adults which stretch from poor to so-so to satisfactory to good to wonderful, children tend to have polarised reactions which flip between fantastic' and the pits'. This is an important thing to note, because it underlines the fact that in addressing children as consumers you can win -- or lose -- only by a knock-out; there are no 'winners on points'. If you lose once you won't get a second chance.
   Service: The child's reaction to the absence of signs at India House is an indicator of another way in which children wish to be acknowledged. As in the case of adults, children too expect service which is congruent with the situation. So, if one expects to be left alone to browse in a bookstore and fawned upon at a restaurant, so do kids. The disappointment or dissatisfaction with the cricket cards is not, to my mind, an issue of dealing with children but about how to manage such schemes.
  Price: While this is not an issue specifically raised in the case, I think it is important to note that pricing has even more importance in the case of products bought by children (compared to those bought for them by their parents). This is because the sense of limited funds to satisfy several wants is higher in a segment that does not have its own earning' and depends on others to provide these funds.
  Content of advertising: The issue of what is suitable content for messages targeting children is a matter of concern across the world -- from whether the massacre at Columbine High could have been avoided if there were no Quake and Doom computer games to rating systems for movies. The only comment I would make on Sarla's reaction to Pranav's choice of ice-cream is: "What did you expect?" After all the commercial appears on prime time TV. If she finds it offensive (as I do), the best course is to exercise her right as a consumer and lodge a protest with the makers of the commercial. One other point regarding communicating with children -- they smell a fake a mile away. If anything rings false, children will notice it.
  Influence on adult product choices: We have seen a growing role played by children in decisions related to adult' products in recent years. I believe there are several reasons for this. One, the relationship between children and their parents is more equal than in the past, and children's views are not dismissed out of hand. Two, given the keen interest with which they imbibe the content of messages they are, often, better informed (brand MNO has real fruit pieces or brand DEF TV has auto-colour balance). And finally, I suspect that many parents indulge the child by accepting his or her brand choice out of a sense of guilt at not giving them enough attention.
  To sum up, I would say that the key issues in marketing to children are no different than in marketing to any consumer segment. We need to remember that if the Cadbury Dairy Milk ad remind us that there is a child in every adult, this case reminds us that there is an adult in every Pannu and Chhotu.

  GO EASY ON THE CHILD

  PRATIK BASU is CEO  of Buena Vista Television India--the Walt Disney Company's joint venture in India.

  There is no denying the strong logic in Pranav's argument that the majority of marketers in India do not fully understand, or respect, kids' needs and aspirations as an increasingly knowledgeable, economically decisive and forcefully discriminating consumer group.
  A worldwide study, some years ago, placed the Indian child among the lowest in the stages of evolution, placing a 13-14-year-old Indian child and a 7-8-year-old American child at the same level. 
  The reasons: the Indian child's limited exposure to, and consumption of, any form of entertainment and the non-working status of most mothers, which led to the protective and closely monitored upbringing of their progeny.
    Today, the likes of Pranav are exposed to more hours of TV programming in one day than Rajdeep had as a child in 5 months. The impact of satellite TV on the psyche of the Indian child and its moulding of him into a discerning, knowledgeable individual on a par with his age counterpart in any part of the world cannot be underestimated. With more and more mothers working, the kid's emotional dependence on his parents has also diminished. With access to worldwide lifestyles, news, technology and products at the click of a button, Pranav cannot but be smart, aware, intelligent, eager and true to his generation. And, therefore, demanding of respect as a consumer.
In our rush to tap another market, we should not thrust the child into the role of a consumer.
  Having said that, I feel that catapulting the Indian child to the level of an economically influential consumer group presents a somewhat one-dimensional profile. Smart, knowledgeable and information retentive, yes, but equally, materialistic, deal-seeking, self-centred and indulgent. That's not necessarily true. On the contrary, it perpetuates an image that we, as parents, marketers and media-men have ourselves created.
 For parents, with their accelerated lifestyles, over-indulging children, materially, may have been a way to compensate for the lack of quality time with them. For marketers, and admen, kids, like puppies, may have served as ornamental accessories to attract attention in a cluttered market, irrespective of the relevance of the product category -- what I call the "creative bail-out". And mediamen, in their urgency to attain TRPs, may have ignored that, over four-fifths of TV-owning homes are still "one- set households." Kids watch the same programmes (and ads) that their parents do, encouraging marketers to adopt a "catch-all" strategy that neither differentiates between target groups nor questions the relevance of a creative execution. In our rush to tap into another potentially-rewarding market segment, we should not deprive the Indian child of his innocence by thrusting him into the role of a consumer. This is not marketing sacrilege. 
  Most TV-advanced nations have broadcast policies that regulate programmes and ads beamed at times when there is likely to be a large kid audience. 
  Most developed nations have a code for ads targeted at children. I quote:
"No ad is allowed which leads children to believe that if they do not own the product advertised they will be inferior in some way to other kids or will be ridiculed for not owning it."
  "In an ad offering a free gift, a premium or a competition for children, the main emphasis of the advertisement must be on the product with which the offer is associated".
  There are even codes for children featured in ads: "Situations in which children are to be seen in ads should be carefully considered from the point of view of safety...children should not appear unattended in street scenes... should be seen following traffic rules...should not be climbing high places..."
  Let's try and remember there is more to Pranav than his pocket money and his pester power.
Designed by: Epsilon Systems - SQ-80, Hauz Khas Appts, New Delhi-16. Phone: 685-3301, 685-0878, 98100-06431

Case Study 13


22 Nov 1999		 DEALING WITH FAMILY VALUES.
MEERA SETH

Shirish Deol disagrees with Lata Girdharilal's bid to take over Texoll, But does a professional manager's opinion count for anything in a family-run firm?
  Shirish Deol studied the drooping figure of Gautam Tolani who stood before him. At 28, Gautam, a director of the Tolani Group (TG), had tried hard to don the mantle of director with conviction, periodically wearing his Texas University tie to drive home his academic proficiency. 
  But today, everything had failed him. At the restructuring meeting six months ago, on the advice of Bradley Byars, their management consultants, TG had consented to exit the cables business. Yet, six months later, Lala Girdharilal Tolani, the owner of TG and Gautam's father, was suggesting they should acquire Texoll Cables. Early in the week, an executive committee -- chaired by Shirish, the CEO of the group -- had vehemently resisted this, but Lala Girdharilal was set on it.
  Shirish had joined TG a year ago to spearhead the Rs 1,800-crore business. He was reluctant to work with a family-managed group (FMG), fearing interference and restricted independence. But it was Anup Dey, the partner of Bradley Byars, who had allayed his fears. "This is no ordinary group. Besides, they are committed to their plans and promises," he had said.
  The last year was devoted to the restructuring plan -- hiving off unviable businesses, restructuring capital, the organisation structure and hiring staff. All was hunky-dory, until the reversal of the decision to sell the cables business, Meful Cables. But what bothered him the most was the desire to acquire Texoll.
  "Acquiring a business like Texoll is tricky," said Shirish. "Texoll is a successful firm, and it comes with plans and targets which have to be met in order to sustain its equity. We are not just buying assets, but a going business and its markets. Therefore, if we buy Texoll, we have to create added value to substantiate our decision to acquire and merge it into our business," argued Shirish.
  The senior Tolani had just nodded. Then as the board waited reverentially for his words, he said: "I understand. But everything will fall in place. You don't worry, just go ahead as we have discussed..."
  But Shirish persisted. "Acquisitions are doubly expensive," he said. "We have to start from scratch, building a new identity, new culture, integrate the workforce, rationalise markets... And while we are doing all this, we also have to run our existing businesses as a more successful one. We will have to prove that we have become better. And better is not simply the increased sales that is expected anyway, but an increase in performance over and above what is built into our value. It is like starting a new business. With all the stress and strain of restructuring, can we afford all this?" asked Shirish.
  "These are small issues," said the senior Tolani. "We have the wherewithal to manage all that."
  "But what about the monetary resources?" asked Gautam. "Wouldn't it come from our existing resources? Won't it strain our current plans? Won't we be diverting monies which would have gone into other businesses?" 
 "Do we know if such a diversion of resources will benefit the original business, Meful?" asked Shirish. "Or the new business, Texoll, or the combined business? Or even the overall economics of the restructuring plan? Do we know anything at all for the Rs 1.5 crore we are paying?" At that point, the meeting had broken up for a meeting among the family directors.  And now, the proposal was a decision. What transpired at the meeting was difficult to say, but looking at a despondent Gautam, Shirish knew the young man had fought hard. He himself felt a certain frustration. How was he, the CEO, to manage a decision he was not party to?
  Shirish met Anup that noon. They were to discuss the shortlist of candidates for the chemical business' new R&D unit. "What's the latest on Texoll?" asked Anup.
  "Girdharilal is going ahead," said Shirish. "He's convinced TG needs it." "And you?" asked Anup. "You don't appear convinced."
  "I am concerned about the way the group is moving on this plan," said Shirish. "You tell me they have made quantum changes in their strategic thinking. But Girdharilal's control is still very strong. The approach and mindset do not appear to have changed much."
  "But don't judge the situation too harshly," said Anup. "The family's involvement will continue the way it does, for a while. Girdharilal is clear about consensus management but this instance may be different since he seems to have a viewpoint on the acquisition. I have been working with them closely for three years now and I know that he realises he has to change some of the things they are doing. The pressure on them from the economic environment is high. They are viewing the competitive pulls rationally and are making serious attempts to adapt."
  "The change I am talking about is a complete change in the approach to business and thinking," said Shirish. "The whole attitude. Change, not because there is competitive pressure. Change, not because of internal pressure, but because your vision changes. That is a different quality of change," said Shirish. "Of course that is happening," said Anup. "You can see it all round you. So many FMGs are now initiating the change process..."
  "But look at the smaller groups. Have they changed?" asked Shirish.
  Anup, who had been working closely with a number of FMGs, could see what Shirish was getting at. "Change in the minds of owners are of two kinds, and fear is the common denominator," he said. "There are hard changes and soft changes. Take MNC-driven compensation practices. Tolani was reluctant to increase salaries. Now that is a hard issue. The world typically sees that alone and makes a judgement on the softer issues. For instance, Tolani's reluctance to pay generously might foster the impression that he does not want to yield control. The correlation is not that simple," said Anup.
    But Shirish knew how hard Tolani was on increments. "If he has taken the decision to hire from MNCs, he must realise that the expectations of these managers are not going to change," said Shirish.
  "Just as you expect owners to evolve, Tolani as the owner also has an expectation from professional managers," said Anup. "Till the managers come of age and have the ability to lead the business, they have to learn to manage the expectations of owners."
  For Shirish, who came from an Indian MNC, the learning he brought with him was that the success of a company can usually be attributed to the quality and empowerment of its managers.
   "But how many CEOs can be true entrepreneurs?" asked Anup. "Do you have the same attitude to risk that Tolani has an owner? Are you willing to assume ownership for the risks you take? Or do you just want to be an employee-CEO, where you come with a platter of options, backed by some complex number of tables that point to a financial decision, with the ability to implement it? Does your heart feel the anxiety of possible failure? You know the attitudes here are very different. You could be a top-drawer manager from the world's best management school. But are you a top-drawer entrepreneur?
   Is your attitude to risk as healthy and dependable as the owner's? When you are the CEO of an FMG, there is a tremendous entrepreneurial style and risk-taking ability that is needed, the decision-making capability needs to be highly entrepreneurial. Do you come with that training? No MNC, however successful, can impart that training and that is the risk these owners are taking when they hand over charge to a professional manager, can't you see?"
  "But do they also empower to that extent?" asked Shirish. "Risk-taking goes with empowerment."
  "Empowerment is not the issue," said Anup. "Where Indian businesses fail is in creating processes that manage empowerment. Why, even in MNCs, where is empowerment absolute? There are checks and balances just as in FMGs. Except when FMGs do that, you wonder if there is an anxiety to hand over control. You have business development managers in Nestle, in ITC, in Levers, but is control absolute? Isn't there a process that governs empowerment? That's what FMGs do too. Except, that their processes are not structured.
  "Let us understand," continued Anup, "Tolani as the owner would like to hand over management to a team of professionals, given some safety nets: if his money is safe, if it grows above the average rate of return and three, he derives social value from that. If he is sure of these three things, why should he or any other owner mind letting go?"
  Anup did not see much difference between FMGs and MNCs in the way their decisions were taken. "Consider the subsidiary-parent company relationships of MNCs," he said. "Don't they try and assess the risk of the subsidiary's decisions, just like the owner in an FMG does? What is the difference? Parent owns stake, parent wants to assess risk, parent denies permission to take certain decisions... and that is what the owner in an FMG also does. There you have parent-owner and managers, here you have Indian owner and his mangers." 
  "The difference being that when you talk of an FMG, there is an underlying pessimism surrounding their structures and processes," said Shirish.
  "That is because, when you talk of decision making in an owner-managed company or an FMG, it is typically a single-man decision," pointed out Anup. "In MNCs, the decision-making flows from a group of men -- the board. It is more democratic."
  "MNCs also have their processes standardised across countries,'' retorted Shirish, "Their ability to manage business with the correct processes and efficiency is known, documented." 
  "Those businesses are process-led. Thus, MNC dictates come out of rule books, in FMGs, they come from the owner's head."   
  "You need to differentiate between owner-managed and family-managed companies," said Anup. "Take the example of Sundram Fasteners -- Suresh Krishna is the owner and the CEO, and he is doing a fabulous job. For that matter Bosch, Perfetti, Seagram are all owner-managed companies. Microsoft is an owner-managed firm. The problem arises when the company is managed by a family, a group of people with multiple egos. In owner-managed companies, you don't have the complexity of multiple egos, no clash of opinions."
   Shirish wouldn't agree. At TG, there was a clash between the owners and the professional team. "Then what am I doing there?" he asked Anup. "You have hired a CEO, now allow him to run the business!"
 Which you are doing for the better part, Shirish," said Anup. "Lala Girdharilal has gone along with most of your decisions. Yes, this once he has vetoed you completely. But that can happen anywhere! Girdharilal is trying to drive shareholder value, very much like the managing director of a widely-held company. The only difference is that the owner also has a personal stake in the business by virtue of his shareholding.
  "So, in effect, he is the manager as well as the shareholder. At one level, he is trying to maximise his shareholding, and at another he is also managing the shareholding in order to maximise the same value. Now even if Tolani hands over the management to a professional team, won't his interest in maximising his shareholding value continue? So it isn't all that different from working in an Indian subsidiary of a foreign firm."
  "The difference," said Shirish, "lies in the interference in the management process. Of course, one million GE shareholders cannot interfere on a daily basis. At best, a few can yell at an AGM. But in a closely-held company, they can interfere with the brand of tea that is served to interfering with a decision like an acquisition at the cost of the future value to the business."
  "And the difference also lies in the strength of the processes that go behind such a decision," continued Shirish. "Therefore, to what extent are FMGs actually plotting the real potential of the business? Do they assess, How much can I really be worth? Is what I am doing optimum?' This is what professionally-managed, widely-held companies are doing. Because they do these exercises, they know the gap between where they are and where they aspire to be and they work at bridging this gap. Whereas in FMGs, there is a lack of academic skills, and given that succession is hereditary, the person at the helm of affairs is not necessarily a direction setter.
  "Therefore, do I have the confidence that management debates are based on scientific management principles, backed by academic knowledge or simply a function of an emotional tug-of-war? Fundamentally, that is what contributes to the dilemmas and dichotomies that present themselves in FMGs. For instance, when there is an opportunity to buy out a business, there are two factions operating, one saying yes, one saying no. In such a situation, you the CEO are caught in a family war or power struggle.
 "When I am faced with such a situation, I do not know how to lead that company. In fact, am I leading or arbitrating? Remember I have to be in the good books of both factions. Who is the direction setter here? Me? No, I am the direction suggester, I provide a menu of options, I provide a swot analysis of every suggestion, then I have to accept the final decision.
  What finally comes to be is the owner's vision, which need not be optimal. The decisions Lala Girdharilal is trying to impose are not suited to the group's growth. My desire is to maximise shareholder value, and that cannot happen if his decision prevails."
  Anup could see Shirish's agony. But he was seeing miles ahead. "Don't forget that Lalaji's objective is also the same as yours -- maximise value," he said.
   "But does he have a gameplan to do that?" asked Shirish.
  "He may not have what you are expecting to find. But what he has is entrepreneurial instinct, which you can never eliminate from a total business management process. That's where the mismatch is -- entrepreneurial instinct versus process-controlled intelligence. Where the chief executive has both, chances are he will fit extremely well. And the ability to build the bridge across these two sets of competencies is what defines success. An entrepreneur like Tolani might sweep aside your laboriously crafted sheets of numbers and columns. A professional manager says, 'Look, I have done this, DCF calculations, swot analysis, weighted average and so on... I spent 12 days on it...' But Lalaji says, 'Beta, yeh sab chhodo, tumhara dil kya kehta hai?' What is your gut feel? Lalaji operates on gut feel, which may seem unscientific. Mr Manager operates on scientific strategy and management school theories. As they say, one operates from his mastak, one from his pustak. Both have their merits. Both could be right. How do you build the bridge?''
  HE continueD: Now both decisions may have opportunities. But how is one to know which is right? Who knows? Look these are not insane people. It is just that you and I don't know what frenzied calculations, if at all, happen in their mind. But something is happening. When Lalaji says 'Buy Texoll', remember those are his one and a half crores. His money. So he is not doing it for fun. Therefore, what should be the mix of instinct and analysis? I don't know. But the mix varies from situation to situation. You must know how to manage the mix. That should be the working relationship between the owner and the manager and the bridge is the understanding of how both variables work. When these two things happen, you have a happy marriage.''
  He cited an example from his experience. "You know the automobile industry has been performing disastrously. But take Hero Honda. It grew by an astonishing 45% in the six months over the previous year, whereas the industry figures for growth are nowhere near that. Hero Honda is an FMG by definition, run by a professional manager. Take tractors. The entire industry grew by 4%, Mahindra & Mahindra grew by 16%. If you go through these examples you will find all kinds of relationships and management issues at play."
  "Are you saying that these were due to professional management?" asked Shirish. "Or that the professionals did not make any difference because the instinctive management processes are so well tuned in at the top?"
  "It is, I would say, the terrific synergy of native instinct and modern intelligence," said Anup. "The processes of arriving at that success is different in both places. In one case, the owner is a hands-on manager, in the other case, he is not. Both companies have defied industry norms with their success. In one case, the owner and the manager have learnt to live together and synergise their respective traits. In the other, it is the case of empowerment and letting the manager lead. There are so many factors at play, factors of professional management and factors of instinctive management. Which one led to what cannot be pinpointed, but what can be said with confidence is, both managed the diverse issues capably. To advantage."

  BLENDING EAST WITH WEST


  MALA Sinha is a lecturer at the Faculty of Management Studies, Delhi University. She is also a trainer and a consultant and draws from Vedanta, Zen and Taoism to develop culturally-relevant practices.

 Organisations need to take strategic decisions to survive business pressures and maximise value. The way strategic decisions are taken in organisations, and the processes involved in gaining support for the same, is important. TG, in a bid to professionalise, has hired Shirish as a CEO, who after a year finds that the strategic decisions are getting arbitrary.
 What are strategic decisions? These are long-term plans to maximise value, and value is defined by the owner's vision of where he wants the business to go. Professionals like Shirish would also have the best interest of the company in mind as it is part of management ethics they acquire through education.
   So, where is the problem? It lies in the two different paradigms that are being used to process knowledge to arrive at a decision. Shirish is operating from the logical positivist, scientific rational paradigm that was heralded as enlightenment and the age of reason in Europe, and is the basis of innumerable scientific marvels the world has seen. This paradigm acknowledges only empirical data that can be subjected to the scrutiny of scientific methodology and can be categorised, compared and measured. Reality comprises system of abstract ideas and concepts, symbols arranged in linear sequential structures before it can be assimilated. Fritzof Capra, a physicist, calls it relative knowledge as opposed to absolute knowledge.
 Shirish needs to understand that his operating paradism is not helping him in the present situation and he must look for a new way 
  Eastern philosophies like Vedanta, Zen and Taoism accept knowledge that is intuitive and directly experienced - a more complete representation of knowledge. This includes the lower order empirical apara vidya amenable to scientific enquiry and the higher order knowledge para vidya that can be consciously realised through methods of inquiry like intuition, meditation, insight and indirect symbolism. It is believed that knowledge makes quantum leaps if it transcends the lower empirical mode. The cause of Shirish's consternation is that Giridharilal's intuitive mode of decision-making is not compatible with the postulates of the scientific paradigm.
   According to the Vedanta, institutional consciousness, anubhava, wisdom acquired through experience, is a valid source of knowledge. Being the owner, Girdharilal is invested as a complete person in the business - physically, mentally and spiritually. He is likely to go beyond the empirical and draw greatly from anubhava, while taking decisions. This Shirish cannot understand.
 The scientific paradigm of knowledge is also neutral and value-free, hence, it informs more than it transforms. Thus, we find the CEO wants to implement changes at TG but is not open to change himself. He wants TG to become a learning organisation but is he a learning CEO? Girdharilal has agreed to pay huge MNC salaries, a major change in attitude, but Shirish is unwilling to even look at Girdharilal's decisions. He is worried about the undemocratic way in which the decision has been taken, even though Western research has conclusively shown that decisions that are arrived at through group consensus are not always the best and that individuals are often capable of superior problem-solving and decision-making. The case clearly juxtaposes the elitism of modern, western, scientific education with the eastern, traditional, familial and indigenous.
   The Vedanta has a number of lessons for making learning transformational. The first step is to rid the self of certain mental and emotional attributes that act as barriers to knowledge: viparaya. These are raga - desire to own more power even at the cost of usurping it from others; dvesha - anger and contempt; moha - attachment to own viewpoint as being superior and avidya - ignorance. The TG CEO displays ignorance of higher forms of knowledge. Shirish displays egotism and attachment to his paradigm which makes him rigid.
   So, what should Shirish do? He is experiencing stress and this will jeopardise his effectiveness as the CEO of the company. Shirish first needs to realise that his operating paradigm is not helping him in the present situation and he must look for a new way.
   The Vedanta prescribes sravana, manana, and nidhidhyasana, a three-stage sequential method of acquiring knowledge. Sravana means to listen or absorb information without judging or evaluating it. If Shirish considers Girdharilal as less than himself, he will find it difficult to listen. Manana is contemplation or application of reason and logic to remove doubts. This is Shirish's strength. The last stage, nidhidhyasana, comes after all intellectual doubts have subsided, a stillness through meditation results in a direct experience of understanding and realisation.
   If the CEO incorporates the more holistic eastern paradigm of knowledge, which includes scientific enquiry, he may actually find Lala Girdharilal's decision reasonable. Even if he discovers the contrary, the realisation will be accompanied by calm and conviction and not the distress he is experiencing now.

UNDERSTAND THE ARGUMENT

N.S. Vijaya Kumar is chief coordinator, group chairman's office, at the Hyderabad-based Nagarjuna group .

   The root cause of Shirish's dissonance and discomfort seems to be that he does not see any well-structured process behind Girdharilal's decision-making. This is not surprising since Shirish's experience primarily has been with multinationals where processes are well-defined, well-structured and transparent. On the other hand, Girdharilal's decision-making process does not match what Shirish construes as the correct decision making process. Just because he doesn't see a process, he assumes that there is none.
 Apart from the factors that the consultant Anup mentions, in most family managed groups (FMGs), the CEO is vested with powers on strategic matters only after he has earned the trust of the owner-investor, especially if the CEO has come from an alien culture. What constitutes the basic elements of trust-building may vary from organisation to organisation, culture to culture. At Tolani group, rather than discount his non-FMG experience, Girdharilal would like to assess the CEO's capability in the new environment.
  But one thing is critical - the chemistry between the CEO and the owner-investor. If the chemistry works, trust builds faster. If it doesn't, it may take a string of successes before the owner begins to trust the CEO.
  One of the secrets of successful professional managers in family--owned firms is the ability to find a suitable method to get their point across 
  A second area that Anup seems to have overlooked is the manner in which Shirish is getting worked up over just one decision. The case mentions that the restructuring plan was going smoothly except for the reversal of the decision to sell off the cables business. Broadly, Girdharilal's decision to acquire Texoll's cable business is in consonance with his earlier decision not to exit the sector.
  The chief executive must actually spend more time trying to understand the owner's decision. Is there some aspect of the business that could have been overlooked by the consultant? Is Girdharilal worried about the timing of the decision? Does the owner have any strategic interest (other than emotional) in this decision? These and several other related questions need to be explored by Shirish to facilitate a true management process.
  Thirdly, Shirish does not seem to have looked at the true 'scientific' reasons for the Texoll decision. Shirish's job was to navigate the Rs 1,800-crore business, and he seems to be getting upset over the Rs 1.5 crore that is being paid to buy Texoll. He seems to be concerned about the overall economics of this operation and also feels left out of the decision-making process. The correct stance to take would be to assess the impact by conducting a thorough analysis that can then be used to support his argument.
   Fourthly, Shirish must use Gautam Tolani as a very effective bridge between himself and Lala Girdharilal. One of the secrets of successful professional managers in family-owned enterprises is the ability to find some suitable method to get a point of view across.
  If the manager is sensitive enough to perceive that an idea will be better accepted if it is seen as coming from someone whom the owner already trusts, then he must use that resource skillfully. In the present situation, there appears to be a fit case for such a move.
  Lastly, if even after all the above steps are initiated in the organisation, Shirish still feels strongly about the decision, it is too early for him to throw in the towel. Shirish must look at himself as a harbinger of change not only in the organisation but also in the way Lala Girdharilal looks at things. If, and only if, he does this can, Shirish claim to be a successful professional manager. 
Designed by: Epsilon Systems - SQ-80, Hauz Khas Appts, New Delhi-16. Phone: 685-3301, 685-0878, 98100-06431

Case Study 14


6 Dec 1999		 BREAKFAST: THE CEREAL KILLER
MEERA SETH

If rice and wheat are already core to indian eating, can cereals survive with a strict breakfast
  Aadesh Saxena looked with surprise at the spread on his house guest's breakfast plate. There were two chapatis, a bowl of vegetables, a cup of dahi and a boiled egg. "How can you eat this meal at 7 a.m.?" he asked Om Vasisht, a marketing consultant, and a close friend of the Saxenas. 
  Om looked down at his plate and said, "It is quite light, and very nourishing too!" Aadesh tucked in his napkin as his tray of cereal, fruits and eggs arrived. "You must eat cereal," he said. "So I am," said Om, pointing to the chapatis, "except bigger and rounder!"
  Aadesh who was the marketing head at Energee Foods, shook his head. "You obviously didn't notice these boxes of Healthee wheat, corn and rice flakes," he said. "At least when you are my guest you must eat what my company makes!"
  "Oh, I gave up milk six years ago," said Om. "In any case, I am sure there are enough people out there who are doing Healthee a lot more justice."
  And that is what Aadesh was not sure of. Energee Foods manufactured a range of ready-to-cook and eat foods and instant foods for breakfast, brunch, lunch, tea time and dinner, including instant dosas and idlis, baked beans, jams and canned cooked vegetables. Cereals was a growing market and was perceived as occupying a significant share of the breakfast table. Consequently, last year Energee added cereals to its portfolio.
   But research findings this year were far from encouraging. Healthee was being bought but not as often or as much as expected. Detailing parts of his research, he said to Om: "We found that convenience foods have a maximum chance of succeeding in the breakfast slot. Even there, for those who eat cereal it is not a total meal. They eat a bit with their milk, but that is to wash down the milk. Kellogg's describe cereal as the complete meal, but what is it completing? The per unit consumption is not what we would prescribe for a full breakfast."
   "But that will be so," said Om, "because it can never replace the vast repertoire of breakfast foods we usually eat. You cannot change the eating habits of a people because it is governed by the climate, the culture, what grows, the lifestyles, the affordability... everything."
  Om, in his research, had found that when it comes to food and eating, there were a variety of segments -- varying from time as the limiting factor, to nutrition, convenience and taste. "Let's look at different kinds of consumers, look at each set's breakfast or eating habits. For example, how many eat breakfast at 7 a.m.? For example, the housewife who is busy with getting the kids. At that point will cornflakes satisfy her? Or is she also reaching for a paratha with pickle? The issue is not what you want them to eat Aadesh, the issue is what are they eating anyway? Don't try and only change habits. Stay with current habits and fit your products into those slots. Simultaneously evolve their habits and allow your product range to evolve therefrom."
  "Tell me, " said Om, "Did you adopt one single position? A single position works, you know."
  "What position?" asked Aadesh. "Within breakfast I adopted the convenience slot. Kellogg's has occupied the breakfast slot, and that is what cereals are coming to be seen for. It has already called it a breakfast food; naturally I am also calling it a breakfast food. But it didn't exactly set the Yamuna on fire. The bottomline is: I don't get a good feeling about our sales. Dealers are buying, but not enthusiastically, consumers are buying, but there is something to consumer attitudes and behaviour. Our research shows that the number of families buying are as per our expectations, but not their volumes. I feel there is something more which we haven't identified."
  "Okay, let's look at your positioning then," suggested Om. "The whole idea of positioning cereal as a breakfast food, as a complement or a supplement to the traditional Indian diet, was predominant in your mind. Now the mix that an average Indian eats is most complete in most contexts already. So the niche that you have selected is a narrow one which is where the problem comes in. Why do you think it is a breakfast food?"
   "Okay, at that hour people are in a rush to get to office or school," explained Aadesh. "So we said it can be quickly had. Besides, it is light and nutritious and healthy. We discovered that the rush segment doesn't eat anything anyway, and the non-rushed segment already has a full meal, for whom this breakfast slot is not a valid slot at all."
  "Even the rush segment," continued Aadesh, "while it does consume cereal, and claim it is a healthy diet, does not eat a full cereal breakfast. The add-ons vary from a paratha roll which is eaten on the way to the bus stop or in the car, to a cheese sandwich or a bowl of fruit.
  I also feel, to a large extent that time defines what they will eat. I know one family which has always eaten Champion oats for breakfast 'because it is filling', and suffered the resistance of the kids or the vast quantities of sugar that goes into dressing up the oats.
These days they add generous quantities of Frosties or Chocos to the porridge which sells better with the kids. So on days when the kids get up late, the oats is eliminated and it's just Chocos and milk."
  "You didn't realise that in India it is used as an additive to milk,'' said Om, "and, therefore, unwittingly pitted yourself against Bournvita and Horlicks! So, what then is the role of the cereal?" asked Om. "Breakfast? When I think about it, I don't think Kellogg's has positioned its cereal for the rush segment or for a state of rush. It positioned it as a supplement. Now who is it competing with? With the paratha or dosa, with Bournvita or Boost or Complan which is added to flavour the milk to push it down the throat easily. So where is the cereal landing up? Necessarily it is cornering itself into a position of insignificance.
 This means that if the cereal is not present on the table, it won't make such a big difference. It is incidental. Most families have a menu of options -- honey, Bournvita, oats, dalia, dosas, parathas -- all these have been conventional breakfast options. In such a basket, the cereal is an incidental come-and-go activity. By offering it as a nutritional element at breakfast or a complement to the existing breakfast fare, it is doomed to be a niche player in the basket. Do you see?"  
  "And that is because you have put it into the breakfast slot," said Om. "Therefore by definition, once it is 9 a.m., the box of cereal is of no relevance on the table; it is not visited until the next morning. It does not return to the dining table. That's what you have to apply your mind to. By restricting it to the breakfast table you are reducing its efficacy and utility for the rest of the day.
  "Healthee should seek to be a product which has free mobility on the table, across all meals," felt Om. "Healthee should be an anytime food, in the same way in which Maggi noodles can be had at breakfast, or at lunch or as a filler or at dinner with a variety of vegetables thrown in," suggested Om.
  "How is that tenable? " asked Aadesh. "When the Kellogg's of this world are swearing they are breakfast foods, how can I come in and say, 'Healthee is an anytime food?'"
  "A food is what you position it as," said Om. "Now look at Maggi's positioning. Fundamentally, it can be perceived as a meal-time food. Instead, it is an anytime food. It is two minutes away from satisfaction and convenience. If Maggi had positioned noodles like you have positioned Healthee, it would have ended up becoming another meal. But Nestl researched and found the evening slot as the key slot to take up a position. So it became a snack food. With time, overlaps happened and people started having it at other times, as a filler, as a distraction, as a meal-between-meals and it came to become an anytime food. So the two-minute concept was positioned more as an evening emergency snack for kids and then it caught on. From 'once-a-week-Maggi' it started happening thrice a week."
  Convenience, nutrition, health were just positions to take, felt Om. "And each of these has a place in every meal," said Om, "and you have to decide on the meal slot. For that you will need to look at eating habits at every meal. The day shifts in India because of the huge trading community. So, you have a heavy breakfast which is essentially the lunch fare. Very few people actually eat lunch regularly at a restaurant. Either they carry packed food or have food delivered to them or, like the traders, eat an early 10 a.m. lunch on which they subsist the whole day. For different professions it works differently. But it is the dinner hour that is mostly pegged to 7-7.30 p.m.
  "So, where are the slots where a snacky meal can come in," asked Om, "where convenience is required, where time is the issue, where the meal has to be light. These are the slots for your cereal to fit in. What I am saying is that you don't necessarily have to position Healthee as a breakfast food." Aadesh resisted that. "You can't change something so fundamental!" he said. "Let us look at what you have," persisted Om.
  "You came in with cornflakes because you believed the Indian habit was changing in that direction. Have you examined current eating habits?"
  "The point is, Healthee, or for that matter any cereal, while it is being bought, isn't becoming the core breakfast item on the table," said Aadesh. "Because there are any number of substitutes."  
  "I think there's another reason linked to the product that it's to be had with, namely, milk," said Om. "You can't, for instance, have cereal with dal, though I wonder why not! It's all a matter of serving and positioning it. Come to think of it, Maggi is essentially a commodity. You can have it the way the manufacturer prescribes or do your own thing, with Maggi as the core and garnishes of vegetables, minced meat or eggs.  
  "But as I said, the suggestion of the how to' has to come from you. Since it has to go with milk, there are any number of substitutes -- cocoa, Bournvita, Rooh Afza, chocolate or rose syrups, and even oats. Milk goes well with so many of them. And in any case your product is only a supplement."
  Om felt it was not just a question of what to eat'. It was also a question of when to eat' that went into the perspective of planning. "Take the case of Mother Dairy's Aloo Tikki," said Om. "It is a ready-to-eat tikki, can be had anytime. But unusually, as I have seen, people examine the pack, are amazed it is ready to eat, but usually put it back because there is no message which comes with the product which suggests when it can be eaten! Strange isn't it? An aloo tikki is staple Indian diet, but the minute it comes in a packaged form, if you don't suggest when to eat it, the impetus to buy and consume it is not there. I think you need to tell the woman that she needs a ready-to-serve emergency snack, you need to position yourself into her habits -- only then does she start viewing you with interest."
  "Or take Milkmaid condensed milk," continued Om. "It didn't move when it was sold as condensed milk, the consumer received no suggestions on how else it could be used. Since in its commodity form she had not much use for it, the purchase was very limited. But once the suggestions came, and mind you, these were suggestions that fitted themselves into the consumer's habits, the purchases shot up. The same thing happened with tomato puree. It is such a fundamental ingredient, but they all said tomato made easy'. But once they started talking about good-looking Rajma, smart-looking aloo mutter, the puree moved out of the store shelves into the kitchen."Talking of habits, Om suggested that Aadesh take a look at the new habits in food preparation, storage and eating. "The newest preoccupation today is microwave cooking," he said. "For some time, the woman rejected the microwave because she was convinced Indian food did not lend itself to microwave cooking methods. But when the heating attributes of microwave began to appear overwhelming, some of them purchased it. Then arose this notion that refrigerated food is not as tasty when it is reheated. True or not, there it is. But that little bit of instant ginger paste or tomato puree when added to my reheating process is perceived as refreshing the food! It's amazing, but it is in fact true. For the fragrances come alive and the dal is no more yesterday's leftover. And this is so common to Indian households -- this whole process of re-tadka. You have leftover dal, you quietly chop up some onion, fry it in ghee with ginger and garlic and throw it into the withering old dal and, presto, it comes alive.
  So what has happened? Tomato puree and ginger pastes became generic commodities which could be used to refresh food. Essentially they enabled the housewife to reposition the old dal with a few touches to its basic personality!"
  Fair enough, thought Aadesh. "But where does Healthee cereals come into this?" he asked.
  "You need to learn from the evolution of these products," said Om. "Thus, you can expand Healthee's market if you also address the 'when' issue. But few marketers are doing that. Everyone who is marketing cereal is harping on the same usage attributes: health, nutrition and breakfast. No one is suggesting the 'when' aspect, and that is what is required to grow the market. If the consumer does not eat cereal anyway, Healthee will never appear on her purchase list," said Om. "But if you can find innumerable situations in her life where the cereal can readily fit itself, she will buy the cereal for those other situations too and willy-nilly also have some of it occasionally at breakfast, because it is there anyway!"
   Om went back to his favorite example of Maggi noodles. "As a pasta it could have got stuck in the lunch mould, but Nestl deftly sidelined that and bravely said, 'Anytime'. And mind you, Maggi came 15 years ago, when the Indian was far from adventurous about food. Maggi's maximum consumption is between 3 p.m. and 4 p.m. which is typically the time when you are looking for an emergency snack. It is also the time when you have a madly hungry child on your hands, homework done and an appetite that is soaring and your good sense is preventing you from giving him a samosa or a series of dosas or biscuits because he has to eat dinner by 7 and you want him adequately hungry by then. So what do you give him? Maggi and milk!"
  Om was of the view that the new millennium was the era for change, for innovation, adaptation, the era for creating a revolution in worldwide food habits. "You have gone into the standard mould of putting the cereal in the breakfast basket," he said. "But the issue that emerges is, are you looking at the 'when' and the 'how' and the 'how-else'? It is for you to generate the 'how elses' if you want to grow the market. For, believe me, market share can be won only by those who can grow the market, not by the me-toos."
  As far as he could see, cereals were a commodity, like noodles and tomato puree. "It is for you to create the magic with new 'how tos," said Om. "If it becomes her core ingredient to innovate with, be sure your product has entered the kitchen of every housewife for good. Your cereal has to transcend to the level of becoming an ingredient in the housewife's recipe book. Leave it to her to innovate by showing her that your product is but an add-on for nutrition and health. For that is what a cereal is ultimately. But right now you and Kellogg's are simply saying 'eat breakfast with milk', thereby restricting usage. Naturally, away from breakfast and milk, the cereal will have no salience."
  The other issue, felt Om, was that food was being marketed and researched by men, not women. "This is a country where the woman is the predominant creator of food. She does it for a living!" he said. "When food companies leave the product research and adaptation to women, the market will grow. The way she re-tadkas the dal, or crumbles stale bread and chapatis to create a mesmerising upma, the way she alternates her rasam with lime once, tamarind the next time, tomatoes the third time... the way she stuffs capsicum with cheese rice... If you understand the poetry that food is, you will be able to write new verses."
  Om was confident that anybody entering the foods market must learn the 'how' and the 'when' and the 'how-else' aspects of food if they want to grow the market. Innovation was the key. "If you tap the actual usage of bread," said Om, "what share of it is used as plain bread vis-a-vis in other forms? The traditional form of bread is toasted and buttered. But in India you even have bread pakora, shahi tukra, bread upma... The innovation in breads over time is what is responsible for the acceptance of bread as a product in India. It is not an original Indian product, just as cornflakes and condensed milk are not. So, take your cue from bread if you want Healthee to grow," he said.
  As they got into their respective cars, Om said: " Remember Aadesh, no market share wars are won without growing the market and you can do that when you create new uses for the same product and for that you need to delve into the habits of people. In the beginning there is a fundamental habit of eating. Now go ahead and stimulate desire!"

GROW THE CATEGORY

MANU ANAND IS MANAGING DIRECTOR OF FRITO-LAY 

   Energee Foods seems to have built a solid product portfolio of ready-to-eat foods and has seen entry into breakfast cereals as a logical extension of its portfolio. In jumping into what looks like a large market, Aadesh does not appear to have thought about what his company is doing in this category. Clearly, there are natural fits in this extension, in terms of utilising infrastructure, selling and distribution systems and even manufacturing facilities. But thereafter this category is distinct from the other products that Energee Foods is into and needs to be looked at differently.  
 Given the eating habits of Indians, not surprisingly, the cereal category is small. Over time, Healthee's volumes can grow appreciably through category growth  or else it will be limited to fighting share wars with Kellogg's and other players. Category growth is possible only through a change in eating habits, either by positioning purely as a breakfast cereal for all age groups and trying and changing breakfast habits and/or positioning as an all-time food/snack to select segments and gaining a share of the stomach post-breakfast.
   To position Healthee as a breakfast cereal, Aadesh would have to work hard to change eating habits. As a category, breakfast cereals are alien to the Indian diet. In the West, breakfast cereals play an important role in balancing the diet and providing the necessary fibre and cereal content as the consumption of cereals in other meals is relatively low. In India, cereals are the staple food for all the meals  Oms observation that he is eating bigger and rounder cereals is valid. Most Indian breakfasts have their fair share of cereals  parathas, puris, chapatis, poha, vadas, idlis, dosas are consumed at breakfast by most middle-class households across the country. Wheat and rice remain an integral part of the meals as the day progresses and the relative percentage of cereal consumed during meals increases as you go down the socio-economic pyramid. Cereals are consumed in forms that we have been used to eating, which go well with dals, vegetables and non-vegetarian preparations. So, why does the Indian consumer need to eat more cereal?
Unless the size of the market is increased, energee will be limited to fighting share wars with other players for a share of the pie   
  Changing eating habits is a tough task, even when you have a strong argument to offer. Here the task will be tougher since the basic cereal consumption is already high. If they pursue this route, Healthee would have to appeal to the health conscious, more from the perspective of a low-calorie, low-fat way of consuming cereals at breakfast. They could also advocate it as a good accompaniment to milk and as a quick and healthy breakfast for those on the move, especially school-going children. This would be a slow process requiring considerable communication investment. Aadesh would need to evaluate the relative importance of Healthee in Energee Foods portfolio and assess its ability to sustain this kind of investment. Given the bigger players like Kelloggs in the marketplace, Aadesh may want to let them take the lead in this .
  The other option as suggested by Om is to take the consumption of cereals beyond breakfast  to make this a fun-to-eat category yet retain core nutritional values. As a result of the current position of the category, cereals are today seen only as a breakfast food  as serious eating from a nutritional perspective, with overtones of convenience. It isnt seen as a snack or as an option for a quick meal. There are clear opportunities available to someone who can come up with a differentiated product  a product with an appropriate taste and form to appeal to the target age groups and eaten with different accompaniments. The Indian consumer can be adventurous in trying new products, provided that the pricing is in line with their perceived value. Here it has many choices.
  One option would be to target cereals as an all-time consumption food/snack for children  a product that can be eaten at breakfast and can be carried in tiffins boxes and eaten dry, eaten with milk on return from school or play, and even as a light meal or meal substitute or with milk much before bedtime. To appeal to children, its taste and form would need considerable innovation  exciting flavours apart from chocolate and strawberry would need to be developed and the shape made more exciting. Communication would need to be woven around this as fun for children, yet reminding mothers of its nutritional values and convenience. To ensure convenience, pack sizes would need to be different for home consumption and other occasions where smaller, single-serve packs could be effective. As these children grow up, Healthee would need to hold on to this habit, making cereal consumption a part of their daily diet.
  Another option would be to try and work on the concept of a meal substitute for all age groups, consumed with items besides milk  options like a 'Healthee' lunch of cereals and yoghurt etc. Here again the task is one of changing set habits and it could be easier to focus on teenagers, appeal to their sense of this being the 'cool' thing to consume and over time build the consumption habit. The product availability should shift to make this available at outlets like school/office canteens. Whichever option Energee pursues, Healthee's long-term growth will only come through a major category expansion, which is likely to be long and slow. Good luck, Aadesh.

CHANGING CONSUMER HABITS

  RAJAN CHIBBA IS ASSOCIATE DIRECTOR OF A.E RERGUSON. 

  Energee finds itself in a typical post-liberalisation situation. Having established its brands during the era where market shares were more a function of share of licensed capacities than inherent strategies, the option of diversifying ones portfolio is based on identifying high potential growth categories. Unfortunately, this creates the twin challenge of both selling the product concept and facing strong competition. Energee's move into the cereals market represents such a diversification. The market has a high potential but is small today and has a Kellogg's to deal with.
  There lies the positioning dilemma. The price-based positioning does not generate enough cash to sustain market expansion efforts and a head-on battle with international giants reduces the market share significantly. The situation is further compounded by a bewildering choice of alternative positions, none of which may individually represent an attractive enough segment. The Delta Habit Factor (DHF) framework can provide a perspective to evolve a strategy under such circumstances. The DHF model postulates that successful brands have an inherent ability to alter consumer habits around the use for the product category in terms of either creating new uses for the product, or changing the frequency of use of the product or changing the duration of use of the product or changing sharing habits of the product or changing buying habits of the product.
