  Of course, we have had managers who have declined an interpretation, said Parthiv. And what I tell them is: Thats really your score board, and it is up to you to use it or not. Both copies are with you, the agency will not send a third copy to anyone unless you authorise it. 
   So you can rest assured that it will not be known to anyone but you. Its yours, not ours. 
  Parthiv cited the example of another top-level manager, a highly-rated performer, who contributed greatly on his job. But his balancing scores were very low, he said. Worse, in his own self-appraisal, he had rated himself very poorly, and that astonished us, for at that level, managers are known to sing their own praises.
  On being questioned, he revealed that he felt very distressed by the fact that his children hadnt got admissions to the best schools. That they were studying in an average school bothered him. The manager perceived that he was unable to balance work and home, said Parthiv. I am ignoring my family at the expense of my work. I am a senior manager here and I have not been able to get admissions for my children in the best school simply because there is a tremendous amount of networking and there is nobody to provide any support. How do you expect me to feel good about myself? he asked.
  At Peacock, they believe that if you want to hire top-notch professionals you must provide them with peripheral support. And that is what our administration department did, said Parthiv. They went to the school, got the information required, fixed the test dates and ensured the children were trained and taken for the test. The children performed exceedingly well, they have a good track record and were admitted in due course, said Parthiv.
  Performance is sacrosanct and that has to be delivered, said Parthiv, but you must also accept that the environment must help deliver that performance. The HR department must bring about a culture that, in turn, enables people and aids performance. After all, you are working on organisational growth and individual growth. How do you marry the two? People have aspirations and so does the organisation. And you have to match the two, and that can happen only through developing the right culture.
  The instances Parthiv had talked about at length were so small in nature, but for the organisation, felt Shardul, they were critical enough for them to create the time and space to handle them. Yet, he couldnt help wondering if Peacock Industries method was far too democratic. If HR had to stay at arms length, wouldnt organisational development be impaired, he wondered.

COMMUNICATION CONUNDRUM

Atul Kumar is a partner with Amrop International, a leading global executive search firm.
  The case presents an interesting situation and, in my opinion, requires discussion on two fronts, which are inherently intertwined but best treated separately for now. One, development or appraisal process thats either empowering or threatening. And second, the issue of balancing tensions. Balancing tensions on the job is one of the prime roles most senior managers perform today. Personal life just happens to be one of them. 
  To give you some more: cultural issues of a new country if you are posted abroad, language barriers etc, all impact the performance of an individual. I have known of managers receiving training on what to watch for and how to do business before being posted to another country as well as a course in a relevant language to help a person perform. Evidently, without much doubt, it may be said that the performance of a manager is dependent on the persons ability to manage the different pulls and pushes in ones life. What else is a burnout?
  Typically, it is a case of expending too much energy and time in managing these tensions.
  Balancing tensions is also an issue of having the freedom to communicate issues that build and have an impact on stress. The onus of providing a work environment that allows communication of all kinds lies on the firm and is a factor of policies and broader management and individual styles of bosses and subordinates. The specific issue of Kuldeeps non-performance seems to be a combination of company culture not assisting him in communicating personal problems and seeking help, also his boss preventing a relationship that is wholesome. In generic terms, several firms have policies that allow people to spend time on personal issues, that is, a leave policy and holidays (where taking leave is mandatory), flexi hours which are based on trust and individual freedom etc. In my experience, most positive employers, multinationals or non-MNCs, do work towards balancing these tensions at the workplace.
One has to understand the competencies needed for a specific role, for it will be a good platform to build performance management systems.
  On a broader framework, it is also imperative to understand the competencies that are required for a specific role, for only that would be a good platform on which to build a performance management system. In our experience, managing stress is one of the fundamental competency that senior managers are required to possess.
  Business performance in todays complex organisation is not limited to hard numbers alone. It goes beyond this, into areas like business relationship quality and energy. Evaluation of performance should, therefore, also be by a diverse set of people. Usage of a formal 360-degree process, which seeks feedback from groups of people working under a manager, does seem to be holistic. But the basic process of a 360-degree technique itself doesnt make the tool effective. The winning lies in the manner in which the tool is used and perceived in the organisation by individual managers. A style, which is empowering rather than threatening, is more effective as it breaks down barriers to communication and simply helps rather than hammers. While I would support the involvement of a third party to manage the process of this holistic feedback, I would like to stress the criticality of human resources (HR) in ensuring the success of the process. While HR may not directly control such a process, it must work hard to ensure that the managers understand and utilise the benefits for themselves, their subordinates and peers. In other words, the environment of trust and belief in the process at all levels must be driven by HR and understood and managed by all employees.
  Another issue that needs to be considered is that a point-in-time process such as 360 degree and any other such tool cant be seen as a panacea to performance and development management. Performance management is an ongoing process and, therefore, communication around it must also be continuous. In Kuldeeps case, I am convinced there must have been enough warning signals for his boss to pick up and set up a process to communicate performance issues early in time and, in the process, understand personal problems. Where was the HR manager all this time and why didnt the firm set up processes, other than the 360-degree technique, to understand and manage these issues? Glenn Industries has larger issues to contend with than just these individual cases the issue of building an environment of performance and communication. A whole culture that is enabling versus controlling; communicating versus threatening, and a performance environment that is challenging versus stressful.
   Now the most critical question. Who is responsible for a managers performance? While factors effecting performance may be internal or external to the organisation, the responsibility for performance is that of the managers. The organisation can at best provide the tools and the environment to enable the individual to help himself but it is the manager who needs to take ownership to effectively utilise these tools. Kuldeep needs to own up his performance issues and ensure enough communication takes place at sustained time-frames with his boss or any one else senior in the system to seek resolution and assistance and not let himself completely go down under.
  At the end, I would urge you to evaluate the costs of non-performance and replacing managers versus providing them with tools to improve performance and the resultant benefits in terms of money, time, organisational stress and energy. With stress becoming a part of our lives at both personal and professional levels, organisations and managers have to invest in processes and systems to balance life in general. Organisations need to feel equally responsible for an employees success in life and be an enabler to success against taking the narrow view of only the work environment.

REDIFINING ROLES

  Matangi Gowrishankar is vice-president (organisation effectiveness) for the Cummins Group in India.

  This case is more about how HR facilitates organisational effectiveness, rather than a discussion on how hands-on or arms-length a function is. The case isnt also on the culture in multinationals versus other companies. It is about how organisations choose to use various tools and techniques available to them, whether companies view organisation development and organisation effectiveness as critical to HR, and how the organisation views its people.
  HR professionals need to increasingly look at how they promote this organisational view, and how they facilitate the right balance for the individual and the organisation. They need to understand that the individual is the sum total of what he or she brings to the organisation and, at the end of the day, there must be give and take to ensure success.
  The 360-degree feedback tool is essentially used as a process for opening up the organisation. It is a way of encouraging managers and leaders to listen to other stakeholders in their organisational role: the boss or the peers or subordinates and customers. The real learning happens when they look at the degree of convergence or variance between self-perception and that of others, and the degree of convergence or variance with others in their peer group. The critical success factor lies in how it is implemented and what the results are used for.
HR needs to know that the individual is the sum total of what he or she brings to the firm, and there must be give and take to ensure success
  Organisations that have used the tool successfully began using it as a self-development tool. External agencies are used for design, implementation, assessment and subsequent support for counselling and action planning. This creates a safe environment for the individual and shows the firms commitment to helping individuals make significant behavioural changes to support effectiveness at work. This is what Peacock does.
  If the organisation uses it for an evaluation of the capabilities or effectiveness of a manager or leader without ensuring that the culture has the maturity to provide effective and real-time feedback, this is a recipe for disaster. If the results are turned in to HR, which then uses the information to make people decisions, many things happen:
 * The selection of raters is based on the old back-scratching principle. This vitiates the purpose of getting effective and meaningful feedback.
 * The depth and quality of feedback is limited, and most raters give a perfunctory score without investing time in qualitative feedback, which is critical.
 * Commitment to making real progress in terms of development and lasting improvements is relegated to the background and improving perceptions is what the individual works on.
   The most devastating effect is, of course, what happens to Kuldeep his ratings are seen as the basis for condemning him without really providing a forum for discussion, counselling and helping the individual to address key issues and concerns.
   Thats about process. The other significant element in this tool is the dimensions on which feedback is sought. The 360-feedback process is designed to augment and reinforce the values and leadership behaviours organisations want to build in the culture. If work life balance is not something the organisational culture actively promotes, then the tool will not provide any way of surfacing this issue. What will, in fact, surface is dysfunctional team and peer relationships, inability to deliver on commitments etc all attributed to slipping performance. This perhaps happened in the case of Glenn.
  The situation is exacerbated by the fact that the analysis and support is provided internally by the organisation if the culture in the organisation is that personal problems are not to be taken to work and their impact not seen as significant, the individual will never have recourse or even the platform for real improvement and development.
  The real challenge for organisations is to spend time on the design of the process, the attributes they want to evaluate and how much they want to support the individuals to align with the organisation. I know of one organisation, which has developed a strong ethos around the 360-degree process despite an overarching focus on performance. In this organisation, with the help of practising organisational therapists, we designed workshops where managers understood the values of the organisation and how these translated into apparent behaviours. The workshops had structured exercises, which helped managers view their behaviours and how these impacted the work groups in a non-threatening environment.
  With this background, they were encouraged to do a detailed self-assessment. They selected their raters who sent feedback directly to the agency. Once the assessment was done, the reports were mailed to the people, and online assistance of counsellors was provided to them for six months. The individuals shared their feedback and action plan with their immediate boss and the consultants were available to facilitate the discussions. Some may say this is a long drawn-out process. Ultimately, it is the effective embedding of culture and behaviours that ensures a sustainable competitive edge.
  HR pros need to balance their roles effectively. While focusing on the business needs, they must ensure that theres clarity on the leadership behaviours. Theres sufficient scope for employee advocacy, and that the tools they use deliver value to both the individual and the organisation. Top leadership must understand that real organisational development occurs only when there is thoughtful implementation and a firm commitment to following through on the what the results tell them. Its easy to throw the baby out with the bath water, but the damage this does to individuals and the organisation is irreversible.
Designed by: Epsilon Systems - SQ-80, Hauz Khas Appts, New Delhi-16. Phone: 685-3301, 685-0878, 98100-06431

Case Study 18


7 Feb 2000		 LIVING IN THE BUFFER ZONE
MEERA SETH
Theres a crisis at consultants bretto india - too many people have been leaving. has the firm been operating for too long in emergency mode, in the buffer zone?
  Prashant Vira flipped the pages of the India Today, glancing at the bold blurbs that dotted the hijack story. Its the same old target practice, he mused. 
  Time wasted, passing the buck, babudom strikes, confused signals, India had no clue, the US did not deliver, flawed perceptions, Vira lazily flipped more pages and finally read, Flaws in our national character make India an easy target.
  That was it! Pulling out a black marker, Vira circled the blurb below the article, which read, Before we decry the soft state, let us consider whether we have it in us to really join the battle. Sticking a post-it on the top, he wrote: VD, KS, RJ, GC - just the circled blurb is enough religion for us.
  Vira was returning after a six-hour emergency management committee meeting called by three of his senior directors at Bretto India, the firm of consultants where he was the president. He recalled the issues discussed and the agitation in every voice that spoke. The trigger had been the recent resignations of three managers of Bretto. But in those resignations, the directors saw the steady deterioration of the firm.
  Not that Vira was unaffected, he too had been anxious and unhappy over the loss. But in the last two years, despite all efforts, Bretto had not been able to achieve the much desired visibility. For one, it faced severe competition from older Indian firms and the newer multinationals. Two, although in its nine years of existence, Bretto had grown a clientele that was impressive and valuable, the spectacular growth of the first seven years had begun to plateau. The response: severe cost-cutting that took away perquisites and liberties, which in turn led to a team of disgruntled managers.
  Vira, who had started Bretto nine years ago, had behind him a 30-year career in consulting, both in India and abroad. So he had taken the plateauing growth in his stride, hopeful that revival measures would find support among his senior directors. But todays meeting showed that the unrest and anxiety had percolated up to afflict his most-trusted top management.
  Vira had heard the agitated ranting of his directors. Its not just the fact of their leaving, said Vipul Deo, one of the directors, referring to the three managers who had resigned, but the reasons they left. In those reasons and other situations facing us, we see the symptoms of a malady - Bretto is going downhill in an era where the only direction allowed is uphill. And, the sad part is, we are doing nothing about it.
  Vira heard patiently as Vipul went on, We decided to take a look at all the exit interviews in 1999 - mind you, 12 managers resigned in one year! They all said it is an excellent place to work in, they all liked the culture of openness, friendliness. 
  They enjoyed the variety of experience they got here. But, we dont see any commitment to grow, to excel, to be a winning team, that is their verdict! 
  Everyone wanted to be part of a winning team, but they did not see Bretto as a winner anymore. I dont think we have done anything really spectacular, said Atul Dani. The leadership has not played the role of being an engine for growth, or developing people, or its image, said Vipul.
  Others joined in the dissection. Let me tell you what Regrow Pharma India told me last week, said Raghu Jairam. As it happened, Bretto had lost a major account in a competitive pitch. The presentation had been stunning, the opinion was that the account would go to Bretto. But in a personal interview, Regrows chairman Partho Vyas said, I really enjoyed the presentation, but my anxiety is fundamental. Do you have the people to handle this account which will run over two years? I have information that tells me seven people have left your firm in the last six months. I am afraid this will affect the consistency and continuity and even the quality of the assignment. So what kind of team are you promising me? What guarantee do I have that the top-profile team you are promising me will be there throughout? For instance, will you be there?
  Raghu looked at Vira and asked, Now, how are we going to grow with an image like this? Good managers leave after we put in years of investment in them. Its our promotion policy, our lack of drive! Others chipped in with their diagnoses - poor quality, complacency, absence of vision, short-term focus, too wide a product mix, not enough manpower...
  Vira heard the tirade patiently. Taking off his glasses, he said in his characteristic soft voice, Let me tell you guys a story. A beverage company I know, has a suit slapped against it for releasing date-expired stocks that allegedly caused food poisoning. The MD set in motion a strong damage-control exercise through his PR firms and he also deployed the finest brains in his organisation to get at the root of the episode. We went through the whole process and the first person who got the flak was the sales depot guy. He was amazed. He showed documentation that indicated rejection in October 1999 and despatch to the rejected goods area for onward despatch to R&D for testing.
'It's not just the fact of their leaving, but the reasons they left. In these we see the symptoms of a malady -- bretto is going downhill in an era where the only direction allowed is uphill'
  It transpired that the rejected goods were lying in the area for cleared goods. As it happened, when the stockists men came, a helper in the sales depot simply picked up stocks which included the rejected stocks and handed them over. But the supervisor in charge of rejections was emphatic that he had placed the goods in the bay marked for rejects. When they probed some more, the store helpers said that the reject bay had to be cleared to make place for some plant accessories which had arrived that morning, since the plant was being cleaned, and we had to receive it in the rejections bay. But we removed the rejects and placed them in this corner which is not being used anyway as there is some seepage in this part.
  Now who is to be blamed? asked Vira. The plant guys for scheduling a cleaning activity on an unscheduled day? Or the rejections man for putting the goods in the cleared goods bay? Or the store keeper of the cleared goods section for not checking who was handing over goods or for not checking what was being despatched? Or the rejections guy for not putting the rejects back where they belonged? How did the system not pick it up when the report showed despatches of stocks that bore reject codes? Every man claims he did his bit - the firm is in trouble, nevertheless.
  The point is there will always be snafus and mishaps, changed schedules and unplanned movements. We will have lethargic workers, impatient transporters, lazy staffers, foot-draggers, silent risk-takers, power failures, unscheduled emergencies... Its very sad, they should not happen and we wish they will not happen. But are we going to sit back and finger point and feel sorry for ourselves? Can we move ahead if we all collectively decide to sit back and feel powerless?
  Today, you have enumerated 12 flaws. Did you discover all this last night, or have you lived with this feeling the last two years? If that is the case, then on a day-to-day basis what are we doing to gain control?
  Control what? asked Raghu. Today we have three resignations, over and above the 12 who left last year. Between those 12 and these three, did we do anything to control the attrition? Did we try and change anything at all here? Sarla Khanna said she was victimised for being a woman, because she took three extra months of maternity leave. But I feel there was more to her cumulative anger and disappointment. Or take Shantam Vij. A quiet, composed man, he never complained; we did not give him his grade last year thinking he will continue not to complain and no harm done if we deferred it by a year. Except, Shantam resigned.
  Hey, one at a time, interrupted Ganesh Chitale. Let me tell you about Sarla Khanna. She need not have taken those extra 12 weeks, but she said to me, You have given other women six months, so why cant I? The difference is that when you are a senior manager, I expect you to manage your lives responsibly, commensurate with your seniority. And at the time she asked for an extension, we were bang in the middle of seven assignments and hard pressed for people. She probably thought she would manage, cope and survive and eventually the grade change would be hers anyway. But no, that couldnt happen.
  So, I did pull her off the fast track for that reason, said Ganesh. I dont think her resigning or feeling victimised is any reflection on the firm. In any case, we were not taking away her promotion for good. All I said was, prove yourself. But Shantams case is worth examining. Such a stellar chap! Who stymied his promotion? Our HR policies! Last year he was due to become assistant vice-president. Since the year had been bad, and the year ahead looked worse, the firm took a stance on costs and rewards, naturally. Promotions were granted to just three managers, and Shantam was not one of them!
  Karan Sobti now held up his hand to speak. You know there is something interesting here, he said. In every steady state system there is a buffer, a slack to provide for an emergency, but I find people usually end up operating only in that buffer zone. Like Sarla, she operated in the buffer zone of possible additional leave and in Shantams case, I think we as a firm operated in the buffer zone of his tolerance, and pushed him to the brink. We could have considered his promotion last year but for reasons we thought were more critical we deferred his promotion.
  Vira grew very interested. This is very intriguing, he said. Tell me more about this buffer-zone syndrome.  
  Very simple, said Karan. Take our own local area network. When the memory was low, the free memory was 30 MB. But when the memory was increased 50 times or 100 times, within two months, I found the free memory was just 40 MB! Why?
   Because our staff had unwittingly moved into the emergency or buffer zone. There is some sort of comfort there. Either you want to stay in limbo, that is, make no decisions, no housekeeping, it is a free zone with no rules.You keep stretching it, till it snaps. Here at Bretto, I think we are all operating in the buffer zone of every area - be it time, quality, people or profits, and in the process pushing the firm to the brink. Its like the railways, went on Karan. A train can arrive two hours late and yet reach on time. How? It requires 1,400 people all along the route to make tweaks to their system which means each one dips into his buffer zone so the train catches up. The flip side is, if a train reaches 10 minutes late, no one asks Could that have been avoided? Instead they feel good that the driver did not exceed his provided buffer time.
  How are you relating that to Shantams case? asked Vira.
  In Shantams case, said Karan, we thought we could delay his promotion by a year, he would still cope. After all Shantam is known for his tolerance. But we did not create a contingency plan to deal with a situation where our decision could cause unhappiness. Did we have a contingency plan that said, if this decision to defer his promotion results in unhappiness, then this is what we will do? But last year, we were at the tail tip of our cash flows, our morale, our profits. Our patience itself was stretched. And Shantam snapped. After all, Shantams promotion was held back not because he was incompetent, but because we took a policy decision to limit promotions. When I create these buffer zones, these slacks in the system, and they all come into play, then an alarm should go off if such a decision results in a crisis. How do we identify the beginnings of a crisis so that corrective action can be taken before we encounter the cumulative impact? That is what we need to identify.
  In the same way, said Raghu, if our HR polices are not giving comfort to our managers, where are the alarms in the system? If HR allows Sarla to take three more months of leave, but it created chaos in other sub-systems, why didnt the total system register it? Or if it did, why didnt someone talk about it then and not now? Unless you are now saying that our firm does not encourage communication - but that is not so!
  Likewise, said Karan to Raghu, last year when you decided to withhold Shantams promotion and he said he was disappointed, why did the feedback not percolate upwards?
  Raghu was annoyed, since Shantam worked under him. Okay, if I had brought it up to you, what would you have done? he asked angrily. Given him the promotion? Anticipate that he would leave? In hindsight everything seems possible. But at that time last year, we, the same people sitting here, had decided that the total promotions for the year would be just three! Out of a total strength of 125 people, we had decided on the three, how many could get 25% increments and how many normal and also how many had to be offloaded, remember?
'We have thrived on the edge of chaos. Any change we make requires a conscious decision on our part to be prepared to lose sometime in order that we come out better eventually'
  When we took that decision, said Karan, we should have known there would be some unhappiness triggered in the organisation and therefore developed a fallback plan in the event of a crisis. After taking a sub-optimal decision to rein in promotions, we did not change the rest of the system to deal with it. We continued running the business on an as-is basis, after taking some critical non-optimal decision and expecting that everything will resolve itself - sab theek ho jayega! Whereas, if we had also assumed that it is a problem and that such a thing could happen, and gone on to create a mechanism to seek recourse to the president to at least mention the unhappiness...
  Suresh Samarth, who had been listening keenly, spoke up. All this points to the very arbitrary, knee-jerk reactions of the firm. And, that has to do with overall attitudes within the firm. Apart from what we saw in the exit interviews, there is this data which you will find valuable, he said pulling out a sheet of paper. I examined various jobs we did in 1999 and the time taken to deliver. Out of 46, only 19 were delivered on the due date. In all the rest, I noticed at the first review we sought an extension. Why? Essentially, it means that in nearly 50% of assignments we overshot the deadline! Is it that we did not enable the client to define the scope of his work, or we did not suggest to the client additional areas that he needs to look at to make his assignment comprehensive and efficient? asked Suresh.
  Let me answer that, said Brijesh Singh, another director. Even where we recognise that we need to suggest to the client that he needs to look at additional areas, the point is it has cost implications. That inhibits us! Why? Because, as someone said, we dont feel like winners. Consequently, we feel we cant command the price we want, we become defensive. Even where we realise that the scope of the work is more than what the client defines, we are wishy-washy - hedging, dodging, inhibited, apprehensive, apologetic - so that we at least bag the assignment. So, it is also linked to that interesting observation made by one of the exiting managers, that our image is very poor, so much so that it is percolating into individual perceptions!
Tarini Duggal decided to offer her viewpoint. The reality is, in many cases, not defining the scope of the work exhaustively works in our favor. We think we can manage it and we are able to manage it. So we are able to tell the client that he does not need that extended scope of work and he stays with the original brief. In 60% of cases, we havent been able to manage it, because the client has honed in on those areas which you decided not to suggest in the first place fearing a high fee, and consequent rejection. So, when he does push for those additional areas, you have no choice but to offer it to him and that results in a time overrun. In reality, in all our cases we are allowing for that ambiguity in order to cut down price, in the belief that once you have bagged the assignment, you can manage it later. Isnt that also a case of operating in the buffer zone?
  It went on, each director narrating different episodes that pointed to lethargy, inefficiency, while Vira watched them all even as he heard their views keenly. Then he said, I am glad you called me into this meeting. As I see, yes, there is a lot of pulling up of socks that we need to do. But essentially, I can see in all the issues you brought up that we have thrived on living on the edge of chaos, in the buffer zone, as Raghu puts it. But we must remember, any change we bring about, any decision we take, requires a conscious decision on our part to be prepared to lose somewhere for sometime in order that we come out better eventually. And that requires each of us to become accountable, to be cognizant of losses, errors, slip-ups, sub-optimality...
  Thats what winners do. That requires an ability to recognise your mistakes as they happen, not try and cover your tail or feel sorry for your plight. But if you fear a finger will point at you, you could end up justifying your wrong actions. The more you wallow in feeling sorry, the more you will seek to save face rather than find solutions. It will also lead to short-term, patch-work repairs, not long-term sustenance. If you want long-term, enduring corporate success, then we must learn to own responsibility, become accountable.
  Yes, as you all say, it finally appears that the culprit is circumstance. Bad year, low profits, attrition, poor image, aggressive competition, deep foreign pockets, lack of people... Perhaps. But then what are we all here for, if not to predict change and manage it? Isnt that what our clients pay us for anyway? Then why arent we using those skills at home? Even the best system is periodically validated. Why? To review it against a changing environment - what you call circumstance. If you are happy to discover that the circumstances add up to predictable failure, then you have one more peg to hang failure on. Markets dont fail, governments dont fail, systems dont fail, people do. Because only people can engineer success.
  Before we depart, let me say this - if we focus on transactions, we will only find transactional solutions. Instead, let us focus on our attitudes. Let us encourage a culture that does not condemn mistakes but gives us the space to learn from them, deal with them and share the learning. That will promote what we need in healthy measures - accountability for results!
  As his car climbed the flyover, Vira said aloud, What we lack is an organisational character.

TIME TO WALK THE TALK


Devi Singh		   Devi Singh, director of the Management Development Institute, Gurgaon, is a fellow of the Indian Institute of Management, Ahmedabad, and has over 20 years experience in academics in India and abroad.

  G. Balaji, professor of operations management at MDI, has a PhD from the Indian Institute of Science, Bangalore. 

  
  Viras sigh that the organisation lacks character clearly indicates that the finger-pointers have successfully initiated a new member into their midst.And in this case, this seems to be the most sinister issue for the organisation. A theory about character of an organisation or softness of a state make interesting reading but it is of little use to take corrective action. By succumbing to the demands of the hijackers, India has not become a soft state. Painting India as a soft state is aimed at covering up a series of administrative failures in a practically useless theory.
  Likewise, talking about the buffer-zone syndrome of individuals and organisations is perhaps an elegant and a very clever way to rationalise the failures of the past without accepting individual responsibility for them. Vira must first come out of this vicious circle of arguments and theories. The symptoms in the organisation are indeed clear and stark. Like a doctor, he has to handle both the symptoms as well as the disease and take decisive steps to bring the organisation out of its stupor.
  Vira should send clear messages down the line about the accountability and responsibility of senior managers.
  Three major symptoms seem to emerge from the text of the case. First in order of seriousness is that the unrest and anxiety had percolated upwards to afflict his trusted top management. The consulting industry has seen a sudden influx of several global majors in the past few years and the external environment has undoubtedly had an impact on the fortunes of Bretto. The initial growth of the company was demand-led, but competitive dimensions have since changed. For Bretto to consolidate its place in the new structure, it was required to do things differently be innovative.  
 But innovations require resources and time to happen both of which have to be planned for. The fact that resources were spread thin over a wide product range and manpower was inadequate is a clear pointer to the absence of a strategic response to the changed environment. One may venture to conjecture that their quest for getting business in all possible areas is also a clear symptom of insufficient specialisation which is increasingly is an important characteristic of successful consulting firms. While Vira has to bear the cross, the strategy of the company is not solely the responsibility of the CEO his trusted lieutenants have probably not played their part. Vira will have to introspect and see why they did not play their roles as co-partners in the growth of the firm. Is he adequately delegating? Are the directors adequately empowered? Is the organisation too dependent on him for direction? Being a professional consultant, is he focusing more of his attention on tasks and projects and less on his role as CEO?
   The second symptom, inherently related to the first, is the failure of the organisation to respond to data. Exit interviews provide a mine of information about the organisation. If they have not been acted upon, the process is dysfunctional. Do manufacturing organisations that are cash-strapped avoid preventive maintenance of their machines? In a consulting firm, the assets are consultants. Should cash flow inhibit the payment of incentives to key consultants? If yes, was Shantam adequately compensated in other ways so that the organisation could buy time? More importantly, is the HR department sensitive to the needs of its people? If 50% of the projects had overshot, why was this information not acted upon earlier? The directors and the CEO are collectively responsible for the design and maintenance of these processes. Why did they abdicate this responsibility?    

G. Balaji		   The third symptom, clearly arising out of Tarini Duggals viewpoint, is that most projects have inadequately-defined scope. In the early years of the organisation, business development processes focused on creating an impressive client list and paid less attention to costs. However, as the organisation grew, it was necessary to modify this practice and pay equal attention to costs. Todays clients are more discerning about the quantity and quality of work and have been successfully out-negotiating Bretto on 60% of the projects. Thus schedules are upset, work-plans of consultants are stretched, cash inflows delayed and probably depressed.   
  All this points to the inconsistency of processes with the emerging realities of the market. Also, process parameters are not consciously tracked, which is probably why this information does not cause top management to be sensitive to these issues. The emphasis is on projects and tasks in both the formal and informal MIS in the organisation the developments of systems and processes have been neglected.
   Collectively, these symptoms refer to a lack of strategic foresight, inadequate resources for innovation, non-adaptive processes, and poor MIS. While the unwillingness to be firm with clients on the scope of work may be interpreted as this company does not have guts , the underlying malaise comes from improperly designed processes. 
Talking about the buffer-zone syndrome of individuals and organisations is a clever way of rationalising failures
 The continued application of irrelevant or sub-optimal processes tends to reduce the credibility of the system, thus resulting in a lackadaisical approach to management and operating on each others buffer zone. Data, good or bad, then seems irrelevant to decision making. Organisational character, like individual character, is a function of the processes and programmes that run automatically upon the stimulus from external environment.
  To ensure the integrity of these processes, one has to continually reassess and revalidate ones responses. If ineffective management implies the lack of an organisational character then, yes, this organisation lacks character. Besides looking at his own portfolio of activities, Vira might try infusing some new blood into his top management team. Also, he should probably send clear messages down the line about accountability and responsibility of senior managers. Management consultants preach incessantly about the process view of management. It is time Bretto walks its talk.

CHANGE OF FOCUS


Debashish Mitra

 Debashish Mitra is the chief operating officer of Global Green Company, a part of the L.M. Thapar group. He was previously the COO of the international foods and agri business division. Also, he was the group head of human resources between 1992 and 1997.

   Vira and Bretto exemplify a situation that a number of companies face as they try and cope with the competitive pressures of the new era of global business. In todays era of cyber time, nine years is too long a time to exist without reinventing yourself. A key issue the case brings to the surface is the point of buffer zones. It is important to understand that there are no buffer zones at the top, in an organisation, or in the market place.
  Winners and leaders dont live off buffer zones. They focus on the zone of difference, between the leader and the rest of the pack.
  Leaders try and increase it, followers try and narrow it down. This is the key dynamic factor that influences the behaviour and performance of companies. This is where the top team of Bretto and Vira are losing out. In knowledge-driven businesses such as consulting, this is even more prominent. If you look at what influences the zone of difference in these companies, it is people. If you dont manage your people well, you dont have a business.
  When things go wrong, it is only to be expected that the Im right, you are wrong syndrome takes over. This is when the leader and leadership becomes critical. It seems that the entire Bretto top management suffers from that.
It is important to understand that there are no buffer zones at the top. Winners and leaders don't live off buffer zones.
 The first thing they need to tackle and focus on is the question of accountability and then the method of solving problems, especially the people-related problems. Solving a problem entails accepting first that there is one, then identifying what kind of problem it is, the reasons for it cropping up, and then deploying the best resources to handle the problem. In doing so, it is important to understand the strengths and the limitations. In the case of Bretto, for example, there was an overdependence of belief that the strength of their culture would retain employees, even if they didnt get rewarded or if their particular personal problem wasnt dealt with, or they felt discriminated against. You cant do this with people anywhere, much less with knowledge workers. To believe that everyone will make a sacrifice all the time for the greater past glory of the company without much future is to be naive to say the very least. To put it down to lack of character is obfuscation.
  The chief executive should have taken the initiative to enter into a dialogue with a cross-section of people across the organisation, not just the top management. They should be given a vision of the future and their role in making it happen. It is important to recognise that the WIFM (whats in it for me) syndrome is getting stronger with each passing day. It is very essential for a leader to understand this. Today, people dont want to just follow. They want to lead. It is this quality that winning firms have harnessed effectively.
  You can no longer have a philosophy of just follow me, it will be okay, do as you are told and expect that to translate into a winning performance in the market place. Bretto and its leadership needed to involve the people a lot more in solving the problems plaguing the company.
   Creating teams, for example, for specific issues. If the recommendation for cutting promotions to reduce costs had come from such a team and then communicated across the organisation in a dialogue mode as opposed to a tell mode, things would definitely have been different.
  Vira should perhaps read less of India Today and more of its employees minds to undestand what needs to be done to put Bretto back on the winning path.
  In summary, the challenge before Vira and his senior colleagues is to assess their commitment to Bretto and their accountability to each other rather than look for problems or solutions outside of themselves. 
Designed by: Epsilon Systems - SQ-80, Hauz Khas Appts, New Delhi-16. Phone: 685-3301, 685-0878, 98100-06431

Case Study 19


21 Feb 2000		 OF CHALLENGES AND CHANGES
MEERA SETH
Patent needed a vendor who would respond faster to its needs, Its present vendor couldn't. should it outsource or revert to manufacture at its own plant?
  Vaman Vittal cracked his knuckles impatiently. It was 3.30 p.m. and his next shift was about to commence. Work on 175 cars which were due for despatch was nearly over, and seven days before the due delivery, the seats hadn't still arrived from his supplier, Buckle Seats. 
Vittal had been on the telephone with his operations manager who had promised to send them by noon that day.
  Vittal was the operations head of Patent Automobiles, who had engineered the creation of Buckle Seats in a joint venture with Patent nine years ago. According to the arrangement, Buckle would manufacture seats for Patent's cars in accordance with their design specifications. While Buckle could outsource some of its requirements within the cost framework agreed with Patent, it was not allowed to manufacture or sell to anybody else.
  Vittal was angry and also helpless. In the last two years, Buckle had let down Patent on a number of occasions. Until three years ago, such a crisis was unimaginable. But at that point in time, Patent produced just two car models. Managing the production and inventory control of two models appeared far easier in comparison to managing four models which were out on the road and a fifth in the design room. Worse, competition was so intense, he could ill-afford to have tardy suppliers.
  Buckle was born in an era where car manufacturers believed in in-house, vertically-integrated manufacture. Even so, Vittal had foreseen the future and convinced his management of the need to set up an independent seat manufacturing unit. But now he wasn't sure. The temptation to revert to in-house manufacturing was overwhelming.
  But the market was changing, and the changes in other industries were impacting the way the overall economy and manufacturing practices were evolving. Specifically, in the automobiles industry, consumer was demanding variety. Earlier, Patent could be satisfied with producing 100 units of one model, but today it had to manufacture 20 of five different models. If all that activity led to market place excitement, it also made Vittal's life a nightmare.
  Why Patent, even biggies like Telco which was originally only in the heavy commercial vehicles (HCVs) segment, had added light commercial vehicles to its stable as the HCV market had saturated. More importantly, it had become imperative since the total investment that went into components was enormous. Therefore, catering to one market was not enough. It was necessary to straddle two markets. "You can obtain economies of scale in components, only if you have economies of scope in the final market," said his technical head, Pradip Sinha. "You can make seats in small numbers but you can't manufacture gear boxes or radiators in small numbers. The investment required for research and development and setting up a unit is so large that you cannot be viable and competitive on price!" 
 Vehicle manufacturers did not look anything like the happy drivers in their advertisements. The dormant industry was suddenly faced with change and the only way they could deal with this change was by exploiting the economies of scope.
  For sometime, Vittal too had found his peace in mouthing this manufacturing mantra. Until, the same common sense triggered off a corollary, by challenging the make-or-buy intellectual debate and almost declaring outsourcing as a manufacturing truism, for survival in a fast changing market. Traditionally, most organisations manufactured the entire product -- including its components -- because that was how they sought to bar the entry of competition. However, a credible-on-quality components industry hadn't evolved. Today, that had changed. Manufacturers had become so tuned in to innovation that it made sense to outsource most components.
  But what made that possible was the recognition of a new variable called 'transaction costs' as against the 'traditional production cost', which tilted the balance in favour of domestic production in that era. In the absence of an evolved components industry, third-party manufacturing meant huge transaction costs of stock outs, which were so high that manufacturers preferred to manufacture in-house. But that constraint had disappeared with the emergence of quality component manufacturers and modular components. Consequently, and with the emergence of information technology, transaction costs had plummeted, so that in a make-or-buy decision, outsourcing became manufacturing common sense. While firms like Patent were still struggling with outsourcing seats, others had managed to make outsourcing a competitive advantage, reducing transaction costs significantly.
  Only last month, Vittal was speaking to a friend in Ralco Steels, which also supplied steel to Patent. He was unnerved to hear what ground the majors were covering.
   Ralco was approached by a large foreign car manufacturer, Luxum, for supply of steel for its cars. Strangely enough, Luxum would not settle for a mere certification of how good the company's steel was. "We want to see what happens to the steel when it is being made, when it comes out from the blast furnace, its properties when it was heat-treated, annealed," said Luxum. "We want to know the entire story, so that we can modify the steel to suit our needs by performing other operations on it when we buy it. The quality of the paint finish is a critical parameter for us. The treatment to steel influences how well the paint responds to the surface."
'With the emergence of information technology, transaction costs had fallen, so that in a make-or buy decision, outsourcing became manufacturing common sense'
    However, Luxum wasn't willing to place one of its employees at Ralco as that would hike its transaction costs. Instead, Ralco could store the data on each process, which was being collected anyway, and offload it on its website from where Luxum would directly access it, thereby creating no storage crisis for Ralco.
  "That's how the majors are fine-tuning transaction costs," said Vittal's friend at Ralco. "Globally, the quality drive has become so specific and the demand for variety has gone up; therefore, managing the manufacturing environment and the related costs is what will ensure viability. With the ease of information technology, it is possible for Luxum to reduce its transaction costs and get exactly the kind of steel it wants."
  Plummeting transaction costs made it possible to outsource many things which would have otherwise been produced in-house. "This is one of the new common senses of manufacturing design," said his friend, "especially as far as automobiles is concerned. The attitude in manufacturing has undergone a change. Manufacturers are becoming more careful about the processes that goes into even bulk products. They recognise that different consumers can have different needs and variables in their products. Ergo, if you want to reap the economies of scope, since you now have a larger variety in your portfolio, then you must try and outsource nearly everything and focus on innovation. For that is the dimension of competition in automobiles!"
   Vittal knew that he must look at outsourcing manufacturing. But he shuddered at the very thought as the delays by Buckle surfaced, sending him through another bout of frantic phone calls.
  Yet, there was the Telco example. It was known as India's largest vertically-integrated manufacturer of vehicles. But, today, it had spun off component manufacturing to a sister company, Tata Autocomp, doing only the assembly itself. Said Vittal's Ralco friend: "It will amaze you that worldwide 70% of value addition comes from the purchase department. This is the trend we are going to see. Value addition comes only from bringing things together." If these were the emerging trends, he wondered, could Patent design manufacturing strategies in line with these changes?
  But that also required an agile and proactive component industry in India, he felt. Vittal knew Maruti had achieved some of that. But Patent? For the moment, he had to overcome his growing problem with Buckle, who he had at one stage hoped would become another Tata Autocomp. But the difference lay in the basic differences between the two companies, he mused. Tata Autocomp had the freedom to sell to other manufacturers. Whereas Buckle was set up by Patent to manufacture and sell only to Patent. At that time, it was a big move for Buckle because none of the other car manufacturers would have thought of outsourcing, for those were days of in-house vertically-integrated manufacture. Naturally, the question of allowing Buckle a free run did not arise. 
  Despite all the managerial talent deployed by Patent, Buckle was weighed down by labour problems that created bottlenecks in the way of supply. When pressure was put on the company to speed up delivery, it led to fall in quality.
  Vittal now wondered if it was sensible to revert to manufacturing seats at Patent's plant or should he look for an alternative supplier and split the volumes. He was already averse to outsourcing from more than one vendor as in the seventies he had been plagued by variable quality across the five vendors who were supplying components to Patent. But the seventies were different and so were the eighties, and the nineties had let loose a very exacting consumer who now had a host of brands to choose from.
  There was the issue of displeasing Buckle, but that could be managed, since it had reached its manufacturing capacity and if fresh investments had to be made for increased capacity, why not look for a new manufacturer, wondered Vittal.
  Uniquely, the suggestion had come from someone in the trade. Vittal met Digvijay Dua, who was already running a seat manufacturing unit for another automobile manufacturer. He said: "What I would like to do is to import the raw material and manufacture the foam, but everything else I will outsource. Even the steel components that go into making the seats are available in modular form. Mine will be a pure assembly unit, just like the unit I have for Sensor Motors."
   Vittal now decided to involve his management consultant, Binny Walia, before entering into further discussions with Dua. Walia noted the many points he observed. One, Buckle bought fabric in bales and converted them into seat covers using tailors. Two, it was manufacturing the foam and certain components of the seat assembly at its plant. Buckle itself traded off when it was cheaper to procure a part. But where it had spare capacity, Buckle decided to produce it in-house. "One has to look at the make-or-buy decision, not just the cost angle but also the strategic angle," said Binny. At Buckle, since the initial capacity was built so high, it willy-nilly ended up making most parts itself. Of course, in many cases, there wasn't an external supplier, and it was working on a local cost benefit analysis than a global one.
  Thus, if it had 20% spare capacity on a machine, Buckle sought to consume it, but that also resulted in expending some costs of executive time on coordination. "Using spare capacity to spread costs creates clutter value," said Binny. "It takes away energy, focus and attention, design time, production time, and even leisure time."
  Binny also noticed that Buckle had people with different skills at its plant. So, there were people working on fabric who were not as skilled as people working on a CNC machine. Or, there were the ITI-trained fitters who were more in demand because the labour market valued them highly. All of them made seats, but had different market values. They were being paid according to the going price for their skills which, in turn, created internal parity problems on wages. "I see a potential industrial relations problem at Buckle, waiting to blow in your face," said Binny. "Making seats is a mix of highly-skilled assembling and machining and also critical but unskilled labour. That's because you have different trades and differing skill levels operating together. 
And because there is a lot of in-house manufacture. What Buckle needs is a focus in manufacturing."  
  Vittal processed Binny's observations in his mind. He could straightaway see that Dua's venture would eliminate the problem of multiple trades and skills. What he would have is pure assembly workers. But Dua was pitching for the entire business, not just a part of it. "I will float a separate company named DD Patent Auto Components, where Patent will have an equity stake. It will be a dedicated unit for Patent. You can rate my performance, and based on that, you must agree to raise my share of the business by 10% compounded annually, so that in five years, I have your entire business."
  "A vertically-integrated unit like Buckle has its own problems," said Binny. "If a design change is introduced, almost all design changes will have to be managed internally. And that is not simple. It will mean a change in the foam, the fabric design, the steel tubes, and even the button position. It was okay when you had just two models, but today when you have five models, Buckle does not possess the ability to manage variety."
  Vittal and Binny then met Sinha. Sinha was certain that the design change response time at Dua's proposed unit would be faster. "It's like this," said Sinha, "when we had just one or two models, Buckle suited our needs. But today with the increasing demand for more and more models, we must have a vendor who can respond faster to change. Managing varieties will be tough. We need vendors who can manage varieties in their supplies because now they will have to manage smaller batches across 10 models. They have to be swift enough to switch between models."  
'The whole ethos in manufacturing has undergone a change. Therefore, if you want to reap the economies of scope, you must try and outsource nearly everything and focus on innovation'
  Dua, in fact, had greater versatility, for he had three other collaborations with other car manufacturers for windscreens, light fittings and seats. That gave him the experience to straddle different parts, different buyers, multiple suppliers and naturally different models. Of course his asking price was higher, but what he offered in turn was excellence and promptness and a learning which Buckle did not have.
  One issue which caused Vittal anxiety, was Dua's desire to set up the foam manufacturing unit as a separate company, which would have the ability to supply to a wider market, not just Patent. And he wanted Patent to invest 20% in the foam company too, almost as a pre-condition.
  But Sinha didn't see that as an issue. "Just take that as an investment decision!" he urged. "Earlier, Telco was making radiators. Today, its sister concern Tata Autocomp is manufacturing radiators. If Telco makes radiators, it would add to its fixed costs and break even points would be higher. By outsourcing, Telco's break even point had come down. Second, Tata Autocomp is also a Tata venture. But its decision to remain in the radiator business is a business decision, not a manufacturing decision," pointed out Sinha. "If tomorrow Tata Autocomp, which is manufacturing various components, finds that the radiator manufacturing business is not earning as much as it expected, it can exit that business and outsource radiators for Telco. That will be a business decision."
  "Likewise, we can today give our corporate strategy flexibility by modularising the investment and the production strategy. Today, Buckle or even Dua's seat unit is a need, a manufacturing decision. If we agree to invest in his foam company, it will only reap for us economies of scale since he'll be using the foam from that company for our seats. But if tomorrow Dua's foam unit grows too wide, and we find that our 30% equity in the firm is not earning much money, we can pull out our investment and that decision will not change our relationship with Dua as far as manufacture of seats is concerned," added Sinha.
  Sinha was confident that an investment in Dua's venture was sound, but he would not recommend an investment in Buckle for the same purpose. "Buckle's major problem is the design of its manufacturing systems," he said. "Over the years, it has become set in its ways and I can't see any scope for change or improvement."
  "When you look at manufacturing strategy, keep in mind the needs of the changed market," said Binny. "In the automobile business today, model dynamism is very high. In America, for instance, auto companies are talking of one model every six months. Earlier, the competitive edge in car manufacturing meant cost and quality of production. Today, people buy cars based on a segment where they see themselves in. Within that segment, they are indifferent to price. The manufacturer has to, therefore, focus on innovation, aesthetics. In each segment, there is a lot of model dynamism. In such a situation, imagine the impact on Patent, on Daewoo, on Maruti and on all the other component manufacturers down the line and on their production systems. The key parameter in manufacturing is production system agility. And the response time to change!"
  Sinha was clear that Patent needed to fine-tune its manufacturing strategy for the future. "It may be seats today, but we have to give it the same attention as we would say, radiators," he said. "We have to make our manufacturing agile and responsive to change. All five dimensions of a product: price, quality, service, flexibility and innovation, are prone to change. But, at any point in time, in any market, one of them becomes the competitive dimension. Today, in the American car market, quality is no more a dimension of competitive edge, because every player has reached that threshold level of quality. Today, it is a question of who can introduce models faster. We are already witnessing that in India. Suddenly, aesthetics, innovation and features have become the basis of competition.
  "So, when your competitive dimension keeps changing, is your manufacturing able to keep pace? For long, we have played on costs to remain competitive. But have we responded yet to Santro? Maruti has answered with the Wagon R, but Patent? A world-class manufacturer is one who has got a certain amount of threshold on each of these dimensions and is good at one of them. We need to create agility in manufacturing, and I can see Dua's unit being able to handle one part,'' said Sinha.

A CLARION CALL FOR CHANGE

  V.S. Ram is the chief executive of Asian Paints.

  Traditionally, a make-or-buy decision meant an economic choice between producing raw materials or components in-house and buying from an external source. It has now moved into the realm of senior management decisions, under the fancy label of strategic sourcing. Is this another case of wrapping in jargon what operating managers have always done without any fuss?
  Not really. To understand this, look at the changes taking place in the business environment. Customers of cars, as indeed of any other product or service, are relentlessly seeking greater value in this age of competition. Quality, service and quick delivery are now universally available and these attributes are no longer perceived as offering distinctive value.
  To retain customers and to protect market shares, car makers are looking for new ways to differentiate their products by offering a wider choice of models, focusing on aesthetics, speeding up model introductions and adding new features. The sheer magnitude of these changes have resulted in new demands on manufacturing systems.
  Vertically-integrated firms (meaning those which made key components in-house), looked appropriate in a slow-paced era in which competence meant making rather than designing, selling or servicing a car. The absence of a dependable component industry imposed a potential vulnerability of market failure. Self-sufficiency was the answer, and it seemed logical for car makers to build elaborate plants with proprietary component-making facilities. While these features were universally true in the course of the evolution of the auto industry there were additional contextual factors that encouraged vertical integration in a range of industries in India. The restricted market size set limits on expansion and forced companies to seek value capture through vertical growth. High tax rates on profits coupled with generous allowances for fresh investments were a virtual invitation to backward integration. A cascading tax structure dictated the setting up of such facilities as divisions of the buyer but often the component unit was a tightly-controlled subsidiary or a joint venture as in the case of Buckle. The essential features of these forms of internal sourcing consisted of captive supply, low value-addition, and a high degree of control.
 If buckle is unable to respond to change, it will be a pernnial drain on the resources of patent. The solution: transformation of buckle
  Today, with the evolution of the components industry, manufacturing components in-house would be justified only if it offered a sustainable competitive edge such as a lasting cost or quality advantage or a unique marketable feature. Honda, for example, restricts in-house production to only those parts which the customer sees, hears or feels, safety-related components and areas where the company has a long-term advantage. Such a clear policy restricting in-house production can help a company maximise returns on resources by applying them to what the enterprise does best.
  Outsourcing will free managements time, enabling it to pay more attention to the core of its business. Specialisation, up-to-date technological capabilities and sharper focus help outside suppliers make better quality consistently, and at a lower cost. In-house production entails continuing investments in R&D, personnel and infrastructure. While these tend to be underestimated when firms do a cost comparison, the progressive cuts in transaction costs arent factored in. In a famous benchmarking study, Ford Motor discovered that many of its internal suppliers quality practices and costs were nowhere near those of external suppliers. No matter how competent a firm is, it can be good at performing only a limited set of tasks by itself. The key to market success lies in selecting such tasks carefully so that they yield the greatest contribution to a firms competitive standing. 
  In-house manufacture doesnt have the potential to confer a competitive edge on Patent. It has no exclusive knowledge in this area. Moreover, outsourcing seats doesnt appear to entail risks associated with an imperfect market. On the other hand, in-house manufacture will fritter away internal resources of Patent in an area outside its core competency with no unique benefit to its end customer. It would reduce flexibility, and delay design changes.
  Often, in-house manufacture may seem to be the only protection from unreliable vendors who violate delivery schedules, fail to meet specifications, or do not achieve cost-cutting targets. It is useful to ask if such vulnerability is the result of a market imperfection (too few vendors etc), or if it is merely in the perception of managers trying to cope with delivery deadlines. When there is depth in the market, a potential buyer is unlikely to be more efficient than the best supplier.
  Mitigating a vulnerable position through in-house manufacture can render the buyer more vulnerable in a rapidly-changing market. Set in its ways and not desiring change or improvement, Buckle is the product of a captive market, inadequate commitment of resources, diffused management attention and lack of scale. Abandoning Buckle in favour of a new captive supplier without addressing these underlying causes would only create yet another Buckle over time.
  The case for outsourcing is thus strong. If Buckle is unable to respond to the changing demands, it will be a perennial drain on the resources of its parent. Over time, Patent runs the risk of getting dragged down with it. The solution: transformation of Buckle from an inefficient captive supplier to an independent market-centred manufacturer supplying to many customers. Such a transformation will call for a massive change agenda and will include Patent relinquishing direct control. The worlds successful car makers are among the most demanding customers. Ceding control over Buckle can, paradoxically, give Patent greater sourcing control by giving its managers the freedom to seek the best. 

THE SUPPLY CHAIN WEB

  Ravi Bhamidipati is executive director at PricewaterhouseCoopers.

  This case is about two paradigms of manufacturing and the attendant supply chain configurations. We have a choice between Buckle, a fully-integrated seat manufacturer, and Dua, an assembler of seats. On our choice would rest Patents future manufacturing strategy.Some of the issues highlighted in the case are:
 * Delivery reliability and quality consistency being viewed as success factors: These factors can reduce transaction costs. Vehicle manufacturers stock component inventory to protect themselves from erratic supplies and quality. A reliable supplier obviates the need for this inventory as well as monitoring costs (chasers and people in inward inspection, for example). Therefore, a reliable supplier gains a cost advantage.
 * Capabilities and competencies being built up by organisations: It is interesting at this stage to refer back to Taylors theories of management and his stripping down of activities into sub-activities and specialisation. What was true for individuals in Taylors analysis is true for businesses today. Businesses seek to achieve higher levels of efficiencies by stripping themselves of non-core elements and focusing on certain core activities. For instance, Telco becoming an assembler and Duas vision of a seat assembler. By focusing on a clear and well-defined related set of activities, the manufacturer or assembler is able to ride up on the learning curve faster and provide innovative solutions to his customer. He has less to learn and, therefore, learns better. Executive skills do not have to be spread over a larger spectrum.
The component manufacturer's climb upu the learning curve would create entry barriers for new entrants in terms of cost advantage
* Information technology and the Internet being used to build a robust manufacturing process: The example of Luxum is an important point on information sharing a key attribute. It is leveraging the Internet to build reliability into its production processes. It is also interesting from another perspective. Luxum expects its supplier, Ralco Steels, to be transparent in its operations. For long, manufacturers have held details close to their heart and have been most reluctant to divulge cost and other process data. The break-up of the closely-integrated manufacturing set-up has necessitated the need for sharing of information between all parts of the supply chain. Changing customer preferences mean that the downstream customer interface has to communicate the changed circumstance to the most upstream of the suppliers. The Internet has emerged as the medium for this information transaction.
  Coming back to the case, Buckle was the creation of Vaman Vittals vision but the execution fell short. The decision to prevent Buckle from selling to other vehicle manufacturers insulated it from the market and impaired its capability to learn and innovate. Thus, Buckle was born emasculated. The mould into which Buckle was cast was also defective. Low-skilled and low-technology-oriented activities such as tailoring were situated in the same area as CNC machine operations. A process-oriented operation such as foam-making, which would benefit from a larger scale of operations, was built into the project. This outlines the fact that a tightly integrated framework of operations may subserve the objectives of the organisation. Make-or-buy decisions, in the past, had a tendency to be myopic. They have been typically led by rule of thumb, which stated:
 * Low value addition or non-critical buy.
 * Cheaper outside buy. 
  Manufacturing strategy played no part in ensuring that the vendor base capabilities were in sync with evolving customer expectations. The idea outlined by Dua sets out the broad framework of a supply chain web based on capabilities the model for the future.
   Duas vision of a seat manufacturer builds on sets of competencies, seeks to marry the different skill sets required in machining, tailoring and foam-making at the final stage assembly.
  Now, the different skill sets needed, suppliers would be free to develop at their own speed and cost, unhampered by the developments in other fields. This is the vision of a multi-tiered automotive industry and modular supplies.
  Suppliers would be encouraged to develop their faculties and become experts in their fields. And, like Dua, offer proactive suggestions to problems faced by their customers. In this evolved relationship, there would be service-level agreements between the vendor and the customer (as Dua suggests) and innovation would be the differentiator.
  The supply chain web would delegate manufacturing downwards the customer and the vehicle assembler at the apex of the pyramid, the module assemblers supplying to the car manufacturer, the component makers at the bottom of the supply pyramid supplying distinct components to the module assemblers. The vehicle assembler feeds information on demand through the module assembler to the component manufacturer. In turn, the component manufacturer keeps the vehicle manufacturer informed about production processes and parameters. Information flow would be quick and transparent.
  The component manufacturers climb up the learning curve would create the required entry barriers to new entrants in terms of cost advantage and innovation offered to the module assembler. In the next stage of evolution of the Supply Chain Web, component manufacturers would develop into pillars of expertise and would proactively manage innovations for the vehicle assembler addressing latent customer needs.
 Thus, the supply chain web becomes the source of competitive edge. Therefore, the choice before Vittal is to grasp at this new model of business and re-orient Patent. There is also another challenge before Vittal and Patent how to recast Buckle in the image of this new model. Their success in this endeavor will determine Patents standing in the new order.
Designed by: Epsilon Systems - SQ-80, Hauz Khas Appts, New Delhi-16. Phone: 685-3301, 685-0878, 98100-06431

Case Study 20


3 Apr 2000		 REBUILDING BRAND IMAGE
MEERA SETH
An upcoming rival is not all that karuna has to contend with. its image has been tarnished, and the hospitals own doctors dont seem to have a stake in its healthy future
  This is the second part of the case study on hospital brand management and image-building. The first part dealt with brand positioning.
  Dr Mahi Kunjamma Titus was startled.How long will you stare at that barium meal X-ray? asked her colleague, Dr Satish Pradhan, who had been standing there for the last six minutes. That stomach looks okay to me, so whats on your mind?
  Mahi turned around and looked at Pradhan, who was the nephrologist at Karuna Hospital & Nursing Home, where she was a gastroenterologist. Satish, we need to do some serious talking, she said finally. Headhunters have been calling me for a new state-of-the-art hospital coming up in the city (read: Recovery Clinics & Hospital).
  Send in your resume, said Satish, Recovery stands for all our dreams. I was expecting this to happen, after all, doctors like you arent easy to find...
  This isnt the time for humour, said Mahi. Remember, we joined Karuna to build such a dream. I think its time we did some talking. As Satish sat down, Mahi said: Recovery, I have heard, has hired a team of consultants to look into its marketing strategy. Remember, 10 years ago, this is exactly what we suggested for Karuna? But nothing happened. Now we have no choice, we must initiate some action. Karuna is a good hospital with good doctors, but poor management practices. Unfortunately, that has affected our image. Whats worse, no one even realises it. And when Recovery is up and running, Karuna will be forgotten. We must meet our chief executive, Tarun Arora, and insist on corrective measures to save Karuna.
  Over the next two days, they managed to get the support of six senior doctors and sought an appointment with Arora.
  You must have heard about the progress on Recovery Clinics... began Satish when they met the CEO. We need to work on Karunas image before Recovery goes on-stream.
  Arora was annoyed. This is great! he said angrily. A few disgruntled doctors find a rich boy to fund their adventure! How can a hospital be born on that kind of platform?
  The point is, said Satish, we must gear up to face competition. After all, they were a part of Karuna and a splinter group setting up competitive trade can be bad for us.
  Of course, everyone likes a new kid on the block, said Arora. But Recovery will not be able to hold out because a lot is involved in running a successful hospital. You can create capacity if you have money, but to get occupancy you need more. Because the health business is about resilience and quality.
  I think its time for us to stop denying the threat from Recovery and accept competition, said Dr Vilas Datar.    What competition? asked Arora angrily.Recovery? Healthcare is not a business where you come in the morning and expect to be cured by evening. It takes time, effort, diagnoses... We have been improvising for the last 10 years, look at our success rate, statistics. We have had important people visiting and staying here, foreigners and top-level corporate sorts. They have only had words of praise for us!
  True, said Satish, because there is no other place in this city which they can trust like Karuna. But when a new hospital with credentials and attributes like Recovery comes in... Interrupting him, Arora said: What is the Recovery team saying which we are not? Its not what they are saying, said Satish,
  its what they are doing and how they are going about it. Recovery is speaking the language of the times. They have recognised that health services will soon become a buyers market. For example, they have hired a consultant to run it like a business. They have studied the health market and targeted their consumers. Their marketing department is planning to position Recovery as a brand that offers quality service; they are not selling like we do, and they are confident that this is what they need to do. Recovery expects to convert corporate houses to its health system. They are talking of putting their corporate clients on a structured preventive healthcare and fitness plan...
