'Recovery is speaking the language of the times. They have recognised that health services will soon become a buyer's market. For example, they have hired a consultant to run it like a business'
   It is working on image-building, positioning and marketing the brand - something we have never done. One year from now, if you ask an average consumer what health service brands he can recall, hes sure to say Recovery. Because there isnt a brand called Karuna.
   Mahi who had been silent until now, said: Mr Arora, our image is not very good today. We have neither tried to build our image in a structured manner nor are we attempting damage control when things go wrong. We believe we are the best. Truth is, we are not living up to that image.
  We need to look at the agents of our image and work on them, continued Mahi, while simultaneously presenting Karuna as the trusted name in health services. But to get there, we have to accept that we have problems, our staff members are dissatisfied, our doctors are overworked, our systems are sluggish, our customers think we charge more. Also, we have become complacent and our quality is deteriorating.
  Rubbish, said Arora. Its all got to do with the newspapers splashing the death of Ajay Sobti. Until then, there was not even a whimper about our image! Lets understand one thing. Sobti died because of an ordinary problem, not neglect, as the press made it out to be!  
  But why did it hit the headlines? asked Datar. Because our hospital is popular, the question asked was If such a hospital could not do anything to save him, what about the rest? It had all the amenities, but still the patient died due to neglect. Karuna is insensitive to patient care, only money matters.   
  These are common to all in healthcare, said Arora. Deaths will happen, patients may not get cured, this is a hospital, not heaven, so the success rate wont be 100%. So, to blame us for neglect if a patient dies under normal circumstances is too much! 
  I agree, said Mahi. But unwittingly, that has tarnished our image. Deaths happen in Charubai Charitable Trust too, but they dont get talked about like Ajay Sobtis death did. And that is because we have a premium image, we charge high fees and we have this aura of exclusivity which naturally raises consumer expectation beyond limits.
   Karuna was caught in a high-cost, high-expectation trap. Typically, patients said: We are paying you so much so you should be able to do anything. But what they forget is that this is a hospital and it can have deaths too and unsuccessful treatments, said Arora. The hospital cannot guarantee success, it can only assure good care. We are not Gods. A good hospital does not mean that we can save every patient.
    But then a good hospital does not have ordinary problems like nursing staff going on strike! said Mahi.   That was not a strike, it was simply bad behaviour! said Arora. You must understand we also get difficult patients. More difficult than is warranted by their illness, mind you.
  As it happened, a patient got annoyed over the temperature of the water that was brought in for his sponging, which led to an argument when the nurse tried to reassure him. But when the doctor did not arrive, things got out of hand. In a fit of fury, he threw away his breakfast tray. Get him here or I will start breaking things, he yelled. The nurse got anxious and called in the security staff while she herself went to see the resident doctor about sedating the patient. The security had to hold the patient, for, by then, he had become unmanageable.
Now, please understand that these things do happen, said Arora. So often have we faced difficult patients!
  If you believe the patient was being difficult, why was the nurse sacked? asked Mahi.
  You know she misbehaved with the doctor in the patients presence! said Arora. Worse, she should not have called in security! That was the worst thing to do. The patient was a senior bureaucrat, what happens to our image of customer care?
  The fact is, said Vilas, our nurses are not trained to handle crisis. When the patient starts throwing things around, it was only natural for the nurse to call in security. 
She had the interest of the hospital in mind when she did it. She is not trained in human psychology to know what to do. Instead, we ended up having a strike, that was not good for our image either!   
  Now, read this instance along with Ajay Sobtis death-by-neglect story - and what we have is a poor image, said Satish.
  What does the press know about neglect? asked Arora angrily. What does it know about running a hospital? What do you know about the doctor who took the decision? It is easy to sit back and speculate, that is what media thrives on. Does anyone give credence to our side of the story?
  Because we are a premium hospital, a private hospital willy-nilly, the expectations are obviously high, insisted Satish. So, the patients family expects you will heal, since they have paid high fees.
  How can you say that? asked Arora. Often, there are situations where we cant heal a person, but that does not mean our medical skills are questionable! The public is being unfair. But as a hospital, how do I do damage control? So, here is a situation where some doctors have left to start their own hospital. And we fear theyll use unfair means to garner sympathy and support and build an artificial saliency for their hospital.
   What frightens me, said Satish, is that in the process of thumping their chests and selling their wares, they will badly tarnish our image.
  Then let us address the issues, said Mahi. We have similar problems that other hospitals have, nothing extraordinary. We have had our share of strikes, deaths, angry patients... Its an occupational hazard. But I also feel that we need to address the peripheral issues. Let us set Recovery aside for the moment. The fact is we have serious image issues and we need to address them. We have serious lapses in our human resource policies. We have serious issues in our marketing effort, rather we have no marketing effort. We need to address all these issues.At this stage, Mahi pulled out a sheet of paper and said: Before I came here, I met some senior doctors. Based on their observations, the issues are as follows: we are high on price and, consequently, seen as very commercial...
  Yes, we are high on price and there is a reason for that, said Arora. We are offering a premium quality of service which naturally means we are using sophisticated equipment which requires specialists to manage them. Our methods and processes are sophisticated... therefore, we are high-priced.
  Mahi continued: Unduly long hours of waiting and billing time, discharge very often not as planned, no patient management system...
  Let me add to that, said Satish. Unstructured delegation of critical tasks by senior doctors. Arora looked surprised. What exactly are you saying? he asked.
  I will explain, offered Satish. We have some specialist doctors and surgeons who are attached to us and also work outside. Consequently, their time is at a premium. These surgeons only perform the creative cut, and core surgery. Thereafter, they walk out of the operation theatre leaving the junior doctors to tidy up. We need to look at this again. An operation is not merely the surgical process. It is also about keeping blood pressure steady, monitoring patient trauma, pulse etc, in fact, upto six hours after the surgery.
  I feel the total delivery of the value is complete only when the patient has left the ICU. The fact is, that very practice has resulted in a quality problem. In Ajay Sobtis case, a post-operation fever and eventual death has raised a lot of questions. How the infection set in, at what stage it happened, that is debatable. One can go into all that and more, but the bottomline is there has been a perceived erosion of quality, said Satish.
'Yes, Karuna is high on price and there is a reason for that. we are offering a premium service which naturally means our methods and processes are sophisticated... therefore we are costly'
  Look, its not as if this has happened only to us, said Arora. Go to any hospital, they are facing it much more. It is because we are so quality-driven, we dont have many such instances. It is not because we had junior doctors attending to the case that this happened, although that is what the press reported. The infection could have been caused by anything...
  Mahi agreed. We work here, so we are aware how keenly the quality is being monitored. But unfortunately, in the medical profession, success hinges on the performing surgeon. And it is success that stands for quality. The flip side is, non-success is seen as poor quality and the flak for that is carried by the hospital. Since it is the image of our hospital that we are concerned with, you have to put down the questionable practices and quality issues so that it can be developed into key result areas.
   Arora now took off his glasses and wiped them in slow deliberate movements while a silence hung over the group. We can go on like this, you will keep asking me questions and I will keep defending, there will be no end to this. But let us understand, no one has been dishonest. Things happened the way they did. It is one perception that we neglected, it is our perception that we have exercised extra caution always. In any going concern there will be a threshold-level of care and concern that is delivered. Occasionally, things may go wrong, but we need to manage such situations too, said Arora.
  If this new hospital is going to come in and say it is better and we are negligent, then we also need to show before they arrive that we have our team in place.
   Isnt that what you are saying? asked Arora. We need to tell the world all over again what we are all about. How do we market an image in the service sector? We arent in the hotel business where having beautiful girls at the reception matters. We have terrific doctors and a great health delivery system. That is what we need to project.
  Then looking hard at the group before him, Arora said: I am not blind to what is going on here at Karuna. Our worst enemies are our own doctors who are saying to patients dont come to Karuna, it is expensive, get your tests done at other labs. That has to change. It is easy for doctors to say that because that does not cost them their revenue, but the hospital infrastructure not getting used means a loss for us! Then again, went on Arora, some of our doctors charge less in their private clinics. As a result, patients prefer to meet the doctors outside rather than here. This is a serious problem which impacts our perceived image, and our revenues...
    An embarrassed silence hung over the group, even as Arora said: So, I have seen some subtle competitive games at play. Maybe I am wrong, but there it is. For instance, if you put together the fact that our doctors charge less outside, that they run down other doctors within the system, that they ask patients to see them at their clinics, that they send them out to local labs for tests, what I hear is, We work at this hospital not because we are crazy about it, but because it is valuable to be attached to a big name and, however little, the money is good. But otherwise it is pretty crummy and we can get on with our business by seeing the more distant patients where they would like to see us. Now, what do you say to that?
   That means four things, said Mahi. One, our doctors are unhappy. Two, they do not see themselves as custodians or stakeholders of the hospital image. Three, they are taking business away. Four, there is a significant segment of customers who are finding it difficult to access Karuna regularly, they would like Karuna to be near their residence.
  So, I see a need to examine the setting up of satellite centres and to build team spirit and a cohesive vision among our doctors #and# our support staff. A sense of belonging. Let me give you an example. Someone who was here recently to see me was waiting at reception to be escorted to my room. She asked the lady there - now dont use this against her - who the best dentist was? The receptionist said we have A and B and C, but if you are serious about getting better, I would recommend C. Do you see this? At the point of sale, there is a dichotomy. These are people who have to be ecstatic about every doctor, because they have to be ecstatic about the hospital and what it stands for. Instead, she sends out a message that says, look we are not consistently the best, we also dabble in the mediocre.
  The agents of our image are not merely our doctors. Take our security staff who have often been accused of high-handedness. At the end of the day, our customers are not in a celebratory mood. They are either ailing patients or an anxious family. You cant be tough with them. Yelling at them for not having a pass or frisking their bags, or instructing them to leave their bags in the portico... all rankles of autocracy. Hotels dont treat you like that, do they?
  Its all very well to say this, said Arora, but you have no idea how unmanageable the visitors and family are. They sneak in bhelpuri, and then they blame the hospital for neglect! Or they walk in when the visiting hours are over and use pressure tactics to meet the patient. This is not a hotel or a hostel. Patients have fixed hours for resting, examination, investigation, eating... we cant have visitors ambling in all the time.
  So, there are numerous issues to look into, said Mahi. If our security staff are also stakeholders of our image, no doubt we need to empower them. But such empowerment must be preceded by training and environmental systems. Therefore, training in customer needs, HR and customer management at the security staff level. Which again brings us back to what we were saying at the beginning: our HR and training must be structured and driven by customer care and service.
  Arora nodded. I agree, he said. Theres a lot we need to do on our internal management, but simultaneously we need to draw up an image management programme. How do we renovate our image? he asked.

HOW TO CARVE OUT A NICHE

Ramnik Parekh is director of occupational health practice at Sedgwick Parekh Health Management.
 
  Karunas monopoly made its growth and survival effortless in the healthcare market. Despite being insensitive to consumer needs, it continued to hold high occupancy, till the news of Recovery shook its sense of false security. It now wants to review its image and relaunch itself. To rebuild its image, Karuna will have to analyse its present system of functioning for deficiencies and take corrective measures.
  
  The case reveals that not only is the organisational goal diffused but also the goals of departments and individuals working in Karuna. The CEOs vision is limited to high occupancy. Quality of service does not even feature as part of his objective.
  He assumes that high occupancy equals high customer satisfaction, not realising that this is only the result of Karunas monopoly. His defensive attitude indicates that he is hiding from reality. Rebuilding Karunas image will have to begin at this stage.
  Karuna needs to hire an HR consultant to redefine jobs and goals for every department and employee. It also needs a mission statement that is bought by all stakeholders. Low customer satisfaction reveals that people involved in delivering the service either do not know what service to deliver, nor their goals and roles in the organisation.
  Once goals and targets are established, patient service processes and protocols should be spelt out and communicated effectively to patient service staff. Training sessions on communication skills and customer satisfaction will facilitate smooth delivery of service.
  The marketing cell should transform the 'Commercial' image that Karuna has acquired to that of a caring and quality providor.
A review of Karunas HR policies will be crucial at this level. It must ensure that it has a happy workforce. No hospital can expect to deliver quality service through striking employees! The management must redefine its policy so that it is mutually beneficial to the employee and the organisation. For instance, the staff should be given an incentive for good performance. Karunas employees will be its strongest marketing tool.
  Similarly, the doctors will play an important role in marketing Karuna and building its image. At present, even the doctors are working to serve their own interests. They only wish to be associated with a big name. Management practice has fallen short at this level too. The doctors are referring patients outside, and are not committed to comprehensive patient care, which is explained by the unstructured delegation of critical tasks by senior doctors.
  These problems are serious and responsible for hampering Karunas image to a great extent. The management must review its policy about consultants if it has one or form one if it does not. The policy must state that consulting doctors have a business relationship with the hospital. A periodic review by the management of doctor-patient flow would allow it to assess doctors contributions to growth and assist in better decision making.
  The CEOs diplomacy will help in maintaining a partnering relationship with doctors. He must realise that one of the core competencies of Karuna is its doctors and it would be best to maximise this strength to build a better image. If the doctors speak well about the hospital there is no way that the patient will not get influenced. He must, therefore, not only evaluate the doctors performances but also take their inputs on the functioning of out patient and in patient departments. An open forum should be held to have constructive meetings with doctors, which will allow them to voice their opinions and decide on a plan of action. The open forum should benefit both doctors and management. This will help doctors build trust in the hospital and feel a sense of loyalty resulting in reduced referrals outside.
   Protocol must be established by the director of medical services (DMS) for management of critical cases. Regular reports on recovered and expired cases must be generated to establish an indicator of a doctors success rate. Medical audits of expired cases must be done by a team comprising the DMS, medical superintendent, pathologist, head nurse, admitting consultant and specialists. An infection-control committee should be set up, headed by a microbiologist. A report specifying infection rates among ICU patients should be generated and monitored to control infections and guarantee quality.
  Finally, the marketing department should gear up to improve Karunas image. It should actively transform the commercial image that Karuna has acquired to that of a provider who is caring and offers quality. Marketing strategies will have to be designed keeping this objective in mind. It could hold free mobile health check-up camps in the neighbourhood or establish an evening OPD for few hours for poor patients under the CMO and the medical superintendent. The marketing department will have to focus on changing the consumers perception of Karuna.
  Additionally, since the media has influenced Karunas image, a public relations officer must be appointed to deal with the media for damage control and publish information that is conducive to Karunas image in a proactive manner.
  What will also help Karuna to revamp its image from being commercial to caring is a rationalisation of prices. It must be ensured that pricing is not arbitrary and justifies the quality of service. A utilisation review of expensive equipment may also help in this regard. For, even though equipment and infrastructure are expensive, high volumes would make it easy for Karuna to break even and make a profit. A brand is not built by just talking about how good it is, as rightly pointed out in the case. It is built by commitment to goals and actually delivering the expected service.

NEED FOR A REALITY CHECK

  Sharanita Keswani is the chief operating officer of Quadra Advisory, a strategic marketing consultancy.
  Karuna, first and foremost, needs to recognise that as a health service provider, it too is operating in a market environment. Just as for any other business, Karuna must understand that it has, most importantly, customers whose needs must be satisfied and competition that is emerging rapidly. This needs to be understood and internalised by the top management. Before addressing the issue of image-building, Karuna needs to first reassess its business strategy and open its mind to the fact that there is a world beyond just having good equipment and good doctors.
  It needs to develop a strong proposition and an action plan to deliver the value. It should try to pre-empt competition rather than be complacent and let the latter encroach on its territory.
  Karunas chief executive, Tarun Arora, appears to be defensive, even as he denies the threat of competition. He must first hear what his team has to say and then develop a methodology for repositioning his brand. He must realise that Karuna is a brand which has a value, attributes, image, and a position in the consumers mind. Karuna must first focus on building the brand and then communicate its position and build its image.
  I would like to suggest a methodology for addressing the problem. Arora should begin by having a brainstorming session with the top executives in the company, asking them to table their aspirations for Karuna. The objective: arrive at a common goal, develop a clear value proposition and then define a desired brand position for the Karuna brand. This means that the team should be able to articulate the value that they propose to deliver to their customers and hypothesise on the reasons why customers should go to Karuna.
  Karuna's top management would need to modify its brand proposition to ensure that it is compelling and competetive.
  Once the team has established the desired aspiration for the business, Arora should appoint a professional to conduct a study among its stakeholders to determine their perception and image of Karuna. Stakeholders include various groups who have an interest in the brand and impact its performance or are important for its perception in the market. These include employees, customers, government and shareholders. A reality check on the stakeholders perceptions is what would be essential to convince Arora that there is a need to initiate action. Arora needs to develop out-of-the-box thinking. He is product-focused and not customer-focused, and hence, is continually talking about the quality of equipment and doctors.
  It would, thereafter, be useful for Karuna to have its proposed hypotheses on brand proposition and desired position tested among a sample of customers and employees. The perception of the key stakeholders regarding the credibility and relevance of Karunas proposition must be understood. Based on the differences that emerge between consumer perception and the desired brand position, Karuna will be able to identify the key perceptual shifts that should be focused on in communication.
  Karunas top management would need to modify its brand proposition to ensure that it is compelling and competitive. An example of a relevant core proposition for Karuna may be as follows: Karuna is the only hospital that offers total patient care it promises to offer top-of-the-line patient care which is a combination of world-class treatment, diagnosis and sensitivity in a cheerful environment.
   Before freezing its proposition Karuna should answer the following questions: is the brand proposition unique and differentiated from competition? Is the brand proposition in line with the managements capabilities? Is the brand proposition relevant and desired by customers? 
  To achieve its desired position, Arora needs to exhibit strong leadership qualities and would need to develop an action plan. This would entail the following: (a) formation of a dedicated core team that is motivated, united and committed to a common goal enlisting the support and motivating his team would probably be Aroras biggest challenge, (b) reassessing compensation, roles and responsibilities of its staff to ensure that they are motivated, and (c) developing a plan to ensure that the internal product is truly strengthened to deliver the selected brand proposition.
  This may entail focusing on obtaining specific international technology tie-ups, equipment, processes and training. This should go beyond the proposed offering of patient-teams planned by the key competitor Recovery, (d) redesigning the interiors of the hospital and perhaps providing enhanced recreational facilities to be in line with the core proposition of providing a cheerful and friendly environment, (e) organising training for all medical and non-medical staff to ensure that they are aligned towards delivering the chosen brand proposition, (f) establishing a feedback system to ensure that theres consistency in performance, and (g) planning a communication programme to deliver the desired brand position and focus on the perceptual shift required in the consumers mind.
  The communication plan should comprise a public relations programme which focuses on the specifics of Karunas strengths its core proposition and how it is unique and different from others, specific facilities and equipment; training doctors and support staff; the new interiors and recreational facilities. This should be supported by a focused doctor-contact programme doctors can be periodically met to update their knowledge on any innovations in the hospital. Karuna can also take the initiative of setting up a website that provides information on innovations in medicine. This would be in line with and support its desired image of technical superiority. Above all, for a service of this nature, word of mouth is the best form of communication. Hence, ensuring that the patients total experience is complete from technical competency to comfort and total care would be essential. 
Designed by: Epsilon Systems - SQ-80, Hauz Khas Appts, New Delhi-16. Phone: 685-3301, 685-0878, 98100-06431

Case Study 21


6 MARCH 1999		 OF GROWTH PANGS AND PLANS
MEERA SETH
Should Chennur tea look at an entirely new business for growth and move away from its tea business? And if it does, should it set up a new company?
  Prem Warrier sat up with a start. He was listening to his wife who had just returned from visiting their son in Chicago, even as he was preoccupied with a decision that faced him at work. She said: "The only thing I didn't like was his apartment; he calls it a studio. He cooks, sleeps, eats and even studies in that room. 
  How can you get focus? His books, clothes, soup packets and CDs all co-exist! I wonder if he will get his doctorate in the midst of a whirring washing machine, stale dishes...''  Prem felt a familiar confusion return. Everything was somehow linked to his company's new business plans, even the apartment his son lived in. From Chicago to Chennai, it was a long chain of chaos, he rued. Chennur Tea, the Rs 300-crore Chennai-based tea company where he was the head of corporate strategy, was contemplating change. And after last week's meeting with his management consultant, Riaz Latif, the change seemed much bigger than he had anticipated. For not only was Riaz suggesting moving away from the tea business -- something Chennur was reluctant to do -- he was even redefining the business model, almost stating that it was critical to success in the new business.
  Chennur had been looking at growth avenues, but couldn't decide between improving its existing tea business with packaging innovations and convenience, and investing in ready-to-eat foods like a variety of microwaveable dals, curries, vegetables and rice. Growing the tea business found immediate favour with most, while managing director Deepak Rai was determined to enter new businesses.
  "The question is what kind of growth are we seeking?" asked Prem. "We can innovate forever since 'drinking tea' isn't likely to go out of fashion. But how much will we grow? And where will the growth come from? New users? New markets? Will that be enough?" "Let's understand one thing, we are in the beverages business," said Anup Tandon, the marketing head. "The entry of lifestyle brands has led to some loss of market share. Clearly, lifestyle aspirations are now more critical to the consumer and we need to address that. Growth will come from staying close to the consumer and engineering innovations that will improve the value delivery to the consumer. That's the only way we can align our investments with consumer needs."
  As the debate warmed up, it became apparent that the argument was not in favour of entering the ready-to-eat foods segment. Anup suggested that they innovate on tea and take it beyond current blends -- iced tea, flavoured tea, and convenience packaging that would hone their skill sets. "What competencies do we have in foods anyway?'' he asked. ''Haven't we seen enough and more debris by the wayside, of companies that tried to enter foods and are languishing?" "Besides," said Sumeet Panday, the chief financial officer, "how are you going to justify investments in areas where consumer needs don't even exist? Enough and more's going into competitive warfare and fine-tuning the present blends."
  But Prem who had warmed up to the new idea said: "I feel we must leapfrog into areas that address next generation needs." "Tell me, how many people eat out of foil packets?" asked Anup. "Let's not forget Indian food habits. Which housewife would want to restrict cooking to a mere snip of the scissors and pressing a button?"
  "As long as you continue fulfilling the needs of your existing consumer set, you won't be able to pull Chennur out of the low performance trap that it is in," said Rai.
  It was at this stage that Riaz cast his ballot in favour of an innovative shift. "Attempts at packaging innovation etc will, at best, help you gain some market share and perhaps add to your sales," he said, "but not a quantum leap.
  If we want to take the company up many rungs, you must try and think of innovation in the broader area of foods. No doubt you will fine-tune your strategy in tea, innovate on packaging of and project a demand that will be up 15 times in five years. But what next? Yes, you can keep on nibbling at the competition, converting users -- which you have been doing anyway. And each time sales goes up by a few percentage points, it will give you an aura of long-term success. But you have to start looking at real growth, and that, I feel, lies in anticipating future trends and slotting yourself into the expected or anticipated future needs or lifestyle of your consumer set."
  Prem was willing to play along for the time being, but he said: "Whatever future trends you may talk of, it must have some bearing on what the market has revealed," he said. "Therefore, it may be okay to venture into areas like cereals and even pasta or soup, which have been fairly successful with consumers. These are days of intermediates, ready-to-cook or ready-to-use. But where is the market for ready-to-eat foods apart from potato chips and namkeen?" 
  "But where was the market for potato chips when Lehar launched its brand?" asked Riaz. "Even then, the market said why fear. I agree the concept will appeal to few initially and that sales will be limited. Was there a market for garlic paste when Dabur launched it? It is possible that the consumer even today does not rate its performance attributes highly, but we have seen in many cases that the product does improve at a rapid rate and even replaces existing habits or perceptions of a category."
'You have to start looking at real growth, which in anticipating future trends and slotting yourself into the expected or anticipated future needs or lifestyle of your consumer set'
  Staying close to the consumer's current needs could blinker a marketer from innovating, felt Riaz. "But once a player decides to look at the future, he sets in motion change, by which time it is too late for existing players to retain their markets," he said. "Take the automobile industry. In an era of least competition, it stayed secure in the belief that it had created sufficient entry barriers and new players wouldn't come in. It didn't even believe that a change was worth the effort. But it paid the price by allowing Maruti to shape the emerging market. And Maruti was offering a better product at a lower price."
  Riaz felt there would always be scope for improving the current needs of consumers, but to see that as an investment for growth is where he disagreed. "The past is always a reassurance of what worked well then," he said. "So, you could continue tweaking your packaging, and offering multiple blends. But look at the bigger picture: there is a discernible shift in lifestyle and aspirations. Decode that correctly and anticipate the shape of things to come. So, why not ready-to-eat foods?"
  "But there has to be market, a felt demand, a need," said Anup. "After all we have to fit ourselves to consumer needs; that is the best way to have a winning product. The felt need and demand is for intermediates. Salt, atta, gravies, soups etc There is a market, albeit small, but it is possible to estimate a demand, target market potential and revenue streams. But something whose need has not been felt, doesn't even exist -- how do we justify an investment on that? How do we even know if these costs can be recovered?"
  "Of course," said Riaz, "a maverick new category which is futuristic will not proffer attractive financial yields. And yes, costs will be huge. But that is the price you have to commit to for real growth.
"But tell me," continued Riaz, "what kind of information about the market was available to Reebok when it entered India? Was there a market for technically-engineered shoes, to begin with? Who knew? But there was aspiration, there was an interest in fitness. But was it enough to justify an investment of X million rupees which Reebok put in? What, if at all, was available was the size of the total footwear market, which included the market for Hawaii chappals! "And again, if Reebok had stayed close to the existing consumer need of functional shoes at a cheap price, there wouldn't have been a market for its technically-engineered shoes! "Conventional market research is of no use in such a situation, for no market existed for technically-engineered shoes. 
    So, Reebok had to 'develop' market information such as: 'Who could be the potential consumer? What did such a consumer look for in terms of value or performance delivery?
  What were his aspirations and where in that hierarchy could a need gap be identified to step in with a pair of Reebok? 'To my mind, Reebok developed the data by actually going ahead and putting its shoes on the shelves, believing in the compatibility of its value delivery with the aspirations and trends in consumer lifestyles and, importantly, whatever be the product, consumers were aspiring to improve the way they lived.
  "And that is how it sought to give shape and contours to a non-existing market and focused on the few variables that were available, albeit without any profile," said Riaz. "But it was one laborious move after another. But in that phase, Reebok learnt what consumers wanted. Uniquely, it was the consumer who came back and said that he did not want high price. But he said something finally: 'Give us good-looking shoes like these that are affordable.' A point of communication was reached. And when the consumer starts talking to you, you know you have a place in the market."
  "It was then the second phase began," continued Riaz, "when it redesigned its shoes, did some spectacular value engineering and adapted its product to the evolving mindset of consumers in India. That naturally brought down costs and, hence, the price. At that point, I think, Reebok started setting targets and calculating budgets. Not before it entered the market." "Kellogg's graph is almost identical," said Riaz, after a pause. "It went ahead in a market that gave no clues on its size. But Kellogg put the cereal on the shelves, threw in trial incentives, watched consumption and buying habits, improvised, watched, and created data, which it then set to improve. Now tell me, how could Kellogg or Reebok believe there would be no market simply because there was no market? If consumers were buying age-defiant creams, how was a packet of cereal any different? If you were eating parathas at breakfast, you were also using chandan to preserve your skin. The fundamental truth was that the consumer aspired for improvement."
  The following two days, Prem and Atul were involved in intense debate. Yes, they felt, the market lay in anticipating future needs. The idea of entering ready-to-eat foods seemed less daunting now, although they felt the typical anxiety that preceded a vital move. Many new entrants had blazed the trail of new categories, but as Prem said: "I wouldn't go by their examples alone. After all, some were seeking new markets. But yes, we can find encouragement in what Dabur, Lehar and RCI did. These are firms like ours which sought to grow beyond their existing businesses. And, if both Reebok and Kellogg succeeded in breaking the myths behind their expected failure, I'd think it was also because they were multinationals with deep pockets."
  "Wrong," said Riaz, when Atul repeated that argument. "Because they were both start-ups and not divisions of an existing company." Atul was surprised, even as Riaz said: "And that is the other aspect of a new business. If you have decided to enter the ready-to-eat foods segment, understand that its success will depend on the business model too. Because a new product category, one that is pathbreaking or even different from the mainstream product category of an existing company, needs an environment where there are no benchmarks, no history, no common ledgers, where small wins create energy and not invite ridicule, and provide focus and drive."
  "That might explain why Cadbury failed in ice-creams, why Marico's fabric care business may be slow. So the point I am leading to is: if you decide to enter the ready-to-eat foods business set up let it be a separate firm and not run it through a division of Chennur,'' said Riaz. And that led to a fresh round of agitation and anxiety in Prem's mind. "Where is the need for that?" he asked. "As it is entering a new category, it's going to strain our bottomline; we hardly need additional costs to render the idea unviable. I am not sure I agree with you," he said.
  "Let me explain,'' said Riaz. "It is difficult for established firms to undertake new ventures and sit patiently through the start-up stages. Mind you, the new business is not going to reap revenues in year one or two or three. It is going to be a slow, tedious process of trial, error and repairs. The tea division will not sit back and watch quietly. There will be the tug and pull of resource allocation and you will unwittingly expect the same financial performance standards from your new business which apply to the more successful tea business. What will happen in the process is that you will set tough targets for the new business, harass its every move and curb its independence.
  "Maybe," said Prem. "But pre-warned is forearmed. We can bear this in mind and ensure complete independence for the new business. But setting up a separate company doesn't make sense. Firstly, we do not even know if the new business is going to pay for its upkeep. If it is a division, it can readily use our existing market research, and financial services facilities, the administration and even the sales team. Those are huge costs: it does not make sense to duplicate these services and costs. Once the new business starts to reap revenues, it can decide..."
'A division divides vision, and splintered divisions do not sanction success. A new business requires a distinctive vision. Also, it will have lower profit margins than a mainstream business'
  "I agree," said Anup. "After all isn't Hindustan Lever Ltd (HLL) running five distinctive businesses as divisions? It's a question of good company practices and ensuring focus. In fact, that's the reason Walls ice-cream is able to hold out. If it had been a separate company, it would have folded up by now. As a division, the support costs are borne by a common pool."
  But Riaz wouldn't agree. "It is risky, let me warn you," he said. "Besides you are not HLL. That company has evolved and grown so many businesses that it has covered a better part of the learning curve. But it has had its share of stumbling too. For example, consider why Lipton did not succeed in its various forays into foods like pasta and fruit-based beverages.
  So, it is not just start-ups -- even existing companies planning a different business need the environ of a separate organisation. An established business thinks and behaves differently from a new business. Thus, when it entered ice-creams, Cadbury may have believed it was dealing with an established market. 
   There may have been a market for ice-creams, but to go out there and create a fever for your specific brand, even create a parlour habit, was something new. That attitude of the mainstream business has a rub-off effect on the new division and I suspect that the Dollops team too thought it was there to enhance corporate growth when, in fact, it was there to explore a new product category. Besides when you are on support, you can't feel only for your division, you harbour feelings of anxiety for the support the mainstream business is giving you.''
  The advantage of starting alone, felt Riaz, was the ability to develop a distinctive vision for the new business without the tedium of constant referrals to the mainstream business' success. "A division 'divides vision' and splintered visions don't guarantee success," he said. "Your new business will have lower profit margins than your mainstream business, a different consumer set to cater to, maybe a different distribution strategy. Left alone, it will develop the initiative to look at the market from a zero-base. Combined with the tea business and an access to a common resource pool, it will willy-nilly focus on cost-benefits, resource optimisation and the path of least resistance."
   Ready-to-eat foods was an entirely different business with different consumer needs, felt Riaz, which necessarily demanded a separate organisation."Examples abound, but it is for you to learn from their experiences," said Riaz. "Even when there is a market for the new category, you still need to set up a separate organisation. Why did Glaxo eventually exit its foods business? Foods was a division of this large pharmaceuticals company, which was geared for ethical selling and not consumer selling. Reckitt, on the other hand, has set up its pharmaceuticals business as a separate firm -- Reckitt Piramal. It knows there is a market for over-the-counter products, yet it has chosen to set up a separate company. It will work since the visions are independent," said Riaz.  
  "Does that mean that each time we want to enter a new business we have to float separate companies?" asked Prem. "Well, I suppose as long as they are strategically different from the mainstream business," suggested Anup. "But I also think that once they have grown they should be merged with the mother firm. Because by then, their independent costs will get to be economically unviable and, in fact, an advantage is to be had in the economies of scale the merger will proffer."
  Riaz grew thoughtful. "That I am not sure of," he said. "It will depend on the nature of the business. But essentially, the same reasons for their separation will also operate in their merger. Those reasons don't disappear. If sharing resources is the theme, you will be back in the argument room over budgets, share of resources and rationalisation of product lists." 
   Prem disagreed. "HLL has merged its foods and skin care firms into its mainstream soaps and detergents firm. Surely, you aren't suggesting that will be sub-optimal?" he asked.
  Riaz dodged that one. It was difficult to answer that, even as he knew that for a multi-product multi-business firm with such a fine-tuned business model, this was unlikely. Instead, he said: "Every business has its own vision and lifespan, its own strengths and weaknesses. Within that, there will always be a re-arrangement of business focus. What is mainstream today may become secondary tomorrow. What if your ready-to-eat foods business outgrows your tea business?" he asked.
  This was one big move; the choice of business was no more an issue with Atul; what worried him was Riaz's suggestion that if it was a business clearly distinctive from the core business, it should be handled out of a separate organisation.

TREAD CAUTIOUSLY

Utpal Sengupta is president of ITC AgroTech.

  The debate among Prem, Riyaz, Anup and others at Chennur Tea throws up several interesting issues. Before dealing with whether Chennur's new venture should be a division or a separate company, let us look at some other issues: Sticking to the knitting: It is not clear what skill-set Chennur expects to bring to the ready-to-eat foods category. Growth aspirations need to be based on reality. There is no evidence of a SWOT analysis having been done by Chennur, and if at all it was conducted, whether it would support the bold new initiative the company is contemplating.
  Arguments that move back and forth are personal viewpoints or prejudices rather than objective facts. A new venture needs to be based on market analysis, consumer research and hard-headed evaluation of competencies. As a matter of fact, when Anup asks for evidence to justify the new venture, his is a voice in the wilderness. By then, the others in the group are 'warming up to the new business idea'.
  Staircase vs leap-frog: Dramatic shifts in business strategy may sound exciting but in today's competitive market place, may not make prudent business sense. Deepak and Prem, spurred on by Riyaz, are getting ready to bet on the company based on the unsubstantiated hope that the ready-to-eat food category will change the company's fortunes. Research shows that such dramatic shifts in business strategy are fraught with risk and seldom succeed. Successful growth companies engineer changes in strategy one step at a time. They take the 'staircase' approach to growth rather than hope to seize it in one leap. With each small step, they add to their learning and competencies. Such steps, taken frequently, add up to a giant leap in a reasonably short time.
A new business venture needs to be  based on market analysis, consumer research and hard-headed evaluation of companies
  Riyaz's examples in this context of the new ventures attempted by Kellogg and Reebok are misleading. These are Indian entities of large multinationals that have intimate understanding of their respective businesses and product categories. They have a great staying power and are in the process of creating market positions for themselves in India for the future. The strategy adopted by these companies can't serve as a beacon for Chennur.
  There are, however, several Indian companies that have come up with innovative new products and succeeded beyond their wildest dreams. Nirma, Dabur, Ranbaxy are such examples. In today's IT-dominated world, the examples of Wipro, Infosys and Satyam are rapidly becoming legendary. However, it should be remembered that these are examples of companies that succeeded. Several companies with innovative new products sank without a trace even though they may have shown great promise at the beginning.
  Does this mean Chennur should not attempt any innovations? Certainly not, but it needs to do its homework first. It should look for ways to reduce the risks of entering a new business by following a more thorough process to understand the new category. This is not the time or place to make a case on the basis of consumer research. However, it needs to be said that it is wrong to suggest that 'conventional market research is of no use in such situations'.
  Division vs independent company: The question of how to structure the new business, as a division or an independent company, puts form before substance. Given the nature of the new venture and its 'fit' with Chennur's existing business, success or failure is likely to be determined by the intrinsic soundness of the business plan rather than anything else. Having said that, it is worthwhile examining the issues raised by Riyaz in support of a separate company.
  'Mindset' is certainly a key factor that determines success. However, to say that the right mindset cannot be created within a divisional set-up flies in the face of accumulated experience. The case itself cites several examples of disparate businesses successfully co-existing as divisions within a single company.
  Another celebrated case is that of General Motors' Saturn division. When the automobile company wanted to do something truly radical in what was otherwise seen as a bureaucratic organisation, it created Saturn. Other organisations habitually spin off 'skunk works'. These consist of small, highly creative and motivated groups of people working towards focused objectives, while remaining within the organisational umbrella. They create a separate identity for themselves, distinct from the main organisation. Intrapreneurship is the name of the game.
  In this age of mergers and acquisitions, evidence suggests that there are several fiscal and operational advantages of operating within one organisation with a divisional set-up. It has also been seen that it is possible in such a set-up to provide autonomy for each business so as not to adversely impinge on the achievement of its objectives.
  As a matter of fact, it can be argued that shorn of administrative responsibilities (which would be inescapable in a separate company), a divisional structure helps in realising goals because it leaves people to focus solely on the main task at hand. Besides, a division of a large company is more likely to attract high quality talent, critical to a new venture, as compared to a struggling start-up.
  It has to be recognised. However, the same solution may not fit all. The answer to the question faced by Prem and his colleagues can only emerge from a dispassionate evaluation of the benefits and risks of a truly liberated mindset in a separate company. This will need to be weighed against the synergy benefits that can be achieved in a divisionalised structure in their specific case.

ENGINEERING GROWTH

SANJAY SEHGAL is vice-president (marketing), Nestle

  The Chennur Tea case throws up issues pertaining to how an organisation should plan and engineer long-term growth. As the saying goes: "The art of progress is to preserve order amid change and to preserve change amid order." This is, essentially, the dilemma that Chennur faces.
  Two fundamental decision points emerge in the case of Chennur:
  How should the organisation ensure growth? Should it focus on its current operations and competencies or does it need to enter new categories to ensure long-term relevance?
  What business model should it follow? Should it operate in the current set-up or should Chennur be split into two distinct companies?
  Has the current business reached saturation levels, offering no opportunities for growth? This is unlikely. The product category may be mature but Chennur has to examine its operations in the light of its competitive position. It seems to be a small or medium-sized operation in a huge category. While rapid growth is unlikely for Chennur, it must retain its interest in tea or face loss of consumer franchise and share erosion. Innovation and renovation can help Chennur freshen up its product offering(s) within the tea category and drive growth through share gains and expansion into new locations or segments.
It is important for chennur to create an environment and a mindset that encourages growth within the organisation. 
 Remember: "There are no mature brands or products. Only mature managers." What should be the basis for determining new opportunities? Business opportunities can't be determined in isolation of the organisation's ability to tap them. An in-depth understanding of the organisation's strengths or weaknesses and core competencies must be carried out before entering new categories. The consultant forgets that before diversifying into new business, Chennur has to go through mild introspection.
  The management needs to consider what Chennur's current competencies are. For instance, does it have brands that can be extended naturally into other segments? What kind of distribution muscle does it have? Does it have an edge in understanding consumer needs in certain business categories? Does it have technical expertise or buying muscle in handling certain raw materials or commodities?
  It needs to identify which "high potential" categories it is best equipped to enter. Next, it needs to look at the current players operating in that segment and whether Chennur will have the financial strength to grab or develop business. Is the opportunity identified by the consultant -- ready-to-eat foods -- realistic and relevant for Chennur? Food, in many ways, reflects the core of society's value system. While the Indian consumer is willing to experiment at the periphery, the core meal remains unchanged -- more-or-less traditional, relying on fresh produce, not packaged convenience foods.
  Ready-to-eat foods offer convenience, but at the same time, expect a change in mindset of the economy, and freshness-conscious Indian housewife. Moreover, ready-to-eat food products often threaten her role as the family's provider and efficient manager of the kitchen. 
  Hence, the evaluation of this business appears impatient and does not look at some of the larger issues at play. Second, on what area of competency will Chennur enter ready-to-eat foods? The more logical choices would be other commodity food products (Chennur might be adept at handling low value-added, high volume, low margin products) or beverages (it may have consumer insights or distribution strengths through long-term experience). What it perhaps doesn't have is the technological expertise or consumer insight in the area of culinary products to move into ready-to-eat foods.
  Given all these issues, one is almost tempted to rephrase the question: "Why not ready-to-eat foods?" to "why ready-to-eat foods?" Chennur will be able to generate long term, sustainable growth only by leveraging its current organisational strengths and expertise.
   First, Chennur must innovate and renovate within its current business area to ensure that it remains competitive in tea and drives real internal growth.
Within its existing business territory, it needs to identify changes and trends in consumer behaviour that will offer growth opportunities (for example, ice tea or flavoured tea). When it comes to expanding its operations, the cost of entering a new category can't be left undefined. New business should strengthen the parent firm by offering operating synergies. In short, Chennur must stay focused on where its competencies lie. That doesn't mean it should only market tea. It must continue to innovate or renovate its tea business and enter areas where it can leverage its experience. Is a separate company necessary to provide focus and the environment for growth? The consultant's view that when it comes to entering a new category we need to carefully nurture the new business venture is well taken. However, we must not swing to the opposite extreme wherein we increase complexity of operations and risk the core business.
  What is critical is to create an environment and a mindset within the organisation that encourages growth. In order to ensure that new business is given the opportunity to grow, we need to develop an independent vision and business structure for it. But its vision must be derived from the larger vision of the parent organisation. The relevant business model, therefore, is not the creation of an independent company but setting up an independent operation within the parent company, and a change in organisational mindset to one that supports growth and nurtures the new operation. That means:
  * A revitalised organisation that exploits its current strengths and is focused on its core competencies.
  * Is governed by a clear vision that defines its goals.
  * That invests in its future by drawing on its past.
Designed by: Epsilon Systems - SQ-80, Hauz Khas Appts, New Delhi-16. Phone: 685-3301, 685-0878, 98100-06431

Case Study 22


20 Mar 2000		 GETTING A FIX ON POSITIONING
MEERA SETH
For Recovery to make a mark, it has to be positioned as a hospital brand which is customer-responsive. But then how does one position a health service?
  This is a two-part case study on positioning and image management in the health sector. In this issue, we look at the positioning issues of Recovery Clinics, while the next case will examine the image problems at Karuna Hospitals, some of whose doctors have left to set up Recovery.
  Pulin Kayastha was simply amazed. He had seen all forms of hostility and marketing warfare in the consumer goods industry, but to see similar warfare in the health industry fascinated him. Clearly, he told himself, doctors had found consumers in their patients. At least, that's what the concept note sent by Dr Ajit Varman, country manager of Recovery Clinics & Hospitals, seemed to indicate. Varman and seven other senior doctors had left Karuna Nursing home and hospital to set up Recovery, which, as the note said, "...would be entirely devoted and dedicated to customer responsiveness."
  Pulin was a management consultant and was recommended to Recovery by the marketing director of Regrow Pharma, a large pharmaceuticals company in Mumbai. In fact, the suggestion to set up Recovery came from a non-resident patient, Dinesh Shah, who was undergoing treatment at Karuna's large speciality hospital in central India. It was in the course of his interaction with the doctors that Shah sensed their unhappiness with the system. This prompted him to suggest the idea of Recovery. Varman had joined Karuna 10 years ago, assured of a challenging career in a hospital that was promising to be different and driven. But over time, disillusionment set in as Karuna's image and response to the environment diluted its equity.
    "Now that we have decided to do this, we do not want to repeat old mistakes," Varman had told Pulin during their first meeting. "Having worked at Karuna, we can see its weaknesses and why it's losing saliency. Ten years ago, when it was established, we believed it was going to add value to our careers. We became a part of it because we were told that we were specialists who would bring exclusivity to the hospital. But soon, the focus shifted to fetching business and revenues. The management started hiring specialists and private practitioners, offering them cabins and consultancy arrangements at Karuna. The strategy was that these doctors would bring in their patients and use the infrastructure so that the hospital would start earning money." 
  The Karuna management wanted to derive short-term benefits, than gradually build up clientele. But the strategy, it appeared, did not pay off.
 As Varman said: "Because there were many doctors and the business was not large enough in the first few months. Consequently, competition for business became cut-throat between doctors.''
  Despite modern amenities, state-of-the-art systems and numerous doctors, the image of the hospital was that of being too commercial -- a fallout of the stigma of being a private hospital. It was common among doctors to slot every hospital under either category -- a place where you get neglected to death or a place where you get researched to death. Karuna earned a new label -- a place where you got cross-referred to death, for doctors at Karuna slowly took to enhancing each others' earnings as they sent patients back and forth to doctors and specialists. At the end of his diagnoses, the patient ended up paying a huge amount of money for his treatment.
  According to Varman, the high-cost, high-expectation syndrome hit the hospital. The initial promise of exclusivity and quality was lost. Karuna had the best doctors, but after-care was abysmal. With a view to keeping costs low, nurses, housekeeping staff and even the front office staff were hired cheap, training was virtually absent, and no attention was paid to end-user needs.
  Kayastha could see Recovery trying to be all that Karuna was not and desiring not to be all that Karuna was. If Kayastha thought Varman was hiring him for routine systems design and a patient management manual, he was mistaken.
   For Varman said: "We want to get Recovery's positioning platform right and work on a sound marketing plan. What we want you to do is to help us build this brand, help ordinary doctors like us understand what brand-building entails and how it is managed in a service industry."
  The following day, armed with his primary understanding of Recovery's concept, Kayastha met Varman. "There are a number of issues that have cropped up since I sent you my concept note," said Varman. "While we have an overall strategy in place, I feel our success will hinge on two key factors: a) our organisation structure and b) our marketing strategy.
  "As I mentioned earlier, Shah has taken three buildings of a charitable hospital trust where his father is the chief trustee. Fundamentally, the buildings are okay, we have renovated the plumbing and electrical systems. The equipment has been ordered and we have already hired a top drawer bio-medical engineer. In another six months, the place should be ready to function.
'For recovery to avoid the mistakes that karuna made and build its brand, it will have to get the positioning platform right and work on a sound marketing plan'
  "But we are faced with a dilemma on what our organisational structure should be. The one at Karuna was not delivering. I have been reading modern management thinkers, but I still have some issues to contend with. At the very outset, who should be the CEO of Recovery? Should a doctor be at the helm or a professional manager, or like most hospitals, should he be from medical services? Then again what should be the role of the director of medical services (DMS)? I believe their roles overlap and may create problems. So, do we need two functionaries or can they be rolled into one?"
  "What is the role of the DMS?" asked Kayastha.
  "A DMS decides on the equipment, medical services to be provided, deals with problems that doctors confront," said Varman. "In short, he is a strategist and a business manager. At Karuna, the DMS has the medical department heads like pathology, radiology etc reporting to him, while these men also have a dotted line to the CEO.
  "The CEO is the man the DMS reports to, as do all other functional heads, like the head of foods and beverages, marketing and finance. Some hospitals have also created a super head called the managing director to whom the CEO reports. Will this structure help? Because I do feel that having a DMS and a CEO creates two parallel power structures and disharmony, not to mention unnecessary approvals and sanctions." "Why do you say that?" asked Kayastha.
  "Let me explain," said Varman. "At Karuna, we had a DMS who was a doctor, a CEO who was from the hotel industry. What we found was that if the doctors wanted a new medical service in place, and the DMS did not approve, they would negotiate with the CEO, the system allowed for that. Once he said yes the DMS had no say in it. If you look at their roles, both have a different orientation. The CEO looks at numbers, not consumer needs. Numbers should not always be the criterion for setting up a new department. For instance, number logic will tell you that the investment should go into cardiology for that is my profit centre, whereas the DMS, based on his knowledge of health needs, might feel that money should go into, say, orthopedics -- so if you leave it to the CEO, you might get what is good today, and not what will be needed in the next 10 years. So, there were always these clashes."
 "Should doctors," continued Varman, "manage departments or should professional managers do it? Should the F&B cell have a doctor above the F&B manager, for instance?"Karuna, explained Varman, had an F&B man who was brought in from the hotel industry. "He comes from an industry where creativity and imagination is the USP, but for hospitals, it is nutrition. I am wondering whether it was a bad idea to take someone from an industry where the function has such a radically different slant, rip all that learning off him and ask him to focus on nutrition! He isn't prepared for that. So, should we get someone who is a dietician?
  "Then again, in the hotel industry," said Varman, "F&B is a profit centre. In a hospital, F&B is a no-profit, no-loss department and the F&B head doesn't get spoken about. It is really the difference in the trades, but where do we strike a balance?"
  Karuna had adopted the hospitality industry model, probably because its managing director was from the hotel industry. "That may be one model," said Varman, "but it has its downside. The department managers of the hospital were managers from hotels, from banks, other companies etc. But none of them had any knowledge of the hospital business. While both hotels and hospitals symbolise care and comfort, hospitals are also about compassion and tolerance." It was worse in the case of marketing, explained Varman.
  The marketing team was chosen from the pharmaceuticals trade -- which was trained to sell products to doctors. "Here you are selling neither to doctors nor to organised customer groups," said Varman. ''The marketing team used the tried model of the doctor route, whereas the doctors aren't the only opinion leaders for a hospital," said Varman. "If you look hard, you will find that the true opinion leaders are the users themselves! So Karuna's marketing men became sales guys without a vision of where the brand should be or its positioning. They came from pharmaceutical firms and there you don't position a drug. You sell a drug for a specific illness. There is no positioning involved. You get the doctor to prescribe your brand and if he does, the patient will necessarily use it. "But when you are looking at the health industry you have to talk about positioning, make the branding focused. And that is what they did not do. Instead, they went to doctors and corporates and said, "look, we have MRI scans, we have so and so doctor", but what they did not talk about and, therefore, not sell, was the service aspect. They were talking about features, but not brand benefits! What they forgot or ignored was that those features could be easily acquired by anybody who can spend money!"
  Kayastha heard intently, as Varman continued: "So what they ended up selling was a product, whereas they needed to sell a service. As a hospital that claims to be a specialty unit, you're expected to have X-ray machines, MRI scanners and blood oxygenators. What's your USP is the way you administer healthcare. Does a toothpaste firm say we have automated tube-filling machines? That's only a part of how the product is produced, isn't it? In just the same way, our machines and equipment are only about how we produce our service, but it isn't the service itself. Having said that, how should we market or even define our service, based on all that I have said so far?"
  Kayastha could see Varman's anxiety clearly. "What is service?" he, therefore, asked. "You pick up a need that people have and fulfill it. In the case of Recovery, you must address how you will fulfill it. For that you keep talking about the consumer's need all the time. That's how Airtel sells its service. Airtel sells you your need to talk to people. 
  It says, 'use our service and this is why...' It will never sell you airtime without selling you your need. Therefore, airtime is the means to fulfilling the service. But what it sell is your need."
   Varman was delighted. "The problem at Karuna was," he said, "the marketing people sold the product without highlighting the need. The need those days was, 'I should not be investigated unnecessarily, I should be diagnosed by courteous doctors, I should be informed about my illness.' Although that need was being fulfilled at Karuna, it was not being sold! Karuna even has a mission statement on customer care, but where in all this do you see it being realised? It was not exemplified either in the selling or in its behaviour. That was the USP which was not communicated and eventually lost.
  "Instead Karuna's care level came down to this, 'Sit down, fill this form, you will have to wait, pay in advance, bring your doctor's reference...' Because we did not have competition we behaved like a monopoly -- take it or leave it. In fact, I'd even say that we got that attitude early on when suddenly everyone was talking about us, everyone wanted to consult at Karuna, everyone wanted to even get their common colds treated by us. We peaked suddenly... and plateaued equally fast,'' said Varman, wistfully.
  Kayastha was curious. "What caused this?" he asked.
  "I think it was the nursing staff strike last year," said Varman. "A small situation blew out of proportion. We had a patient, who was very ill, also very wealthy. So naturally, he came with the expectation that his money should be respected and value must be delivered. A nurse who came in to sponge him, inadvertently brought in water that was not warm enough. The patient was annoyed, he yelled at the nurse, the nurse was curt and abrasive, that made the patient madder, he called the doctor on his cellphone and let out his grievances. The doctor, of course, took his own time to come, he didn't think it was an emergency, but when he did come, he called in the nurse, blasted her and later suspended her. The next thing we knew, the entire nursing staff went on strike. It shook Karuna's management, they had not expected this!"
  Varman felt the situation was a result of poorly defined jobs, roles and authorities. "We need to craft our HR policies on lines of corporate entities, so that there is no role that is nebulous," he said. "In that case, they made the nurse the scapegoat, when, in fact, it was the doctor's fault for not being there on call. So you can see how HR functioned at Karuna."
'Recovery can derive its equity from its doctors and the after-care. also, like any other product, it too will need a good distribution network to deliver its services'
  Varman was clear that Recovery would derive its equity from its doctors and the after care, which he said was very high on their list of key result areas. "Our equity will be the doctors and our care givers -- the nursing staff, so we will headhunt and hand-pick our professional team," he said. "But yes, like any other product, we too will need a good distribution network to deliver our services. We will not indulge in franchising like Karuna did. That leads to dilution of quality. Karuna gave its name to some 15 clinics and nursing homes and called them 'recognised by Karuna'. That was foolish because none of those doctors are from the clinic, they don't even know what comprised quality. 
     Two, their diagnosis methods and treatment may not be in line with how we would do it. Patient handling cannot be handled like a computer institute.
    "But we will set up feeder units in far-flung places or even around the city which will be #our# clinics and the doctors will be #our# paid doctors who will conduct the initial examination and primary treatment at the clinic." 
  And that was where Varman derived the USP for Recovery. "We want to be more customer-responsive," he now said. "We believe that is what the consumer needs. As doctors, we can no more concern ourselves with the ailment only to the isolation of the patient as a human being with needs. But you have to take that apart and detail its constituents for us, so that we can convert them into actual features. For instance, if we want to be humane, we must not talk about bills first, but address the admission issue first. At Karuna, when a patient is being wheeled in, the staff ask for a deposit or mode of payment. Naturally, we will build in our own checks and balances. Therefore, you will also have to design for us a comprehensive system which will handle patient management capably. 
  "Thus, we want to decrease the number of days' stay in the hospital without compromising on treatment. Neither do we want patients to go through meaningless investigations. Our promise to people will be a conjoint examination, which will reduce investigations."
  "And what do you have in mind?" asked Kayastha.
  "We plan to set up conjoint clinics," said Varman. "Which means, rather than have departments of this or that, we will look at the composite service. Thus, in case of a heart problem, you will be seen by both the cardiologist and the cardiothoracic surgeon, as also the dietician and the lifestyle-change specialist -- all in one room, they will evaluate you and present a conjoint treatment.So, it will be a comprehensive solution-based treatment rather than one by trial and error. Yes, you will pay slightly extra to be managed by a team, but look at what you get.
    "We are planning to introduce the patient management concept, which is what a patient really needs. At Karuna, what we find are a lot of specialists but when a patient is admitted he has usually more than one problem."
  Explaining this, Varman went on to detail a case of patient mismanagement filed against Karuna. This cancer patient was going through chemotherapy, dialysis, had undergone a stomach surgery for cancer, apart from which he was also a heart patient. "So he had a kidney doctor, a chemotherapist, a cardiologist -- each with an orientation skewed to his area of expertise. As a result, no single doctor knew his case and each doctor was making suggestions that were not compatible with the other treatment. And when he died, no one could pinpoint the cause. But I do believe that there should be one overall patient manager doctor who can understand what the specialist is recommending and audit it against other complications."
  Kayastha had by now understood the broad aspects of Recovery's product. Varman was saying, "Karuna made the mistake of not positioning its product. In fact, I didn't think their product had a definition. Nor was there a marketing effort. What there was, was a frantic revenue drive, which resulted in loss of credibility. Now that I have told you our proposed profile, how do we go about positioning our product so that our uniqueness and our customer responsiveness stay salient as attributes in the minds of customers?"

PLAN IT RIGHT

  Ramnik Parekh is director of occupational health practice at Sedgwick Parekh Health Management.

  Dr Varmans vision of Recovery Clinics & Hospitals is nothing short of utopia. It not only promises every attribute valued by the consumer, but also an administrative set-up highly desired by any hospital administrator personalised customer care, trained personnel, committed doctors and so on. This is not to imply that the possibility of realising such a dream is remote. 
   Fame and revenue are both important for a good enterprise and are not mutually exclusive with customer care and medical excellence. With proper planning and sound marketing the prospect of establishing such an enterprise is bright. The case focuses on Varmans worry with regard to the positioning of Recovery. The problem can be tackled on multiple fronts by Varman, namely, through the marketing department, by way of internal marketing and administrative systems.
  The marketing department can play a crucial role in portraying the image of Recovery as a hospital which provides excellent medical treatment and complete customer care in an exclusive environment. The image is sure to strike a chord with the consumer. Having achieved the quality of services targeted by Varman, the focus will rest on communicating this to the consumer. This will entail designing appropriate marketing strategies. The strategies may include organising continuous medical education programmes aimed at doctors in cities to reinforce its image as an excellent medical provider, launching schemes for eye or kidney donation which will project its humanitarian outlook and render a caring image to the hospital. Recovery must also have a strong public relations department. Patients feedback must be obtained on a daily basis and their grievances attended to.
 Recovery can position itself  for its customer responsiveness by ensuring commitment by all concerned to the organisational goal.
  The hospital staff will play an important role in reaffirming the profile promised to consumers by the marketing department. To ensure that right message is communicated to the patient, it is vital that the staff understand and internalise the hospitals mission. Internal marketing is crucial in the service industry and every employee whos interacting with the consumer must market its services. This will help in building brand image. Courteous staff, particularly in the frontline departments, nurses trained not only in clinical management but also in patient interaction must be able to meet patients demands and convey the feeling that the hospital exists for the patient. If satisfied patients are to be made opinion leaders they may actually assume an important role as Recoverys salesforce.
  To ensure that the hospital staff look after patients, it has to be empowered to meet patients demands. A system of administration designed by keeping the patient as the central focus, and not the facilitation of internal processes will enable the staff to deliver what the hospital promises. For instance, the extent of authority given to an admissions executive to accept a night admission without the mandatory initial deposit will leave an agreeable impression on the patients mind.
  Also, Recovery must regularly train the staff. This will equip staff members to deliver services in a customer-friendly manner. For example, behavioural training and understanding the value of teamwork are suitable programmes for the staff.
  The other issues raised in the case relating to the organisation structure and staffing pattern can be resolved by understanding that the hospital industry like any other industry the requires professional management. People trained in the field of hospital management should be hired to manage a hospital since they understand the unique nature of its services. The question of having a CEO and director of medical services (DMS) is not an either-or question. A hospital needs technical medical and professional management input, which can be delivered when the CEO and DMS function in coordination. In order to balance the power between the two, the hospital can appoint a technical committee for decisions relating to upgrading any particular department. The committee will be responsible for cost-benefit analysis and determining the feasibility of the suggested plan for starting a new department, or buying expensive equipment.
