  Recovery can have a managing director who will coordinate the activities of the CEO and DMS and concentrate on strategic planning for the hospital. His function is, thus, distinct from that of the CEO, who is responsible for all non-medical areas and day-to-day operations.
  The other issue raised by Varman is the management of doctors. Taking a commission on doctors fees often works adversely, leading to confrontation. The best solution: have a partnership with doctors, which would ensure doctors commitment to the hospital. Similarly, the organisational problem of running an F&B department can be solved by recruiting an F&B manager from the hotel industry who will be responsible for preparation of food as per the dieticians plan. The quality of food will be his responsibility. The hospital also has a choice of outsourcing F&B services. However, even in such a situation, the dietician will remain indispensable.
  Varmans idea of having conjoint clinics is ideal and will require a high level of coordination. If coordination fails it could turn into an administration nightmare. Patient management will acquire top priority to ensure patient satisfaction. It would be a good idea to admit the patient under one doctor who refers the patient to other specialists if need be. The specialists must give a feedback in order to ensure comprehensive care. In conclusion, one can say that Recovery can position itself for its customer responsiveness by ensuring commitment by all concerned to the organisational goal and by delivering not only what it promises but more than what it promises.

POSITIONING THE BRAND

  Sharanita Keswani is the chief operating officer of strategic marketing consultancy Quadra Advisory.

  The first thing that I would like to emphasise is that medical services have reached a stage where they need to be treated as an industry. Having said that, the focus for Recoverys CEO is to perceive it as a business, which has been set up to satisfy specific consumers at a profit. My observations are: 
 * Identify the target consumer 
 * Define a clear value proposition
 * Develop a unique brand positioning
 * Define the structure needed to implement and support the above 
  This analysis will focus on value proposition and brand positioning, which are critical for the clinics strategy. Value proposition: Based on the target consumer, the CEO needs to define a strong value proposition. It is the template that drives all activity in an organisation. It defines the organisations reason for being.To quote Michael J. Lanning, a business or a value proposition is conceived and managed as the intentional delivery of a chosen scenario of resulting experiences to a group of potential customers. The resulting experience includes a series of related (physical or mental) events that happen in the life of the customer due to the action of the business organisation.
   If the enterprise can deliver a superior value proposition at a total cost that is below its revenues, the business succeeds. Varmans first job is to explore value-delivery options; make a choice of a profitable superior value proposition. Thereafter, all functions, departments, specialties teams etc must be designed. Finally, the communication will be delivered through the marketing team and delivery of the proposition by specialists and support staff in the clinic.
  What recovery needs to do is to strengthen its proposition so that the entire organisation is aligned to delivering the chosen value.
   I think Varman already has a winning proposition in mind that of a conjoint examination system for patient management. The concept is relevant to consumers, is unique and differentiated from competition. What Recovery needs to do is to strengthen its proposition so that the entire organisation is aligned to delivering the chosen value. Recoverys value proposition can be strengthened by answering the following question: who is my customer? What best alternatives does he or she have? If they do not do business with us, what will they do? We can see that in the case of Karuna, the value proposition had not been defined. There was inadequate consumer understanding, hence, the clinic was not delivering any significant value to its consumers.
    Once the CEO has defined the value proposition, the marketing director needs to translate this into consumer language and develop a unique brand positioning statement. Both value proposition and brand positioning mirror each other and are linked by one common element the organisations customers. They both draw inspiration from a deep consumer understanding that is focused on satisfying consumer needs in a unique manner and both drive organisational activity. Value proposition is broader, covers all organisational functions, helps to develop a total value delivery system, and is driven by the CEO. The brand positioning will further serve the purpose of defining inputs for communication and brand activity at every point of consumer contact. We define brand positioning as the target consumers reason to buy a brand in preference to others. 
 The team would need to understand the consumers needs in detail, their motivations, and need gaps, what would truly delight them. Based on the data available, I would like to define some of the elements of Recoverys brand positioning in the following manner:
  The target consumer: The Recovery consumer is a male or female, is discerning. The patient is fed up of being cheated by other medical institutions, which bring in specialists who dont have a forum to confer the right treatment.
  Core proposition: A holistic, patient care system with well-coordinated patient management teams.
  The patients functional reasons to buy: The Recovery clinic has a well-coordinated facility for patient care, it has strong patient management teams, which take care of each patient as a separate client account. It has world-class facilities and equipment, and has trained doctors.
  Emotional reasons to buy: When I go to Recovery, I have total faith that the treatment will be holistic. I wont be neglected, I feel reassured that the staff truly cares for my well being. Brand supports or reasons to believe the brand claims: The clinic has been set up by experienced professionals, it has world-class facilities, trained doctors and nurses, very conducive environment top-class hygiene standards; pleasant surroundings, patient recreation and recovery facilities.
  The brands personality: Recoverys personality is knowledgeable, efficient, transparent and, above all, soft and caring.
  We can see that by defining these brand elements, the marketing director will be able to link these up to define the brand positioning statement. These elements will form the basis for all brand activity. Recovery must have a strong public relations and a focused doctor-contact programme.
  The starting point should be the vision statement in the form of a value proposition, developed by the CEO. This, in turn, should be translated into specific brand elements, which would serve the purpose of clearly positioning the brand. The CEO must develop the vision and define a strong value proposition. The CEO must have top management abilities and must be (a) customer-driven (b) highly people-oriented (c) must be both topline and bottomline driver. A medical director who drives the technical function in the hospital must support the CEO. All relevant technical departments must, of course, be manned by medical specialists while support departments like accounts, F&B etc, can draw from other industries.
Designed by: Epsilon Systems - SQ-80, Hauz Khas Appts, New Delhi-16. Phone: 685-3301, 685-0878, 98100-06431

Case Study 23


17 Apr 2000		 ETHICS AND THE LAW
MEERA SETH
Advertising communication is meant to encourage consumption. is it enough if such communication is legally correct? Or should it also be ethical?
  Jatin Pandya rolled the ad copy line over and over in his mind. Something rankled. "The best fitness and health plan for you from Ambrosia fruit juices -- real fruit, real juice and no sugar!" went the main copy line. Jatin felt uneasy.
  "Something is wrong here," he said to Armaan Arya, the marketing manager. "Can we say 'best for health' when we know it contains preservatives and food colour?"
  "What's wrong with that?" asked Armaan. "It ceases to be the best, that is what is wrong," replied Jatin.
  "But that is hyperbole," pointed out Armaan. "All the detergent brands say 'best washes'. It is a manner of communicating the superiority of your brand."
  "You are talking about health, not fabric," said Jatin. "You could say 'a great way to plan your fitness drive' or some such thing. You are saying real juice, real fruit, no sugar... but you have not said 'and preservatives and food colour'. All you need is for a consumer to contest your claim."
  Armaan thought, then said, "Let's get a legal opinion, if that is your fear."
  Their lawyer Chetan Kaul said: "Advertising is a means of giving the consumer information, promoting competition in a market, thus offering alternative choices to the consumer. False advertising is when you say x=y and that isn't true. But if the ad is misleading, that falls in the ambit of unfair trade practice."
  Pointing to the ad copy for Ambrosia, Jatin asked, "Would you say this statement can mislead a consumer to believe that Ambrosia is best for health?" Armaan interrupted. "We are not saying Ambrosia is best for your health. The words are health and fitness plan. That is different."
  "Hyperbole like best, fairest, are permissible," said Kaul. "This is something a consumer will not take seriously -- and that is the issue. When Brand X says its television is the best, the consumer knows it is in a manner of speaking and he does not truly believe that Brand X is the best. But when a company tries to give credibility to its claim, it goes beyond mere hyper. Like if you say I am the best toothpaste because I score 98% whereas others score 80%, then that is a claim you are making and it goes beyond mere hyperbole. Then you are trying to give it a scientific basis. That enters the realm of false advertising. It is a misrepresentation under the MRTP Act.''
   ''Ambrosia may pass the legal test," said Jatin, "but ethically speaking, it won't be correct."
   "But you are not making a claim," said Kaul.   
"Then see this," said Jatin, pulling out another campaign for Ambrosia which showed a fitness instructor of repute, holding a tetrapack of Ambrosia Orange, and his words were, 'I trust Ambrosia for my fitness and good health...' "Now isn't that a claim on behalf of the brand?" asked Jatin.  "Depends... if he is an expert," said Kaul. "If he was a doctor, a nutritionist or a dietician, then those words could be deemed a claim made by an expert and could be contested. For example, if a doctor says Ambrosia is very good for health, the point is, has he really conducted a test? If he has, then has he conducted it in an independent manner? Has he done the test to deliver a certain result, was he financed by somebody? That will be considered an unfair trade practice. If a consumer complains that the ad is misleading or damaging, the MRTP Commission could grant an injunction that the ad be withdrawn."
   Armaan thought for a while. There was another campaign which lauded tetrapack drinks, stating that drinks in bottles stood a greater chance of contamination. At its heart, the campaign was part of Ambrosia's fitness and health positioning. But now he wondered if that could be contested by manufacturers of bottled drinks.
  "Comparative advertising is healthy, but you must be clear about the claims you make," said Kaul. "Clearly, you are saying Ambrosia is good because it comes in a tetrapack and bottled drinks are not as good. This is a direct attack on bottled drinks. Companies do not reveal all the parameters they have considered in arriving at their conclusion of 'best'. You may select some major ones. But the company may choose to highlight the trivial ones and ignore the major ones. These things happen and these are not strictly speaking provided under the law. To establish unfair trade practice, there must be prima facie evidence of damage or misrepresentation."
  "We are legally safe," said Armaan. "We will re-word this campaign, but our other campaigns have passed muster."
   But Jatin felt the other ads were misleading too. "We have to also take an ethical view," he said. "Legally you may not be making a claim, but please hear what your ad says." The Ambrosia TV commercial showed an ailing old man. His wife gets ready to extract juice from some oranges, but the daughter-in-law sweeps all that aside and pours out Ambrosia.
   Said Jatin, "This ad says to me: 'Ambrosia is convenient, Ambrosia is as good as fresh oranges, Ambrosia is good for the ailing.' That misleads."
   "Don't be absurd," said Armaan, "The proposition here is convenience." "Don't forget your primary platform is health and fitness," pointed out Jatin. " And in this convenience campaign you are creating an impression of 'also good for health'. I believe that as responsible advertisers, we have to be more concerned about the ethical than merely the legal. Many advertisers are resorting to this ploy. Take the ad for Signor Hair dye. The TV commercial shows a lady in a white overcoat, sitting behind a huge glass table, telling a consumer, 'But Signor is good for your hair...'
'The use of an expert lends credibility to an advertisiment's claim and comfort to the consumer. It is this sense of comfort that persuades the consumer to use the product'
   "What they have done is created an 'impression' of the lady being a doctor. That is misleading. You may be educated, literate, but if you are lulled into believing it is a doctor, then that certainly does give you comfort. And it is that sense of comfort which persuades you to use the brand."
  "But tell me," said Armaan, "where do they say she is a doctor? Did she carry a stethoscope? Did you see a red cross? So many people wear white coats! Scientists, lab assistants..."
  "Let's be fair Armaan," said Jatin. "The intention was to give you comfort. Otherwise why the white coat? In this ad the frame showing the lady appeared for exactly four seconds, during which time you saw a huge glass table, a white overcoat, a sterile sort of environment, not a boutique, or a school or a playground. You see a person who has the bearing of a person in a position of authority.
   "Now how does the viewer's mind work? The whole power of persuasion, which is what advertising seeks to play on, works on certain set images we have developed over time about doctors, teachers, housewives and white coats. Why do all toothpaste ads use men in white coats? The white coat creates an illusion in your mind that this is coming from a position of authority, from an expert.
   "Then again, look at the overall presentation. There is a diffident consumer, shy and inhibited who asks, 'but what about my skin, eyes?' And this lady who is shown in a position of authority says, 'Brand X is good...' The whole thing was a flash of three seconds and done so expertly that you were left with the impression that the medical opinion is that that dyes do not harm you."
   Jatin had by now won the interest of both Kaul and Armaan. He went on stating his point, "Now can you see how it misleads? From a legal viewpoint, 'Signor is good ...' does not make a claim. But it is a response to 'but my eyes...?' Sit on the viewer's chair and listen to the message that comes through. What you hear is 'as for your eyes, no harm will come if you use Signor'. And the point is viewers receive messages from the words, visuals and the general environment in the ad. It is the message that persuades and that message has to be ethically correct.
  "Then again, by putting that woman behind the table and lending an antiseptic aura to the environment, you have tried to pass her off as a doctor or researcher. Of course legally there is nothing there that indicates she is a doctor or that it is a clinic. But when you see that frame which lasts 3-4 seconds, sure enough the feeling is she is a person of medical authority. Again, legally safe, but ethically damaging. You have tried to create an impression of authority, of a clinical environment.
   "It's like this: in a court of law you will lose if you try to claim damages for being misled. But the fact is you tried to persuade the consumer with the use of an amiable voice behind a glass table, which person is clearly not your Vim-using housewife, not your Dove-using beauty... So who is she?
  Certainly a credible voice of authority. Believable, because she is advising the use of the brand in the manner of an advisor. Isn't that enough? Then again why address eyes if you did not already think that was an area of consumer resistance? By replying with 'Signor is good...' you have sought to pass that off as an answer to a nagging consumer anxiety. It's blatant! 
   
  Have you put a vast sample of consumers under test for six or seven years to validate the claim that your dye is not harmful? If they had, believe me they would not have made covert references. They would have screamed from the rooftops that we researched and we now have conclusive proof that it does not harm you.
   "There are so many such instances. Take the ad for Relief tablets for painful joints. They show this lady who is presented as a doctor. Again, there is a white overcoat. She says she has a backache. Another doctor says, 'then you must have a Relief'. She does, and she is soon feeling very good. Then the camera moves back and you see a person with a bandaged leg, on crutches, walking across the frame, helped by someone in white. Now, what impression are you left with? That this is a medical environment, that these people who are conversing are doctors, after all an advertiser would not show two artists or photographers or jewellers talking of painful backs in an environment where there are people in bandages? What is the message that you get from the collage of visuals? 'I am a doctor and I am the one who is using the product'." Armaan's mind was in a whirl. Yes, an attempt was being made to 'create an impression' of comfort and safety with authority. But Ambrosia wasn't doing any of that. "Where is Ambrosia misleading, Jatin?" he asked.
   "It's not 'where', but 'how'," said Jatin. "For that, let us answer the question, who is your ad targeting? Who is watching that ad and receiving messages? Kaul, you and I may discount a white coat. But look at the general viewer. Take a student who has yet to gain the maturity to say 'that ad is trying to overstate an issue'. Or take a clerk in a school. When he watches the hair dye ad he will say, 'Achcha, yeh to doctor saab keh rahen hain. So it must be right.' His worldly experience will lead him to accept the white coat as that of a doctor. Now take Ambrosia's ailing old man ad. My driver or even clerks in our office will see that as an endorsement of 'good for the ailing'."
 Jatin's anxiety also stemmed from a recent encounter with his dhobi's wife. She had had a baby after 11 years. Naturally, she and her family wanted to shower it with the best. She said to Jatin's mother, "Bibiji, I have seen an ad, I am giving my baby Brand X health drink. It is supposed to be very good."
   "Now what do you say to that?" asked Jatin. "That is the segment of the population which is being misled. Don't forget income levels and earning potential have shot up, so a lot more people are using branded products and are being impacted by these ads. Just as you and I aspire to own an LG multiple system refrigerator, they are aspiring to give their kids the best in foods for that is the first level of aspiration in their income class. 
   Then again when ads are produced in regional languages, what are you doing? You are targeting the not-so-educated, the not-so-literate. When you say Yeh dil maange more, you are not just targeting the Hinglish-speaking executives, but also the Hindi-speaking belt which covers just about every class and creed of consumer across varying income and education levels. And why not, after all they are today earning much better, however their earning is not matched by a commensurate awareness. So my driver gives his four kids Brand X health drink because it is supposed to be a complete food, little realising that it is so only in a manner of speaking and that a child needs much more.
   "As far as the company is concerned, it may have complied with the rules, but there is an ethical issue here. We may have a very narrow objective of increasing market shares, but at what cost?" he asked. The law needed to also look at the ethics of communication, he felt. Kaul could see the point Jatin was making. "The action that the law can take is not about the dhobi's wife getting misled or confused," he said. "Action is taken if the company makes a wrong claim or a misleading statement. Now the firm can very well say I did not mislead you, you got misled. So, there is a difference between the confusion in the mind of the consumer and willful deception by the company.
   What we are concerned with is positive deception by a company. Now we can have a number of consumers who can say I was misled. I thought this is what the brand does. So what? But has the company given you an impression that this is the claim they are making about their product?"
'A consumer receives messages from words, visuals, and the general environment projected by an advertisement. This message must be ethical because it persuades consumption'
  Turning to Armaan, Jatin said, "This is where we come to the line between what is legal and what is ethical. You may be legally on the right side, but your act could be unethical if your ad in words or pictures could lead the consumer to believe something about the product which is not true. Like the bandaged man in crutches in that ad. Or the aura of a doctor in the hair dye ad. Was the bandaged man adding value? The fact it was put there was meant to create an impression that this information or opinion is coming to you from an environment where there are usually people whose opinion you trust as being medically correct. Otherwise why use visuals?"     Armaan agreed. "Ads should have certain parameters," he said. "If it is a quantification ad then the firm should be asked how the numbers have been arrived at. That code for advertising should be validated upfront. Why should the commission not question purported research and numbers even without a complaint being lodged?"      Jatin mentioned a food advertisement that showed a PTA meeting discussing the influence of nutrition on a child's intelligence. The headmistress introduces the school nutritionist, a Dr Ravi Khera, who says he has researched four brands and displays his ranking: Brand W 89%, Brand X 72%, Brand Y 66% and Brand Z 50%. The ad which was for Brand X rated it the best because it contained the highest level of fortified vitamins and proteins.
   On probing it was found that the expert had in fact not conducted such a detailed research. Besides, the manner of giving weightages was found to be arbitrary. "That is misrepresentation," agreed Kaul, "To make a tenable claim, your results have to be published in periodicals so that scientists can challenge the validity and then it must be accepted by the body that must also accept the scoring system."
  "The point is not about right or wrong only," said Jatin. "It is about making limited claims in a short span of television commercial time and creating an impression that intelligence and mental faculties were dependent on the usage of Brand W. It is about parents who believe in the opinion of a projected expert and feed their children the brand, believing in the authority of the 'expert', and his expertise, in the ranking, that such tests were conducted and finally believing that what they are seeing is the universal truth on foods! In reality, no consumer calls in to ask, 'Now tell me what kind of parameters were tested and what sort of ratings did you use?' No, that does not happen.
  "Likewise, when you say my brand can turn you fair, help you become slim, you are targeting a vulnerable section. Their vulnerability will persuade them to try the product. A young man who is balding is emotionally vulnerable. You can say this product will make his hair grow. He will try the product. The sad part is that he will deny he is trying it, and if there is damage he will not talk about it fearing ridicule. Because it is exactly to stop the ridicule that he is using these nefarious products! Please understand that advertisers who mislead you belong to the same genre of people who ridicule or look down upon baldness. They know you are vulnerable and they will cash in on that mental state. So what view does the law take in these situations?" asked Armaan.
   "You must also consider the environment in which the commission is operating," said Kaul. "Very often marketing warfare has led companies to 'put up' complainants against their rival. The law is aware of this, hence, it does not readily take an extreme view. But in comparative advertising, if Brand X's claim that it scored 98% and Brand Y 70%, is again disputed, it is not enough. Sometimes, the court may not want to go into the details of 'is X better than Y?' After all, they will see if on the face of it such advertising is misleading or damaging. Moreover, when the ad uses an expert like a doctor, usually the MRTP Commission would feel 'they are experts, it is a matter of evidence -- if they say they have conducted these tests it must be true, we cannot stop the advertising'. So, the injunction could be denied. This prompts marketers to use 'experts' in their ads. It is unethical, but there it is.''
   "I feel the responsibility lies at our doorstep," said Jatin, "to ensure that our ads and communication are ethical. The consumer is not just the literate urbanite anymore. He is also the rural TV watcher. Companies are intelligent bodies. There is underlying consumer faith when you make a statement or create an impression. That trust cannot be abused. Ethics is paramount, even if it passes legal muster. And ethics has to be the driving force behind every organisation."


  Ethics In Advertising

Suzy Singh is a brand strategy consultant based in New Delhi.

  Jatin Pandya and Armaan Arya are embroiled in a conflict of values, where the probe for deeper motivations that lie at the source of a firm's actions becomes inevitable. While both appear to be right in their approach, Jatin's view emanates from the belief that an organisation must earn profits by delivering honest value to its customers via its products and services, while Armaan is focused on mainly selling the brand. I have intentionally used 'honest value' to stress the nature of benefits that a brand must offer to its customers. The simple way to evaluate whether or not it can do so is to ask the following questions:
   
  Can the brand genuinely deliver those benefits or are the claims superficial?
  Is the advertising and the brand's intended position being retrofitted to suit an existing brand as happens in most cases, or has the brand been developed with the goal of satisfying real consumer needs?
   Situations in which products are force-fitted into the consumer's life qualify as the seller's approach despite the organisational belief that it is marketing the brand. These are potentially more vulnerable to adopting deceptive communication since they are profit-driven as opposed to value-driven. A true marketing approach which is based on developing products to satisfy felt needs among consumers is intrinsically designed to deliver honest and meaningful value.
The best way to ensure that your brand delivers honest value is to show its terminal value, which defines the philosophy behind the brand 
  As a marketing manager, this is the first question Armaan should address. Is Ambrosia truly offering health and fitness benefits, and how? The use of tetrapacks for packaging could certainly be one of the substantiators to allow for retention of freshness and superior hygiene. However, this in itself is, at best, only a peripheral support to the position. It is not central to the formulation or recipe of the product or its performance, and hence, cannot be the only lever on which the fitness and health position rests. What Armaan should be exploring is alternate positioning options rather than developing different executions of advertising if he wants to deliver honest value. Ambrosia could well deliver refreshment or exciting taste more honestly than promising health and fitness. While Armaan might argue that neither of these positions is unique in a growing category environment, there are still several ways in which differentiation can be created on both platforms: by satisfying higher order needs, employing alternative segmentation strategies or using interesting target imagery. Honest value can emanate only from honest performance of the product.
  The best way to ensure that your brand actually delivers honest value is to articulate the brand's terminal value, which defines the philosophy and source of inspiration for the brand's existence. If Armaan were to actually attempt writing the brand charter on Ambrosia fruit juices, he might find himself in dialogue with his product development team rather than seeking a legal opinion from Chetan Kaul. Leading from the fitness and health position, it is logical to assume that Ambrosia's terminal value lies in the realm of creating a healthy world.
  If so, the firm should design and develop products that truly deliver fitness and health-related benefits or else give up that position.
  Often the desire to lend competitive advantage to a brand in the absence of real differentiators results in the search for superfluous supports that can lend authority to the brand as in Ambrosia's case. Since the brand is intrinsically incapable of delivering honest value, the marketer is challenged to invent a competitive edge that may or may not exist, but one that will undoubtedly motivate, persuade and even compel unsuspecting and vulnerable consumers to buy the notional benefit that the brand appears to be offering. Absence of a strategic branding approach that results in retrofitting relevance and gainful meaning to a brand lies at the very heart of deceptive advertising. 
   It isn't the intention to mislead the consumer that motivates advertisers and marketers to employ subtle verbal or visual 'persuasion boosters' in their communication but rather the lack of something worthy or different to talk about in their product that drives them to adopt such means. 
   However, I must mention that such deception is rarely unintentional. It is carefully crafted to leverage the brand's position in the consumer's mind. As in the ad for Signor hair dye, where the consumer's key concern about the negative effects of hair dye on the eyes is sharply brought into focus, and indirectly addressed to create an implicit impression that Signor is safe for the eyes while others may not be.
   White coats are used to lend authority to the brand in its area of expertise. Unfortunately, the urge to compete in a highly dynamic marketplace where speed of response governs the fortunes of many brands there is little or no time to validate the claims and perceptions being communicated either explicitly or implicitly. Even well-meaning organisations that are research-savvy sometimes succumb to the less arduous route of being legally safe than being guided by ethics in order to ensure that they outperform competition on pace.
   An interesting paradox in the argument that Kaul brings to attention is that "consumers do not take hyperbole seriously". Strangely, that is precisely what any brand employing puffery is trying so hard to achieve. Perhaps, it is this dichotomy that has given birth to the white-coat solution where the marketer hopes to persuade the consumer to take its brand seriously through the silent testimony of the expert in its advertising. It goes without saying that clearly this is an attempt to manipulate consumer action and belief.
   But in the final analysis, the decision about whether or not brands should employ deceptive communication must mainly be governed by organisational culture and brand values.


Don't Con The Consumer

  Manali Singhal is an advocate working with Amarchand & Mangaldas & Suresh A. Shroff & Co.

  The present case raises vital legal and ethical issues on product advertising. Today, advertising has become the most important means of empowering consumers with information, giving them alternate choices and promoting healthy competition in the market. Ads reach the most remote parts of India via various media. This reach entails an obligation of responsible advertising. But recently, there has been a growing tendency to exaggerate -- sometimes grossly -- as also to convey half-truths giving responsible advertising the go by.
   Having said this, one notes that there are different kinds of ads and the potential for harm depends on the type of product that is being promoted in the advertisement. For instance, advertisements lending a medical assurance must be based on objective and credible testing because the potential for harm caused by misleading the consumer here is the maximum. The same is true for any other item like food and medicines which may affect the health of the consumer. There is a high ethical and legal obligation on the part of the company to guide and inform the consumer. Misinformation negates information. Ads should not cash in on the gullibility of the general viewer.
   Branded products, whether soap or milk products, carry with them an assurance of quality and desired result. There are also comparative advertisements, where one brand touts its superiority to that of rival brands as seen in the toothpaste advertisement of Hindustan Lever Ltd (HLL), which claimed New Pepsodent was superior to Colgate.
  The claim of HLL that its product had 102% anti-bacterial superiority was not found to be true. The MRTPC held that the injury caused to public, in general, could not be compensated in terms of money as the feeling of being duped could not be evaluated in monetary terms. (This matter has been referred to a body of experts to verify the claim made by HLL.) Such advertisements must be based on adequate scientific data. In quantification advertisements, the figures and the numbers mentioned in the advertisement must be authentic.
  Companies have a high ethical obligation to guide the consumer. Advertisements should not cash in on the consumer's gullibility
    The ethical aspect of an ad is as important as its legal aspect. Ads use the media to persuade consumers and enable them to make a choice in the open market. That is why it is incumbent on the company not to mislead even by silence or omission.
   Legally, a false statement regarding a particular standard, quality, grade, composition, model, is an unfair trade practice under section 36A of the Monopolies & Restrictive Trade Practices (MRTP) Act. As laid down by the Supreme Court in National Lakhanpal's case, the object of section 36A is to introduce honesty in the relationship between the manufacturer and the consumer. When a problem arises as to whether a particular act can be condemned as an unfair trade practice, the key to the solution would be to examine whether it contains a false statement and is misleading. What effect does such a representation have on the consumer? Does it lead buyers to wrong conclusions?
  In the US, the law under the Federal Trade Commission Act of 1914 has declared 'unfair methods of competition' as unlawful. The US courts in various cases have held false and unsubstantiated advertising claims to be unlawful. The Perrier group was prohibited from falsely advertising that its sparkling mineral water was not processed or filtered before being bottled and from making false claims regarding the manner in which the water was carbonated.
     The issue can't be resolved by merely examining whether the representation is correct or incorrect in the literal sense. The position will have to be viewed with objectivity, in an impersonal manner. It is the impact on a viewer that is decisive.
  This case deals with the ad campaign of Ambrosia which contains information that is misleading: "The best fitness and health plan for you from Ambrosia fruit juices -- real fruit, real juice and no sugar." The hyperbole exaggerates the quality and credibility far beyond the reality. Equally, its claim that it is superior to freshly-extracted juice, by calling it real fruit, real juice and no sugar, seeks to conceal that it contains harmful ingredients like preservatives and colour. This advertisement, therefore, squarely attracts the provisions of unfair trade practice under the MRTP Act.
   The second advertisement shows the fitness instructor of repute holding a tetrapack of Ambrosia Orange which reads: "I trust Ambrosia for my fitness and health.'' The advertisement is certainly designed to induce a consumer to rely on Ambrosia in preference to "natural fruit juice" for fitness and good health. Prima facie, this advertisement is contrary to the legal and ethical obligation required in a fair advertisement.
   The third ad on the TV showed an ailing old man. His wife gets ready to extract juice from some oranges, but his daughter-in-law sweeps that aside and pours out Ambrosia for the old man. Convenience for a consumer is important but the efficacy of the product is more important. Clearly, the advertisement assures an ailing man that Ambrosia is a healthier drink.
   In the case of the advertisement for Signor Hair Dye, the advertisement suggests firstly that the hair dye is effective. Secondly, it is safe both for the skin and the eyes. It dodges the question of safety of the skin and the eyes by saying that it is a good brand. The advertisement conceals more than it reveals, clearly attracting the mischief of unfair trade practice. What is important is that the companies promote campaigns conscious of their legal and ethical duties. Is a consumer who purchases a product not entitled to fairness from the company?
  Companies marketing products like hair dyes, vitality pills etc., are not aware that they have a captive market and a vulnerable consumer. If these companies misrepresent facts, it would amount to unfair trade practice.
Designed by: Epsilon Systems - SQ-80, Hauz Khas Appts, New Delhi-16. Phone: 685-3301, 685-0878, 98100-06431

Case Study 24


1 May 2000		 CHANGE: TO BE OR NOT TO BE
MEERA SETH
Three years and Rs 17 crore later, mebrisk India discovered its system revamp was a completely futile exercise? But was it so profitless after all?
  Adip Arya was disconsolate. Plan Revamp, the system he had partly engineered, and put in place painstakingly four years ago, was today being torn to shreds by the new director of operations of Mebrisk India, Kevin Mathais. To make matters worse, Kevin had asked Adip: "Why have you done this? What did you save?"
  They had debated it for three days. The verdict was that Rs 17 crore which the company had spent on Plan Revamp was unnecessary. Uniquely, at the end of the three days, Adip could not fault Kevin's arguments. Worse, he even conceded that the old system could work, albeit with some changes.
  Confused and troubled, Adip met Hemant Trivedi, his boss and general manager (commercial). "How is it that I did not think about it?" he lamented. Hemant smiled. "Such is change," he said. "Three years ago, could we have even thought about getting yesterday's sales and stocks position in by today morning and at every location? It was unimaginable! Today, you have forgotten what it was like not to have information in real time."
  The events of the last four years flashed before Adip. It all started in 1997 when operations and finance was taken over by Parthiv Vyas. Parthiv was bothered by the climate of competition that had seized the environment. More than that, Parthiv, who came from an entirely commercially-driven environment at Delaware where he was general manager (operations), was irked by the virtual inertia of commercial at Mebrisk. The tug-of-war that raged year after year between sales and production could have been prevented if only commercial had been proactive, he felt.
  Almost immediately Parthiv told his team: "I am going to change you from being bean counters to businessmen. First, I am going to question everything that you have been doing. What is our working capital doing for us?"
  They went through a nerve-wracking exercise, at the end of which, Parthiv declared, "I want a zero or negative working capital. And this is how: one-month credit from the suppliers, a business cycle that is less than a month, and before you have paid for the raw materials you should recover money from debtors, thin stock positions say, three to four days, to allow for transit time and you will be making money out of thin air."
  Parthiv ran a fine toothcomb over every activity in the supply chain. Arduous debates later, he said: "I will ask my vendor to supply me on a just-in-time (JIT) basis. Every morning, every factory must be able to tell the vendor what it plans to make in the next three days, and that is what the vendor will supply. But at every factory, the mantra for inventories will be JIT. On the distribution side, he found that the production plan was not in sync with the sales plan. Parthiv blamed the performance parameters of the factories: man hours per tonne, machine hours per tonne and idle capacity reduction. 
  So, the factories produced what they desired irrespective of what the market needed, while proudly displaying never-before rates of man hours per tonne.
   And how did they do this? Irrespective of stockpiles in their stores, they produced what was good for the health of their performance indicators. "No wonder your closing stock of finished goods is made up of mostly large packs, while sales demand stands unmet for smaller pack sizes," pointed Parthiv.
  Annoyingly, no one would take ownership for the unsold stocks. The sales team said they had not asked for it. The production department said it was exactly as per plans. And the difference between sales forecast and actual sales was usually 60%.
  But these forecasts became the template for everything that transpired at Mebrisk, for production planning, ordering inventories... The whole pipeline got disrupted, and consequently, everybody pitched for higher safety stocks. "Can you see?" asked Parthiv. "One frantic phone call from Dibrugarh for 1,000 1-kg packs of cooking oil invariably sent the whole system into convulsions, resulting in frenzied stock movements from one depot to another, from one factory to another and unscheduled ordering of raw materials. And the unbeatable explanation for forecasts? 'But the markets are so dynamic!' '' mimicked Parthiv.
  "And you commercial men should have blazed your radar on the factories and examined why they did not carry stock as per forecasts!" said Parthiv. The resistance to changing the production plan was endemic in the system, from the factory manager who watched his machine hour per tonne to the workers who watched their bonus for delivering a certain productivity, which was measured in man hours per tonne. So, everyone resisted change.
'Early man didn't wait for James Watt to fire the steam engine to cover distances. He used a crude version of an angular plane, the wheel, the horse, and i'd say that's what powered watt to think steam'
  Parthiv was demanding a paradigm shift. "What I found is, if the sales forecast reached on time, it was incorporated into the production plan. If it did not reach by the fourth or fifth day of the planning cycle, production plans went ahead on their own. Naturally. So the first thing to do is to connect everybody -- factories with depots to regions to head office."Permissions and sanctions simply fell in place as Parthiv ordered every location to be provided with V-Sat connections at a huge cost, wiring the entire company across the country. The result was unbelievable. The information on stock levels reached head office every morning.
   Parthiv went on: "If I have to plan production for tomorrow, I must have data today." There were gasps blew across the length and breadth of the company. The men's room mantra went, 'Parthiv has flipped it.'
  Truth was that he hadn't. Data flowed in miraculously, much to the amazement of the team which slaved on it night and day. Parthiv's vision stayed: everyday's production had to be planned. Nothing would be produced if it was not planned. In other words, he was going to reduce the production planning cycle from one month to one week, and eventually, to one day.
  He did not stop at that. The company had a mix of products and pack sizes that were either slow moving or fast moving. Since demand varied erratically for its hygiene products and ready-to-cook products, it created bottlenecks in the production and trucking plans. It was worse in the case of small volume items like talcum powder. Thus, for instance, if he had 15 stock points in the country, then talcum powder had to be stocked at these points. The transport logistics demanded that a full truck be sent. As a result, the factories dispatched 150 tonnes of talc, when, in fact, the annual sales was just 22 tonnes. "That means we produce and stock eight times!" said Parthiv.
  Parthiv declared that it made sense to manufacture the entire year's need of slow-moving products and dispatch it to the zonal stock points (ZSPs), which he was about to set up. That way the production set up and re-set up runs were minimised.
   Rather than ship the entire production to the various depots, which usually resulted in uneconomic trucking loads, or large stock piles at every stocking point, Parthiv suggested the setting up of ZSPs which would carry each zones' needs of the slow-moving items. If such items were sent to the stockist, he would end up carrying more than his weekly requirement of the item and that was deemed cost ineffective. Therefore, such items were sent to the ZSPs and held in stock from where the dispatches could be made to the stockist based on his demand. So, ZSPs were set up and systems put in place. These would operate on a replenishment basis.
  The same system was put in place for faster-moving items like hygiene care. Whatever the stockist sold of these high-volume items each day was replenished the next day.
  As for the factories, one single objective was placed: 'everyday in the morning you will get a dispatch plan. You will merely dispatch and plan your next day's production plans.' The sales branches were assigned a volume target, but he also demanded accuracy of forecasts.
  And, thus, it went on for two years, to everyone's joy and profit. The plan was successfully executed, stocks were in sync and the old tussle between sales and production stopped. The factories got used to several changes to their production plan and, in fact, enjoyed the challenge. 
  Better still, working capital, which was 33% of turnover, came down to 3% and soon nil. In short, the new supply chain system was a work of art.   
  Then came January 2000, when Parthiv left to join the dot.com bandwagon. Kevin took over and the unexpected happened. After a month of patting the boys for winning the Y2K war, Kevin turned his sights on Plan Revamp. Soon after joining, he said to the commercial team: "This is too high a cost to pay. Additional space cost of the ZSPs, and the entire system management cost is prohibitive. I don't think anything has been achieved."
  "But look at the working capital savings!" said the commercial team.
  "That is manageable in a different manner," said Kevin. "Can the production logistics not be changed? Can't the truck loads be rationalised differently? Let the factory worry about what to produce and when to produce, let it do the logistics, the dispatch mixes. For example, if the factory at Pune is supplying jams to Nashik, then typically, the Nashik stockist's godown could be a conduit for dispatches to the stockists in Maharashtra. It should be the point at which load mixing takes place for other depots in western India. So why have ZSPs?" And he went on to detail a very complex but tenable argument for his case.
   "Likewise," he went on, "factories can produce the low-volume items in bulk, that's okay, but there is no need to stockpile these items at the ZSPs. Move it to the stockists directly and hold them there." In short his objective was: shift stock- keeping back to the factories or to the stockists. Move production planning back to the factory, which will decide what and how much, and accordingly, shipments will take place, it will not work backwards. Sales will not tell production what to produce. In essence, Kevin was reverting to 1997. Sizing the mood, he said: "Plan Revamp is pointless. Moving stocks from factory to zones is pointless. Stocks can be produced in bulk, but should be stocked at factories. In fact, if I have reduced working capital, stocks, I should also reduce space." Turning to Adip, he asked: "How did you agree to Plan Revamp? What have you saved?
  What had changed was the comfort level that IT had brought into Mebrisk. It had changed the whole paradigm of running the business. "Then you discovered IT," said Kevin, "and put together a change. It may have reduced the chasm between sales and production plans, but your working capital savings are illusory. For all it had done was shift the stocks from the factories to the zones."
  Now in less than three years, they were moving three steps ahead. As Hemant now said to an agonised Adip: "Today, a factory and a depot are so well-connected, despite some being in remote places, that we don't need anyone either from Bangalore or Uttar Pradesh to speak to each other. The system will tell them what to do.
   You don't need a manager in UP to monitor it, you don't need an officer in Ludhiana to chase trucks, you don't need the marketing cell to grope around watching slow-moving items. The factories and stockists today are well-connected and wired, they can handle it independent of head office sleuths! 
   The problem is we have never used IT at this level. Certainly, not in the sales and stocks system," ended Hemant.
  But for Adip, life was changing so fast that he was unable to even stop and evaluate his iterations.   
  Hemant went on, "1997 was when e-commerce came to us from a foreign consultant. So we said, to internalise the value of e-commerce, we must do all this. But what we missed was that it is not the new system that is reaping results, but a new way of managing via e-commerce. We have just stepped on the learning curve from an era when we did not have the tools to put a viable system in place.
  "Creating a system which can capture sales, stocks, churn out data and information is possible for most organisations. But only when it is woven into a communication system that can network all the data points and move the data back and forth that we have intelligent, useful, actionable information everyday. As a result, we can create a more meaningful and result-oriented supply chain system as is being suggested by Kevin. Yet, who knows? Three years down the line, even this may appear inefficient!
  "Today, things have become so dependable, easy that you can revert to the factory-driven planning and production system. But that is viable only because you have a communication system which is so strong that you can make sure that factory and sales do what they are expected to do. Today, your information system and network is so intelligent that when a stockist sells something, the data simply goes and sits in the factory system. You don't need any of the old intermediaries to hound, watch and holler. Everything is online and immediate. So, we have the software, hardware and the communication networks to make it possible."
'The difference between kevin's amazement and your embarrassment is infotech. It's a difference of more than two years. Without it, managers at mebrisk would have moved on without the learning'.
  Adip was not consoled. "That makes me look like a joker!" he said. "Kevin's argument is fair, but how do I justify my 1997 decision? For me to explain to him the situation that was prevailing in 1996 was difficult. I told him we were producing one thing when we needed to sell other things. He was amazed. Here is Kevin, five years younger, 10 years smarter, speaks in bytes and chips, takes one look at my Plan Revamp and gasps!
  "He has come to Mebrisk when everything is available. He has come after the entire country has been wired. He finds it difficult to comprehend that as recently as the late 90s, fundamental stuff like daily sales, daily stocks, who is sending what and how much to whom etc., was not possible. That until 1997, we had seven managers chasing truck routes, looking for trucks that had left the factory four days ago and not reached the depots! Yet, there was always tremendous activity. But no optimality," said Hemant.
  "The difference between Kevin's surprise and your embarrassment is IT," said Hemant gently. "And it is a difference of just two and a half years. This change is terrific for organisations. Maybe Rs 17 crore went into it. But are we saying this was a waste? If it hadn't happened, would this whole revolution in IT have happened at Mebrisk? These are what I call step changes. You can't reach level three without traversing level one or two. If the 1997-2000 period had not happened, we wouldn't be at this stage of evolution today.
   "In the 80s, when we hired a consultant to devise a system, we expected it to last 10 years at least. That was because the environment did not change a great deal, did not demand so much agility from companies, competition was not so sophisticated and intelligent. 
Today, smaller companies have the nerve to invest Rs 15 crore to wire up the whole country and every retail outlet, just to be able to know who sold how much.
  "Recall 1984 when the first batch of computers came in, what did we say? Rs 1.8 lakh per PC? No, no, too much money! Don't forget that too was an opportunity to move over to more sophisticated methods, which we bypassed. Yes, we had a 1406 mainframe, but it did not proffer the ability that we could have had with PCs. Again, that was an era when it was not even conceived that PCs could lend sophistication and agility. By the time we invested in PCs it was 1990. We lost five years of learning, because it was only in 1990 that we started experiencing a more agile and efficient operation in a number of areas.
  "So it is like this: the difference between having made this change three years ago and not having done it, is similar to what we lost by not embracing the PC culture in 1984. You must change with technology, that is how you learn to appreciate the change and decide in what direction you want to grow. I'll say the direction of your growth becomes a choice available when you are willing to enter the tide and be tossed around. Not by standing on the banks and waiting for the tide to change.
  Even today what are they telling me? 'Listen, wait, naya package aa raha hai, it will be Y2K compliant, it will be all comprehensive,... abhi ruk jao.' But you have to jump in at some stage, spend the money, learn, reiterate, spend more money, modify, revise... only then the learning takes place. Early man did not wait for James Watt to fire the steam engine so that distances could be covered. He used a crude version of an angular plane, the wheel, horses and I'd say that's what powered Watt to think steam.
  "Today, we are wiser. Having done it four years ago, we have realised what is possible. But if we hadn't done that, a generation of people would have moved on without the learning. And they would have reached a stage in their evolution when the change would not have impacted them. And as many people who participate in this start appreciating it, start thinking like James Watt and contribute to new thinking.
  "The environment is never going to be static. You have to start somewhere, either 1997 or 2000. Those who adopted the change in 1985 made wise changes in 1990, those who made the change in 1995 will make wiser changes in 2001 and those who didn't will battle those who have adopted change, then concede, then start learning in 2001 by which time others have covered bigger ground."
  Patting Adip on the back, Hemant said: "Don't fret. It's the price we pay for evolution. If this opportunity had not been participated in, it could have created serious lapses in our strategic thinking. Today if you can ask, 'why didn't I think about this?', it is because you energised your mind once to think differently. If you hadn't, you would not be perceiving the difference today."

SHAKING THE FOUNDATION

Saurav Adhikari is the president of HCL InfiNet, an ISP operating in the B2B & B2C space. 
 The ecommerce era arriveth. Poor Adip Arya. Little did he know that operation revamp would cause a turbulence that would reverberate across Mebrisk. Actually, there is little to fault Adip Arya for. Nor Hemant Trivedi. Nor Parthiv Vyas.
  In fact, I would fault Kevin Mathias for his roughshod ways in introducing his e-commerce initiative. In fact, had Mathias seriously dwelled on the historic basis for operation revamp, he would have found little conflict between Vyas's initiatives and what he was setting out to do. In fact, it is my contention that Vyas laid the basis on which Mathias could subsequently build on.
  Let us quickly look at the issues in this case of operations revamp at Mebrisk:
  First is the critical issue of the production -- sales conflict and the wide variance in the sales forecast -- plans.
  Second is the issue of productivity measures at the factory level and, importantly, lack of inventory level measures at the sales end.
  Third is the issue of the Rs 17-crore investment in V-Sats and communication infrastructure to drive operation revamp.
  Fourth, the immediate benefits of a tight process re-engineering covering the entire supply chain.
  Fifth is the change of guard from Vyas to Mathias, coinciding with the e-com revolution.
   I will attempt to answer these issues not necessarily sequentially, but in support of my previous contention that Vyas laid the basis for the e-commerce initiatives to follow. 
  The e-commerce wave has rolled out at a relentless pace worldwide. Unlike physical assets and technology which require long lead times and absorption rates, IT can be more easily configured into a company's operating structure, subject of course to the willingness to change.
The paradigm shift that vyas engineered was significant. He shook up a lethargic system and made it an accurate forecasting base model 
  Too much has been made of the mystique of e-commerce. In reality, e-commerce is nothing more than commerce transacted over the Net using all the capabilities that existing hardware, software and connectivity that comes with it, which enables business to be more responsive to the entire business cycle.
  Today, a business is no longer seen as individual elements comprising sales, marketing, production, logistics, finance, as part of the internal enterprise. Nor are suppliers, transporters, warehouses, distributors seen as distinctive elements of the external enterprise. The concept today is of an integrated extended enterprise where all elements, both upstream and downstream, are linked into one chain using a host of IT applications such as enterprise resource planning, supply chain management and customer relationship management (CRM). Even the consumer today gets linked into the extended enterprise with CRM, thanks to the power of the Net to seamlessly do all this. This phenomenon even in the mid-90s was not forecast by many enterprises. Witness the moves by many large corporates including General Motors and Ford, who are Web-enabling their business so as not to be washed away by the tide of e-commerce. Yesterday's decision for many of them seems irrelevant, if not antiquated, in the context of the Net economy.`
  So what did Vyas do right ? First, he brought a sense of discipline into Mebrisk's planning cycle. A difference of 60% between sales forecast and actual sales is unacceptable and reflective of lax management. The fact that the production team could go ahead and produce irrespective of market needs just to meet man-hours-per-tonne criteria is patently absurd.
  Second, Vyas introduced productivity and incentive measures in tune with the requirements of business. Producing to plan and forecasting accuracy within plus-minus 3% was essential. Without this basic alignment between production and sales, two commonly antagonistic departments in every corporation, no progress could be made.
   Thirdly, vendors were put on to just-in-time as an integral basis for planning and working capital reduction.
   Fourthly, he invested Rs 17 crore in V-Sat connections. This linkage allowed free and easy flow of data leading to high accuracy in the planning process. In fact, this laid the basis for the e-commerce data flows that Mathias was to inherit in Y2K.
  Finally, and most importantly, the paradigm shift that Vyas engineered at Mebrisk was his most significant contribution. He shook up a lethargic systems and made it work. And what did Vyas do wrong ? 
  The only accusation could be that he did not automate Web-enable the sales, logistics and production systems. But logically this had to happen over time and only after the business process had been reengineered.
  Today we take Virtual Private Networks and enterprise- wide software for granted. At the time Vyas was trying to introduce his changes, it may have been inappropriate to introduce these without a sense of discipline. Despite wide availability of software, forecasting still remains the domain of sales managers, as subtle changes in market trends and demands cannot be forecast with accuracy without the correct inputs by software. As we know, good input leads to good output.
  What Mathias shook Adip Arya with was a historical progression from the era of system-driven to software and e-commerce-driven enterprises. If only all of us have the ability to forecast the future with great accuracy we could all be visionaries.
  Vyas, in my opinion, laid the basis for the e-commerce initiatives that Mathias found so easy to initiate in a more disciplined Mebrisk. Bravo Vyas! Good luck, Mathias! And Adip, you don't need to fret. Enjoy the e-commerce era.

A STEP IN THE RIGHT DIRECTION

  V.V.S. Mani is asssociate vice-president (management consulting) at Siemens Information Systems.

  In a large number of organisations, when decisions pertaining to information technnology taken a few years back are reviewed in today's context, many appear to be wrong or inadequately appraised. This is largely due to significant growth and changes in technology, combined with substantial reduction in prices of IT products, brought about by intense competition in the market place. 
 Perhaps, what is required is a shift in the traditional way of appraising investments in IT
  The "engineering" mentality of appraising projects has to give way to one that evaluates, inter alia, non-quantifiable benefits that accrue from investments in information technology. Decision makers in most organisations are neither sensitive enough to understand IT -- its capabilities and limitations --- nor are the IT personnel savvy enough to comprehend business requirements and their nuances.
   Mebrisk has not been an exception. Three years ago, while reeling under competitive forces in the market, it felt the need to improve its operational efficiencies. The supply chain, the backbone of Mebrisk was handicapped by an inefficient forecasting system (which was the basis for production, procurement and stocking decisions), delayed availability of data on products and stocks, a misaligned performance measurement system for the factory (the measures had no relevance to satisfying customer needs).   
  Surely, considerable amount of time and effort would have been spent on resolving inter-functional conflicts. The results were there for anyone to see: significant forecasting error, mismatch between market requirements and inventories and an inflexible attitude at the factory. What was required for Mebrisk was a system that provided information on all the relevant supply chain parameters, perhaps, on a real-time basis, which would provide: 
  A common database for all functions / departments to aid decision-making and resolve inter-functional disputes. 
  A flexible manufacturing set-up that reacted fast to the market and is not constrained by manufacturing efficiencies.
   A shortened planning horizon which would reduce the ill-effects of forecasting errors, and allow managers to concentrate on value-adding activities rather than 'chasing' data.
The experience of integrating infotech into the main business, is the most valuable benefit that has been gained from earlier investments
  Parthiv Vyas, the man who took over the operation and finance function in 1997, recognised the needs of the company correctly. As a pre-requisite all the depots, factories, regions and the head-office were networked. Vyas changed the method of planning, realigned the factory performance measures to be market-oriented, created buffer stock points to take care of product-mix variations. His decisions were proved right by the fact that stocks were in sync and the working capital reduced from 33% of turnover to 3%, which is very significant, and the production function became flexible enough to meet market requirements.
  The decision about creating separate zonal stock points may be questionable but the project was definitely a success. The investment in IT for this project was quite significant (Rs 17 crore). But then, the benefits justify it. Looking back after three years, the investment in the project appears to be high. The price of enterprise resource planning, for example, are about 50% of what prevailed four years back. Should this mean that organisations need to wait for three years to take any IT initiative? 
 Three years for an organisation in a rapidly changing market environment is a long period. The benefits that have accrued in terms of improved supplies to market and reduced inventories have justified the investment in IT. While these are quantifiable benefits, it is important to understand and appreciate that Mebrisk as a company has built the capability to become market-oriented (and not manufacturing-dictated), to use new technology and weave IT into the main fabric of its business.
   Now Mebrisk is capable of further forays into the use of IT. It is the experience of introducing, familiarising and integrating IT into the main business, which is the most valuable benefit that has been gained from earlier investments. IT has not been left as a standalone cell engaged in processing data. 
  The next initiative will be to look at e-commerce based on Net technologies which offer a exciting cost-efficient supply chain models where trading on the Internet is child'splay with benefits of speed and almost zero cost of transactions (barring physical movement of goods). Unlike most firms, Mebrisk has the experience in dealing with organisation-wide changes, and e-commerce is one of them. 
   E-business helps organisations to operate at a never-imaginable rate of agility. A critical question: when should a company go in for e-business? Option one: market is highly dynamic and hence, the responsiveness of an organisation requires e-speed. Option two: The market is not that dynamic but company e-enables the processes to set new rules in the market and gives customers more than what they expected. Either of the alternatives requires a high level of change management, more so the second option. With experience behind them, Mebrisk has developed the capability to manage quantum changes. Hence, it can leap into the future with a high level of optimism.
  Could Mebrisk have adopted e-commerce initiatives had they not gone through the first phase of change? Difficult to answer with certainty, but based on the experience of large and mature (old) organisations, one can boldly say the risks of failure would have been far higher and hence, the costs too. The leadership organisations are those that set the trend, albeit at a higher initial cost, but that is a small price to pay for long-term success.
Designed by: Epsilon Systems - SQ-80, Hauz Khas Appts, New Delhi-16. Phone: 685-3301, 685-0878, 98100-06431

Case Study 25


8 Nov 1999		 THE SIGNS IN THE AISLES
MEERA SETH
The Signboard Advertising The New Pasta From Chef's Choice Must Be Placed In The Right Place To Attract Shoppers, So, Where Exactly Would That Be?
  Pramod Ahuja stood long at the entrance of Shop Point supermarket and scanned the store, looking at all the signboards around. Fifteen days ago, he had commissioned a signboard for Chef's Choice's new pasta, Dente -- a tubelit colourful four by three -- which he had asked to be positioned strategically at Shop Point. But the signboard was nowhere in sight.
  Pramod, the sales director of Chef's Choice, then sought the attention of the store manager, who led him to a spot next to the alley that stocked rice, wheat flour, lentils, spices and sugar. He was numbed. There stood his signboard, lost in a corner, where the lighting was not exactly beckoning attention. Worse, right in front of it lay two huge stacks of wheat flour. All that could be seen even from up close was the top two feet of the signboard -- certainly not the message line. Annoyed, he asked for the flour bags to be removed. Meanwhile, he called Anuj Patki, his assistant sales manager and asked him to reach the store immediately.
  The space now cleared, Pramod viewed his stunning signboard with pleasure. He adjusted the stand, cocked his head on one side, viewed it again, walked back 10 steps, surveyed the board, turned left and surveyed the supermarket area that led to the signboard.
  Yes, that was where the shoppers came in from. But no! The signboard faced the alley at an angle, which meant the shoppers entering the store would not see it, or even if they did, they would have to turn around to study it.
  Pramod walked away and stood near the cosmetics counter which ran perpendicular to the grains alley... and watched. Shoppers walked straight into the alley without even glancing at the signboard. Pramod went up and turned the board around, so that it now faced the entering shoppers.
  Next, he decided to go into the alley to see if the signboard impacted the shoppers. He ambled down, trying to appear like any shopper, glancing at the shelves, eyes darting now and then to study the shoppers... until he reached the very end of the shopping alley.
  Dente was not among the wares. For that matter neither were Maggie, Top Ramen, Bambino or Spiro.
  Pramod was looking around for the store manager when Anuj strode in. "This is absurd!" said Pramod in a half-whisper. "That signboard has been stuck in an obscure corner and you didn't even think of the clutter. Have you been monitoring it? And where is Dente? It's not even on the shelves!"
  Anuj was confused. He had personally placed 30 packs of Dente only two days ago. That's when he realised Pramod's mistake. "You're looking for it in the wrong alley. 
  Pasta is over there, with all the ready-to-cook foods," he said as he began to lead Pramod two alleys away.   
  Pramod was annoyed. "Then why is the signboard here?" asked Pramod. "Shouldn't it be where the shopper finds pasta?"
   "But there is no place there -- no wall space," said Anuj.
  "Find a solution!" said Pramod in a low growl. "The idea behind having that signboard announcing a new product is to convert the message into interest and interest into purchase. And that means placing the product within two minutes of the product announcement. For that is how long a message remains fresh in a shopper's mind. His mind says Dente, Dente, Dente, and bang! he sees Dente and picks it up."
  "Let's take a look at the pasta alley," he said to Anuj. As they walked past the two alleys, Pramod noticed some stickers on the dividing walls: Soon arriving, Chef's Choice range of healthy soups -- Cup-a-day'.
  Pramod winced. Cup-a-day was launched eight months ago, but the stickers remained. What was worse, it was likely to confuse and even deter prospective shoppers of Cup-a-day, he thought. Turning to Anuj, he said: "This is real absurd. Don't we have a system that also pulls off old point-of-purchase material (POP)?"
  By now, both men were facing the pasta shelves. The top two shelves were piled high with Maggie on one side and Burly's on the other. The third shelf was an assortment of Top Ramen, Bambino and a few packs of Dente, while the fourth shelf was entirely Dente. Pramod watched the shoppers pick up multiple packs of Maggie. That was natural, he thought to himself. Just then a lady came up, accompanied by her teenage daughter. "Let's buy Top Ramen," urged the girl. "Remember the ad? Shahrukh was too good!" The mother picked up four packets of Top Ramen noodles.
  Pramod nudged Anuj. "Go on!" he said. Anuj walked up to the lady and asked: "You just chose Top Ramen. Not Maggie?"
  "We always buy Maggie. When these kids are studying for an exam they are perpetually hungry. Maggie is easy that way," she said. Smiling, she said: "My daughter likes the new Shahrukh ad so much, she wants to buy Top Ramen."
  "Since you eat so much pasta, you must have tried Dente?" asked Anuj. The lady looked thoughtful. "No..." said the lady. "Never heard of it."
  "Oh, I just saw a signboard for Dente, fortified with vitamins and all that," said Anuj casually. "What signboard?" asked the lady. No, she hadn't seen the signboard.
  "That's what I mean," said Pramod later. "She remembers Shahrukh so she buys Top Ramen, but had she seen the Dente signboard she may have reached for the Dente packs and read it. Even bought it. Shoppers are strange animals. There is no saying why they buy. Supermarkets are a marketer's delight. It's a sure-fire vehicle for impulse purchase."   
  Anuj wouldn't agree entirely. "See, many are walking around with lists. They know what they want," he said. "Therefore, if a shopper has come to buy Flora cooking oil, she will buy just that, not Saffola."
   "Sure," agreed Pramod, "There are some products, typically cooking medium or toothpaste where brand loyalty is high and rarely does a shopper switch over. But in casual food or snack food items, you will find a high level of adventure. So, there is a list and much of the list is bought.
 But examine their cart and you will find at least 40% of the contents don't feature on their list. They buy what they see -- a product or some message that has just been introduced into their database which keeps ticking till they encounter the product. Want to test it? Okay, this lady is stopping at the soaps section, go, ask her 20 questions."
  The lady picked up two each of Rexona and Imperial Leather. "So you are a Rexona family?" said Anuj. "Nothing like that," said the lady, a trifle self-conscious. "I just picked it up."
  "What brand did you come to buy?" asked Anuj. "I change my brand once in a while," replied the woman.
  "Okay, let me ask you that differently," said Anuj in a friendly manner. "What brand were you looking for? Why Rexona and not, say, Liril?"
  "Oh, there is this offer on Rexona. Buy two, get one free..." said the lady with a grin.
  "That's nice," said Anuj. "But you must have a family brand..." The lady laughed. "Not really, just Cinthol. Over the years our bathroom has smelt of Cinthol, somehow one gets used to things like that. But we also use Lifebuoy Gold, Lux..."Pramod smiled at Anuj. Walking back towards the pasta shelves, he said: "See? She saw all the soaps, surveyed, decided on Rexona. It's not even her brand.
    So, you see how the psychology of buying works? It's fascinating Anuj -- there is a lot to learn by just watching these shoppers. This place is like a virtual TV screen. Try and hear what is going on in their mind. Watch their eye movements. At what point in their browsing are they pausing, getting distracted? Isn't that the point where your POP and signboard should come in? So, where can we place the signboard so that it is noticed? Now, standing here, can you see the signboard?" he asked.
  "How can you, Pramod?" asked Anuj. "It is two alleys away." "Precisely!" replied Pramod. "This is the foods alley, the place where new food products signages must appear regularly. But our Rs 1.5-lakh signboard for Dente pasta is sitting next to Annapurna Atta! What's the point? Or it should be the first enormous thing you see when you enter the store!"
   Anuj examined the signboard area. Shoppers came in from the west end of the store, picked up their trolleys from the right side alcove and rode into the first alley, the nearest one -- cereals, milk food, cooking oils, ghee... Zigzagging through seven alleys, they emerged at the exit of the eighth alley where the bulkier items like rice, wheat flour, lentils and sugar were stocked. It was the point where Dente's signboard stood, bold and bright, but right in the way of the overloaded carts and exhausted shoppers.  
  "Well, they see it as they are leaving," said Anuj. "That's pointless, isn't it?" said Pramod. "Why do I want a Rs 1.5-lakh signboard at a place where shoppers are not going to be impacted? At a point in their buying process when it does not matter anymore? If they are going to see it while leaving, if you can't get their attention as they are entering, you may as well make a bonfire of the signboard. More likely they will see it then!"
  "Please understand supermarkets are designed for impulse purchases, for spot decisions," he went on. "It is not a place where you collect information, store it and say, 'Okay, next time I will buy Bristo's pet food.' The decision to buy happens on the shop floor, on the spot... tadaak, like that! You see, you decide, you buy." 
  Pramod was by now unhappy at the way the whole store was laid out. "Did you notice where Dente was placed?" he asked Anuj. 
 "Fourth shelf..." said Anuj, ready for more outbursts from Pramod.
    "And didn't I say it must be right at the top? At eye level?" asked Pramod. "It must be the first pasta you see as you hit the section. No-no-no-no-no! This is a waste of all our efforts. Fourth shelf! You know when you see the fourth shelf? When you haven't been able to make a decision on shelves one, two and three. When a shopper has made her decision on shelf one and left, your brand sitting on shelf four has just died. And it will rest there in peace till our bankers retrieve it to recover our loans! And where were our POP danglers?"
  "At the head of the alley," said Anuj, hopelessly.
  "What's the point?" asked Pramod. "If you have the POP at the start of the alley, why didn't you place the product right there? The point where you inform must be the point of display and sale. That's the role of merchandising. You can't have product information at one point and display at another. Shoppers don't cause such connectivity. The buying decision is more random than linear in a supermarket."
